interval training: High
intensity training technique
that varies load, repetition,
distance, and rest using a variety
of exercises such as swimming,
running, and cycling.

You  develop  the  immediate  energy  system  by  engaging  in  exercises  that  develop  high
levels  of  muscle  tension.  Research  suggests  that  merely  having  high  levels  of  strength
is  not  enough  for  powerful  movements.  You  must  develop  the  capacity  to  exert  force
rapidly during very specific movements. Training for these activities includes resistance
training, plyometrics, short sprints (i.e., starts), and body weight exercises.

Fitness for Sports and Exercises Lasting Three
Seconds to Two Minutes
These movements emphasize the non-oxidative energy system by training the muscles
to  generate  substantial  power  for  short  bursts  and  quick  recovery. This  type  of  fitness
is  valuable  in  tennis,  football,  basketball,  volleyball,  soccer,  softball,  field  hockey,  and
downhill skiing. In these sports, the other energy systems are also important and should
also be trained.

Fitness  for  this  metabolic  component  is  best  developed  through  interval  training.
Interval training involves repeated bouts of high intensity exercises. Usually, a short rest
occurs between each exercise repetition. Fitness is highly specific‚Äîone must overload
the  body  so  it  adapts.  For  example,  train  with  cycling  intervals  to  improve  fitness  for
cycling  and  swimming  intervals  to  swim  faster.  This  type  of  fitness  is  also  intensity
specific. If the goal is to run faster for 800 meters, you will not improve much if you run
repeated 800-meter runs at a slow pace or jog slowly for longer distances. The bottom line
is this‚Äîtrain fast if you want to move fast.

Fitness for Sports and Exercises Lasting More
Than Two Minutes (Endurance Exercise)
As  exercise  duration  increases,  the  body  begins  to  rely  more  on  the  oxidative  energy
system. This requires lungs that can deliver oxygen to the blood stream and eliminate
carbon dioxide and help to control fatiguing hydrogen ion levels. The body requires a
strong heart and blood vessels to transport blood rapidly to its trillions of cells. And
you must have a well-developed chemical system within the cells to process fuels and
oxygen rapidly.

The most important benefit of endurance exercise is its ability to improve the capacity
of the heart, lungs, and circulatory system to deliver oxygen to the body‚Äôs tissues. In a
fit  individual,  the  heart  pumps  more  blood  per  beat,  blood  volume  increases,  resting
and  submaximal  exercise  heart  rate  slows,  the  number  or  red  blood  cells  increases,
blood  supply  to  the  tissues  improves,  and  resting  blood  pressure  decreases.  A  fit
cardiorespiratory system does not have to work as hard at rest and at low levels of exercise
because it functions more efficiently. A trained heart can also better withstand the stresses
and strains of daily life and meet the occasional emergencies that make extraordinary
demands on the body‚Äôs cardiorespiratory resources.

The best measure of the body‚Äôs capacity to deliver and use oxygen is maximal oxygen
.
consumption or
VO2max. This variable reflects the body‚Äôs internal chemical capacity.
.
VO2max  by  measuring  how  much  oxygen  the  lungs  extract  from
Scientists  measure
.
the air during intense exercise on a treadmill or stationary bicycle. The
VO2max will be
discussed in more detail in the next unit of the course.

Endurance  training  also  develops  cell  mitochondria.  This  cellular  structure  processes
oxygen and fuels to produce ATP. Endurance exercise increases the size of this cellular
component which helps you use more fat as fuel and consume more oxygen.

.

.

100 meters at 90 percent. Depending on the given child‚Äôs fitness level, each interval might
take 60 to 200 seconds.

.
Scientists discovered that
VO2max depends not only on muscle power but also on the
capacity of the heart and lungs to transport oxygen. Problems with chemical processes
within muscle that limit muscle power can cause fatigue and limit the heart and lungs‚Äô
ability  to  deliver  oxygen  to  the  tissues.  Muscle  power  and  oxygen  transport  capacity
should  be  developed  at  the  same  time  so  the  individual  can  sustain  high  intensities
during endurance exercise.

Mitochondria capacity can be developed by over-distance training or through interval
training. Over-distance training has sometimes been called ‚Äúlong-slow-distance‚Äù or LSD
training.  This  type  of  training  involves  prolonged  exercise  lasting  anywhere  from  10
minutes to several hours. Over-distance training must be specific to the sport‚Äîrunners
should run, cyclists should cycle, swimmers should swim, and wrestlers should wrestle.

obesity: Excess body fat,
typically defined as a body fat
content that exceeds 20 percent
in males and 30 percent in
females.

insulin resistance: Condition
where the body produces
enough insulin, but the insulin
receptors do not respond to
its action. This may happen
because the person is inactive or
overweight and has an excessive
number of fat cells that do not
respond well to insulin.

to insulin resistance and also
referred to as adult-onset
diabetes. It is more common
in middle-age, overweight
people, and is usually treated by
exercise, body weight reduction,
and oral hypoglycemic agents.

SuMMARY

METAbOLISM AND
ThE YOuNg PERSON
Developing a robust metabolism is critical for any young person. Young athletes require
metabolic fitness to optimally convert the energy in food nutrients into energy the body
uses  for  muscle  contraction.  More  importantly,  all  growing  children  need  to  develop
metabolic fitness to set the stage for a healthy life. Studies show that metabolic problems
that are now epidemic in adults‚Äîobesity, insulin resistance, and type 2 diabetes‚Äî
begin during childhood. Regularly performed exercise and good nutrition are the two
best things children can do to develop metabolic health that carries over into adulthood.

Metabolic fitness is central to success in sports. The successful sprinter, football player,
soccer  player,  swimmer,  gymnast,  or  ballet  dancer  can  convert  chemical  energy  into
mechanical energy quickly and efficiently. The more energy the child can generate, the
greater the power production.

You  must  develop  metabolic  fitness  slowly‚Äîtaking  into  consideration  the  child‚Äôs
maturational  level,  experience,  motivation,  and  talent.  Do  not  become  a  slave  driver.
Instead, try to get the child to enjoy the process of training. Ultimate success in sport
requires self-discipline. The child will never reach high levels of performance unless he
or she has a burning desire to train and improve. Youth specialists should help young
athletes develop this attitude.

Most children will never reach elite sports levels. Part of your responsibility is to make
sports and physical activity enjoyable and fun. Teach them the skills necessary to train
efficiently and effectively for the rest of their lives. Instill in them a real love for physical
activity. Achieve this, and you give the child the gift of wellness that will provide dividends
well into adulthood.

Metabolism refers to the sum of the physical and chemical processes that occur in the body. Physical fitness
refers to the capacity to increase metabolism and meet the demands of physical effort. Children increase oxygen
consumption faster than adults at the onset of exercise. This is functionally insignificant because children are
physically smaller and have less muscle mass. The body has three energy systems to carry out life processes:
immediate, non-oxidative, and oxidative. The immediate energy system provides for activities that last 3 seconds
or less. Examples include weight lifting, jumping, swinging a tennis racquet, spiking a volleyball, and throwing a
discus. The non-oxidative system is used for speed events, which last 3 to 60 seconds. Examples include 100- and
400-meter runs. The oxidative system provides energy for events lasting more than 2 minutes, such as distance
running, swimming, hiking, and most team sports (e.g., soccer, water polo, baseball, basketball, and ice hockey).
The child‚Äôs exercise program should emphasize use of the energy system as the most important to the individual‚Äôs
fitness goals. Developing a robust metabolism is critical for any young person. Young athletes need metabolic
fitness to optimally convert the energy in food nutrients into energy the body can use for muscle contraction.
More importantly, all growing children need to develop metabolic fitness to set the stage for a healthy life.

At  rest,  the  cardiovascular  system  in  the  healthy  young  person  has  little  difficulty
supplying oxygen and fuels to the body‚Äôs tissues and removing waste products. During
exercise,  the  demands  on  this  system  increase  considerably.  Oxygen  delivered  to  the
working  muscles  increases  with  exercise  intensity.  The  body  accomplishes  this  by
increasing cardiac output, directing blood to working muscles and vital area ‚Äîsuch as
the heart and brain‚Äîand directing blood away from inner organs and inactive muscles.
Blood is also directed toward the skin surface to dissipate internal heat produced by the
body during exertion.

The  capacity  of  the  cardiovascular  system  to  transport  oxygen  to  working  muscles  is
critical  in  determining  performance  levels  in  endurance  exercise.  The  body‚Äôs  ability
to  transport  and  use  oxygen  during  exercise  is  limited.  Remember,  this  limit  is  called
.
maximal oxygen consumption, or
VO2max largely determines how
fast a person can perform endurance exercises and for how long. It is the best measure of
cardiovascular capacity and an important factor in endurance performance.

.
vO2max. The

Several important differences exist between adults and children in terms of cardiovascular
function. Children do not breathe as much air during exercise as adults do. Their breathing
frequency  is  greater  during  exercise,  but  their  volume  per  breath  or  tidal  volume  is
less. Children have higher resting and maximal heart rates than adults. Young children
may  have  maximum  heart  rates  in  the  220-beat-per-minute  range;  maximum  heart
rates in adults rarely exceed 205 beats a minute. Children have smaller stroke volume
(blood pumped by each ventricle per heart beat). Children also have less muscle mass,
so they cannot develop as much muscle power as adults. Finally, children have immature
temperature regulatory systems, so compared to adults, they are more susceptible to heat
injury (heat exhaustion and heat stroke).

amount of oxygen delivered

.
VO2max.

tidal volume: Volume of air
inhaled and exhaled with each
breath.

Youth FitnessCardiovascular & Pulmonary Exercise Physiology  |  39

This  unit  of  the  training  course  discusses  the  responses  of  the  cardiovascular  system
to  exercise  and  the  effects  of  training  on  cardiovascular  function.  The  cardiovascular
responses to exercise are the same in children and adults‚Äîexcept as noted in the
previous paragraph.

ThE PuLMONARY SYSTEM
AND ExERCISE
The  pulmonary  system  includes  ventilation  (a  process  by  which  the
lungs and heart work together to bring air into the lungs), diffusion
of oxygen and carbon dioxide across microscopic lung air sacs
called  alveoli,  and  movement  of  blood  through  the  lung
(called  pulmonary  perfusion  or  lung  blood  flow).  These
functions‚Äîventilation, diffusion, and lung blood flow‚Äîare
closely linked. They work together to: (1) deliver oxygen to
the blood, (2) rid the blood of carbon dioxide (CO2), and (3)
control metabolic acids. (Figure 3.1)

Ventilation
Ventilation involves the movement of air in and out of the lungs and is quantified in terms
of liters per minute. The volume of each breath is called tidal volume, and the number
of breaths per minute is called breathing frequency. The purpose of ventilation is to
replace oxygen taken up by the blood and eliminate CO2 that moves into the lungs from
the blood.

Ventilation patterns in children differ from those of adults at rest and during exercise.
Children  have  a  higher  breathing  frequency  and  lower  tidal  volume.  Children  reach
adult  levels  for  ventilation  at  approximately  16  to  18  years  of  age.  However,  younger
children who train for endurance will develop adult-type ventilation patterns in which
breathing frequency decreases, while tidal volume increases.

atria: Two chambers found at
the top of the heart that pump
blood to the ventricles.

found at the bottom of the heart
that pump blood to the lungs
and general circulation.

supply oxygenated blood to the
tissues.

between arteries and veins that
deliver oxygen and fuels to

blood toward the heart; all the
veins except the pulmonary
carry dark or oxygenated blood.

internal diameter of a blood
vessel that increases blood flow.

vasoconstriction: Decrease in
the internal diameter of a blood
vessel that decreases blood flow.

temperature regulation:
Physiological processes that
maintain body temperature at a
relatively constant level.

sympathetic nervous
system: Part of the autonomic
nervous system (the
parasympathetic nervous system
is the other part) that innervates
heart and blood vessels, sweat
glands, viscera, and the adrenal
medulla. Most sympathetic
nerves release norepinephrine as
the neurotransmitter.

norepinephrine:
Catecholamine neurohormone,
the major neurotransmitter of
the sympathetic nervous system.
It is stored and released from the
adrenal medulla.

Diffusion
Diffusion refers to the spontaneous migration of substances from regions where their
concentration is high to regions where their concentration is low. Children have a lower
lung diffusion capacity than adults because their lungs are not fully developed.

Lung Blood Flow
(Pulmonary Perfusion)
Lung  blood  flow  depends  on  the  heart‚Äôs
pumping capacity. Children do not pump
as  much  blood  as  adults,  limiting  their
ability to oxygenate the blood at the lungs.
However,  they  also  are  smaller  in  size,  so
their lung function is appropriate for their size.

To lung

From lung

Matching Ventilation
and Lung Blood Flow
Specialized centers in the brain closely match breathing and lung blood flow, coordinating
heart and lung function during exercise. Lung disease, heart disease, or a dramatic change
in altitude can disrupt this coordination and cause shortness of breath (also known as
dyspnea) or chest pain called angina. Children are not immune to lung or heart disease;
regular exercise is often a good way to detect possible cardiovascular problems.

ThE hEART, bLOOD vESSELS,
AND ExERCISE
The heart is a four-chambered organ consisting of two atria (top chambers) and two
ventricles  (bottom,  main  pumping  chambers).  The  right  heart  pumps  blood  to  the
lungs, while the left heart pumps blood to the rest of the body.

The heart pumps blood into an irrigation system of blood vessels consisting of arteries,
capillaries, and veins. Arteries are large muscular blood vessels. As they approach the
tissue, they can become larger (vasodilation) or smaller (vasoconstriction), which
helps direct blood flow to specific tissues. In the capillaries, oxygen, ‚Äúmetabolic‚Äù fuels,
and hormones enter tissues, while waste products exit tissues. Blood returns to the heart
via the veins. The veins contain about two-thirds of the body‚Äôs blood (Figure 3.2).

Blood  flow  control  occurs  in  the  brain.  The  brain  coordinates  special  centers  that
regulate  breathing,  heart  rate  and  contraction  strength,  body  movements,  skin  blood
flow and temperature  regulation, and metabolism. Blood flow control also occurs
in  the  tissues  themselves.  If  an  athlete  does  10  reps  of  curls,  for  example,  local  tissue
changes will increase blood flow to the biceps.

The system‚Äôs number one priority is maintaining blood pressure. During intense exercise,
the brain directs blood away from working muscles to preserve blood pressure control.
One  of  the  purposes  of  exercise  training  is  to  ensure  that  the  body  maintains  blood
pressure while directing as much blood as possible to the working muscles. This principle
is the same in children and adults.

Figure 3.2 (A) The Chambers of the Heart. Blood enters the right atrium and is pumped to the lungs via the right ventricles. It re-enters the
heart at the left atrium and is pumped into the general circulation from the left ventricle. (b) Basics of Circulation. The right ventricle pumps blood
to the lungs where it picks up oxygen and releases carbon dioxide. Blood returns to the left heart where it is pumped into the general circulation by the
left ventricle. It goes to the tissues, crosses the tissue capillaries and returns to the heart via the veins.

cardIovaScular reSPonSeS To exercISe
The heart and blood vessels respond to the metabolic demands of exercise by increasing
blood flow to active areas and decreasing blood flow to less critical areas. The six principal
cardiovascular responses to exercise are:

1.  activation of the ‚Äúfight-or-flight‚Äù system, or sympathetic nervous system,
the system that releases the stress hormones norepinephrine and epineph-
rine (also called noradrenaline and adrenaline);

2.

increased cardiac output (heart output) that results in increased oxygen and
fuel delivery and increased CO2 and waste removal in active skeletal muscles
and the heart;

3.

increased skin blood flow to help remove heat;

4.  decreased blood flow to the kidneys that results in less urine formation;

5.  decreased visceral (internal organ) blood flow that results in reduced diges-

tion and glandular secretion; and

6.  maintenance of blood flow to the brain.

epinephrine: Adrenal hormone
that increases systemic vascular
resistance and improves
coronary and cerebral blood
flow. It increases myocardial
electrical activity and strength
of contraction, stimulates
carbohydrate metabolism, and
increases heart muscle oxygen
requirements and automaticity.
It plays an important part in the
body‚Äôs response to danger and
arousal.

cardiac output: Amount the
heart pumps each minute. It is
determined by stroke volume
and heart rate.

visceral: Refers to the internal
organs.

International Sports Sciences Association42  |  unit 3

systolic: Active or pumping
phase of the cardiac cycle.

diastolic: Relaxation phase of
the cardiac cycle.

The  cardiovascular  responses  to  physical  activity  depend  on  the  type  and  intensity  of
exercise.  Dynamic  exercise  requiring  a  large  muscle  mass‚Äîsuch  as  running‚Äîcauses
the  greatest  response  from  the  cardiovascular  system.  Cardiac  output,  heart  rate,  and
systolic blood pressure increase, while little change occurs in diastolic blood pressure.
Strength exercises, such as weight lifting, markedly increase systolic and diastolic blood
pressure and heart rate.

Oxygen consumption increases in proportion to exercise intensity until it plateaus near
.
VO2max, further increases in
maximal effort (Figure 3.3). After the body has reached
exercise intensity do not lead to a further increase in oxygen consumption. Very little
variability  exists  between  the  oxygen  consumption  of  trained  and  untrained  people
performing at the same power output or exercise intensity. If a world-class cyclist and
a  sedentary  person  ride  a  bicycle  at  the  same  speed,  their  oxygen  consumption  will
be similar. The ride represents a lower percentage of maximum for the cyclist, but the
oxygen cost remains the same.

Figure 3.3 Oxygen Consumption Versus Exercise Intensity. Oxygen consumption increases with
exercise intensity until the person reaches a ceiling in the capacity of the heart to pump blood and the cells
ability to use oxygen. This point is called maximal oxygen consumption.

Heart Rate
Heart rate is the most important factor in increased cardiac output during exercise. In
dynamic  (endurance)  exercise,  heart  rate  increases  with  exercise  intensity  and  oxygen
.
consumption, levelling off at
VO2max. In children, heart rate increases from a resting
value of approximately 70 to 90 beats a min to a maximum heart rate of 200 to 220
beats a minute or more. The rate and extent of the increase depend on the type of exercise,
and the fitness, age, and sex of the person.

maximum heart rate: Highest
rate the heart can reach‚Äîusually
during exercise‚Äîin one minute.

Youth Fitness

Cardiovascular & Pulmonary Exercise Physiology  |  43

During  submaximal  exercise,  heart  rate  rises  and  then  levels  off  when  the  oxygen
transport  requirements  of  the  activity  have  been  satisfied.  At  increasing  intensities,
this leveling off in heart rate takes longer and is more difficult to achieve. In prolonged
exercise, the heart rate increases steadily at the same work rate. This phenomenon, called
cardiovascular drift, is caused by a diminished capacity to return blood to the heart
because  of  decreased  plasma  volume  (due  to  filtration  and  sweating)  and,  perhaps,
decreased  sympathetic  tone  (nerve  control  of  blood  vessels).  Cardiovascular  drift
results from a decrease in stroke volume, so that the heart rate must increase to maintain
cardiac output and blood pressure at the same level.

At rest and during low intensity submaximal exercise, heart rate may be elevated above
normal  due  to  factors  such  as  anxiety,  dehydration,  ambient  temperature,  altitude,
and  digestion.  Resting  heart  rates  of  over  100  to  130  beats  a  minute  are  not  unusual
before anxiety-producing situations, such as treadmill tests and sports competitions‚Äî
particularly in children unaccustomed to high stress situations.

Heart  rates  tend  to  be  lower  during  strength  exercises‚Äîsuch  as  weight  lifting‚Äîthan
during  endurance  exercises,  such  as  running.  During  resistance  exercises  like  weight
lifting, the heart rate increases in proportion to the muscle mass used and the percentage
of  maximum  voluntary  contraction.  Thus,  a  dynamic  lift‚Äîsuch  as  a  clean  and  jerk
performed  with  heavy  weights‚Äîincreases  the  heart  rate  more  than  a  one-arm  biceps
curl using minimum resistance.

At  the  same  absolute  power  output  (exercise  intensity),  heart  rate  is  higher  during
upper  body  exercise  (i.e.,  arm  ergometer)  than  it  is  during  lower  body  exercise  (i.e.,
.
VO2,
bicycle  ergometer).  In  addition,  upper  body  exercise  results  in  a  higher  level  of
systolic and mean arterial pressures, and total peripheral resistance. The greater
circulatory  load  probably  results  from  the  smaller  muscle  mass,  a  greater  isometric
exercise  component,  and  a  less  effective  muscle  pump  (resulting  in  decreased  venous
return). Heart rate can be valuable in creating an individual‚Äôs exercise prescription. By
measuring the heart rate either during or immediately after exercise, one can estimate the
metabolic cost of the activity.

cardiovascular drift:
Tendency for the heart rate to
increase gradually with time
during exercise.

plasma: The fluid (noncellular)
portion of the circulating blood.

plasma volume: The volume
of plasma in circulating blood.

sympathetic tone: Signals
sent from the sympathetic
nervous system to the blood
vessels that keep them partially
closed at rest or during
submaximum exercise.

power output: Intensity of
exercise expressed in watts.

mean arterial pressure: One
third of systolic pressure plus
diastolic pressure. Most people
have a mean arterial blood
pressure of 100 mm Hg.

peripheral resistance: Total
resistance to flow of blood in
the systemic circulation. It is
determined by dividing mean
arterial pressure by cardiac
output.

stroke volume: Volume of
blood pumped from a ventricle
each beat. Over a few beats,
stroke volume from the left and
right ventricles remains equal.

Stroke Volume
Stroke  volume‚Äîthe  amount  of  blood  pumped  by  each  ventricle  per  heart  beat‚Äî
.
VO2max  and
increases  during  exercise.  It  increases  steadily  until  about  25  percent  of
then levels off or decreases. After peak stroke volume has been achieved, cardiac output
increases because of an increase in heart rate.

Stroke volume is one of the most important factors that accounts for individual differences
.
VO2max  and  cardiovascular  capacity.  This  is  readily  apparent  when  comparing  a
in
sedentary  man  with  a  champion  cross-country  skier.  Both  men  have  maximum  heart
rates of 185 beats a minute, yet the maximum cardiac output of the untrained man is
16.6 liters a minute, while the maximum cardiac output of the skier is 32 liters a minute
(nearly double). The maximum stroke volumes of the skier and the sedentary man equal
173 and 90 ml.

arteriovenous oxygen
difference: Difference in
oxygen content between the
arteries and the veins.

Arteriovenous Oxygen Difference ((a-V)O2)
Arteriovenous  oxygen  difference measures oxygen extraction by the tissues from
blood.  Oxygen  extraction  increases  as  a  person  transitions  from  rest  to  maximum
exercise. Oxygen extraction reaches almost 100% when (a-V)O2 difference is measured
across a maximally exercising muscle.

Blood Pressure
Blood pressure is determined by cardiac output and the tissues‚Äô resistance to blood flow,
or  peripheral  resistance.  It  is  vitally  important  that  blood  pressure  increases  during
exercise. Blood flow must be maintained in the critical heart and brain areas to satisfy
the blood requirements for working muscles and skin.

During exercise, blood vessels that supply nonworking tissues experience vasoconstriction
(where  they  close  or  get  smaller)  while  vessels  supplying  working  muscles  experience
vasodilation (where they become larger). This causes an overall decrease in peripheral
resistance.  Though  peripheral  resistance  decreases  during  exercise,  blood  pressure
increases. This occurs because of the overwhelming increase in cardiac output.

Systolic blood pressure rises steadily during exercise from about 120 mm Hg at rest to
180  mm  Hg  or  more  during  maximal  exercise.  In  children,  maximum  systolic  blood

Youth FitnessCardiovascular & Pulmonary Exercise Physiology  |  45

pressure rarely exceeds 160 mm Hg. Blood pressure in children and adults follows the
same general trend as heart rate.

Diastolic pressure changes little during exercise in normal children and adults. Typically,
either no change or a slight decrease of less than 10 mm Hg occurs during exercise; a
small decrease of less than 4 mm Hg occurs during recovery. A significant increase in
diastolic pressure (greater than 15 mm Hg or above 110 mm Hg) is a warning sign of
heart disease.

Blood Flow
Blood is redistributed from inactive to active tissues during exercise. Critical brain and
heart tissues are spared the vasoconstriction that occurs in other body areas.

Resting  blood  flow  to  the  spleen  and  kidneys  averages  about  2.8  liters  a  minute.  It
reduces to about 500 ml during maximal exercise. Sympathetic stimulation (sympathetic
innervation is particularly potent in these organs) and the circulation of ‚Äúfight-or-flight‚Äù
hormones, such as epinephrine and norepinephrine, cause the markedly reduced blood
flow in these regions.

In  the  kidneys,  reduced  blood  flow  during  exercise  is  accompanied  by  post  exercise
proteinuria  (protein  in  the  urine)  and  occasionally  by  hemoglobinuria  and
myoglobinuria (hemoglobin and myoglobin in the urine). Extremely active teenagers
will sometimes fail athletic physicals because of protein in the urine. This finding should
not be of concern and will settle down if the child takes a few days off from exercise.

Skin  blood  flow  increases  during  submaximal  exercise,  but  decreases  again  as  skin
temperature cools and exercise becomes more intense. The relative skin blood flow (as
a  percentage  of  cardiac  output)  changes  little  during  exercise.  Peripheral  perfusion  of
cutaneous vessels during exercise, particularly in the heat, contributes to cardiovascular
drift and fatigue during endurance exercise.

Blood flow to the heart‚Äôs coronary arteries increases with increasing exercise intensity. A
large coronary artery reserve is extremely important because it provides adequate blood
flow  even  in  the  face  of  significant  coronary  artery  disease.  Coronary  artery  blood
flow is not thought to limit oxygen transport capacity in people without coronary artery
disease.  A  moderately  intense  warm-up  several  minutes  before  endurance  exercise  is
important for increasing blood flow during the early stages of exercise.

the stomach that produces

of hemoglobin in the urine.

blood vessels.

changeS In cardIovaScular
SYSTem wITh TraInIng
Endurance  training  produces  adaptive  changes  in  many  aspects  of  cardiovascular
function. The heart improves its ability to pump blood mainly by increasing its stroke
volume. This occurs due to increased blood entering the chamber and a small increase in
the strength of the heart muscle.

Endurance or aerobic exercise is best for improving the capacity of the cardiovascular
system. Such exercise requires the use of at least 50 percent of the body‚Äôs muscle mass in
rhythmical exercise, performed for at least 15 to 20 minutes, three to five days a week,
.
VO2max. Considerably more training is required to
and at above 50 to 60 percent of
develop the high levels of fitness characteristic of endurance athletes.

Adaptation  to  endurance  training  is  specific.  Swimming  improves  cardiovascular
performance in that activity but will not improve endurance in running. This principle
is critically important in multi-endurance triathlons; successful triathletes must practice
cycling, running, and swimming.

Interval  training‚Äîrepeated  bouts  of  short-  to  moderate-duration  exercise‚Äîalso
improves cardiovascular condition. This mode of training manipulates distance, speed,
repetition,  and  rest.  Interval  training  allows  the  athlete  to  train  at  a  higher  intensity
than typically employed during competition, and thus serves as an overload. This type
of training optimizes the development of cardiovascular capacity yet is less effective in
eliciting  the  biochemical  changes  critical  for  fatigue  resistance.  Endurance  athletes
should practice interval and over-distance training.

Oxygen Consumption
Improvement  in  maximal  oxygen  consumption  with  training  depends  on  current
.
condition, type of training, and age. Most studies find that
VO2max can improve only
about  20  percent.  It  appears  that  an  athlete  must  be  endowed  with  a  relatively  high
capacity  oxygen  transport  system  to  be  successful  in  endurance  events.  This  is  not
to  say  that  training  is  not  extremely  important,  but  rather  that  there  is  only  a  limited
.
VO2max.
opportunity to improve the maximal aerobic capacity or

Heart Rate
Endurance training reduces the resting heart rate. It is not unusual to see resting heart
rates of less than 40 beats a minute in champion endurance runners. Children experience
a decreased heart rate with endurance training, but not to the same extent
as adults.

Training  also  decreases  the  heart  rate  during  submaximal  exercise.  The
metabolic  requirement  of  the  workload  remains  the  same  (once  the
movement patterns have been learned), but the decline in heart rate does
not reduce cardiac output because of an increase in stroke volume.

Training has a small effect on maximal heart rate, usually decreasing it by
about three beats a min. Maximum heart rate remains relatively stable, but
tends to decrease only with age. It can serve as a reference point for judging
the relative intensity of exercise. For example, suppose a person achieved a
heart rate of 140 beats a minute running a 10-minute mile. The person then
returned after six months of endurance training and ran another 10-min

Youth FitnessCardiovascular & Pulmonary Exercise Physiology  |  47

mile, only this time the heart rate achieved only 130 beats
a  minute.  The  workload  no  longer  represented  the  same
percentage  of  maximum  heart  rate  (which  remained  the
same). The heart rate decreased at an identical submaximal
exercise intensity, yet running speed would have to increase
to achieve the same relative intensity of exercise.

Stroke Volume
Endurance  training  increases  the  heart‚Äôs  stroke  volume  at
rest  and  during  submaximal  and  maximal  exercise.  Most
studies  indicate  that  training  increases  stroke  volume
by  no  more  than  about  20  percent,  the  same  percentage
.
change as is in
VO2max. World-class runners, cyclists, and
cross-country skiers with exceptional stroke volumes were
probably born that way.

The  training-induced  increase  in  stroke  volume  is  due
largely to a decreased heart rate because the heart has more
time  to  fill  up.  Also,  more  blood  volume  causes  a  larger
stroke volume. Finally, the stronger heart can pump more
blood per heartbeat.

Blood Pressure
Endurance
to  reduced  resting  and
submaximal  systolic,  diastolic,  and  mean  arterial  blood
pressures  during  exercise.  Diastolic  and  mean  arterial‚Äî
but  not  systolic‚Äîblood  pressures  are  reduced  at  maximal
exercise. The mechanism of reduced blood pressure at rest
remains unknown.

Blood Flow
With  training,  coronary  blood  flow  usually  decreases
slightly  at  rest  and  during  submaximal  exercise  due  to  a
decreased heart rate. Coronary artery blood flow increases
at maximal exercise with training to support the metabolic
requirement of increasing cardiac output. Coronary artery
blood  flow  accommodates  the  metabolic  load.  Training
does not increase blood vessels that supply the heart muscle.

Endurance  training  increases  blood  vessels  in  skeletal
muscles, which aids in the transport of oxygen, metabolic
fuels, and waste products in the exercising muscles. During
maximal exercise, muscle blood flow increases by about 10
percent  after  training  because  of  a  greater  cardiac  output
and increased muscle blood vessels.

Endurance training does not affect skin blood flow, but skin
vessels receive increased blood during exercise along with
an  earlier  onset  and  increased  sweat  rate.  This  helps  the
body cool itself during exercise.

International Sports Sciences Association48  |  unit 3

SuMMARY

During the transition from rest to exercise, there is an increase in cardiac output,
an increased blood flow to active muscles and the heart, maintenance of blood
flow to the brain, and decreased blood flow to most of the body‚Äôs internal or-
gans. Training increases cardiac output, stroke volume, maximum muscle blood
flow, and blood and plasma volume. It slows resting and submaximal exercise
heart rate. Training improves the regulation of the cardiovascular system, which
makes it easier to supply oxygen, metabolic fuels, and hormones, and rid the
body of waste products and heat.

During exercise, children do not breathe as much air as adults. Their breathing
frequency is greater during exercise, while tidal volume or volume per breath is
less. Children have higher resting and maximal heart rates than adults. Young
children can achieve maximum heart rates greater than 220 beats a minute,
while adults rarely attain 205 beats a minute. Children have a smaller stroke
volume, or amount of blood pumped by each ventricle per heart beat. Children
also have less muscle mass and cannot develop as much muscle power as adults.
Finally, children have immature temperature regulatory systems, so they are more
susceptible to heat exhaustion and heat stroke than adults.

Children can train with weights and gain strength and some
muscle  size.  However,  children  should  not  begin  formal
weight training too early. If a young person is not willing to
train two to three days a week, the activity will provide little
benefit. Most children do not have the discipline to train on
a regular basis. Forcing them to do so will only make them
despise the activity. Build up the training ethic gradually so
young athletes become self motivated.

Children  can  perform  exercises  and  play  games  that  build
strength.  Children  who  can  handle  their  bodyweight  on
playground bars are setting the stage for more serious strength
training in the future. Personal trainers who work with young
people should understand how muscles increase in strength
and  size‚Äîeven  though  many  children  will  not  train  with
weights.

Until recently, scientists knew relatively little about the best
ways to increase muscle strength and size. Athletes were on their own‚Äîwhatever seemed
to work entered into practice. Scientists did not have the tools to study the way muscles
grew; the technology simply was unavailable. That all changed in the 1960s and 70s with
the electron microscope, muscle biopsy techniques, and radioactive tracers that allowed
scientists to view the ultrastructural details of the muscle from the inside.

Today,  scientists  know  a  lot  about  the  way  muscles  become  stronger  and  increase  in
size. New technologies and the opening up of the former communist eastern bloc sports
medicine laboratories have added immensely to our knowledge about muscle and how
best  to  train  muscle.  If  you  systematically  apply  the  incredible  amount  of  sports  and
exercise  science  information  available  to  you,  your  clients  will  make  gains  far  greater
than you ever believed possible.

MuSCLE STRENgTh: SIzE, NEuRAL
ACTIvATION, ELASTIC RECOIL, AND SKILL
Optimal  muscle  performance  requires  the  following:  (1)  good  muscle  size,  (2)
coordination between the muscles and nervous system, (3) efficient use of the muscles‚Äô
elasticity, and (4) specific skill development. Each factor depends on the others for peak
muscular development. Muscles develop best when subjected to tension. The neural and
elastic properties of muscle, in addition to muscle protein, ultimately allow for fast and
impressive gains.

Youth FitnessMuscle Physiology  |  51

muscle fiber: Cellular
components of a skeletal muscle
that contain myofibrils.

myofibrils: Slender threads in
a muscle fiber that run parallel
with the long axis of the fiber
and are composed of numerous
myofilaments.

hypertrophy: Development of
large muscle fibers.

fast-twitch muscle fibers:
Skeletal muscle fibers having
high myofibrillar ATPase
activity, high glycolytic enzyme
activities, and an intermediate
glycogen content that produces
a fast twitch. Types include type
IIa and type IIb.

slow-twitch muscle fibers:
Skeletal muscle fibers having
low myofibrillar ATPase activity,
low glycogen content, high
myoglobin content, high
mitochondrial oxidative enzyme
activities, and an intermediate
mitochondrial content that
produces a slow twitch and is
fatigue-resistant.

fasicle: a bundle of skeletal
muscle fibers surrounded
by connective tissue called
perimysium.

muScle STrucTure and STrengTh
Muscles move the body and enable it to exert force because they move the skeleton. When
a muscle contracts (shortens), it moves a bone by pulling on the tendon that attaches
the  muscle  to  the  bone.  Muscles  consist  of  individual  muscle  cells  or  muscle  fibers
connected in bundles. A single muscle consists of many bundles of muscle fibers covered
by layers of connective tissue that bind the fibers together. Muscle fibers, in turn, consist
of  smaller  units  called  myofibrils.  When  the  muscles  receive  the  signal  to  contract,
protein filaments within the myofibrils slide across one another, causing the muscle fiber
to  shorten.  Weight  training  causes  the  size  of  individual  muscle  fibers  to  increase  by
increasing  the  number  of  myofibrils.  Larger  muscle  fibers  mean  a  larger  and  stronger
muscle. hypertrophy refers to the development of large muscle fibers (see Figure 4.1).

Muscle fibers, classified as fast-twitch or slow-twitch, vary according to their strength,
speed of contraction, and energy source. Slow-twitch fibers are relatively fatigue resistant,
but they do not contract as rapidly or strongly as fast-twitch fibers. The aerobic system is
the principle energy system that fuels slow-twitch fibers. Fast-twitch fibers contract more
rapidly and forcefully than slow-twitch fibers but fatigue more quickly. Although oxygen
plays an important role in the energy system that fuels fast-twitch fibers, these fibers rely
more on anaerobic metabolism than do slow-twitch fibers.

muScle

FaSIcle

mYoFIbrIl

FIber

Figure 4.1 Basic Structure of Skeletal Muscle: fascicles, fibers, and myofibrils.

International Sports Sciences Association52  |  unit 4

motor unit: The functional
unit of muscular contraction
consisting of a motor nerve and
the muscle fibers it innervates.

Most muscles contain a mixture of slow-twitch and fast-twitch fibers. The type of fiber that
goes into action depends on the type of work required. Endurance activities like jogging
tend to use slow-twitch fibers, whereas strength and power activities like sprinting use
fast-twitch fibers. Weight training can increase the size and strength of both fast-twitch
and slow-twitch fibers, although fast-twitch fibers are preferentially increased.

To exert force, the body recruits one or more motor units to contract. A motor  unit
consists of a nerve connected to a number of muscle fibers. The number of muscle fibers
in a motor unit varies from two to hundreds. When a motor nerve calls on its fibers to
contract, all fibers contract to their full capacity. The number of motor units recruited
depends on the amount of strength required. When a person lifts a light weight like 5
pounds, he or she uses fewer motor units than when lifting a heavier 10-pound weight.
Weight training improves the body‚Äôs ability to recruit motor units‚Äîa phenomenon called
muscle  learning,  which  increases  strength  even  before  muscle  size  increases.  Muscle
learning  represents  an  important  way  to  gain  strength  during  childhood  because  low
testosterone levels make it difficult to significantly increase muscle size.

In summary, weight training increases muscle strength by increasing the size of individual
muscle fibers and improving the ability to activate motor units to exert force (See Figure
4.3 on page 55).

hYPerTroPhY or hYPerPlaSIa?
For  over  100  years,  scientists  have  argued  about  whether  or  not  strength  training
increases muscle fiber number or only muscle fiber size. Most studies show that strength
training  makes  muscle  fibers  larger  (hypertrophy),  not  more  numerous  (hyperplasia).
However, there is some proof that muscle fibers can increase in number under certain
circumstances. Also, some extremely strong people may be born with more muscle fibers
than others. Generally, though, it is muscle hypertrophy that makes muscles stronger.

how muScle FIberS
become bIgger
Muscle  fibers  become  bigger  by  increasing  the  number  of
myofibrils‚Äîin other words, muscles increase in size by adding
protein.  As  the  number  of  myofibrils  increase,  both  muscle
strength and muscle size increase. The more myofibrils added
to  the  muscle,  the  greater  the  increase  in  strength  and  size
(Figure 4.2).

Protein Synthesis
The cell nucleus serves as the master control center for protein
production in muscle cells. Most cells in the body contain a
single nucleus. Muscle cells, in contrast, contain many nuclei,
which  give  them  a  high  capacity  to  produce  new  proteins.
Proteins are constructed from amino acids on cell structures
called  ribosomes.  The  nucleus  sends  a  message  to  the  ribosomes  telling  them  exactly
how to arrange the amino acids to form a specific protein. The cells also manufacture
proteins such as enzymes. Enzymes play important roles in cell function and physical
performance. Four factors influence protein production in muscle: (1) muscle tension,
(2) hormones, (3) amino acid concentration, and (4) nutritional status.

Figure 4.2 Micrograph of
Myofibrils Showing the Sarcom-
eres‚Äîthe basic unit of the fiber.

Youth FitnessMuscle Physiology  |  53

Muscle tension
Muscle  tension  is  the  most  important  factor  in  increasing
the  rate  of  muscle  protein  development.  Muscle  tension
speeds the movement of amino acids into the muscle and
triggers the nucleus to build new muscle. The faster amino
acids move into the muscle, the greater the rate of muscle
protein  synthesis.  In  other  words,  optimal  muscle  growth
depends on triggering tension receptors in the cell, and on
the  rate  at  which  amino  acids  enter  the  cell.  Any  training
program  designed  to  stimulate  muscle  growth  should
maximize the intensity and duration of muscle tension.

Hormones
Testosterone,  growth  hormone,  and  insulin  are  the  three
most  important  hormones  that  affect  protein  synthesis  in
muscle. Each of these hormones influences muscle cell nuclei
and their messengers, causing them to speed the production
of  muscle  proteins.  Insulin  also  speeds  the  movement  of
amino  acids  into  the  muscle  cell.  Most  amino  acids  enter
muscle cells by a process called the sodium pump. Insulin
speeds the action of the sodium pump, which increases the
rate of amino acid transport into the cell. This is critical for
muscle growth‚Äîthe more amino acids transported into the
muscle cell, the greater the rate of muscle hypertrophy.

Other hormones cause protein breakdown. Among the most
important of these are the corticosteroids produced by the
adrenal glands (hormone secreting glands that lie on top of
each kidney). Corticosteroids, such as cortisol, are produced

during times of stress. They increase after a hard workout or
during periods of overtraining. With overtraining, the blood
level of these hormones rises, and the testosterone level falls.
This imbalance produces a catabolic state in which gains in
muscle size are impossible.

Amino Acid Concentration
and Nutritional Status
Optimal  amino  acid  transport  requires  an  adequate
concentration  of  amino  acids  in  the  blood  and  muscles.
Usually,  this  is  not  a  problem  because  most  athletes
consume  enough  protein  in  their  diets  to  supply  the
muscles with adequate amino acids. However, during heavy
training, extensive soft tissue injury, or overtraining, amino
acid  concentration  may  be  inadequate.  Energy  intake  is
also important. If active, growing children do not consume
enough  total  calories  and  their  bodies  will  break  down
structural proteins for energy.

Anabolic-Catabolic Cycles
Training  does  not  lead  to  continuous  gains  in  muscle
size.  Muscle  size  increases  for  a  while,  then  levels  off,
and  sometimes  regresses.  This  occurs  because  of  protein
turnover‚Äîthe constant buildup and breakdown of structural
proteins.  If  you  provide  an  optimal  training  environment
for  the  muscles  (i.e.,  good  muscle  tension  during  training
and ideal concentrations of anabolic hormones and amino
acids),  then  muscles  will  tend  to  hypertrophy  because  the

International Sports Sciences Association54  |  unit 4

delayed onset muscle
soreness (DOMS): The pain
and stiffness felt in muscles
several hours to days after
exercise. Believed to be the
result of microscopic damage
to muscle fibers involved in the
exercise.

motor unit recruitment:
Activation of more motor units
with greater force output.

body remains in an anabolic or growing phase. If training and nutritional considerations
are suboptimal, then training gains will be less, and you may actually lose ground.

The  goal  of  the  training  program  should  be  to  remain  in  an  anabolic  state  and  avoid
catabolic periods. Older children who are serious about strength training can optimize
tension in the workout by incorporating cycles. Intense workouts increase muscle size,
but adequate rest is essential for intense training. People who push to failure every time
they train will never recover enough to train hard. The quality of the training stimulus
is  the  key  that  maximizes  muscle  protein  synthesis.  The  training  program  should  be
designed so the workout regimen leads to more intense workouts.

Delayed Onset Muscle Soreness (DOMS)
Delayed  onset  muscle  soreness  or  DOMS  refers  to  muscle  soreness  one  to  three
days after a workout. In the past, lactic acid build-up or muscle spasms were believed
to produce muscle soreness. Scientists now know that cell damage causes sore muscles.
Heavy workouts can damage fibers and calcium storage depots within the cells. Calcium,
a  chemical  trigger,  causes  muscles  to  contract.  Unfortunately,  high  concentrations  of
calcium break down muscle. Following muscle damage, calcium leaks into the muscle
and causes the muscle proteins to decompose. This causes pain and muscle inflammation.
After  a  few  days,  the  muscles  form  protective  proteins  that  temporarily  protect  the
muscle from further damage and soreness. If you skip too many workouts, you lose these
protective proteins and become susceptible to additional muscle soreness.

MuSCLE AND
ThE NERvOuS SYSTEM
As  discussed  previously,  muscle  fibers  receive  the  signal  to  contract  from  nerves
connected  to  the  spinal  column.  Nerve‚Äìmuscle  combinations  are  called  motor  units.
Powerful muscles, such as the quadriceps in the legs, have large motor units‚Äîeach motor
nerve is connected to many muscle fibers. Smaller muscles, such as those around the eye,
have smaller motor units.

The three types of motor units include (1) fast glycolytic (FG), (2) fast oxidative glycolytic
(FOG), and (3) slow oxidative (SO). They are subdivided according to their strength and
speed of contraction, speed of nerve conduction, and resistance to fatigue. The type of
motor unit selected by the body depends on the requirements of the muscle contraction.
The body chooses FG fibers for lifting heavy weights or sprinting because they are fast and
powerful.  FOG  fibers  are  used  mainly  during  mid-length,  higher-intensity  endurance
events  such  as  the  1-mile  run  or  the  400-meter  swim.  The  SO  fibers  are  selected  for
prolonged standing or slow walking because they are more fatigue resistant.

The body exerts force by calling upon one or more motor units to contract. The process
of  activating  motor  units  to  contract  is  called  motor  unit  recruitment.  When  you
want to pick up a small weight, you use few motor units to attempt the task. However,
you use many motor units when you want to lift a heavy weight. When a motor unit calls
upon its fibers to contract, all the fibers contract to their maximum capacity.

Motor units are activated according to their size‚Äîsmaller motor units engage for light
loads. Large, powerful motor units are only activated when the body must exert maximum
force. The muscles fibers in large, high threshold motor units can increase in size more
than smaller motor units. These fibers are not trained to any extent unless you push them.
It is important to include heavy reps in a resistance training program if you expect to
recruit these hard to train motor units. Failure to do so limits increases in muscle size.

Increased Strength Through Improved Motor Unit Recruitment
Training  with  weights  improves  the  nervous  system‚Äôs
coordination and recruitment of muscle fibers. It is a kind
of  ‚Äúmuscle  learning‚Äù  and  an  important  way  to  increase
strength.  As  surprising  as  it  may  seem,  neurological
adaptations (rather than increases in muscle size) account
for most of the increases in muscle strength during the first
three weeks of a resistance training program.

Most  athletes  concentrate  on  muscle  tissue  when  trying
to  increase  strength.  Elaborate  diets,  ergogenic  aids  such
as  anabolic  steroids,  growth  hormone,  and  intense  load
cycles  are  usually  employed  to  help  increase  muscle  bulk.
However, strength depends not only on the size of muscles,
but also on the nervous system to activate them. Strength
training causes the nervous system to more fully ‚Äúturn-on‚Äù

International Sports Sciences Association


men.  Higher  testosterone  levels  in  men  provide  a  partial
explanation for such findings.

Some motor units are more difficult to recruit (or activate)
than  others.  The  high  threshold  units  are  the  hardest  to
activate, but they are also the strongest and most powerful.
Two  related  neural  adaptations,  enhanced  recruitment
of  high-threshold  motor  units  and  increased  time  high-
threshold  motor  units,  have  important  implications  for
overall  strength  development.  A  motor  unit  is  trained  in
direct  proportion  to  its  recruitment.  Stated  somewhat
differently,  high  threshold  motor  units  (the  muscle  fibers
and their motor nerve) will not be trained unless they are
recruited during training. Neural adaptations that activate
high threshold motor units must occur before these motor
units  can  be  trained.  When  trying  to  develop  explosive
power, for example, you must concentrate on recruiting the
high threshold motor units and turning them on for as long
as possible.

In summary, to achieve maximum strength, you must train
high threshold motor units. These units are only trained if
you  recruit  them.  To  recruit  these  motor  units,  you  must
train intensely and concentrate on accelerating your reps.

56  |  unit 4

the important muscles required in specific movements and
to  better  coordinate  their  actions.  These  neural  changes
produce greater muscle force production.

Muscles  increase  force  by  recruiting  more  motor  units.
When  going  for  a  maximum  lift,  the  ideal  situation  is  to
recruit  as  many  motor  units
as  possible.  Greater  motor
recruitment  produces

Increased electrical activity of the muscle occurs relatively
early in the strength training process. The early increases in
strength achieved by the novice during the first few training
weeks are due primarily to improved neural activation rather
than enhanced muscle growth. The early gains in muscular
strength are due to an increased recruitment of motor units.
In  children  and  women,  neural  changes  become  critical
for  maximum  strength  because  muscle  hypertrophy  does
not occur in women and children to the same extent as in

Youth FitnessMuscle Physiology  |  57

ELASTIC MuSCLE ENERgY
Muscles  have  elastic  properties  that  can  increase  strength
and add to training gains. Most top athletes very effectively
use  elastic  muscle  energy.  It  makes  their  movements  look
smooth  and  explosive.  Elastic  muscle  energy  allows  them
to exert much more force than it is possible to exert during
normal muscle contractions.

Elastic muscle energy is much like a rubber band. When you
stretch the rubber band and let go of the ends, it rebounds
back  to  its  original  shape.  Muscle  works  in  a  similar  way.
If you stretch a muscle suddenly, it tries to rebound to its
resting state. The elastic energy is captured in the muscle as
potential energy. Potential energy in muscle is stored energy
that can be used later to help exert force.

When muscle stretch is immediately followed by a muscle
contraction,  the  rebound  from  the  stored  elastic  energy
greatly  increases  contraction  force.  It  is  much  like  getting
a  push  when  you  start  to  run  or  an  active  assist  during  a
weight  lift.  The  stretched  muscle  rebounds  back  to  its
shorter length. When the muscle contracts at the same time,
the combined forces of muscle contraction and elastic recoil
produce more force than either factor alone.

Stored elastic energy must be used quickly or it is lost as heat.
The secret of using elastic energy effectively is to coordinate
the  nervous  and  muscle  contraction  systems  with  muscle
stretch.  After  the  stretch,  the  muscles  must  be  turned  on
instantly to take advantage of the muscles‚Äô elastic recoil.

Many sports movements provide good examples of elastic
loading. Discus throwers elastically load the arm, chest, and
shoulders by driving with the legs during the initial part of
the throwing motion. The leg drive puts the upper body on

the stretch. The elastic loading of the upper body increases
the total force during the throw. The elastic loading causes a
whip-like motion required for long throws.

is

Stretch  is  no  stranger  during  weight  training.  Even  in
the  controlled  environment  of  a  weight  lifting  contest,
stretch  greatly  affects  how  much  one  can  lift.  Weight  lifts
combine eccentric and concentric muscle actions. Eccentric
contraction
force  development  while  the  muscle
lengthens.  Concentric  contraction  is  force  development
while the muscle shortens. Lifters often make the mistake of
concentrating only on the pushing or active phase of the lift.
Concentric muscle contractions (i.e., the active phase of the
lift)  are  not  isolated  movements.  The  push  always  follows
an eccentric contraction, during which the muscles used in
the lift stretch. Energy is stored in the muscles during the
stretch  phase.  This  stored  energy  can  be  used  during  the
active phase of the lift to produce more force.

Coordination  of  stretch  and  muscle  contraction  is  very
important  during  the  bench  press.  The  pectoralis  major,
triceps,  and  deltoid  muscles  work  eccentrically  to  control
the bar while the weight is lowered to the chest. The muscles
are  elastically  loaded  as  the  bar  reaches  the  chest.  With  a
minimal delay, the stored elastic energy can assist with the
following  pushing  motion.  The  elastic  recoil  in  the  chest,
shoulders, and arms helps one push more weight.

In  an  ideal  lift,  eccentric,  elastic,  and  concentric  phases
of  the  movement  combine.  Coordinating  these  processes
allows  one  to  push  much  more  weight  than  is  possible
during  a  purely  concentric  bench  press  (i.e.,  a  lift  starting
from the chest).

International Sports Sciences Association58  |  unit 4

PhYSIologY oF
muScle elaSTIcITY
Muscle  contains  elastic  structures  that  can  be  stretched,
absorb  energy,  and  enhance  force  development.  The
elastic  properties  of  muscle  can  be  separated  into  series
elastic and parallel elastic components. These components
work  together  with  the  muscle‚Äôs  contractile  component
to  produce  force.  The  contractile  components  include  the
various structures of the sarcomere. The sarcomere is a unit
of muscle that produces contraction. Each muscle fiber has
many linked sarcomeres, and the linked sarcomeres form a
contractile unit that produces force.

The  series  elastic  component  includes  the  tendons  and
cross  bridges  of  the  muscle  fibers.  The  cross  bridges‚Äî
small  structures  within  the  muscle  fiber‚Äîdevelop  muscle
force.  The  series  elastic  component  is  the  most  important
component  for  translating  stretch  into  force.  The  parallel
elastic
structures
surrounding  muscle  bundles.  These  provide  stability  and
protection to the muscle.

includes  membrane

component

The  contractile  and  elastic  components  are  concepts
rather than structures. Both components have elements in
common. For example, the cross-bridges are included in the
contractile and elastic components. They affect contraction
and  give  elasticity  to  the  muscle.  Even  in  a  motion  that
is  not  preceded  by  an  eccentric  muscle  contraction  (i.e.,
lowering the weight in preparation for the lift), such as the
dead lift,  there is  elastic loading of the  muscle. The cross-
bridges are elastically loaded as soon as tension develops. If
the contraction occurs rapidly, the elastic recoil of the cross
bridges assists the force of contraction.

Elastic muscle energy enhances muscle force development
in  a  process  called  the  stretch-shortening  cycle.  Elastic
structures  stretch  during  sudden  jumping  or  throwing
movements.  The  stretch  represents  potential  energy  that
enhances the force produced by the contractile component.
Immediately after the muscle stretches, it actively contracts.
The elastic elements‚Äô recoil assists in force development.

Many types of human motion incorporate stretch-shortening
cycles. When jumping in basketball or high jumping, the leg
muscles  are  stretched  immediately  prior  to  the  jump.  The
elastic  loading  of  the  leg  enhances  the  force  development
during  the  jump.  Hitting  a  baseball  or  golf  ball  includes  a
stretch-shortening  cycle.  Movement  of  the  legs  and  hips
leads movement of the upper body, which creates an elastic
load on the torso and a subsequent whip-like motion of the
upper body. More power is generated under such conditions
than occurs using the upper body muscles alone.

Muscle force increases during lifts by timing the recoil from
muscle  stretch  with  the  activation  of  muscle  contraction.
This advice is contrary to that given by many trainers who
teach  clients  to  perform  exercises  in  a  slow,  controlled
manner.  While  slow,  controlled  training  certainly  has  its
place, some portion of the training program should attempt
to  activate  elastic  muscle  energy  because  of  its  incredible
capacity  to  increase  muscle  force  (and  ultimately  muscle
size). Note that rapid training is appropriate for young power
athletes but not young bodybuilders. Personal trainers must
always consider the needs and physiological demands of the
individuals they supervise and instruct.

Even  in  the  controlled  setting  of  a  weight  lifting  contest
or  when  performing  low-rep,  high  intensity  lifts,  you  can
use elastic energy stored in the muscle. Teach clients to get
into position, stay tight, and ‚Äúexplode‚Äù when they lift. This
allows the nervous system to turn on muscles rapidly and
effectively use the elastic recoil of the muscle stretch.

There  are  many  things  sports  scientists  still  need  to  learn
about the role of the stretch-shortening cycle in producing
the best lift. Can the elastic component in muscle be trained?
In other words, can you increase elasticity in muscle? If you
can,  then  you  should  elastically  load  muscles  as  much  as
possible during training. It is probable that muscle elasticity
cannot be trained very much. If that is the case, then working
on timing during lifts is important. Whatever the outcome
of research in this area, it is obvious that the personal trainer
must consider muscle elasticity when designing individual
training and conditioning programs for young athletes.

Strength  training  programs  must  consider  the  contractile,
neural-muscular,  and  elastic  components  of  muscle
contraction and movement. Many athletes and coaches focus
on  contractile  tissue  adaptations  when  designing  training
programs. However, maximum strength, maximum power,
and achieving maximum muscle size requires coordination
of neural activation, storage and release of muscular elastic
energy, and contraction of muscle.

SKIll
Motor skills are highly specific. They involve precise motor
unit  recruitment  patterns  orchestrated  by  the  nervous
system.  Franklin  Henry,  a  research  scientist  from  the
University of California at Berkeley, compared this process
to a computer program stored on a disk. The program, which
is imprinted on the brain, plays back as a motor reflex. Years
of research in motor learning show that skills develop best
when practiced at the same speed and in the same manner
as  in  competition.  Research  on  most  high-power  sports
shows  that  athletes  benefit  more  from  improvements  in

Youth FitnessMuscle Physiology  |  59

linked motions: Sequential
activation of muscle groups
and joint movements when
performing motor skills.

skill than improvements in strength. This is a very important concept. Skill development
should never be sacrificed for strength and fitness training.

Most skills involve linked motions‚Äîactivation of body segments in sequence. Effective
movement  requires  activation  of  each  sequence  in  a  precise  order,  while  exerting  the
correct  amount  of  force.  In  addition  to  skill  training,  physical  conditioning  should
prepare each segment for optimal activation. ‚ÄúLinkage versus leakage‚Äù is a critical goal
in maximizing the performance of a skill. The personal trainer and coach should strive
to teach children proper technique and movement skills so they can perform smooth,
linked motions as they age.

coordInaTIng cellular, neural, elaSTIc,
and SKIll comPonenTS oF STrengTh
Maximizing force development requires coordination of the four strength components.
Evidence  suggests  that  training  slightly  alters  muscle  stiffness  and  muscle  elasticity.
However,  elastic  energy  is  most  useful  if  it  can  be  translated  into  motor  movements.
Neural  changes  occur  early  in  the  strength  training  program.  Cellular  changes  occur
later and probably are strength‚Äôs main limiting factor. Improvements in skill acquisition
can occur throughout life and contribute to maximum force development.

International Sports Sciences Association60  |  unit 4

SuMMARY

Strength development involves muscle cells, neuromuscular activation, elastic
energy, and skill. Muscle hypertrophy requires tension, anabolic hormones,
nutrients, and rest. Muscles increase in size by adding myofibrils rather than
new muscle cells. Neural activation of motor units plays an important role in
increasing muscular strength. The body increases force output by turning on
more motor units. It regulates force by the number and amount of time it ac-
tivates specific units. Successful athletes, particularly those just beginning their
careers in sports, must learn to use elastic energy. Activating muscle elastic
recoil can greatly increase muscle force output. Improving one‚Äôs skill level at
any early age represents the fastest and most efficient way to increase force
output in sports. Never substitute conditioning for skill development. Both are
important and they complement each other.

Principles of Temperature regulation

Summary

uNIT 5

# Temperature Regulation

62  |  unit 5

Key Terms

poikilotherm

homeotherm

heat stroke

pilomotor center
