Personal trainers who work with kids should have a good working knowledge of human
temperature  regulation  during  exercise.  Intense  exercise  increases  heat  production  10
to 20 times above rest. Humans are mammals and must maintain a near constant body
temperature. Death occurs quickly if body temperature increases or decreases too much.

Children have immature temperature regulation systems. They cannot sweat or redirect
blood flow from one part of the body to another the way adults do. Children get cold
more  quickly  than  adults  and  are  prone  to  hypothermia.  Children  are  less  capable  of
high intensity exercise, are metabolically less efficient, have a lower cardiac output at the
same exercise intensity, and have a lower sweat rate compared to adults. They also have
a large body surface area compared to their muscle mass, which can aid in protection
from heat injury.

Animals either have body temperatures that vary with the environment (poikilotherms)
or  that  remain  constant  (homeotherms).  Examples  of  poikilotherms  include  fish,
amphibians, and reptiles. Birds,  humans,  and  other  mammals  are homeotherms. They
can function relatively independent of the environment because they maintain constant
temperatures.  Processes  such  as  oxygen  transport,  cellular  metabolism,  and  muscle
contraction remain unimpaired in hot and cold environments if a homeotherm maintains
an internal temperature compatible with life. The body‚Äôs various physiological functions,
such  as  transmission  of  nerve  impulses,  work
well provided the body temperature remains in a
‚Äúnormal‚Äù range. Abnormal increases and decreases
in  body  temperature  are  deadly.  Important  cell
structures  begin  to  deteriorate  at  temperatures
above  41¬∞C.  heat  stroke  and  permanent  brain
damage  can  happen  quickly  if  the  rising  body
temperature is not quickly brought under control.
At  temperatures  below  34¬∞C  (hypothermia),
cellular  metabolism  slows,  greatly  leading  to
unconsciousness and abnormal heart rhythms.

poikilotherm: Cold-blooded
animal such as a fish or
reptile, whose internal body
temperature varies with the
environment.

homeotherm: Organism that
maintains a constant internal
body temperature.

heat stroke: Severe and
often fatal illness produced
by exposure to excessively
high temperatures, especially
when accompanied by intense
exercise. Symptoms may include
increased body temperature,
lack of sweating (50% of victims
continue to sweat), hot dry skin,
and neurological symptoms,
unconsciousness, paralysis,
headache, vertigo, confusion.
In severe cases very high fever,
vascular collapse, and coma
develop.

hypothermia: Low body
temperature due to exposure
to the cold. Opposite of
hyperthermia: Abnormally high
body temperature.

Youth FitnessTemperature Regulation  |  63

# Principles Of
TEMPERATuRE REguLATION
Normal  resting  body  temperature  ranges  between  36.5  to  37.5¬∞C.  Temperature  varies
throughout  the  body.  The  internal  temperature  of  the  core  (from  the  brain  to  the
rectum) remains relatively constant, whereas skin temperature comes closer to that of
the environment. Core temperature reflects metabolic rate and the balance between heat
production  and  heat  loss,  while  skin  temperature  reflects  environmental  temperature,
metabolic  rate,  clothing,  and  body  water  levels.  Body  temperature  refers  to  the  core
temperature which includes the temperature of key parts of the brain and the temperature
of vital internal organs such as the heart, liver, and kidneys.

# Hypothalamus

The body regulates its temperature by controlling the rate of
heat production and heat loss. When the rate of heat
production equals the rate of heat loss, the body
achieves heat balance. Without this balance, the
body either gains or loses heat. The hypothalamus
in the brain controls heat balance, with feedback
from heat and cold sensors in the skin. The
hypothalamus works like a thermostat; it
triggers heat production when body temperature
falls and triggers heat loss when it rises.

The  body  regulates  temperature  using  physical
and  chemical  methods.  Physical  temperature
regulation  works  by  changing  the  resistance
to heat flow, while chemical methods increase
metabolic rate.

heaT ProducTIon

Metabolism
Metabolism is the body‚Äôs source of internal heat production. Metabolism refers to the sum
total of all the chemical reactions that occur in the body. Most of the energy exchanges
during  metabolism  end  up  as  heat.  The  body  produces  heat  even  during  deep  sleep.
During  exercise,  heat  production  increases  considerably.  The  principle  mechanisms
of  heat  production  include  voluntary  (exercise)  and  involuntary  (shivering)  muscle
contraction  and  biochemical  heat  production  caused  by  the  secretion  of  thyroid
hormone, epinephrine, and norepinephrine.

Shivering
Shivering  is  the  primary  mechanism  for  increasing  heat  production  in  response  to
cold.  Maximal  shivering  can  increase  the  body‚Äôs  heat  production  by  up  to  five  times.
Compared with muscular exercise, it is an efficient way to increase body temperature
because shivering muscles do not do any work, and most of the expended energy appears
as heat. However, shivering adds to heat loss by increasing the thermal gradient (the
difference in temperature between the person and the environment). In the short run,
shivering keeps you warm. In the long run, shivering speeds heat loss.

hypothalamus: Portion of the
lower brain  which secretes
substances that control
metabolism through
the pituitary gland.
The hypothalamus
helps to regulate body
temperature, appetite,
water balance, blood
sugar, and fat metabolism.
The hypothalamus also regulates
other glands such as the ovaries,
testes, parathyroids, and
thyroid.

shivering: Physiologic method
of heat production in humans
and other mammals that
involves involuntary trembling
or quivering from contraction or
twitching of the muscles.

thyroid hormone: Thyroxine
and triiodothyronine, the
hormones secreted by the
thyroid gland, influence growth,
and metabolism.

thermal gradient:
Temperature differences in two
parts of the body or between
the body and the environment.

International Sports Sciences Association64  |  unit 5

Children lose heat more quickly than adults do because they have a high body surface
area compared to their muscle mass. A child could be very cold‚Äîdangerously so‚Äîand
the adult may not feel anything. Be very careful when working with kids in the cold. They
do not have the cold tolerance that adults do. Shivering is a danger sign in a child.

Heat-generating  hormones  increase  metabolic  rate.  This  can  be  seen  in  increased
thyroxin secretion from the thyroid gland, norepinephrine release from the sympathetic
nervous  system,  and  epinephrine  (adrenaline)  secretion  from  the  adrenal  glands.
Thyroxin  increases  the  metabolic  rate  of  all  the  body‚Äôs  cells.  Norepinephrine  triggers
the release of fatty acids, which also increase metabolic heat production because fat use
generates more heat.

Q10 Effect
Increased  temperature  can  be  self-perpetuating  and  dangerous.  This  is  because  the
metabolic  rate  increases  with  rising  temperature  due  to  the  Q10  effect  (i.e.,  the  rate
of  chemical  reactions  doubles  with  each  10¬∞C  increase  in  temperature).  At  high
temperatures, the hypothalamus begins to lose its ability to cool the body.

Unfortunately, the rate of temperature increase occurs faster at these higher temperatures.
At  core  temperatures  above  41.5¬∞C,  the  only  recourse  is  external  cooling  because  the
hypothalamus may no longer be functionally effective.

Radiation, Conduction, and Convection
People  can  gain  heat  passively  in  one  of  three  ways,  through:  (1)  radiation,  (2)
conduction,  or  (3)  convection.  These  processes  will  be  described  in  the  heat  loss
section that follows.

Q10 Effect: Rate of chemical
reactions double with each 10¬∞
C increase in temperature.

radiation: Heat loss or gain in
the form of infrared rays.

conduction: Transfer of heat
between objects.

convection: Gain or loss of
heat to or from air or water.

absolute zero: Lowest possible
temperature (0¬∞ Kelvin, -273.15¬∞
Celsius, -459.67¬∞ Fahrenheit)
where all molecular motion
ceases.

# Radiant Heat

# Radiation

heaT loSS
The  body  loses  heat  by  radiation,  conduction,  convection,  and  evaporation.  At  room
temperature, when skin temperature exceeds air temperature, most
heat  is  lost  by  outward  heat  flow  caused  by  the  negative  thermal
gradient. In the heat, or during heavy exercise, evaporation becomes
the dominant mechanism of heat dissipation.

Radiation
Radiation is the gain or loss of heat in the form of infrared rays.
At room temperature and at rest, it accounts for 60 percent of
the total heat loss. Radiant heat loss varies with body position
and  clothing.  Any  substance  not  at  absolute  zero  will
radiate  such  waves.  However,  the  body  is  both  radiating  and
receiving them at the same time. If the body temperature (the
temperature of the nude body) is greater than the surrounding
environment, then more heat radiates from the body than to it.
If  the  temperature  of  the  radiating  bodies  around  it  is  greater,
then the net flow of radiation is inward. The insulation provided
by  clothing  affects  gain  and  loss  of  heat  by  radiation.  Children
gain and lose considerable heat by radiation because of their large
surface area compared to their relatively small muscle mass. People
can gain heat by radiation when they sit in the hot sun.

# Convection

# Evaporation

# Conduction

Temperature Regulation  |  65

heat transfer: Movement of
heat from one place to another.

windchill: Effects of air
movement on reducing the
effective temperature.

evaporation: Change from a
liquid to a vapor (gas) form.

insensible water loss: Water
loss from the body that does not
occur through the sweat glands.

Conduction
Conduction transfers heat from the body to an object. About three percent of the total
heat loss at room temperature occurs by this mechanism. A good example of conduction
is the transfer of heat to a chair while a person sits on it. Heat loss in the urine and feces
is another example of conduction. People gain heat from objects such as heating pads
and hot water bottles.

Convection
The conduction of heat to a fluid (air or water) is called convection. This accounts for
about 12 percent of the heat loss at room temperature. In convection, heat conducted to
air or water moves so that other particles also can be heated. As heat is transmitted to the
surrounding air, it rises, allowing additional heat transfer to the surrounding air. Heat
loss by convection occurs much more rapidly in water than in air. People gain heat from
convection when they sit in a hot bath or hot tub.

Heat loss by convection is greater in the wind because warmed air is quickly replaced
by colder air, lowering the effective temperature. For example, the heat loss at 10¬∞C in a
2.2-mph wind is the same as that at 10¬∞C in still air. The effect of wind on temperature is
called windchill and is expressed in kilocalories per hour per square meter (kcal/hr/m2)
of exposed skin surface. Wind speeds above 40 mph have no additional effect on heat loss
because the heat transfer to the skin does not occur rapidly enough.

Evaporation (Sweating)
At  rest  in  a  comfortable  environment,  about  25  percent  of  heat  loss  occurs  due  to
evaporation  of  sweat,  the  watery  substance  secreted  from  the  sweat  glands.  It  is  the
only means of cooling at high environmental temperatures and is critically important
during exercise. When the environmental temperature exceeds that of the skin, the body
gains heat by radiation, conduction, and convection. Body temperature rises if the body
cannot lose heat by evaporation under these circumstances. This occurs in a steam bath,
athletic whirlpool, or hot tub.

The body loses 0.58 kCal of heat for each gram of sweat (water) that evaporates. Sweating
is effective for cooling only if it evaporates. The rate of evaporation is greatly reduced or
totally prevented during high humidity so that the sweat remains in the fluid state. Lack
of  air  movement  hampers  effective  evaporation  because  water  vapor  saturates  the  air
surrounding the body, just as it does inside a sweat suit or football uniform. This explains
why any type of fan helps with cooling on a hot day. Younger children (ages 5 to 10) have
about one-half the sweating capacity of teenagers and adults.

Evaporation occurs because fluid (from sweat or insensible water loss) changes to a
gas as the temperature of the fluid increases. Water evaporates insensibly from the skin
and lungs at a rate of about 600 ml daily. This amounts to a continual heat loss of about
12 to 18 kCal hourly. Insensible water loss cannot be controlled and it occurs regardless
of body temperature. Regulating the rate of sweating controls evaporative sweat loss.

Except for insensible water loss, sweat rates are essentially zero when skin temperature
is low. In hot weather, an unacclimatized individual (person not used to the heat) has a
maximum sweat rate of about 1.5 liters an hour, while an acclimatized person can sweat
up to four liters an hour. During maximum sweating, an adult can lose 3.6 kg body weight
per hour (nearly eight pounds of fluid), while a pre-pubertal child loses about one-half
that. However, the size of sweat droplets is smaller in children, so they tend to evaporate
more sweat, which results in greater cooling per volume of sweat.

conTrol bY The hYPoThalamuS
The hypothalamus located in the lower brain houses the temperature regulatory center.
Heat-sensitive nerves in the anterior (front) hypothalamus control the responses to heat.
The posterior (rear) hypothalamus controls responses to cold. Some temperature control
is attributed to skin and upper brain receptors, but the hypothalamus is by far the most
important.  Hot  and  cold  skin  temperature  receptors  transmit  nerve  impulses  to  the
spinal cord and then to the hypothalamus, which initiates the appropriate response such
as sweating, shivering, and goose bumps.

Overheating the hypothalamus triggers heat loss mechanisms of sweating and increased
skin  blood  flow,  which  speeds  heat  loss  to  the  outside  air. The  anterior  hypothalamus
stimulates  the  sweat  glands,  resulting  in  evaporative  heat  loss  from  the  body.  The
hypothalamus  also  sends  signals  to  the  cardiovascular  control  center  in  the  brain  to
increase skin blood flow. (Figure 5.1.)

Cold receptors, located primarily in the skin, send signals to the posterior hypothalamus
to  increase  heat  production  and  to  protect  the  brain  and  vital  organs  (the  core).  The
hypothalamus signals high brain centers to restrict (vasoconstrict) blood flow to the skin.
However, the skin blood vessels open up periodically, particularly in the hands and feet,
to maintain the health of the skin. Stimulation of the shivering center triggers shivering.

Cold stimulates the pilomotor center, which causes piloerection (goose bumps), a
reflex from our ‚Äúhairy evolutionary ancestors‚Äù to increase the insulation air space over the
skin. The posterior hypothalamus also causes the release of norepinephrine to mobilize
fatty  acids  and  increase  metabolic  heat  production.  This  section  of  the  hypothalamus
indirectly  increases  thyroid  hormone  production,  which  causes  a  general  increase  in
metabolic rate.

The  hypothalamus
the  body‚Äôs  central
functions  as
thermostat  by  keeping  core  temperature  within  a  normal
range. When core temperature rises above or below its ‚Äúset-
point,‚Äù  the  hypothalamus  immediately  initiates  processes
to  increase  heat  production  or  heat  loss.  Under  normal
conditions,  sweating  begins  at  almost  precisely  37¬∞C  and
heat production begins below this point.

Temperature regulation mechanisms during exercise in the
heat  respond  to  relative  exercise  intensity  (i.e.,  percent  of
maximum  effort)  rather  than  absolute  intensity.  Children
do  not  exercise  as  intensely  as  adults  during  sports  and
exercise training. They exercise at lower intensities because
they are smaller and have lower metabolic capacities.

Human behavior is an important component in temperature
regulation; a person who feels too hot or too cold usually
does something about it. When hot, the person may drink
a cold glass of water, remove some clothing, or turn on the
air  conditioning.  When  cold,  he  or  she  may  put  on  some
clothes, turn up the heat, move around, or go inside. Parents,
coaches,  and  trainers  must  remind  children  to  bundle-up
when it‚Äôs cold and to drink fluids and stay out of the direct
sun when it‚Äôs hot.

The National Institute of Arthritis and Musculoskeletal and
Skin Diseases (NIAMS; www.niams.nih.gov) has presented
the  following  five  sensible  guidelines  for  preventing  heat
injuries in children during exercise and sports:

1.  Schedule regular fluid breaks during practice and
games. Kids need to drink eight ounces of fluid‚Äî
preferably water‚Äîevery 20 minutes, and more
after playing.

2.  Have the child wear light-colored, ‚Äúbreathable‚Äù

clothing.

3.  Make player substitutions more frequently in

the heat.

4.  Use misting sprays on the body to keep cool.

5.  Know the signs of heat-related problems, includ-
ing confusion; dilated pupils; dizziness; fainting;
headache; heavy perspiration; nausea; pale and
moist or hot, dry skin; weak pulse; and weakness.
Seek medical attention immediately if the child
experiences any combination of these symptoms
or doesn‚Äôt seem quite right.

Figure 5.1 Thermal Balance. Thermal balance occurs when heat production is equal to heat loss.

International Sports Sciences Association68  |  unit 5

SuMMARY

Humans can tolerate extremely hot and cold climates because of a well-devel-
oped ability to control body temperature within a fairly small range of temepra-
tures. Controlling the rate of heat production and heat loss regulates body tem-
perature. Mechanisms of heat production include basal metabolic rate, shivering,
exercise, hormone-induced thermogenesis, Q10 effect, radiation, conduction, and
convection. The body loses heat by radiation, conduction, convection, evapora-
tion, and excretion. The hypothalamus is the main center for temperature regula-
tion.

Children have immature temperature regulatory systems‚Äîthey cannot sweat or
shiver as well as adults can. Also, they have a large body surface area compared
to their muscle mass. They are at increased risk for cold injury. In the heat, they
cannot sweat as well as adults but tend to be able to regulate temperature be-
cause they do not exercise as hard and they evaporate more sweat because their
sweat droplets are smaller. In warm (not hot) climates, children lose heat faster
than adults, which compensates for their lower sweat rate. At high temperatures,
they are at increased risk. In the cold, children lose heat faster than adults do‚Äî
again, due to their large body surface area. As with adults, increased physical
fitness and making sure the athlete is adequately hydrated are the best protectors
against serious heat injury.

ExERCISE IN ThE hEAT
Children have immature temperature regulatory systems. They do not sweat as much as
adults, they have lower cardiac outputs at a given exercise intensity, and their bodies are
less efficient during aerobic exercise. They also have less muscle mass and shorter bones,
so  they  are  less  capable  of  high  intensity  exercise.  For  many  years,  scientists  believed
children had a lower heat tolerance during exercise than adults. However, more recent
studies indicate that heat tolerance during exercise is similar in children and adults.

A nude human lying in still, dry air maintains a constant body temperature when the
outside  temperature  ranges  between  54  and  60¬∞C  (129  to  140¬∞F).  Exercise  in  the  heat
increases the risk of hyperthermia (elevated body temperature). The rate of exercise is
the  most  important  factor  in  this  increased  level  of  risk.  Core  temperature  increases
proportionally  with  increasing  intensities  of  exercise.  Considerable  variability  in  core
temperature at any absolute exercise intensity exists, yet little variation occurs with the
load expressed as a percentage of maximum capacity.

The extent of the effect of environmental temperature on exercise capacity depends on the
body‚Äôs ability to dissipate heat and maintain adequate blood flow to the active muscles.
During exercise in the heat, the combined circulatory demands of muscle and skin can
impair  oxygen  transport  capacity.  An  added  problem  is  that  the  general  vasodilation
caused by the inhibition of the vasomotor center increases the amount of blood in the
venous  system  (capacitance  vessels).  Additionally,  plasma  volume  decreases  during
exercise, becoming increasingly acute as the intensity of effort increases. The decrease
in plasma volume can be augmented by the loss of body fluids through sweating. This
can become a potentially acute problem during dehydration. During exercise in the heat,
to  put  it  simply,  there  may  not  be  enough  blood  to  go  around.  Decreases  in  central
blood volume can compromise the return of blood to the heart. This can be a problem
in children because they have relatively lower cardiac outputs than adults at any given
exercise intensity.

unacclimatized humans have several physiological mechanisms for dealing with heat
stress  during  exercise.  Sweating  is  the  primary  means  of  heat  dissipation  under  these
circumstances. First, heart rate increases during submaximal exercise. This mechanism
becomes less effective at higher exercise intensities because the heart rate approaches its
maximum level. When the individual achieves her highest level of physical exertion (i.e.,
maximum),  peripheral  vasoconstriction  overrides  the  vasodilation  originally  elicited
in  response  to  heat  stress.  This  helps  to  maintain  blood  pressure  and  cardiac  output.

Youth FitnessExercise in the heat and Cold  |  71

Unfortunately, this response negatively effects heat transfer.
In  this  case,  circulatory  regulation  overrides  temperature
regulation. These circumstances explain the danger of heat
injury during exercise in hot climates.

Maximal  oxygen  consumption,  as  measured  during  a
laboratory treadmill test, does not decline in the heat unless
the  person  is  overheated  when  the  test  begins.  Athletes
rarely  have  the  luxury  of  going  from  the  comforts  of  air-
conditioned, normal room temperature into the heat of an
environmental  chamber  for  athletic  contests;  they  usually
have  to  sleep,  eat,  and  train  in  the  adverse  environment
before they perform. Dehydration, lack of sleep, and anxiety
can accelerate the degree of physiological and psychological
stress. Most studies show that maximal oxygen consumption
decreases by six to eight percent in preheated subjects. The
effects of heat exposure during maximal exercise in children
are not well studied.

During  the  first  week  of  heat  exposure,  the  body  makes
several adjustments that lower heart rate, core temperature,
and  skin  temperature  at  rest  and  during  submaximal
exercise.  Additionally,  as  exercise  continues  for  longer
durations,  stability  occurs  in  blood  pressure.  The  primary

physiological  adjustments  of  acclimatization  to  heat
include the following five factors:

1.

Increased peripheral conductance

2.

Increased plasma volume

3.

Increased sweating capacity

4.  Fall in the threshold of skin temperature for the

onset of sweating

5.  Better distribution of sweat over the skin

Acclimatization to heat remains incomplete unless exercise
training accompanies the exposure. Children acclimatize to
heat more slowly than adults.

Blood flow to the skin decreases with acclimatization. This
adjustment  helps  to  restore  central  blood  volume‚Äîblood
available to return to the heart‚Äîwhich is vitally important
for maintaining stroke volume and muscle blood flow during
exercise.  The  decrease  in  skin  blood  flow  is  accompanied
by  a  large  increase  in  sweating  and  evaporative  cooling
capacity  that  produces  greater  peripheral  conductance  of
heat. Acclimatized humans have a lower core temperature
during exercise.

International Sports Sciences Association72  |  unit 6

aldosterone: Steroid hormone
produced by the adrenal cortex
that controls salt and water
balance in the kidney.

dehydration: Excessive loss of
body water.

heat cramps: Muscle
spasms and pain caused by
intense exercise in the heat;
sometimes related to salt
deficiency, hyperventilation, or
overindulgence in alcohol.

heat exhaustion: Heat
illness related to dehydration.
Common symptoms include
fatigue, lightheadedness,
nausea, vomiting, headache,
rapid heartbeat and low blood
pressure.

heat stroke: Severe and
often fatal illness produced
by exposure to excessively
high temperatures, especially
when accompanied by intense
exercise. Symptoms may include
increased body temperature,
lack of sweating (50% of victims
continue to sweat), hot dry skin,
and neurological symptoms;
unconsciousness, paralysis,
headache, vertigo, confusion.
In severe cases very high fever,
vascular collapse, and coma
develop.

Acclimatization to heat causes a 12 percent increase in plasma volume, provided exercise
training accompanies the heat exposure. The increase is caused mainly by an increase
in plasma protein. For every 1-gram increase in plasma protein, 15 grams of water are
added  to  the  plasma.  The  increased  plasma  volume  helps  to  maintain  stroke  volume,
central blood volume, and sweating capacity. It also allows the body to hold more heat.

The  increase  in  plasma  volume  is  accompanied  by  an  almost  threefold  increase  in
sweating  capacity  from  about  1.5  liters  an  hour  to  4  liters  an  hour.  Concurrently,  a
more complete and even distribution of sweating across the skin takes place, which is
an  advantage  when  exercising  in  heat  accompanied  by  high  humidity.  Sweat  losses  of
sodium chloride decrease because of increased secretion of aldosterone.

The earlier onset of sweating keeps core temperature under control during the early stages
of exercise. As discussed previously, increased temperature can produce further increases
in temperature from the Q10 effect. The early onset of sweating serves to partially negate
this influence.

Exercise training is essential for heat acclimatization. Training by itself does not provide
a full measure of heat adaptation. Training for endurance in a hot room (i.e., turning up
the heat to ‚Äúhigh‚Äù in a gym during workouts) can acclimatize a person from a temperate
environment who must compete in the heat.

Genetic studies show individual differences in fluid losses during exercise. Small openings
in the cells call aquaporins are more active in some people than others. Genetic variation
in aquaporin activity can double fluid loss during endurance events such as the marathon
or 10-kilometer run.

Thermal dISTreSS
Thermal distress and heat injury are more common in children than adults. However, the
higher incidence is probably more related to child abuse and dependence issues rather
than an increased risk of heat injury in children. The medical literature has few reports of
heat injuries during sports or exercise in children. In general, heat injuries have become
more common in the general population because of the popularity of competitive and
recreational sports. Children can perform reasonably well and safely in the heat if they
are  physically  fit,  consume  enough  fluid,  and  allow  for  adequate  recovery  between
competitions.

Fortunately,  one  can  prevent  heat  stress  and  injury  by  taking  simple  precautions.
Thermal distress includes dehydration (loss of body fluid), heat cramps (involuntary
cramping  of  skeletal  muscle),  heat  exhaustion  (low  blood  pressure  and  weakness
caused by circulatory inability to compensate for vasodilation of skin blood vessels), and
heat stroke (failure of hypothalamic temperature regulatory function).

Dehydration
Dehydration  is  the  loss  of  fluid  from  the  body.  Dehydration  can  decrease  sweat  rate,
plasma volume, cardiac output, maximal oxygen uptake, work capacity, muscle strength,
and liver glycogen. Dehydration is a common condition during exercise in the heat, yet
it can occur even in thermally neutral environments. Dehydration rates are similar in
children and adults.

Youth FitnessExercise in the heat and Cold  |  73

A water deficit of 700 ml (approximately 1 percent of body weight) will cause thirst. At
a fluid deficit of 5 percent of body weight, the person feels discomfort and alternating
states  of  lethargy  and  nervousness.  Irritability,  fatigue,  and  loss  of  appetite  are  also
characteristic of this level of dehydration. Five percent dehydration is extremely common
in football, tennis, and distance running. Dehydration levels greater than 7 percent are
extremely dangerous. At these levels, salivating and swallowing food become difficult.
At fluid losses above 10 percent, the ability to walk is impaired and discoordination and
spasticity can occur. As 15 percent dehydration approaches, delirium and shriveled skin
are experienced along with decreased urine volume, loss of the ability to swallow food,
and difficulty swallowing water. Above 20 percent weight loss, the skin bleeds and cracks.
This is the upper limit of tolerance of dehydration before death.

Osmoreceptors  in  the  hypothalamus  stimulate  the  drive  to  drink  fluids.  The  US
Department of Agriculture (www.usda.org) recommends that people use thirst to help
them gauge dehydration. It is crucial for athletes who exercise in the heat to take regular
fluid breaks. It also is prudent to weigh athletes before each practice, especially early in
the season and during hot weather.

Certain types of fluid replacement can compound the inadequacy of the thirst mechanism.
Fluids with a high carbohydrate content (glucose or sucrose greater than 9 grams per
100  ml  of  water)  take  longer  to  empty  from  the  stomach.  Numerous  research  studies
reinforce the benefits of modern athletic fluid replacement beverages such as Cytomax.
Cold water also serves as an excellent fluid replacement beverage. Some athletes prefer
the commercial fluids because they are more palatable. Cold milk and chocolate milk
are excellent fluid replacement beverages for children, provided the child is not lactose
intolerant. As long as a suitable fluid is selected, the wishes of the athletes in terms of fluid
preference should be honored. The important point is to get them to drink enough fluid.
Voluntary fluid intake during exercise is similar in children and adults.

osmoreceptors: Receptors in
the hypothalamus that respond
to changes in the osmotic
pressure (due to solid material in
the blood fluid) of the blood.

International Sports Sciences Association74  |  unit 6

electrolyte: Chemical that
dissociates into ions (has a
negative or positive charge) in
a solution and thus conduct
electricity (nerve or muscle
impulses).

rectal temperature:
Temperature of the rectum.
Often used in exercise studies
as a measure of core or central
temperature.

Heat Cramps
Involuntary cramping and spasms in muscle groups used during exercise characterize
heat cramps. While depletion of salt and potassium from the muscles is involved with
the problem, the primary cause is muscle fatigue. Often, people who do not experience
heat cramps are fit and heat acclimatized. Children are particularly susceptible to heat
cramps, especially in their legs.

The  classic  treatment  for  heat  cramps  includes  oral  or  intravenous  salt  replacement
and  rehydration.  Because  heat  cramps  reflect  muscle  fatigue,  replacing  fluids  and
electrolytes  will  not  have  any  immediate  benefit.  The  best  treatment  is  gentle  static
stretching, fluid and electrolyte replacement, and rest.

Heat Exhaustion
Heat  exhaustion  and  heat  stroke  are  not  distinct  entities  but  rather  two  degrees  of
thermal  injury.  A  rapid,  weak  pulse,  low  blood  pressure,  faintness,  profuse  sweating,
and  psychological  disorientation  characterize  heat  exhaustion.  It  results  from  not
enough  blood  returning  to  the  heart  because  of  competition  for  blood  between  the
muscles  and  skin.  Although  core  temperature  (which  can  be  estimated  by  measuring
rectal temperature) may be elevated somewhat [usually less than 39.5¬∞C (103.1¬∞F)],
it does not reach the extremely high level associated with heat stroke [greater than 41¬∞C
(105.8¬∞F)]. (Figure 6.1)

sunstroke: Severe and
often fatal illness produced
by exposure to excessively
high temperatures, especially
when accompanied by intense
exercise. Symptoms may include
increased body temperature,
lack of sweating (50% of victims
continue to sweat), hot dry skin,
and neurological symptoms;
unconsciousness, paralysis,
headache, vertigo, confusion.
In severe cases very high fever,
vascular collapse, and coma
develop.

The treatment for heat exhaustion includes supine rest in a cool area and fluid ingestion.
In some cases, intravenous fluid administration may be appropriate. The athlete should
not participate in any further activity for the rest of the day and should be encouraged to
consume fluids hourly if possible for the next 24 hours.

Heat Stroke (Sunstroke)
Heat stroke is the failure of the hypothalamic temperature regulatory center; it represents
a major medical emergency. Heat stroke is principally caused by a failure of the sweating
center,  which  results  in  an  explosive  rise  in  body  temperature  because  the  body  does
not sweat enough. Heat stroke is characterized by a high core temperature (greater than
41¬∞C),  hot,  dry  skin,  and  extreme  confusion  or  unconsciousness.  In  exercise-related
heat  stroke,  the  person  may  still  be  sweating.  The  cardiovascular  effects  are  variable,
with some individuals experiencing low blood pressure and others experiencing a full
bounding pulse and high blood pressure. A heat stroke victim should be packed in ice
and immediately transported to a hospital.

PrevenTIng heaT ProblemS
Children have a higher incidence of heat injury than adults, but most cases occur because
of child abuse and poor supervision rather than an increased susceptibility toward heat
injury in children. The problems associated with exercise in the heat can be minimized
by following 10 simple principles:

1.  Ensure that children are in good physical condition. Heat acclimatization oc-

curs more slowly in children than adults, so there should be a gradual increase
in intensity and duration of training until the athlete is fully acclimatized.
Obese children are more susceptible to heat injury than lean ones. This might
be due to lower fitness levels in kids carrying more body fat.

2.  Schedule practice sessions and games during the cooler times of the day. Allow

adequate rest between competitions when more than one game is played on
the same day.

3.  Modify or cancel exercise sessions when wet bulb globe temperature exceeds

25.5¬∞C. Wet bulb temperature determines humidity, and globe temperature
indicates radiant heat.

4.  Plan for regular water breaks.

5.  Supply a cold drink (8 to 13¬∞C) that is low in sugar (less than 8 grams per 100

ml) and that contains a small amount of electrolytes.

6.  Encourage athletes to ‚Äútank up‚Äù before practice or games by drinking 400 to

600 ml of water 30 minutes before activity.

7.  Encourage fluid replacement during the early stages of practice and competi-

tion. As exercise progresses, water absorption from the gut decreases.

8.  Weigh athletes every day before practice. Any athlete whose body weight

decreases by 3 percent or more should not be allowed to participate until they
rehydrate. People who tend to lose considerable weight in the heat should be
identified and monitored closely.

9.  Do not use salt pills. Encourage athletes to consume ample amounts of salt at

mealtime by sprinkling a small ‚Äúpinch‚Äù of salt on their food.

10.  Discourage athletes from wearing rubberized ‚Äúsauna suits‚Äù to reduce weight.
Instead, emphasize attaining an optimal body composition for the sport.

International Sports Sciences Association76  |  unit 6

hypothermia: Abnormally
low body temperature due to
exposure to the cold. Opposite
of hyperthermia; Abnormally
high body temperature.

ExERCISE IN ThE COLD
Until recently, hypothermia was a factor in sports and exercise only when survival was
at  stake  following  mishaps  in  skiing,  climbing,  or  camping.  People  rarely  experienced
cold stress during exercise because increased metabolism largely prevented negative heat
balance,  and  they  could  put  on  more  clothing  when  they  did  become  cold.  However,
beginning  about  25  years  ago,  large  numbers  of  marginally  fit  people‚Äîincluding
children‚Äîbegan  to  participate  in  endurance  triathlons  (competitions  involving  long
distance  swimming,  bicycling,  and  running)  and  marathons  that  often  exposed  them
to  cold  temperatures  for  many  hours.  People  became  hypothermic  because  the  heat
production of exercise could not keep pace with heat loss, and they had little opportunity
to protect themselves with adequate clothing. Children are susceptible to hypothermia
because they have a large body surface area compared to their muscle mass, which causes
them to lose heat rapidly in the cold. The head has a dense blood supply, so wearing a hat
in the cold is important for children. Be aware that because adults lose heat more slowly
from the head, they may have trouble judging the onset of hypothermia in children.

Cold causes problems for athletes, as you can appreciate when watching football games
played in the snow. The numbing of exposed flesh and the awkwardness and extra weight
of  protective  clothing  handicap  the  players.  Cold  impairs  finger  dexterity  needed  for
catching  and  throwing‚Äîcold  effectively  anesthetizes  sensory  receptors  in  the  hands.
Exposed flesh, particularly on the face, is susceptible to frostbite, a potentially serious
medical condition.

Clothing is important during physical activity in the cold. The insulation value of clothing
must be balanced with the increased metabolic heat production of exercise. A person
who wears too much clothing risks becoming a tropical person in a cold environment.
There have been instances of heat stroke in over-clothed people exercising in extremely
cold climates.

Clothes  are  valuable  in  the  cold  because  they  increase  the  body‚Äôs  insulation.  Clothing
traps warm air next to the skin and decreases heat loss by conduction. The best clothing
for exercise in the cold allows sweat to evaporate while providing added protection from
the cold. Clothing should be worn in layers so it can easily be removed as metabolic heat
production increases during exercise. Clothing manufacturers have made tremendous
progress developing lightweight clothing that traps water vapor and provides sufficient
insulation and freedom of movement during exercise.

Exercise  can  partially  or  totally  replace  the  heat  production  of  shivering  during  cold
exposure. Skin blood flow increases during physical activity, which decreases the body‚Äôs
insulation from the cold. Heat production from exercise and shivering must be adequate
to maintain heat balance, or body temperature drops.

Maximal oxygen uptake and the oxygen cost of submaximal exercise remain unaffected
in  the  cold.  The  metabolic  cost  of  submaximal  exercise  by  people  in  wet  clothing
exercising in the wind is about 15 to 20 percent higher than the same exercise performed
in a comfortable environment. Shivering and increased muscle tone increase the oxygen
cost of exercise.

Swimming  in  cold  water  can  produce  marked  deterioration  in  exercise  capacity  and
maximal oxygen consumption. Heat conductance is about 25 times greater in water than
in air. Body fat percentage is an important factor in determining heat loss in cold water.

Youth FitnessExercise in the heat and Cold  |  77

More body fat increases insulation, which slows the transfer of heat to the water. Long-
distance swimmers usually possess considerable body fat, which slows the rate of heat
loss  during  their  prolonged  swims.  Loss  of  flexibility  contributes  to  the  performance
decrement, probably because the cold water increases the viscosity of muscle (fluids in
muscle cells becomes denser) and connective tissue.

acclImaTIzaTIon and
habITuaTIon To cold
People  exposed  to  environmental  stressors  such  as  heat,  cold,  and  altitude  usually
make  adjustments  to  improve  their  comfort.  Adjustments  to  these  environmental
conditions  include  acclimatization  and  habituation.  Acclimatization  is  physiological
compensation to environmental stress occurring over time. Habituation is the lessening
of the sensation associated with a particular environmental stressor. Simply stated, the
body makes physical changes during acclimatization, while in habituation, the person
learns to live with the stressor.

It is difficult to demonstrate cold acclimatization in people not chronically exposed to
cold. Except for primitive societies, humans do not commonly have the opportunity for
chronic cold exposure. There are three basic tests of acclimatization to cold in humans.

1.  Threshold skin temperature that results in shivering. Shivering occurs later in

people exposed to several weeks of cold temperatures. Cold-acclimatized peo-
ple maintain heat production with less shivering by increasing non-shivering
thermogenesis. They increase the secretion of leptin and norepinephrine,
which causes cellular heat production.

2.  Measuring the temperatures of the hands and feet. In the unacclimatized

person, hand and foot temperatures drop progressively with time during cold
exposure. However, the acclimatized person can almost maintain normal
temperature. Acclimatization improves intermittent peripheral vasodilation‚Äî
called the hunting reflex‚Äîthat makes the hands and feet more comfortable.
Habituation also seems to play a part. Some people seem to lose the pain or
learn to tolerate the pain sensations associated with cold feet and hands, even
though little improvement in circulation or temperature occurs. Children are
more susceptible to chilblains, a cold induced condition involving destruction
of capillary beds in the skin, which results in redness, itching, blisters, and
inflammation.

3.  Ability to sleep in the cold. Unacclimatized people shiver so much they cannot
sleep. Sleeping in a cold environment depends on the extent of non-shiv-
ering thermogenesis. Primitive peoples such as the aborigines of Australia
are exceptions. They can sleep in the cold with little or no clothing without
increases in metabolism.

Physical conditioning is beneficial in acclimatization to the cold just as it is beneficial in
acclimatization to the heat and at high altitude. Physical conditioning produces a higher
body temperature in sleep tests in the cold, and sleep becomes more comfortable.

Little is known about cold acclimatization in children. Young skaters who ice skate in cold
skating rinks show reduced cortisol levels, which is a marker of stress and inflammation.
However, other indications suggest that children might adapt to cold in much the same
way as adults.

habituation: Disappearance of
responsiveness to accustomed
stimulation. Getting used to
the stressor with no apparent
adaptation.

leptin: Hormone produced in
fat cells and circulates in blood
that reduces food uptake and
increases energy expenditure.

non-shivering
thermogenesis: Body heat
production triggered by
uncoupling proteins in the cells
or brown fat.

International Sports Sciences Association78  |  unit 6

frostbite: Cold injury
accompanied by pallor,
numbness, and loss of cold
sensation.

hYPoThermIa
The  hypothalamus  cannot  control  body  temperature  at  low  core  temperatures.
Hypothermia  depresses  central  nervous  system  function,  resulting  in  an  inability  to
shiver,  sleepiness,  and  eventually  coma.  Lower  temperatures  also  result  in  a  lower
cellular metabolic rate, creating a vicious circle: decreases in metabolism lead to less heat
production, which further depresses metabolism. Hypothermia can occur when the rate
of heat production during exercise does not keep pace with the rate of heat loss brought
on by the cold environment.

Hypothermia  is  a  serious  medical  problem  in  endurance  competitions  held  in  cold
climates. Death from hypothermia has been reported in road races held in mountainous
regions. Athletes at risk include those who are not properly conditioned, slow runners
(who  are  exposed  longer  and  cannot  generate  sufficient  heat  through  exercise,
particularly during the later stages of endurance races), those who become wet, and those
who become hypoglycemic (low blood sugar). When a risk of hypothermia exists in an
athletic  competition,  race  personnel  should  make  sure  ‚Äúspace  blankets,‚Äù  blankets,  and
warm drinks are readily available as preventive measures.

FroSTbITe
Frostbite, or frozen tissue, typically occurs in exposed areas of the face such as the nose
and earlobes, on the fingers, and on toes. The injury is relatively common in extremely
low air temperatures [-20 to -30¬∞C (-4 to -22¬∞F)] or during periods of high wind chill.
It can cause permanent circulatory damage, and sometimes the frostbitten part must be
amputated because of gangrene. This medical emergency occurs when blood flow to a
body region shuts off, producing infection and tissue death if not treated within hours.

Treat frostbite by placing the frostbitten area in water heated to 37.8 to 42.2¬∞C (100 to
108¬∞F)  until  the  tissue  thaws.  The  water  temperature  should  be  monitored  to  keep  it
from becoming too hot. Tissue damage may occur if the water temperature rises above
42.8¬∞C (109¬∞F). After the body part thaws, it should be kept cool to lessen metabolism
and minimize inflammation. The affected area should not be thawed if there is danger
of refreezing. The affected part should also be covered. The person should be kept warm
with blankets to promote circulation. If left untreated, a gangrenous infection can cause
shock, kidney failure, and delirium.

SuMMARY

The outcome of exercising in high ambient temperatures depends on how well the body dissipates heat and
maintains blood flow to active muscles. Sweating is the primary means of cooling body tissues in the heat. An
increased sweat rate is the most important adaptation that occurs with heat acclimatization. Exercise training is
required to maximize this process.

Thermal injuries can lead to serious medical conditions in both competitive and recreational sports. Thermal in-
juries include heat cramps, heat exhaustion, and heat stroke, injuries typically preceded by dehydration. Children
have a higher incidence of heat injury than adults, but the incidence in sports is relatively rare. Preventive meas-
ures include adequate physical conditioning, exercising during cooler periods of the day, planning regular water
breaks, and limiting workout sessions until adequate acclimatization is achieved.

The type of clothing worn is the most important limiting factor when exercising in the cold. Swimming in the
cold or exercising with wet clothing greatly diminishes performance from accelerated heat loss. Acclimatization
to cold is difficult to demonstrate but does occur. Children have a larger body surface area than adults and tend
to lose heat rapidly from the head on cold days.

Childhood obesity is a health calamity. In the 1960s and 1970s, only about 5 percent of
American children were overweight. Unfortunately over the next 40 years, that percentage
skyrocketed. The most recent study concerning obesity among children and adolescents
reveals shocking results. For youth ages 12 to 19, the prevalence of obesity has climbed
to 18.1 percent. It is even greater among 6- through 11-year-olds (19.6 percent), and is
10.4 percent among 2- through 5-year-olds. Among non-Hispanic black and Mexican-
American  adolescents,  the  prevalence  of  overweight  increased  more  than  100  percent
between 1988‚Äì1994, and 2008 obesity rates are 26.8 percent in Mexican American girls
and 29.2 percent in non-Hispanic blacks. (Figures 7.1 and 7.2 on next page)

Obesity  is  ‚Äúnot  cool‚Äù  among  kids,  and  this  in  part  has  led  to  a  runaway  epidemic  of
eating disorders. Seventy percent of elementary school aged kids believe they are fat, and
15 percent meet the diagnostic criteria for eating disorders. The personal trainer who
works with kids can help to establish exercise and nutrition habits designed to thwart
the advances in obesity. The trainer and allied health professional also can guide children
toward developing a realistic body image through positive sports and fitness experiences.

Being too fat in childhood creates both immediate and long-term health effects. Many
children develop early symptoms of Type 2 diabetes‚Äîparticularly insulin resistance‚Äî
as well as risk factors for cardiovascular disease that include high blood pressure, high
blood  cholesterol  and  triglycerides,  low  blood  HDL,  and  blood
clotting abnormalities.

Childhood obesity impairs socialization and early learning and can
pyramid into psychological trauma that lasts a lifetime. Childhood
obesity  usually  continues  into  adulthood,  which  creates  life-long
health problems. The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention
(www.cdc.org)  speculate  that  the  present  generation  may  be  the
first  to  have  more  ‚Äúdiseases  of  civilization‚Äù  and  decreased
longevity  than  their  parents.  Figure  7.3  lists  some  of  the  health
problems linked to childhood obesity.

Figure 7.3 Health Problems Related to Obesity and Eating Disorders
in Children. Lung Problems: Asthma, Sleep Apnea, Sleep Disorders; Cardio-
vascular Risk Factors: High blood pressure (hypertension), Abnormal blood fats
(Dyslipidemia), Syndrome X (high insulin, high blood pressure, abdominal fat,
high blood fats, blood clotting problems), Type 2 Diabetes (NIDDM), Insulin
Resistance; Other Health Problems: Menstrual Abnormalities, Gall Bladder
Disease, Liver disease, Learning disabilities, Growth plate
fractures, Psychosocial Effects/Stigma, Eating Disorders, Body Image.

International Sports Sciences Association82  |  unit 7

CAuSES OF
ChILDhOOD ObESITY
Most overweight kids become overweight from overeating
(whatever  the  psychological  reasons)  and  not  getting
enough  exercise.  Recent  studies  of  elementary  and  high
school  students  show  that  children  become  progressively
less physically active as they age. Twenty years ago, physical
education was required for four years in most schools. Now,
most  high  schools  only  require  one  or  two  years.  School
districts also have cut back on school sports and supervised
recreational opportunities, so children have fewer choices to
remain physically active throughout the day.

Portion sizes of food are much larger in the United States
than in other countries. It is more profitable for companies
to  compete  by  offering  larger  portions  than  by  offering
smaller portions for less money. Children today eat 200 to
300 more calories daily than just 10 years ago. As a person
trainer,  you  can  help  youngsters  develop  appropriate
exercise and nutritional habits.

WEIghT REguLATION
IN ChILDREN
Children need enough food to grow properly. You can help
to promote growth and development (and hopefully make
a  difference  in  the  obesity  epidemic)  by  teaching  children
to eat more grains, vegetables, and fruits, including low-fat
dairy and other protein-rich foods, and to exercise and play
sports on a daily basis. Encourage them to limit the amount
of  time  they  spend  watching  television  and  playing  video
games, and strongly encourage active participation in sports

and games. Teach parents and guardians not to restrict fat
intake for children younger than age two. You can also help
older children learn about heart healthy diets.

Older  overweight  children  should  curtail  saturated  fat
intake.  Restricting  consumption  of  high-fat  fast  foods
and  switching  to  low-fat  milk  rather  than  whole  milk  are
not  dangerous.  Check  with  your  physician  or  health  care
provider before you change a child‚Äôs diet.

MEASuRINg bODY
# Composition
IN YOuNg PEOPLE
The  personal  trainer  should  have  a  good  idea  of  a  young
person‚Äôs  starting  point  before  suggesting  nutrition  and
exercise programs. Scientists measure body composition by
dividing the body into three compartments‚Äìmuscle, bone,
and fat. This is oversimplifying body composition, but it is
useful for measuring progress and assessing the effectiveness
of  the  exercise  and  wellness  program.  Measuring  body
composition should be a key procedure for personal trainers
and coaches who work with children:

1.  Body composition assessment provides a starting
point upon which to base current and future deci-
sions about weight loss and weight gain.

2.  Body composition assessment helps set realistic
goals about changing fat and non-fat compart-
ments.

3.  Body composition assessment helps to establish

general health status and can play a crucial role in
the health and fitness goals of everyone.

Youth FitnessObesity in Children & Measuring body Composition  |  83

body mass index (bMI):
An index of the proportion of
weight to height. BMI= weight
(kg)/(height (m)2; BMI= [weight
(lb)/height (in)/height (in)] x
703.

It  is  a  good  idea  to  measure  changes  in  the  body  fat  and  lean  components,  including
individual  girths  and  the  sum  of  trunk  and  extremity  skinfolds,  over  the  course  of  an
exercise  program  because  this  helps  to  gauge  the  effectiveness  of  the  program.  It  also
provides  realistic  feedback  to  the  participants,  coaches  and  trainers,  and  parents  and
guardians. Results can also act as motivators for the participant.

heIghT and weIghT normS
For u.S. chIldren
Occasionally,  a  child  will  be  unusually  muscular  and  will  fall  outside  of  the  norms
for  height  and  weight.  In  general,  body  weight  should  be  proportional  to  height.  The
following figures from the Centers for Disease Control present norms for weight, height,
and weight for height. The norms provide guidelines only. Look for the obvious when
evaluating  body  composition‚Äîexcess  body  fat  and  large  skinfolds  when  making  field
measurements. A high body mass index (weight, kg/height2, m)‚Äîwhile of limited use
in athletic populations‚Äîoften serves as an indicator of obesity. (Figures 7.4 to 7.11)

heIghT-For-weIghT TableS
The earliest attempts in the early 1900s to predict life expectancy relied on height-for-
weight  tables.  Insurance  companies  developed  statistics  to  determine  insurance  rates.
Later, they expanded their model to include the effects of age and frame size. Unfortunately,
the tables did not provide valid information about the relationship between body weight,
disease,  and  premature  death.  An  athletic  person  could  weigh  more  than  the  average
weight-for-height ideal yet still be ‚Äúunder fat‚Äù in total body fat content. The ‚Äúextra‚Äù weight
could simply be additional muscle mass.

Table  7.1  on  page  90  presents  the  still  popular  height-for-weight  tables  for  adult  men
and  women  published  in  1983  by  the  Metropolitan  Life  Insurance  Company  (www.
metlife.com)  based  on  selected  individuals  whom that  company  decided  to  insure for
life insurance.

Frame Size
Note that the tables have columns for small, medium, and large frame size. Unfortunately,
an acceptable definition for small, medium, and large frame has not been established.
Rough guidelines exist, however, to make the distinction (www.halls.md/ideal-weight/
met.htm),  but  the  method  varies  considerably  and  is  often  confusing  to  compute.
Researchers  are  hard  pressed  to  come  up  with  an  acceptable  definition  of  frame  size.
Even at the extremes of the body weight range, whether a 230-pound person or someone
of slight build (short in stature and low body weight), there is no hard and fast rule for
what  should  constitute  a  large,  medium,  or  small  frame  size.  This  dilemma  offers  an
additional reason why height-for-weight tables that include frame size should not serve
as decision makers about weight loss or weight gain, especially for many athletic groups.

As discussed previously, using arbitrary standards for children is difficult and can even
be  unhealthy.  Growth  requires  adequate  calories  and  a  variety  of  nutrients.  Overly
restrictive  dietary  plans  can  compromise  growth  and  development.  A  good  course  of
action  is  to  increase  physical  activity  and  simultaneously  encourage  the  child  to  eat
healthier meals.

bodY maSS Index (bmI)
BMI is another popular index that incorporates height and weight to assess overweight and obesity. BMI‚Äîalthough badly
flawed‚Äîserves as the principle measure of obesity used in epidemiological studies. BMI was first described by 19th century
Belgian social statistician Adolphe Quetelet (1796 to 1874) and originally known as the Quetelet index. The BMI relates body
mass to height squared (BMI = weight√∑height2). For purposes of BMI calculation, (www.cdc.gov/nccdphp/dnpa/growthcharts/
bmi_tools.htm), body weight is expressed in kilograms (kg) and height in meters (m). Clinicians and researchers use the BMI
as a screening tool to evaluate the ‚Äúnormalcy‚Äù of an individual‚Äôs body weight by following this four-step procedure:

measuring bmI using metric measurements: bmI = weight (kg)/height (m)/height2

Step 1. Multiply body weight (pounds) by 0.45 to convert weight to kg
Step 2. Multiply height (inches) by 0.0254 to convert height to meters (m)
Step 3. Multiply Step 2 answer by itself to obtain square meters (m2)
Step 4. Divide Step 1 (mass) by Step 3 (height2)

measuring bmI using english measurements: bmI = [weight (lb)/height (in)/height (in)] x 703

Step 1:  Measure height in inches (convert fraction to decimal value; e.g., 50 1/2 inches is expressed as

50.5 inches)

Step 2: Divide weight by height and divide the answer by height. Multiply by 703.

What Does BMI Reveal?
At BMI‚Äôs between 20 and 25, the disease risk is rated very low. For each 5-unit increase
in BMI, disease risk increases curvilinearly from very low at BMI 20 to 25, to very high
risk at BMI 40 to 45. While BMI serves as a useful screening tool, it should not be used
as a diagnostic tool. Do not use BMI as the sole basis for designing an exercise, diet, or
weight loss program.

# Bmi-For-Age
Children, even those prone to obesity or overweight, have lower BMI‚Äôs than adults. The
Centers  for  Disease  Control  and  Prevention  (CDC)  recommends  bMI-for-age  as  an
appropriate screening tool for identifying overweight children and their future risk of
obesity or overweight. The CDC noted that a high BMI-for-age relates to risk factors for
cardiovascular disease including abnormal blood fats, poor blood sugar regulation, and
high  blood  pressure.  A  high  BMI-for-age  during  puberty  links  to  abnormal  blood  fat
levels and high blood pressure in middle age.

Figures 7.10 and 7.11 on the following pages show the BMI percentiles for children ages
2 to 20 years.

Children at or above the 95th percentile for their age are considered obese, while children
at or above the 85th percentile are overweight. Those at or below the 5th percentile are
underweight. The  percentile  rank  of  a  BMI  score  is  the  percentage  of  BMI‚Äôs  the  same
or  lower.  For  example,  a  BMI-for-age  at  the  85th  percentile  means  that  85  percent  of
children for that age have lower BMI‚Äôs. The CDC has a BMI-for-age calculator that makes
it easy to perform these calculations (apps.nccd.cdc.gov/dnpabmi/Calculator.aspx). As
discussed, BMI or BMI-for-age is only a screening tool; it should not be used as the sole
criteria for designing diet and exercise programs or determining ideal weight.

bMI-for-age: Used to measure
obesity risk in children. A child
is considered overweight if the
BMI-for-age is greater than the
85th percentile.

COMPOSITION OF ThE huMAN bODY
The  body‚Äôs  three  major  structural  components  include
muscle,  fat,  and  bone.  Dr.  Albert  Behnke,  a  pioneer
body  composition  researcher,  developed  the  concept  of  a
reference man and a reference women to serve as a frame
of comparison among people, much like gold serves as the
standard or ‚Äúworth‚Äù of modern United States currency.

The measurement values for the reference man and woman
are based on the average physical dimensions obtained by
Behnke and other researchers from detailed measurements
of  thousands  of  participants  in  large-scale  military  and
national studies representing many countries.

The reference man is taller than the reference female by four
inches,  is  heavier  by  29  pounds,  has  a  skeleton  weighing
more (23 vs. 15 pounds), and has a larger muscle mass (69
vs.  45  pounds)  and  lower  total  fat  content  (23.1  vs.  33.8
pounds) than the reference female. There are no reference
children. The CDC norms show large differences at any age
in children in height and weight.

People‚Äîparticularly children‚Äîshould not strive to achieve

the  same  body  composition  as  the  reference  models. This
goal would  not only  be  unrealistic but difficult to achieve
because  few  men  or  women  in  the  population  have  the
identical  height  and  weight  as  the  standards.  Instead,
the  reference  models  should  serve  as  a  useful  frame  of
comparison  for  the  fat  and  non-fat  compartments  among
males and females and among individuals within the same
gender and age.

The  personal  trainer  should  apply  the  reference  male  and
female  data  to  identify  an  individual‚Äôs  body  composition
relative  to  the  established  standard  compositional  values.
Using  this  approach,  individuals  will  know  how  they
compare to the standards. They accomplish this with the idea
that if they wish to initiate change in their body composition
(for example, improve their lean to fat ratio), they can do
so in a realistic yet prudent manner, under the supervision
of  their  health-care  professional.  This  principle‚Äîwhile
important in adults‚Äîis critical in children. Trying to affect
large changes in body composition in children is unhealthy
and can interfere with growth.

reFerence woman

Weight: 193.7 lbs

Skeletal Weight: 23 lbs

Muscle Mass: 69 lbs

Fat Content: 23.1 lbs

Weight: 164.7 lbs

Skeletal Weight: 15 lbs

Muscle Mass: 45 lbs

Fat Content: 33.8 lbs

eSSenTIal and STorage FaT
The body‚Äôs total fat content exists in two depots or storage sites. The first depot, termed
essential fat, refers to fat stored in the marrow of bones and in the heart, lungs, liver,
spleen, kidneys, intestines, muscles, and fat-rich tissues throughout the central nervous
system. The brain tissue consists mainly of fat, which emphasizes the role that essential
fat plays in normal physiologic functioning‚Äîparticularly in children. The second depot
is called non-essential fat, or storage fat, and serves as an energy reserve.

essential fat: Fat stored in
the marrow of bones and in
the heart, lungs, liver, spleen,
kidneys, intestines, muscles, and
fat-rich tissues throughout the
central nervous system.

non-essential fat: Storage fat
found in adipose tissue.

adIPoSe TISSue
Adipose  tissue‚Äîor  fat  cells‚Äîstore  energy  as  triglycerides  which  is  a  simple  molecule
consisting  of  three  fatty  acid  molecules  joined  to  glycerol.  Triglyceride  molecules
cumulate  in  specialized  cells  within  the  adipose  tissue  mass.  There  are  two  forms  of
adipose tissue in mammals; white adipose tissue and brown adipose tissue. In humans,
most fat is stored in white adipose tissue. Brown adipose tissue is a special tissue that can
increase its own metabolism and may play an important role in body weight regulation.

Functions of Adipose Tissue
White  adipose  tissue  serves  three  important  functions:  (1)  insulation,  (2)  mechanical
cushioning of internal organs, and (3) energy supply. Consider the subcutaneous adipose
tissue mass found directly below the skin surface. This layer of insulation, which varies
in thickness on the trunk and extremities, conducts heat more poorly than other tissues.

Fat‚Äôs major function is energy storage; it provides a ready fuel source during an energy
imbalance‚Äîless food consumed than energy expended. The person‚Äôs energy imbalance
creates an energy deficit. The energy required to keep the body functioning must come
from sources other than food. Indeed it does; the body utilizes its own tissues to provide
the required energy. When one consumes fat in excess of the body‚Äôs requirements, the
excess accumulates in the fat depots and the person literally becomes ‚Äúfatter‚Äù from the
stored excess.

Fat  provides  an  efficient  way  to  store  excess  energy  because  of  its  relative  absence  of
water. This means that more energy ‚Äústores‚Äù within one  gram  of fat (9 kcal) than one
gram of either carbohydrate (4 kcal) or protein (4 kcal). If animals stored their excess
energy as protein or carbohydrate, the animals‚Äô increased mass would certainly interfere
with  activities  of  daily  living  because  the  animal  would  simply  increase  its  mass  and
hence its volume is out of proportion to its physiologic capacity.

STorage FaT
Fat  is  a  nutritional  reserve.  Though  most  noticeable  as  subcutaneous  fat  found  below
the  skin‚Äôs  surface,  fat  also  can  be  found  surrounding  various  internal  organs,  serving
to protect those organs from trauma. Although the proportional distribution of storage
fat  in  males  and  females  is  similar  (12  percent  in  males,  15  percent  in  females),  the
total  quantity  of  essential  fat  in  females‚Äîwhich  includes  the  sex-specific  fat‚Äîis  four
times higher than in males. More than likely, the additional essential fat is biologically
important for child bearing and other hormone-related functions.

International Sports Sciences Association96  |  unit 7

hydrostatic weighing: An
accurate method for estimating
body composition. Determines
body density.

air displacement
plethysmography: A method
of measuring body density and
estimating body composition by
air displacement. The BodPod
(image below) is an example
of an instrument that uses this
technique.

bioimpedence: A body
composition measurement
technique that measures
the resistance of the body in
response to a small electric
charge. High levels of body fat
increase electrical resistance.

skinfold technique: Estimates
body fatness by measuring
skinfold thickness at key sites on
the body.

subcutaneous fat: Fat stores
lying directly beneath the skin.

COMMON TEChNIQuES TO ASSESS
bODY COMPOSITION
Two  general  categories  of  procedures  assess  body  composition:
(1) direct methods, which involve chemical analysis of the animal
carcass or human cadaver, and (2) indirect methods, which include
hydrostatic  weighing (densitometry, more commonly called
underwater  weighing),  air  displacement  plethysmography
(BodPod; also measures body density), and surface anthropometry
(skinfolds and girths). Other popular indirect techniques include
bioimpedence,  near-infrared  interactance  (NIR),  computed
tomography (CT), magnetic resonance imaging (MRI), and dual-
photon  absorptiometry  (DEXA).  The  indirect  methods  predict
the results of the direct methods, which cannot be used on living
human beings.

MRI of abdominal cavity.

SKInFold meaSuremenTS
Most personal trainers do not have access to laboratory facilities
to  measure  body  composition  by  more  sophisticated  methods.
The  subcutaneous  skinfold  technique  requires  relatively
inexpensive equipment and gives a good idea about whether the
young person has excess body fat and where it is located (on the
trunk or extremities).

DEXA scan of the femur
to measure bone density.
Technique is also used
to estimate body fat and
fat-free weight.

Source: courtesy hologic, Inc.

How to Measure
Subcutaneous Skinfold Fat
The rationale for taking skinfolds relies on the fact that about one-half to one-third of
the body‚Äôs total fat stores lie directly beneath the skin (subcutaneous fat), and this fat
volume relates closely to the body‚Äôs total fat content.

To  measure  skinfold  thickness,  the  thumb  and  forefinger  grasp  a  fold  of  skin  and
subcutaneous fat, pulling it away from the natural contour of the skinfold. The caliper‚Äôs
pincer arms at their point of skin contact exert constant tension. The thickness of the
skin‚Äôs  double  layer  and  subcutaneous  tissues  is  read  directly  from  the  caliper  dial  and
recorded in millimeters.

Youth FitnessObesity in Children & Measuring body Composition |  97

The  most  common  areas  for  taking  skinfolds  include  the
triceps,  chest,  mid-axillary,  subscapula,  and  suprailiac,
abdominal,  and  upper  thigh  sites.  Take  skinfolds  on  the
right side of the body with the subject standing. Two or three
measurements should be made at each site and their average
used as the skinfold score. In the hands of an experienced

tester (hundreds of subjects measured over several weeks at
all of the anatomic sites), reliability of measurement is high.
This  means  the  resulting  skinfold  score  represents  a  good
estimate of a ‚Äútrue‚Äù score and the person can feel confident
the score represents the thickness of fat at that anatomic site.

Table 7.2

Anatomical Location of Eight Frequently Measured Skinfold Sites

Triceps ‚Äì vertical fold measured at
the midline of the upper arm halfway
between the tip of the shoulder and
the tip of the elbow.

Suprailiac ‚Äì slightly oblique fold
measured just above the hipbone.
The fold is lifted to follow the natural
diagonal line at this point.

Thigh ‚Äì vertical fold measured at the
midline of the thigh, two-thirds if the
distance from the knee cap to the
hip on the anterior surface.

Chest ‚Äì diagonal fold between the
anterior axillary fold and the nipple,
taken one inch from the anterior
axillary fold.

Subscapula ‚Äì measure the fold
located along an oblique line just
below the bottom tip of
the scapula

Abdomen ‚Äì measure the fold verti-
cally so that the caliper tips fall one
inch the right of the umbilicus.

Mid-axillary ‚Äì measure the fold in
a horizontal line at the midaxillary
line level with the bottom of the
sternum.

Mid-calf ‚Äì vertical fold measured at
medial aspect of the maximal girth
with the knee and hip flexed to 90Àö
(foot on low stool).

Usefulness of
Skinfold Measurements
The  best  way  to  use  skinfolds  is  to  sum  the  trunk  and
extremity  scores  to  indicate  the  relative  degree  of  fatness
among  individuals.  The  sum  of  skinfolds  also  can  reflect
changes  in  fatness,  as  for  example,  ‚Äúbefore‚Äù  and  ‚Äúafter‚Äù
a  physical  training  regimen  with  or  without  dietary
intervention.  Changes  in  individual  skinfolds  including
the  total  score  can  than  be  evaluated  on  an  absolute  or
percentage basis.

Many  researchers  and  personal  trainers  use  skinfolds  to
predict percent fat and fat-free weight from more than one
hundred  equations  published  in  the  literature.  While  this
sometimes  works  well  with  specific  discrete  populations,
it  is  inappropriate  in  children.  It  is  almost  impossible  to
find  a  valid  body  composition  equation  that  takes  into
consideration age, gender, maturational status, and sport.

Practical Approach to
Skinfold Measurement
ISSA recommends that health-care professionals NOT use
skinfolds to predict body fat, but instead, do the following.
First,  take  skinfolds  at  eight  anatomic  sites‚Äîtriceps,
subscapula,  chest,  mid-axillary, suprailiac, abdomen, mid-
calf,  and  thigh.  Note  that  the  triceps,  mid-calf,  and  thigh
sites represent the extremities, with the remainder being the
trunk region.

Sum  the  skinfolds  at  each  region  to  determine  total  fat
thickness. As young athletes implement changes with either
training or dietary regimens, or both, you will have a nice
comparison for the ‚Äúbefore‚Äù and ‚Äúafter‚Äù conditions. Tracking
body composition changes gives the client ‚Äúreal-world‚Äù data
to  chart  their  progress.  Providing  objective  feedback  on
body  composition  changes  is  a  sure-fire  way  to  improve
client compliance with training programs.

International Sports Sciences Association98  |  unit 7

SuMMARY

Widespread obesity in children is a recent health calamity‚Äîmore than 18 percent
of our children are now overweight, and the trend is accelerating at an alarming
rate, particularly among Mexican-American boys and non-Hispanic black girls.
Being too fat in childhood has immediate and long-term health effects. Many
develop symptoms of Type 2 diabetes‚Äîparticularly insulin resistance‚Äîand risk
factors for cardiovascular disease. These include high blood pressure, high blood
cholesterol and triglycerides, low blood HDL, and blood clotting abnormalities.

The majority of overweight kids become overweight from overeating and not
getting enough exercise. Recent studies of elementary and high school students
show that children become progressively less active as they age, due in part to
increased time devoted to television and video games.

Children need enough food to grow properly. Promote growth and development
and prevent obesity by teaching children to eat grains, vegetables, and fruits, as
well as low-fat dairy and other protein-rich foods, and to exercise and play sports.
Advise parents to limit the time kids spend watching television and playing video
games, and encourage participation in sports and games. Parents must NOT
restrict fat intake for children younger than age two. Older children should learn
about heart healthy diets.

Use the sum of skinfolds at the extremity and trunk regions‚Äîtriceps, subscapula,
chest, mid-axillary, iliac, abdomen, mid-calf, and thigh‚Äîto assess total fat thick-
ness in those regions. As young athletes alter their body composition with either
training or diet, or both, you will have a nice comparison among the skinfold sites
in the ‚Äúbefore‚Äù and ‚Äúafter‚Äù conditions.

Body mass index (BMI = weight√∑ height2) is a screening tool to evaluate the
‚Äúnormalcy‚Äù of an individual‚Äôs body weight. The Centers for Disease Control and
Prevention (CDC) recommends BMI-for-age as an appropriate screening tool for
identifying overweight children and their future risk of obesity or overweight.
