uNIT 8

THE TRAINING RESPONSE:
# Principles Of Training & Adaptation

102  |  unit 8

Key Terms

general adaptation syndrome (GAS)

reversibility

alarm reaction

resistance development stage

stage of exhaustion

specificity of training

individual differences

progression

load

repetition

rest

frequency

overtraining

periodization

Designing  an  exercise  program  for  anyone‚Äîparticularly  children‚Äîrequires  a  basic
knowledge of how exercise affects the body. As discussed previously, children and adults
respond similarly to training with a few exceptions that will be covered in Section 2 of
this training manual.

The basic principles of training are similar for beginning exercisers and more experienced
people.  Both  subject  themselves  to  an  exercise  stress,  and  their  bodies  respond  by
increasing physical fitness. For example, the beginner‚Äôs program might include a brisk
walk or bike ride, while a serious athlete might bench press 300 lbs or run 60 seconds
for 400 meters. Stress and adaptation are the same in anyone who exercises, whether the
individual is a world-class athlete or a junior high school student trying to improve overall
fitness.  Fitness  increases  by  giving  the  body  an  unaccustomed  exercise  stress,  such  as
walking instead of sitting, lifting a heavier weight, running faster or farther, or stretching
a muscle more than usual. The body adapts to the stress by improving its function.

In the late 1940s, Dr. Hans Selye (1907 to 1982), Austrian physician and endocrinologist
and professor and director of the Institute of Experimental Medicine and Surgery at the
University of Montreal for 50 years, formulated the theory of stress adaptation, a theory
that  has  had  a  profound  effect  on  medicine.  His  theory  has  tremendous  implications
for  how  individuals  condition  themselves  for  physical  activity  and  sports.  He  called
the process of stress adaptation the ‚Äúgeneral adaptation syndrome‚Äù or GAS. Selye
studied the causes and consequences of stress and described three processes involved in
response to a stressor (a stressor can be exercise, cold, bacteria, or anything that upsets
the body‚Äôs internal balance mechanisms):

1.  alarm reaction

2.

resistance development

3.  exhaustion

The  Alarm  Reaction  refers  to  the  initial  response  to  the  stressor.  It  is  a  process  of
mobilization  for  the  organism.  During  exercise,  for  example,  reaction  to  the  stress  is
manifested  in  harder  breathing,  sweating,  and  an  increasing  heart  rate.  The  alarm
reaction disturbs the body‚Äôs physiologic balance.

During the Resistance Development Stage, the body attempts to adjust to the effects
of the stressor. As the body improves its function, the stressor becomes less disruptive

general adaptation syn-
drome (gAS): Theory de-
veloped by Dr. Hans Selye to
describe the way the body reacts
and adapts (or fails to adapt) to
physical and emotional stresses.

alarm reaction: First stage of
the general adaptation syn-
drome (GAS) involving the initial
response to a stressor; responsi-
ble for mobilizing an organism.

resistance development
stage: Second stage of GAS,
during which the body actively
attempts to adjust to the effects
of a stressor.

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  103

stage of exhaustion: Third
stage of GAS, during which the
organism can no longer resist
stress.

specificity of training: The
body‚Äôs unique reaction to a
stressor.

individual differences:
People respond differently to
the same stressor largely due to
genetic factors.

progression: People adapt
best to training when there are
increasing levels of difficulty in a
training program.

reversibility: Loss of fitness
gains with removal of the stress
of exercise.

load: Resistance or weight used
during the exercise.

repetition: Number of times
the athlete repeats the exercise.

rest: Amount of time spent not
exercising between sets or work-
outs. Rest is essential for adapta-
tion and should be incorporated
into every training program.

frequency: Number of training
sessions in a given period.

to  overall  physiology.  For  example,  when  lifting  weights,  muscles  increase  in  size
(hypertrophy)  and  strength,  making  the  load  less  stressful.  Resistance  development  is
the goal of physical conditioning. You only adapt if the stress load disrupts the body‚Äôs
physiologic  balance.  During  exercise,  if  the  stress  falls  below  a  critical  intensity,  then
fitness  cannot  improve.  In  contrast,  injury  is  likely  if  your  body  cannot  tolerate  the
training load.

The  amount  and  intensity  of  exercise  necessary  to  improve  fitness  depend  on  the
current level of fitness, age, health, mental outlook, and a number of unknown factors.
For example, running a ten-minute mile may be exhausting to a sedentary, overweight
13-year-old but would cause essentially no adaptive response in a world-class distance
runner. Likewise, a training run tolerated easily one day may be completely inappropriate
on  a  day  following  a  prolonged  illness.  Environment  can  also  disturb  performance.
Extreme heat or cold, high altitude, or polluted air can decrease fitness.

If the stress is too great, then the third stage of GAS, known as the stage of exhaustion,
prevails. This stage is characterized by excessive stress that causes injury. The stress can
be acute (applied all at once) or chronic (occurring over a longer time period). Results
of acute exhaustion include fractures, sprains, and strains. Chronic exhaustion is subtler
and manifests itself in overtraining, stress fractures, and emotional stress.

PRINCIPLES OF TRAININg
A number of factors influence how the body adapts to the stress of exercise. These include
the degree of overload (how hard you push yourself), specificity of training (training
the way you want your body to adapt), individual differences (genetics), progression
(taking it one step at a time), and reversibility (the ‚Äúuse it or lose it‚Äù idea).

The overload PrIncIPle

The basis of the General Adaptation Syndrome is that stressing the body to a tolerable
limit  causes  adaptation  and  improved  function.  This  positive  stressor  is  an  overload,
quantified according to load, repetition, rest, and frequency. Load refers to exercise
intensity. Repetition refers to the number of times a load is administered. Rest refers to
the time interval between repetitions. And frequency is the number of training sessions
per week. For example, in weight training,
young  athletes  might  do  three  sets  of
eight  different  exercises  with  a  weight
they can barely lift for 10 repetitions (load
and  repetition),  with  a  1-minute  pause
between each set (rest). They might repeat
this workout at a frequency of three times
a  week.  The  application  of  the  principle
of overload in an exercise program is not
an  exact  science.  The  child  should  start
conservatively and build up. The overload
is excessive if the athlete becomes injured,
has  trouble  recovering,  or  is  exceedingly
sore. The personal trainer or coach must
be  part  scientist  and  part  artist  when
designing a program that promotes fitness
yet prevents injury and soreness.

International Sports Sciences Association104  |  unit 8

SPecIFIcITY oF TraInIng
The  body  adapts  specifically  to  the  stress  of  exercise.  For
example,  the  adaptation  to  endurance  distance  running
or  swimming  differs  from  the  adaptation  to  strength
exercises‚Äîsuch as weight lifting‚Äîand power exercises, such
as sprinting. Any training program should reflect the desired
adaptation. Train the way you want the body to change.

Doing the wrong type of training can impair certain fitness
gains. For example, training for strength and endurance at
the  same  time  interferes  with  strength  development.  This
is not a problem for a person who exercises for improved
health and appearance. However, it becomes an important
consideration  for  a  person  trying  to  develop  maximum
strength and power.

Sports skills are extremely specific. Scientists have discovered
that  movement  skills  become  ingrained  in  the  nervous
system and are ‚Äúplayed back‚Äù by reflex. The aim of practice
and  coaching  is  to  ingrain  the  correct  motion;  practicing
incorrect  motions  develops  the  wrong  patterns  in  the
nervous system and produces incorrect movement patterns.
The more a person practices a movement (up to a certain
point on a learning curve), the more it reinforces the skill in
the nervous system. Therefore, it is imperative that athletes
practice  only  correct  movements  (i.e.,  good  technique).  A
wise  coach  once  stated,  ‚ÄúPerfect  practice  makes  perfect.‚Äù
Spending hours practicing incorrectly improves the skill but
in the incorrect way. For example, it is far better to practice
a few golf swings with perfect form and concentration than
to  take  50  swings  in  rapid  succession  when  each  swing  is
done with less than perfect attention to detail. The same can

be said for executing a particular lift such as the bench press
or squat. It is far better to do fewer reps with ‚Äúperfect‚Äù form
than to do 10 reps one after the other without attention to
correct form and with poor concentration.

IndIvIdual dIFFerenceS
Anyone  watching  the  Olympics,  a  professional  football  or
basketball  game,  a  golf  tournament,  or  a  tennis  match  on
TV  can  readily  see  that,  from  a  physical  standpoint,  not
all  athletes  are  created  equal.  There  are  large  individual
differences in the ability to perform, learn sports skills, and
adapt to training. Genetics limits your capacity to develop
fitness  and  skill.  We  can  all  aspire  to  play  basketball  like
LeBron James, hit home runs like Albert Pujols, or swing a
golf club like Tiger Woods. But few of us will ever achieve
that  level  of  performance,  no  matter  how  much  time  we
devote to mastering the skills involved. Even though most
people may not rise to the level of the genetically gifted few,
improvement  is  possible  for  anyone  who  remains  focused
on a systematic program.

Genetics  also  plays  a  part  in  general  health  and  wellness.
Some  people  are  genetically  healthier  than  others.  Their
immune  systems  naturally  fight  disease  better  than  those
of other people, making them less susceptible to coronary
heart disease, diabetes, and obesity. Science tells us though,
that  while  some  individuals  may  be  fortunate  enough  to
have a family history of longevity and freedom from many
diseases,  lifestyle  is  far  more  important  than  genetics  in
determining optimal health and well being.

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  105

Discovering  athletic  talent  in  children  is  difficult  but  not
impossible. Research studies show that the very best athletes,
those  who  rank  in  the  top  1  percent  during  their  school
years, are also the top athletes in college and beyond. Tiger
Woods, for example, was an exceptional golfer throughout
childhood,  high  school,  and  college,  and  has  continued
to  perform  with  great  skill  during  his  professional  career.
However,  in  the  majority  of  cases,  it  is  difficult  to  predict
which  athletes  will  excel  in  college  or  professional  sports
based  on  their  performance  during  childhood.  Superstar
athletes  such  as  Michael  Jordan  and  Babe  Ruth  were  not
standouts as young children. Jordon was actually cut from
the sophomore basketball team.

While good genes are important for elite level performance,
hard work and concentrated effort are even more important
for  success.  Malcolm  Gladwell,  in  his  book  Outliers:
The  Story  of  Success  (Little,  Brown,  and  Company,  2008)
presents the idea that success in almost anything requires at
least 10,000 hours of dedicated practice. Great athletes don‚Äôt
require  exceptional  genetics‚Äîonly  better-than-normal
natural  talent.  Beyond  that,  consistent  practice  and  good
coaching are most important.

ProgreSSIon
Adaptation  to  the  stress  of  training  occurs  most  readily
when one gradually applies the exercise stress. High levels
of  fitness  require  many  years  of  training  and  involve
progression  in  small  stages.  Superior  fitness  and  optimal
physical  performance  represent  a  combination  of  many
small  gains.  One  cannot  become  fit  overnight‚Äîonly
gradually, one step at a time.

reverSIbIlITY
This principle basically means, ‚ÄúUse it, or lose it.‚Äù It is the
reverse  of  the  overload  principle.  The  body  adapts  to  the
stresses  placed  upon  it.  Exercising  more  intensely  than
usual improves fitness; doing less reduces fitness. A graphic
example of reversibility occurs when you wear a cast for a
broken bone. When the doctor removes the cast, your arm
or leg is smaller than before. This occurs because the muscles
atrophy or become smaller from disuse or inactivity.

DEvELOP ThE bODY
TO SuIT ThE LIFESTYLE
The  human  body  is  extremely  adaptable.  Coaches  and
trainers  must  determine  what  the  child  wants  from  an
exercise  program  and  then  mold  his  or  her  body  in  that
direction. If the goal is health promotion and the child does
not want to begin a formal exercise program, structure the
child‚Äôs life so that he or she becomes more physically active
during the day. For more lofty fitness goals, determine the
elements necessary to achieve those goals and find a way to
incorporate them into the child‚Äôs routine.

Many  young  people  want  to  improve  performance  in
recreational sports. If the chosen sports require exceptional
endurance,  concentrate  on  the  oxidative  energy  system
and endurance exercise. If the goal is to excel in tennis or
volleyball, training must focus not only on developing skill
but  on  increasing  muscle  power  and  general  endurance,
as  well.  If  the  desired  outcome  is  improved  appearance,  it
is  crucial  to  develop  a  healthy  nutrition  plan  and  exercise

International Sports Sciences Association106  |  unit 8

strategy  to  foster  fat  loss  and  allow  for  the  maintenance  or  gain  of  lean  body  mass.
Exercise will be fun and rewarding if it tracks with the individual‚Äôs goals.

Skeletal muscle is highly adaptable. If it is overloaded‚Äîi.e., loaded more than usual‚Äîit
becomes stronger. Likewise, if deconditioning occurs because the muscle is habitually
contracted less forcefully than normal, the muscle becomes weaker. You build strength
by increasing muscle size, improving the way the nervous system transmits information
to the muscles, and coordinating muscle elasticity with muscle contraction.

Athletes  benefit  the  most  from  their  program  if  they  follow  the  Seventeen  Principles
of  Training.  These  principles  are  a  guide  to  gradual,  long-lasting,  injury-free  fitness
development that leads to improved performance with the smallest risk of injury. They
also serve as a guide to gradual and long-lasting fitness development. (Table 8.1)

The SevenTeen PrIncIPleS oF TraInIng

Table 8.1

The Seventeen Principles of Training

1.  Train the way you want your body to change.

2.  Eat well-balanced, high performance meals.

3.  Establish realistic goals.

4.  Have a workout plan.

5.  Train all year round.

6.  Get in shape gradually.

7.  Don‚Äôt train when you are ill or seriously injured.

8.  Train first for volume (more repetitions) and only later for intensity

(more weight or resistance).

9.  Listen to your body. Know when to rest and slow down.

10.  Vary volume and intensity of workouts.

11.  Work on weaknesses, not just strengths.

12.  Train systematically.

13.  Warm up and cool down.

14.  Train the mind. Learn to focus and delete extraneous ‚Äúinformation‚Äù as you train.

15.  Listen to ‚ÄúCoach Pain.‚Äù Do not be a hero by trying to continue workouts when you know you should not.

16.  Become informed about the physiology of the body. Learn all you can about

exercise and the effects of training.

17.  Have Fun! Keep the exercise program in its proper perspective.

a cloSer looK aT The SevenTeen PrIncIPleS oF TraInIng

1. Train the Way You Want
the Body to Change
This  means  stress  the  body  so  that  it  changes  the  way
you  want  it  to  change.  If  the  child  is  primarily  concerned
with  general  fitness,  choose  a  well-rounded  program  that
concentrates  on  the  major  muscle  groups.  In  addition  to
the  weight  training  routine,  the  program  should  include
endurance and flexibility exercises.

This  is  the  most  important  training  principle.  The  body
adapts  to  stress  in  a  very  specific  way.  For  example,
swimming  will  not  improve  endurance  for  running  or
cycling.  Likewise,  lifting  weights  will  not  ‚Äútransfer‚Äù  and
make  athletes  better  football  players,  discus  throwers,
skiers, or swimmers. The best way to improve performance
in a sport is to practice that particular sport.

Doing  support  exercises  such  as  weight  training,  power
exercises,  and  endurance  training,  eventually  improves
performance in a particular sport. However, improvements
occur  most  readily  when  the  training  combines  specific
skill  practice  with  exercises  for  improved  strength,  power,
flexibility, and endurance.

it  or  not,

Believe
improved  fitness  can  sometimes
temporarily  decrease  performance.  The  nervous  system
programs  sports  skills  very  precisely.  Improving  strength,
power, flexibility, or endurance in a short  time introduces
variability  into  motor  performance;  it  takes  time  for  the
brain to adjust to new capabilities. In the long run, the new
fitness  will  improve  performance.  However,  this  will  only
occur  if  the  nervous  system  can  accommodate  the  new
fitness  into  the  skill.  The  brain  and  nervous  system  must
adjust to the improved fitness.

Rapid  growth  during  adolescence  can  have  similar  effects
on motor performance. Children sometimes grow six inches
or  more  during  the  pubertal  growth  spurt.  This  causes
problems for the precise nervous control systems that direct
movement‚Äîthey become programmed for a specific weight,
height,  and  strength.  Rapid  growth  confuses  the  system.
A child who was highly skilled in a sport two months ago
can suddenly look like an unskilled novice. After a period
of  adjustment,  the  neural  control  system  ‚Äúcatches  up‚Äù  to
the growth and continues to improve motor performance.
Improvement following the growth spurt is not inevitable,
however. In girls, motor performance following the spurt is
sometimes lower than before the growth spurt began.

The  principle  of  specificity  should  be
the  central
consideration in any training program designed to improve

skilled performance. Skill practice in activities such as tennis,
skiing,  golf,  discus  throwing,  football,  volleyball,  soccer,
softball,  and  other  individual  and  group  activities  should
remain  a  central  focus  of  the  program.  Support  activities
designed to improve strength, power, and endurance should
be secondary to skill development.

Physical support activities (e.g., strength and power training)
have  to  be  consistent  and  long-term.  Lifting  weights  for
two  months  before  football  or  track  season  is  practically
worthless. The increased strength does not transfer rapidly
to motor skills. In contrast, a support program that develops
high levels of fitness over a long time can be more effectively
integrated  into  the  sport.  This  will  inject  greater  strength,
power, endurance, and flexibility into the movement skills,
and  performance  improves.  Another  critically  important
factor  about  specificity  of  training  is  that  weight  training,
plyometrics, and speed training transfer to motor skills in
skilled athletes more so than in novices. Consequently, the
personal  trainer  should  work  on  basic  skill  development
in  beginners  before  emphasizing  strength  and  power.  As
athletes  increase  their  individual  skills,  they  should  add
more conditioning exercises to improve sports power.

In  summary,  the  message  behind  this  critical  principle  of
specificity is to train the way you want your body to adapt.
Support activities designed to improve power during sports
movements  create  change  very  slowly.  The  athlete  must
develop  strength,  power,  endurance,  and  flexibility  slowly
and  consistently  in  order  to  integrate  newly  developed
fitness
into  sports  skills.  Improving  fitness  without
practicing the skill will have little or no transfer effects in
terms of a particular sports performance.

Athletes  should  work  on  their  weaknesses,  even  if  the
exercises  necessary  to  correct  them  are  unpleasant.  For
example, in skiers, strong, flexible lower body muscles are
more  important  than  strong  arms  and  shoulders.  Analyze
the  athlete‚Äôs  program.  A  well-designed  program  will  be
more  effective  and  less  time-consuming  than  a  casual  or
unsystematic one.

International Sports Sciences Association108  |  unit 8

2. Eat Well-Balanced High
Performance Meals
During  the  past  20  years,  sports  scientists  have  shown
that  the  right  diet  can  improve  performance  and  keep  off
unwanted pounds of fat. All the training in the world will
not  produce  superior  fitness  if  the  athlete  consumes  an
unhealthy  diet.  Children  should  eat  a  sensible,  nutritious
diet  that  contains  a  balance  of  the  basic  food  groups. The
diet  should  supply  enough  calories  to  meet  energy  needs
but still allow the athlete to control their weight. If children
must  lose  weight,  they  should  do  so  gradually‚Äîlosing  no
more  than  about  two  pounds  per  week.  Along  with  hard
training,  they  should  consume  a  greater  proportion  of
complex carbohydrates and fewer fats.

3. Establish Realistic Goals
Improved  fitness  occurs  when  you  stress  the  body  and  it
adapts. Because of the specificity of training, the nature of
the exercise stress (i.e., training program) must match the
goals.  Goals  should  reflect  the  individual‚Äôs  capability  and
motivation. A young girl should not set a goal of being an
Olympic  discus  thrower  if  she  is  short  and  gains  strength
slowly. If a young athlete is willing to put in the necessary
mileage to run a 10k race, she should go ahead and do it.
Otherwise, she should set more modest goals. Goal setting
is discussed in great detail in the next phase of the course
dealing with sports psychology and the young athlete.

It  is  important  to  set  achievable,  short-term  goals.  A
beginning tennis player might set a goal of keeping a rally
going for ten strokes. A beginning golfer might set a short-
term goal of putting the ball in two strokes on 9 of the 18
holes. A beginning runner might set a goal of completing
a  6-mile  fun  run.  A  more  accomplished  athlete  may  set
more difficult goals ranging from running a 6-minute mile
to making the Olympic team. The principle is the same for
everyone‚Äîset  achievable  goals.  When  you  attain  them,
reevaluate the program and establish new goals. One new
goal might be to maintain the present level or it could be to
achieve a higher level of performance.

The personal trainer is a critical link for goal setting in young
athletes. You can help them set goals they can achieve. Set
small training goals such as improving 1-inch in the vertical
jump or bench-pressing 10 more pounds. If athletes get in
the  habit  of  working  for  and  achieving  short-term  goals,
they will soon be doing things they never thought possible.
More importantly, they will begin to internalize goal-setting
behavior,  a  critical  component  for  long-term  success  in
sports and in life.

4. Have a Workout Plan
Teach young athletes to write down their goals and a plan for
achieving them. An appropriate plan for an overweight child
would not be to look good in a bathing suit next summer.
The child should write down a program for achieving this
outcome.  For  example,  if  a  young  boy  wants  to  lose  20
pounds of body fat and increase muscle
mass, a realistic program for achieving
this  goal  might  be  to  lose  1  pound  a
week and strive to go to the gym 3 times
a  week.  He  might  achieve  his  goal  by
cutting down on desserts or eliminating
high sugar  drinks (and fats in general)
from  lunch  meals  and  snacks  and,
more importantly, by devoting 30 to 60
minutes or more daily to aerobic exercise
and resistance training. Analysis of the
training programs of successful athletes
shows  that  people  must  devote  at  least
10,000  hours  to  mastering  a  sport.
Think  about  that  fact‚Äî10,000  hours
equals 1 year, 1 month, and 21 days of
practice time!

Contrast  this  method  with  a  more
casual  approach.  It  is  January,  and  a
young female is tired of looking bad in
her  swimsuit  during  the  summer.  She
wants  to  lose  20  pounds  of  fat  but  has
no  plan.  She  exercises  and  diets  from

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  109

time-to-time  but  makes  no  progress  because  she
is  inconsistent  in  her  behavior.  Soon,  May  comes
around  and  she  looks  the  same  as  she  did  in  the
wintertime. In desperation, she undertakes a crash
weight loss program and exercise regimen. Despite
great  sacrifice  and  suffering,  she  fails  to  meet  her
goal  and  ends  up  wearing  a  baggy  T-shirt  to  the
beach  (or  simply  chooses  not  to  go  to  the  beach
with her friends). Sadly, this happens all too often.
Without  clear-cut  goal  setting,  the  chances  are
nearly nil of achieving well-intentioned goals.

In  setting  up  a  good  workout  plan,  analyze  the
elements  necessary  for  achieving  the  client‚Äôs  goal.
For example, if the client wants to be a better skier,
he  or  she  must  practice  skiing  and  develop  good
endurance, strength, power, and flexibility. During
the  off-season,  the  client  should  systematically
improve physical fitness. Once the ski season comes
around, shift emphasis to skill development without
compromising fitness.

The  football  player  should  follow  a  similar
procedure. After football season, begin a program
designed  to  increase  muscle  mass  and  improve
power output capacity. Encourage weight training
designed to stress the body‚Äôs major muscles. Have the client
do  flexibility  and  endurance  exercises  to  improve  overall
fitness. In the spring, integrate more power training into the
program such as plyometrics and speed exercises. During
the  summer,  shift  the  training  emphasis  to  maintaining
strength and maximizing power, speed, and skill.

Contrast this systematic approach with the more common
crash  weight  training  program  started  two  months  before
the football season. The athlete might gain in strength, but
little improvement occurs in power, speed, or football skills.
The  athlete  has  wasted  the  two  critical  months  before  the
season doing exercises that should have been done during
the winter.

It is important to keep a training diary. Top athletes create
a  comprehensive,  written,  week-by-week  plan  that  clearly
defines  what  they  will  do  on  any  given  day  of  the  week.
Top athletes also keep check lists and stay focused on their
workout  regimen.  Once  it  is  established,  following  a  path
toward  pre-established  goals  truly  becomes  ‚Äúeasy.‚Äù  This  is
the best way of insuring a systematic approach.

Athletes  should  write  down  their  programs  for  the  next
four to six weeks and follow their progress in their training
diary. The diary does not have to be anything fancy; tell your
client  to  go  to  the  stationary  store  and  buy  a  small  spiral
notebook. Then, tell him to put it in his gym bag and carry
it with him to every workout. He should record such things
as body weight, how he feels each day, exercises (set, reps,

load),  morning  heart  rate,  and  performances  (scores  of
tennis matches, times of intervals, vertical jump distances,
and so on). He should write down tentative workouts in the
training diary for the next six weeks. He also should write
down a rough workout plan for the next year. Writing down
the plan in the training diary will help any athlete get where
he or she wants to be in the next month and the next year.

The training diary is an important part of being consistent
in workouts. Training diaries (kept either in a notebook or
in a record keeping computer program in a word processor
or spreadsheet or on a smartphone) are essential to anyone
committed  to  fitness‚Äîwhether  the  person  is  a  serious
athlete or simply an individual who wants to get into good
shape. Have your clients start their training diaries today!

5. Train all year round
Athletes  who  take  too  much  time  off  from  their  exercise
program lose the gains they have made. They also become
more susceptible to injury if they try to get back in shape
rapidly.  Establish  a  year-round  program  for  your  clients;
have  specific  goals  and  procedures  for  each  period  of  the
year‚Äîand see to it that the athlete sticks to them. Younger
children should do a variety of activities to develop a love
for sport and a well-rounded fitness program.

Make sure your charges have alternative training plans for
bad weather and for times when they do not have access to
a weight room or playing field. For example, if they are on

International Sports Sciences Association110  |  unit 8

a trip, they can substitute calisthenics exercises‚Äîpush-ups
and knee bends‚Äîfor the regular routine.

exercise are introduced gradually, individuals will eventually
become fit and the risk of injury will be reduced.

Most  young  athletes  do  not  train  all  year  long.  Rather,
they train for each sport as it comes. This is not a problem
for  younger  kids.  However,  high  school  athletes  should
develop a training discipline that allows them to maintain
their fitness during the off-season. Great athletes are made
during  the  off-season.  An  athlete  cannot  develop  superior
performance  during  a  season  with  a  duration  of  only  two
to three months.

Athletes  who  miss  workouts  do  not  improve  their  fitness,
and they can even lose the gains they have achieved. Teach
athletes to plan regular times for their exercise programs and
stick to them. Encourage them not to let outside influences
interfere with workouts. All of us have many responsibilities
that  compete  for  our  time.  If  fitness  and  performance  are
important, workouts must be a priority. Teach clients that
workouts are their time and that they should not let others
take this important time away from them.

6. Get in Shape Gradually
Achieving fitness requires a gradual process of adaptation.
Athletes  who  try  to  increase  fitness  too  quickly  become
injured or overtrained. Do not push the process. High levels
of fitness represent many small adaptations. If the stresses of

Many sports programs‚Äîparticularly in high school and even
junior high school‚Äîemploy crash exercise programs known
by  names  such  as  ‚Äúhell-week‚Äù  or  ‚Äúdouble  days.‚Äù  Research
studies are unanimous in showing that such programs are
not only ineffective, but they cause unnecessary fatigue and
often lead to injury. One cannot ‚Äúforce‚Äù the body to become
fit overnight, no matter how hard one works. Attempts to do
so almost always backfire and lead to injury and depressed
immune function. Overtraining makes it more difficult for
the body to fight disease. Instead of achieving a high fitness
level, athletes end up trying to compete with a cold or flu
and  muscles  so  sore  they  have  trouble  moving  effectively.
Contrast  this  with  a  year-long  program  that  gradually
develops  fitness.  Fit  people  are  capable  of  high  levels  of
injury-free performance because they have approached the
conditioning process gradually.

Unfortunately, most young athletes do not train consistently
year  round,  which  presents  coaches  and  trainers  with  a
dilemma: how can they prepare their team for competition
in a short time? The answer‚Äîunfortunately‚Äîis two-a-day
practices. As a personal trainer, insist that your athletes are
fit  for  competition.  Remember,  successful  athletes  are  built
during the off-season.

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  111

Training represents a stress the body must overcome, so the
body requires time to adapt to the stress of exercise. Muscles
are  more  susceptible  to  injury  during  the  early  phases  of
conditioning. Overzealous training, or intense conditioning
when the athlete is ill‚Äìprepared for it, will most likely lead to
injury and delay progress toward the person‚Äôs goals.

Staying in ‚Äúgood shape‚Äù all year long is easier than trying to
achieve fitness in a few months. It is easier to apply a little
pressure instead of trying to undertake a crash-conditioning
program within a relatively brief period.

7. Do Not Train When Ill or
Seriously Injured
The body has problems trying to fight more than one stressor
at  a  time.  Training  when  sick  or  injured  may  seriously
hinder  progress  or  even  be  dangerous.  It  is  particularly
important not to train with a fever (above 100¬∞F). After an
injury,  athletes  can  return  to  intense  workouts  if  they  can
answer, ‚Äúyes‚Äù to the following four questions.

1.  Can you move the injured area normally

(joint or muscle)?

2.  Do you have normal strength and power?

3.  Have normal movement patterns been restored

(more than 90 percent recovered)?

4.  Are you relatively pain free?

If  the  answer  to  any  of  these  questions  is  ‚Äúno,‚Äù  then  the
athlete  should  let  the  injury  heal  through  a  combination
of rehabilitation and rest, or modify the training program
before resuming the normal workout. Many people end up
abandoning their favorite sports because they have injuries
that were not allowed to heal properly due to insufficient rest
and rehabilitation. Dealing effectively with injuries is just as
important as creating a well-designed training program.

8. Train First for Volume (More Rep-
etitions) and Only Later for Intensity
(More Weight or Resistance)
This  principle  seems  to  be  at  odds  with  the  principle  of
specificity.  As  a  tennis  player  or  skier,  you  might  ask,
‚ÄúWhy  would  I  want  to  develop  aerobic  capacity  and
muscle endurance if power output and speed are the most
important  fitness  components?‚Äù  The  answer  is  that  low-
intensity, repetitive exercise prepares the body to withstand
more intensive training with less risk of injury.

High intensity training to develop power and speed pushes
the  body  to  its  limits.  If  you  first  develop  a  ‚Äúfitness  base‚Äù
and  then  increase  training  intensity  gradually,  you  will

develop power and speed with less risk of strains, sprains,
and muscle cramps.

tendons,

tissues‚Äîmuscles,

The  soft
ligaments,  and
connective  tissue‚Äîtake  a  relatively  long  time  to  adjust  to
the rigors of training. If it is the athlete‚Äôs goal is to become
as  strong  as  possible,  begin  the  program  by  doing  more
repetitions,  not  adding  more  weight.  This  will  prevent
injury,  strengthen  the  body  gradually,  and  prepare  it  for
sustained, heavier training.

During later training sessions, when the athlete has achieved
a  respectable  fitness  level,  she  can  add  more  weight  and
perform  fewer  repetitions  to  increase  strength  at  a  faster
rate. If the goal is to create good muscle tone and muscular
endurance,  the  athlete  should  perform  more  repetitions
instead of emphasizing lifting a heavier weight.

International Sports Sciences Association112  |  unit 8

overtraining: Imbalance be-
tween training and recovery.

9. Listen to Your Body
Athletes should not adhere to the planned program too stubbornly if it does not feel right.
Sometimes  the  body  requires  rest  more  than  it  needs  exercise.  Most  research  studies
show that the absolute intensity is the essential factor for fitness improvement. Typically,
an overtrained person has not recovered enough to train at an optimal intensity; thus,
a  few  days  rest  is  sometimes  necessary  to  provide  enough  recovery  to  allow  for  more
intense training in the following days. The hallmark ‚Äúsignals‚Äù of overtraining to watch
for and be aware of include fatigue, decreased performance, irritability, poor sleep, and
sometimes depression.

Overtraining reflects an imbalance between training and recovery. Many active people
live by the motto ‚Äúno pain, no gain.‚Äù Athletes must work hard to achieve high levels of
fitness. Too much training or training that is too intense is counterproductive.

Effective training occurs only if the body adapts positively to the stress of exercise. This
adaptation occurs following the exercise or training workout. If the body does not get the
chance to adapt, it will not become stronger, faster or demonstrate improved endurance.
Instead, fitness development comes to a halt, and the person may become injured or sick.

Proper rest is just as important as intense training to improve fitness. When you work
out, you are trying to get your body to adapt and improve its many functions. Before
every workout, ask this question: ‚ÄúWhat is the purpose of this exercise session, and how
will it cause my body to adapt?‚Äù If you are exhausted or have the flu, the workout may
cause you to regress. You are better off resting for a day or two. When you come back, you
will be ready for an intense workout that helps to improve fitness. Teach young athletes
that improved fitness requires a sensible balance of training and rest.

Exercise training is not an exact science. Sometimes you feel great and can train more
intensely  than  you  had  planned.  Other  times,  you  feel  tired  and  sluggish.  To  train
intensely during these times impedes progress.

Athletes should not use this principle as an excuse to skip workouts. If you listen to your
body and it always tells you to rest, you will never improve fitness. Encourage athletes
to follow a systematic program but be flexible enough to change it slightly according to
how they feel. They should train consistently. They have to work very hard to improve
fitness significantly. It is essential to try to maintain a structured workout program. If an
athlete never feels like training hard, he or she may have a medical problem or simply a
lack motivation.

10. Vary the Volume and Intensity of Workouts
Fitness  components  such  as  speed  and  power  improve  best  with  intense  training.
However, it is extremely difficult to recover from too many intense workouts. Athletes
must balance rest and moderate intensity workouts with high intensity exercise sessions.

If you design the program properly, you can actually plan for effective, intense workout
sessions. This requires considerable trial and error. For example, you might balance an
intense interval training session with a day of rest followed by a more moderate interval
training workout. When the athlete does the next intense interval training workout, he or
she will be recovered enough to train at 100 percent. Recognize compulsive athletes who
believe they must train hard daily so they will ‚Äúnot lose‚Äù previous gains. This is simply a
not true.

The same principle applies to weight training. Do not exercise at maximum intensity every
time you go into the gym. Most people cannot do maximum large muscle exercises such
as squats, cleans, and bench presses at every workout. Emphasize one or two exercises

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  113

each training session. One time, train intensely on the squat; next time emphasize the
bench press, clean, or functional training exercises.

There is no need to perform the same exercises every workout. To develop maximum
strength  and  power,  perform  shoulder  press  exercises‚Äîsuch  as  the  bench  press‚Äîand
pulling  exercises‚Äîsuch  as  cleans  and  snatches‚Äîtwo  times  a  week.  Do  squats  three
times in two weeks. Alternate between intense and moderate workouts for each exercise.
This method insures a proper balance between intense and moderate workouts and the
important rest intervals. Make sure the athlete uses proper technique when squatting or
lifting weights from the floor. Poor technique, particularly using heavy weights, leads to
injury and interferes with progress.

Cycle interval training workouts (for athletes who practice this type of training). Perform
interval training two to three times a week. On interval training days, do shorter, more
intense  workouts  on  some  days,  and  longer,  less  intense  intervals  on  other  days.  Do
over-distance  workouts  (longer  distances  at  a  slower  pace)  between  interval  training
workouts or rest. When doing interval training workouts, make sure to rest completely
(no exercise) one to two days a week.

This technique is sometimes called periodization or cycle training. It allows the body
to recover more fully and to train hard when intense training is called for. The principle
is simple: you should perform a particular exercise more intensely in one workout than
in  another  rather  than  training  at  maximum  intensity  for  every  exercise  during  every
weight training session.

Although sophisticated workout cycles are most suitable for athletes, non-athletes can
also benefit from such workouts because it allows the body to adapt and become stronger
more rapidly. Cycle training is beneficial if the goal is to improve muscle tone and body
composition (decrease the proportion of fat and increase fat-free weight). Try varying
the exercises in workouts. For example, instead of doing bench presses three days a week,
substitute  kettlebell  snatches  one  day.  Cycle  training  makes  working  out  with  weights
more interesting and speeds up progress.

periodization: Cycling the vol-
ume and intensity of training to
maximize the rate of adaptation.

11. Work on Athletes‚Äô
Weaknesses
The inscription etched in marble on the
facades  of  many  libraries  in  America
(and  the  Temple  of  Apollo  in  Greece
at  the  Oracle  at  Delphi)  is  ‚ÄúKnow
Thyself.‚Äù  Builders
should  probably
put  that  inscription  over  the  entrance
of  the  local  gym,  too.  Analyze  your
athletes‚Äô  weaknesses  and  correct  them
systematically.  You  can  do  this  only  if
you establish goals‚Äîthen systematically
assess
capability  of
achieving them.

athlete‚Äôs

the

You  do  not  need  a  degree  in  exercise
physiology to do this. Start with sports
skills.  If  the  young  person  wants  to
be  a  good  skier,  volleyball  player,  or
golfer, assess whether or not their skills
are  sufficient  to  allow  them  to  achieve

International Sports Sciences Association114  |  unit 8

the goal. Coaches and instructors should focus on helping
young athletes develop good sports and activity skills.

Next, assess fitness. Is the person strong or flexible enough?
As  discussed,  a  good  way  to  assess  physical  readiness  for
a  sport  is  to  compare  the  athlete  with  successful  people
in the sport at the level he or she hopes to achieve. Good
recreational skiers or tennis players should have the speed
and  stamina  of  people  who  compete  at  that  level.  If  an
athlete wants to be a professional football player or Olympic
shotputter,  he  should  possess  strength  and  power  levels
similar to successful athletes or he will not achieve such a
goal, no matter how much practice and time he devotes to
training and practice.

Does the athlete have any injuries that have not healed? You
can often relieve chronic back, knee, and ankle pain with a
systematic exercise program. Rather than making excuses,
have  a  physician,  physical  therapist,  or  athletic  trainer
evaluate the injury and start a rehabilitation program that
will set the athlete on the road to recovery. Do not take the
word of a friend or acquaintance about what doctor is ‚Äúbest‚Äù
for treating a particular injury. Seek out professionals in the
area and ask their opinion. The surgical nurse in the hospital
operating room knows who the ‚Äúbest‚Äù doctors are. Ask them
to whom they would go for advice or surgery. Follow up by
doing your homework. You must take charge when it comes
to medical care‚Äîyou want to seek out only the best, and to
do so, you must be prepared to ask questions and interview
those whom you will deal with. If you break your arm, and
you  have  a  choice  between  a  general  practitioner  and  an
arm specialist, who is more likely to do a better job setting
the  bone?  The  general  practitioner  might  set  the  bone  as
well as the orthopedic surgeon who specializes in arms, but
for my money, I would choose the specialist to set the bone.
And so should you.

12. Train Systematically
Plan a proper workout schedule for the coming months, but
do not be so rigid that you cannot change the program to
fit unforeseen circumstances. The most important point is
to have a plan so the athlete‚Äôs fitness improves comfortably
and consistently.

A  coach,  training  partner,  and  training  diary  will  help
keep  workouts  more  systematic.  A  knowledgeable  and
experienced  coach  or  instructor  can  prevent  common
mistakes and help motivate the athlete to meet fitness goals.
A personal trainer is trained to help clients make rapid gains.

A  training  partner  is  important  for  motivation  and
safety.  It  is  easier  to  attend  workouts  if  young  athletes  are
accountable to someone. A training partner can give them
encouragement and help spot them when they need help (a
spotter assists during the exercise). He or she will share the
agony and ecstasy that accompanies training.

13. Warm Up and Cool Down
Muscles  work  best  when  they  are  slightly  warmer  than  at
rest and have plenty of blood. Warming up before exercise
increases muscle temperature, muscle and heart blood flow,
tissue  elasticity,  joint  lubrication,  and  provides  additional
‚Äúpurposeful‚Äù  practice  before  competing  or  beginning
formal  practice.  Cooling  down  helps  to  gradually  restore
normal resting blood flow levels to the inner organs.

14. Train the Mind
One of the most difficult skills to acquire‚Äîbut one critical
for attaining high levels of physical fitness‚Äîis mind control.
Training the mind is thoroughly interrelated with training
the body. Young athletes can achieve almost anything if they
set their mind to it. To become physically fit and to succeed
as  an  athlete,  they  must  believe  in  themselves  and  their
potential, have goals, and know how to achieve the goals. It
requires discipline and is an ongoing process. They must be
able to put themselves in the proper frame of mind in order
to do this.

Consistent training is the key to developing fitness, and it
takes discipline. Many young athletes want instant results.
Unfortunately, fitness comes only after countless small steps.
Gains are balanced with temporary setbacks and periods of
stagnation.  Teach  athletes  to  develop  the  self-discipline  to
train consistently if they want to improve performance and
fitness for sports and exercise.

Make training a priority. Help young people develop enough
mental toughness and discipline to go to the gym, swimming
pool,  or  track  regularly.  Do  not  let  them  procrastinate.
They should go to workouts and get them over with. Often,
kids  put  off  going  to  the  gym.  Consequently,  they  end  up
wasting a lot of time. By the time they spend time thinking
about going to the work-out, finally going to the gym, and
‚Äúlollygagging‚Äù around when they get there, they have wasted
two to three hours. Instead, they could have just gone to the
workout, finished it efficiently, and then returned home in
about an hour. Help young athletes to develop the mental
strength and discipline to exercise regularly.

Youth FitnessThe Training Response: Principles of Training & Adaptation  |  115

15. Listen to ‚ÄúCoach Pain‚Äù
Good technique does not hurt! Athletes will have little joint
or muscle pain if they perform sports movements correctly.
Back, shoulder, and elbow pain are usually due to improper
biomechanics.  Children  with  sports  related  pain  usually
have defects in their technique or training program. Pain is
a good indicator that something is wrong with the program
or their technique.

16. Learn All You Can About Exercise
If  you  know  why  the  various  components  of  training  are
important, you are much more likely to plan an intelligent,
effective program. You will be less liable to jump into every
training fad that comes along and will always be in control
of  your  own  training  program.  Being  informed,  you  will
buy better and more economical sports equipment, manage
many of your own athletic injuries, and have a more efficient
training routine. You need to learn everything you can about
your body and training to get the most from your exercise
program, and you must encourage your young clients to do
the same thing.

A  recurring  theme  of  this  course  is  that  exercise  training
is  as  much  art  as  science.  The  more  that  athletes  (and
personal trainers) learn about the art and science of training
and  movements  skills,  the  sooner  they  can  benefit  from  a
program that works and helps them to achieve their goals.

Encourage youngsters to read books and magazine articles
about training and their sport. Some of this information will
be contradictory or of poor quality. That‚Äôs okay. With time,
they  will  learn  to  filter  and  extract  useful  information  to
improve their training program and skills.

17. Have Fun! Keep the Exercise
Program in Its Proper Perspective
Too often, sports training programs are overemphasized in
a  child‚Äôs  life.  Some  kids  think  of  themselves  almost  solely
as  football  players,  aerobic  dancers,  runners,  triathletes,
or  swimmers,  rather  than  as  child  participants  in  those
activities. While exercise is important, the child also must
have  time  for  other  aspects  of  their  lives.  Leading  a  well-
rounded life does not diminish chances for later success and
will  make  any  training  program  more  enjoyable.  Let  kids
be  kids.  Keep  the  activity  fun.  Even  highly  motivated  and
successful young athletes need more than just sports. They
should strive for success but keep balance in their lives.

An  old  saying  among  exercise  critics  is  ‚ÄúExercise  does
not  make  you  live  any  longer;  it  just  seems  longer!‚Äù  The
program  does  not  have  to  be  a  chore  if  the  participants
choose activities they enjoy. If they like sports but despise
jogging, encourage them to play tennis instead of running
on  a  treadmill  in  the  gym.  If  working  out  with  friends
helps motivate them, they should consider participating in
aerobics classes at the local health club.

Children‚Äôs club sports are extremely popular and are available
for  young  people  ranging  in  age  from  4  to  18.  Training
for  soccer,  t-ball,  baseball,  track  and  field,  volleyball,
swimming, fun runs, cycling races, or softball adds purpose
and  enjoyment  to  the  exercise  program.  Children  do  not
need  to  be  potential  Olympic  champions  to  participate  in
these  sports.  Participation  in  fun  physical  activities  gives
purpose to workouts.

International Sports Sciences Association116  |  unit 8

SuMMARY

The basic principles of training are similar for beginning exercisers, more experi-
enced people, children, and adults. Fitness improves when the body is given an
unaccustomed exercise stress such as walking instead of sitting, lifting a heavier
weight, running faster or farther, or stretching a muscle more than usual. The
body adapts to the stress by improving its function. The stress must be sufficient
to trigger adaptation but not so intense that it causes injury and overtraining.
Five factors that influence training adaptations include the degree of overload,
specificity of training, individual differences (genetics), progression, and reversibil-
ity. Children can attain high levels of fitness, but few will become professional or
Olympic athletes. All children should develop a love of sport and physical activity.
The personal trainer plays an important role in helping kids to develop a positive
attitude toward exercise and sport.

# Endurance Fitness

interval training

Endurance sports are relatively new to children. It is not unusual to watch kids as young
as  8  or  10  compete  in  long  distance  running,  triathlons,  and  endurance  swimming.
Endurance sports carry both risks and benefits for young people.

Many  American  children  are  overweight  or  obese.  Playing  endurance  sports  provides
an excellent way of cutting fat and preventing long-term degenerative diseases such as
coronary artery disease. Endurance sports require more training discipline than soccer,
football,  or  tennis,  however,  and  many  kids  do  not  have  the  psychological  makeup  to
contend with the intense training required in these sports.

Endurance sports such as swimming, track and field, cycling, cross-country skiing, and
rowing  offer  competitive  opportunities  for  children  as  young  as  age  five.  Clubs  offer
training geared to everyone from beginners to elite athletes. Training for these sports can
be intense and time consuming and require a considerable time commitment from the
child and parents. Table 9.1 lists a selection of Internet resources for locating clubs for
endurance sports.

It  is  very  important  to  avoid  overtraining  when  working
with young athletes who want to participate in endurance
events.  Children  cannot  develop  the  same  amount  of
muscle power as adults, and they fatigue more easily when
exercising  intensely.  The  epiphyses  (bone  growth  centers)
are also vulnerable to intense exercise. Trouble signs include
chronic  fatigue,  pain  under  or  below  the  kneecap,  and
shoulder pain.

gOALS
When  working  with  a  young  client,  start  by  setting  goals.
Does  the  child  need  to  exercise  to  improve  health?  Is  the
goal fitness-enhanced sports performance, or does the child
want to want to run, bike, or swim faster just for the sake
of  doing  it?  Be  honest  and  set  realistic  goals  that  can  be
achieved with a reasonable program.

MEASuRINg
ENDuRANCE FITNESS
Measurement  should  become  a  critical  aspect  of  every
personal  trainer‚Äôs  program.  You  will  never  know  if  the
program is effective if you cannot measure progress. Use a
simple  endurance  test  such  as  the  1-mile  run,  the  Cooper
12-minute run, or the 6-mile bike test. For athletes in non-
running endurance sports, select a test specific to the child‚Äôs
activity. For example, use a cycling test if he rides bikes or
a swimming test if she is a swimmer. Basic fitness tests are
described in Unit 14 of the course.

bASIC ENDuRANCE
FITNESS FOR hEALTh

The  Centers
for  Disease  Control  and  Prevention
recommends  that  children  6  to  17  years  old  should  do  at
least 60 minutes of physical activity daily‚Äîmostly aerobic
exercise.  Three  days  a  week,  children  should  also  include
exercises to build bone and muscle (e.g., gymnastics or rope
skipping). They do not have to do all the exercises at once‚Äî
time  spent  walking  briskly,  climbing  stairs,  bike  riding  to
and  from  school,  and  playing  sports  easily  adds  up  to  60
minutes or more per day.

Encourage kids to become more active in everything they
do.  For  example,  suggest  they  play  basketball  after  school
instead  of  video  games.  When  you  take  the  kids  to  the
supermarket, park in the farthest reaches of the parking lot
and  take  a  short  walk  to  the  store.  Have  them  wash  your
car  by  hand  instead  of  running  it  through  the  car  wash.
Encourage them to take the dog for a walk every evening. In
short, encourage them to move more and sit less.

Children  can  increase  fitness  through  ordinary  activities
such as walking to school instead of riding in a car, playing
after  school  sports,  and  doing  physically  active  pursuits
instead  of  browsing  the  Internet  or  playing  static  video
games. The newer generation of video games offers excellent
opportunities  for  challenging  physical  activities  through
fitness-oriented games.

Children will most likely achieve the goal of 60 minutes of
activity a day if exercise is scheduled on a daily basis. Until

International Sports Sciences Association122  |  unit 9

activity pyramid: Model for
meeting the suggested amount
of physical activity.

recently,  health  experts  believed  that  for  exercise  to  have  health  benefits,  individuals
needed to exercise vigorously for up to 60 minutes three to seven days a week. While
those  are  desirable  goals,  benefits  can  occur  from  less  intense  activity.  Given  human
nature, however, most children will not devote much time to physical activity unless it is
scheduled or ‚Äúprogrammed.‚Äù To ensure that children accomplish the goal of endurance
fitness for health, set aside a specific time for a daily walk, run, swim, bike, or other fun-
type activity.

A useful guideline to help individuals determine the proper amount of exercise is the
‚ÄúActivity  Pyramid.‚Äù Everyone should attempt to increase activities at the base of the
pyramid. Children should try to become more active during everyday activities. If they
are  participating  in  a  structured  exercise  program,  try  to  encourage  more  consistent
activities in the middle of the pyramid. For faithful exercisers, add variety to the program
by including recreational sports. Kids should minimize sedentary activities like watching
TV, playing computer games, and prolonged sitting.

ENDuRANCE PROgRAM FOR PERFOR-
MANCE‚ÄìLEvEL PhYSICAL FITNESS
The  basic  program  described  above  contributes  to  health  and  reduces  the  chances  of
heart disease. However, it is not enough exercise if children want to get in shape for their
favorite sports or to control unwanted body fat. To achieve those goals, they must go on
a structured exercise program that carefully considers the type, duration, intensity, and
frequency of exercise. Regular vigorous exercise provides benefits beyond what can be
achieved with a basic 60-minutes-a-day program.

TYPe oF exercISe
The best exercises for developing significant endurance fitness
are  those  that  engage  large  muscle  groups  repeatedly  for  at
least 20 minutes (30 to 60 minutes is preferable). These include
walking,  jogging,  vigorous  dancing,  cross-country  skiing,
swimming,  and  cycling.  Soccer,  basketball,  and  tennis  also
develop  endurance  if  children  are  skilled  and  participate  for
prolonged periods.

Select exercises that can be done regularly. Do not select cross-
country skiing as the main form of exercise if the child lives
in Florida and can only ski on vacation. If young athletes like
to  run  but  live  in  a  congested  urban  area  where  jogging  is
difficult or dangerous, perhaps they could run on a treadmill,
do stationary cycling, or pursue other forms of ‚Äúlarge muscle‚Äù
activity for 30 to 60 minutes at a time at a local fitness facility.

Cross‚Äìtraining  is  a  desirable  training  method.  It  involves  combining  more  than  one
type of endurance exercise in the program at a time. Including multiple exercises such
as swimming and running, stair-stepping, and cycling adds variety to workouts. Also, it
prepares young people for a greater variety of physical challenges.

Be aware that if the athlete wants to excel at any single activity, though, cross‚Äìtraining will
hamper progress. As with any type of movement, performances in endurance exercises
are  highly  specific.  Adaptations  from  running  differ  from  those  gained  in  swimming,
cycling,  or  walking.  In  fact,  training  for  one  activity  may  interfere  with  progress  in
another ‚Äúalmost similar‚Äù activity.

InTenSITY oF TraInIng
Improvements in endurance capacity occur when training above 50 percent of maximum

This  level  of  stress  initiates  changes  in  the  heart,  lungs,  circulation,  and  cells.  These
changes improve the body‚Äôs ability to transport oxygen and use fuels during exercise.

Exercise heart rate can serve as a measure of exercise intensity. A given percentage of
maximum heart rate during exercise will be approximately 10 percent higher than the
percentage of maximal oxygen consumption. For example, exercising at 50 percent of

International Sports Sciences Association124  |  unit 9

standard deviation: Measure
of variability used to describe a
set of data.

target heart rate: Desirable
exercise heart rate calculated by
multiplying the maximum heart
rate by a number between 0.6
and 0.9, which sets the exercise
intensity between 60 and 90
percent of maximum effort.

heart rate monitor: Electronic
device worn on the body to
measure heart rate during
exercise.

Determine  the  maximum  heart  rate  for  purposes  of  using  heart  rate  as  a  measure  of
exercise intensity. Maximum heart rate is most accurately measured during a treadmill
test  conducted  in  a  hospital  or  exercise  laboratory.  Nonetheless,  a  rough  estimate  of
maximum  heart  rate  can  be  obtained  by  subtracting  the  person‚Äôs  age  from  220.  For
example, for a 16-year-old, the predicted maximum heart rate is 204 beats per minute
(220‚Äí16 = 204).

This  technique  approximates  maximum  heart  rate;  however,  an  individual  athlete‚Äôs
maximum  heart  rate  may  vary  from  this  considerably.  The  standard  deviation  (a
measure of variability) for maximum heart rate is about ¬±12 beats a minute. This means
if the predicted max heart rate is 200 bpm, then the actual heart rate may be as high as
212 or as low as 188 in most people.

Measuring the exercise heart rate provides an estimate of training intensity. People can
achieve an endurance training effect when their heart rate reaches 55/65 to 90 percent of
maximum heart rate. This range of heart rates is called the target heart rate. The target
heart rate corresponds to an increased metabolic rate that causes the body to improve
its fitness. Use 55 percent as the lower value if the child is unfit and 65 percent as the
lower value if he or she is fit and physically active. Calculate target heart rate range by
multiplying the maximum heart rate first by either 0.55 or 0.65 (depending on fitness
level) and then by 0.9. These two heart rates represent the upper and lower limits of the
ideal exercise intensity.

You can measure exercise heart rate with a heart rate monitor and quantify exercise
output with a hand-held GPS. Prices for these instruments range from less than $100
to more than $500. They are unnecessary for the average person (unless they thrive on
gadgets), but this is a good purchase for the personal trainer. It helps to gauge exercise
intensity and provides scientific credibility.

You can estimate exercise heart rate by taking the pulse rate for 10 seconds immediately
after  the  child  stops  exercising.  For  example,  suppose  you  are  jogging  around  the
neighborhood and have to stop at a red light. Take your pulse for ten seconds and multiply
the value by six to obtain the exercise heart rate. For example, if you count 20 beats in
10 seconds, your exercise heart rate corresponds to 120 beats per minute (20 X 6 = 120).

Do not worry too much if exercise heart rate varies slightly from the target heart rate. As
long as individuals exercise at a vigorous pace they can reasonably sustain for more than
20 minutes, they will improve their fitness. They will not, however, make much progress
if  training  intensity  is  too  low.  Likewise,  individuals  can  become  injured  if  they  train
consistently at close to maximum intensity. You must teach clients to ‚Äúfeel‚Äù the correct
training pace. Teach them that if they feel exhausted from training too intensely, they
should slow down next time. If they feel they are stagnating, they should try upping the
pace or intensity.

Heart rate response to exercise is exercise specific‚Äîin other words, one cannot compare
a  heart  rate  achieved  during  swimming  with  a  heart  rate  achieved  during  running.
The stresses of swimming, running, and cycling are somewhat different. For example,
swimming occurs in a horizontal position, but running is performed upright. Also, water
pressure  triggers  a  reflex  that  slows  the  heart  rate. Thus,  the  same  relative  increase  in
metabolic rate produces a lower heart rate during swimming than cycling or running.

Two  other  simple  techniques  for  assessing  proper  training  intensity  include  the  talk
test and the rated perceived exertion (RPE) test. With RPE (Table 9.2), children learn
to associate a given exercise intensity on the RPE scale with how they feel during the
exercise. In general, the child should try to exercise at a perceived intensity from hard

to very hard (between 5 and 8 on the scale). The talk test
also  can  help  individuals  to  exercise  at  an  intensity  that
improves endurance yet minimizes injury risk. This test can
be applied during running or cycling but obviously cannot
be  used  during  swimming.  With  this  technique,  have
young athletes exercise at the fastest pace at which they can
comfortably carry on a conversation. This will correspond
to approximately 70 to 80 percent of maximum heart rate‚Äî
right about at the target heart rate.

TraInIng duraTIon
As  discussed  previously,  benefits  are  derived  from  even
brief exercise sessions lasting only a few minutes. However,
significant  improvements  in  endurance  fitness  occur  only
with  sustained  exercise  workouts  of  20  to  60  minutes  or
more. Changes in cell metabolic capacity require continuous
exercise.  Although  children  can  improve  by  training
intensely for short periods (i.e., interval training), this type

Endurance Fitness  |  125

of training is uncomfortable and increases the risk of injury.
Also, long-slow distance workouts can be a pleasant break
for athletes involved in intense programs.

TraInIng FrequencY
Encourage  young  people  to  engage  in  a  minimum  of
60  minutes  of  accumulated  moderate-intensity  physical
activity  daily.  No  excuses.  However,  practice  intense
training  only  three  to  five  days  a  week.  Training  more
frequently greatly enhances the risk of overuse injuries and
overtraining. Overtraining, an imbalance between training
and recovery, can produce chronic fatigue and suppression
of the immune system (children become sick more often),
hormone abnormalities, and psychological depression.

Always  remember  the  purpose  of  training:  to  stress  the
body so that it adapts and improves its function. Rest can
be as important as intense training. The process of exercise
training involves stress, followed by recovery, during which
the body becomes more fit. Without proper recovery, many
of the body‚Äôs support systems quickly deteriorate.

over-dISTance TraInIng
Over-distance  training  involves  exercising  for  sustained
periods.  For  the  beginner,  over-distance  training  might
involve  walking  for  30  minutes,  five  times  a  week.  A
dedicated jogger might go for a 1-hour run, while a serious
swimmer  might  swim  3000  yards.  Over-distance  training
for a bicyclist might include a 25-mile ride to the next town.
The cross-country skier might ski a 5 to 10 mile loop. People
who  are  serious  about  over  distance  training  typically
exercise  3  to  6  days  per  week.  Training  more  frequently
usually  leads  to  overuse  injuries.  Training  less  frequently
can also lead to injury‚Äîplus it is less effective for building
high levels of endurance.

the  number  of
mitochondria  in  cells  (i.e.,  mitochondrial  mass),  which
increases the metabolic capacity. This helps burn fats better,
allows  you  to  take  in  more  oxygen  and  protects  the  cells
from chemical damage from free radicals. Free radicals are
chemicals  produced  during  normal  metabolism  that  are
like ‚Äúbiological rust.‚Äù Free radicals have been linked to cell
membrane breakdown, genetic damage, illness, and aging.

To increase pace and speed during endurance exercise, the
young  athlete  should  practice  interval  training.  Interval
training  builds  aerobic  capacity  quickly.  However,  because
of its high intensity, it is uncomfortable and increases injury
risk. Interval training is most appropriate for older children.

International Sports Sciences Association126  |  unit 9

interval training: High
intensity training technique
that varies load, repetition,
distance, and rest using a variety
of exercises such as swimming,
running, and cycling.

InTerval TraInIng
Interval training involves performing repeated exercises at set distances or times. This
type of training helps the body to move at faster speeds by training the nervous system
to react more quickly, increasing the heart‚Äôs effectiveness by pumping more blood, and
helping cells cope with rapid metabolism.

One can apply interval training concepts to almost any type of exercise and even motor
skills. For example, a middle distance runner might do repeated bouts of 400-meter runs
at 90 to 100 percent of maximum effort. The boxer or fighter might hit the punching bag
for fifteen, one-minute intervals with one-minute rest breaks between exercise punching
sessions. The swimmer might swim 100 meters at 90 percent effort for 15 repetitions with
3 minutes rest between intervals.

Interval training has four main components: (1) distance, (2) repetitions, (3) intensity, and
(4) rest. Distance refers to either the distance or time of the exercise interval. Repetition
refers to the number of times the exercise is repeated, and intensity corresponds to the
exercise speed. Rest is the amount of time between exercises.

Each  of  the  four  interval  training  factors  relates  to  each  of  the  others.  In  general,  the
more intense the exercise, the fewer the repetitions and the more rest that is required.
For example, a runner performing 400-meter runs at 100 percent of maximum intensity
(speed) might only manage 4 to 6 repetitions. A child exercising at only 75 percent of
maximum intensity might manage 8 to 15 repetitions.

In general, children should not practice interval training more than three days a week.
This type of training can be exhausting and easily lead to injury. Base the volume and
intensity of the interval training workout on the child‚Äôs capacity to recover. If he or she is
overly tired training three days per week, cut back to one to two days per week. Likewise,
if  the  child  feels  good,  try  increasing  the  workout  intensity  or  volume  and  see  what
happens. The best advice is to begin slowly and progress conservatively.

Youth FitnessEndurance Fitness  |  127

do intervals running backwards, and soccer players can do
intervals running zigzag patterns.

The  general  principle  for  an  interval  training  program
design is to examine the physical requirements of the sport,
and then pattern the interval training program to meet the
athlete‚Äôs immediate needs and longer-term goals.

Interval Training for Swimming
Interval  training  for  swimming  follows  the  same  basic
principles  as  interval  training  for  running  and  cycling.  In
swimming, however, speed does not decrease with distance
to the same extent as in running or cycling. In swimming,
the muscles simply cannot sustain a higher velocity.

The four variables in interval training for swimming include
the number of swims, the distance of each swim, the speed
of the swims, and the rest interval between swims. Interval
distances  vary  in  length  between  25  meters  (one  length
of  a  standard  pool)  and  increase  from  there.  A  typical
interval training workout for swimming might include ten
repetitions of 100 meters (4 lengths of a 25 meter pool) with
30 to 60 seconds rest between repetitions.

As  with  other  forms  of  exercise,  start  slowly.  Begin
swimming  25-meter  intervals.  Gradually  add  distance
as  the  athlete  improves  her  fitness.  For  example,  start  by
swimming five repetitions of 25 meters at about 90 percent
effort. Gradually increase the number of repetitions to 10.
After the athlete achieves that level, increase the distance to
50 meters. Before you know it, she will be able to perform 10
or even 20 repetitions at 100 meters.

By  manipulating  distance,  speed,  repetition,  and  rest  (the
four  interval  training  variables),  you  can  train  a  body  for
speed or endurance. Swimming longer distances at a slower
pace  develops  more  endurance,  while  swimming  faster
better  develops  speed.  Remember,  change  one  factor  such
as speed, and you usually have to change other factors such
as repetitions.

Interval Training for Running
Intervals range in distance from 15 to 50 meters for a football
player or tennis player to several miles for a distance runner
or race walker. Keep the desired outcome in mind. If the goal
is to develop peak speed and power, keep the distance of the
intervals relatively short. On the other hand, if you hope to
develop  endurance  for  sustained  efforts,  then  increase  the
interval distance.

A  good  beginning  interval  training  program  is  to  run
straight-aways  at  approximately  80  percent  of  maximum
speed  and  walk  the  turns  on  a  standard  400  meter  track.
Start  off  by  doing  two  laps  (800  meters)  by  running  the
straight-aways and walking the turns. Build up gradually to
8  laps  (16  straight-aways).  As  fitness  improves,  sprint  the
straight-aways at full speed. Later, sprint the straight-aways
and jog the turns.

Graduate to 200-meter intervals as fitness improves. Begin
by running two 200-meter intervals and progress to 8 to 10
repetitions. Following each 200-meter interval, walk across
the track infield to the starting position. Track races are run
counterclockwise. In the 200 meters, put more stress on the
inside  leg  (leg  closest  to  the  rail  of  the  track).  If  you  run
many 200-meter intervals, run some clockwise and others
counterclockwise so that you stress your legs equally.

Finally,  progress  to  400-meter  intervals.  These  can  be
extremely  exhausting,  particularly  when  run  at  high
intensities.  Again,  start  by  running  1  to  2  400-meter
intervals. Build up until the athlete successfully completes
6 to 8 of these. Each of the intervals should be completed in
approximately the same time.

There  are  countless  other  variations  of  interval  training.
Athletes can run intervals up stairs in the football or soccer
stadium,  up  hills,  and  down  hills.  Tennis  players  can  do
intervals running side-ways. Defensive backs in football can

International Sports Sciences Association128  |  unit 9

Interval Training for Cycling
Children  can  practice  cycling  intervals  almost  anywhere.
With accurate odometers and heart rate monitors available
for  cyclists,  a  rider  can  pace  interval  training  by  time,  by
distance, or by heart rate.

With time intervals, increase the riding pace (for example,
from  75  to  90  percent  of  maximum  capacity)  for  a  set
amount  of  time.  Suppose  the  athletes  take  a  20-mile  ride
and  want  to  perform  intervals  along  the  way. They  would
ride for one minute at 95 to 100 percent of maximum, coast
or ride easy for one minute, and repeat this sequence 5 to
10 times.

Intervals also can be done at a set distance. Go to a cycling
track  or  park  and  ride  intervals  at  a  set  distance.  Typical
interval distances might be 1 to 2 miles. Ride the distance
at a predetermined percentage of maximum, then ride easy
during the rest interval.

As in running, going up hills requires specific adaptation‚Äî
to succeed at riding up hills, you have to train on hills. An
example of an interval training program on hills would be
to ride up a hill, then coast back to the bottom. Repeat this
sequence 5 to 20 times depending on the size of the hill.

Interval Training for Other Activities

Interval training can be practiced in almost every sport and
exercise. In tennis, athletes could hit against a wall or tennis
ball machine for two minutes, rest one minute, then repeat.
On the stair-climbing machine, an athlete could exercise for
two minutes at a fast pace, rest one minute, and then repeat
the sequence. In basketball, athletes could run four court-
lengths, shooting a lay-up at each end, rest, and then repeat.
Implementing interval training programs is limited only by
imagination and creativity. The basic underlying principles
of interval training remain the same regardless of the sport.

SuMMARY

Training designed to improve the cardiovascular system and metabolism and to enhance muscle power develops
endurance. Endurance is highly specific: building endurance in one endurance activity will not result in a full transfer
of that increased endurance to another activity. Primary training methods for developing endurance include over-
distance and interval training. The key is to build up gradually with either method.
