Flexibility‚Äîthe ability to move a joint through its range of motion (ROM)‚Äîis a key
fitness  component  for  health  and  performance.  The  topic  of  flexibility  is  perhaps  the
least studied and most misunderstood area of fitness. Children initially have excellent
flexibility, but they lose it quickly as they become more physically inactive or play sports
that do not work the muscles through their full ROM.

Good flexibility is important for normal joint function and injury prevention. The goal
should be to attain normal flexibility in the major joints. Extreme flexibility causes joint
instability,  which  can  lead  to  pain  in  the  back,  hips,  shoulders,  and  knees.  Balanced
flexibility  (not  too  much  or  too  little)  provides  essential  joint  stability  and  facilitates
smooth, economical movement patterns. Flexible hip and thigh muscles help to maintain
proper spinal alignment that reduces the chances of back pain. Flexible knees, shoulders,
and ankles help to prevent stresses and injuries to their supporting muscle groups and soft
tissues. Optimal flexibility helps to maintain pain-free joint movement with aging. Some
evidence even suggests that increased strength through resistance exercise may become
impaired with inadequate flexibility. Smooth performance of many movements in sports
and daily living is nearly impossible with less than normal flexibility. It makes sense to
help children develop good flexibility from the beginning. Once lost, re-establishing the
prior level of flexibility requires considerable effort and discipline.

Joint flexibility is a highly adaptable physical fitness quality specific to each joint. Good
flexibility  in  one  joint  does  not  necessarily  mean  good  flexibility  in  another  joint.  A
joint moved regularly through its ROM will tend to have good flexibility. For example,
gymnasts generally have extremely good spinal flexibility, while tennis players tend to
have excellent flexibility in the shoulder. These athletes stress the affected joints to the
limits of their movement capacities, so they require excellent flexibility in those joints.
The main determining factor for flexibility seems to be the extent to which each joint can
be stressed to the limits of its ROM.

People  lose  flexibility  when  they  do  not  exercise  much  or  move  their  joints  regularly
through  normal  ranges  of  motion.  Unfit  people  tend  to  be  much  less  flexible  than  fit
people‚Äîso much so that joint stiffness interferes with movements important in everyday
life. For example, rising from a chair or reaching overhead for objects stored in a cabinet
are  more  difficult  without  good  flexibility.  Deteriorating  flexibility  eventually  impairs
normal joint function and creates a handicapping condition. Performing exercises that

Youth FitnessFlexibility  |  131

collagen: Protein substance of
the white fibers of skin, tendon,
bone, cartilage, and other
connective tissues.

elastin: Randomly coiled and
cross-linked substance that
forms elastic fibers in connective
tissue.

contribute to flexibility (i.e., exercises that work the joints through their normal ROM or
specific stretching exercises) is important for general fitness and wellness.

Children  need  a  balance  between  flexibility  and  stability.  In  the  spine,  for  example,
extreme flexibility can lead to back pain, while spinal stiffness can decrease the risk of
injury to the back, neck, and extremities.

WhAT DETERMINES FLExIbILITY?
The ability to move a joint is limited either by (1) tissues that prevent movement (the
tissues either get in the way of movement or provide resistance to further movement),
and  (2) muscle length established by the nervous  system. While  some factors  such as
bone  structure  cannot  change,  other  factors‚Äîsuch  as  the  resting  length  of  elastic  soft
tissues‚Äîcan be altered through exercise.

TISSueS ThaT obSTrucT range oF moTIon
The bones of the skeleton limit movement in many joints‚Äîthe structure of the bones
forming many joints allows joint movement to proceed only so far. These ‚Äúbony stops‚Äù
help  give  strength  and  stability  to  joints  and  are  of  obvious  importance  in  human
movement. Joint capsules surround the body‚Äôs major joints. These semi-elastic structures
also limit movement. While they can be stretched slightly, they are semi-rigid and resist
deformation. Joint capsules also contribute strength and stability to joints.

The body contains many soft tissues that limit flexibility. Fat, skin, and large muscles can
‚Äúget in the way‚Äù and prevent a joint from moving through a larger ROM. For example,
people with extremely large biceps muscles cannot fully flex (bend) their elbows because
the large upper arm muscles interfere with movement. Most children do not have this
problem, but it can apply to high school-aged bodybuilders. Obese people are prevented
from making certain movements, such as flexing the trunk completely, because excess
body fat impedes the ROM in this region. Obesity with accompanying excess body fat
is a problem in young people because it can decrease flexibility. Tight skin, stretched by
large muscles, body fat, or pregnancy, also interferes with movements.

muScle elaSTIcITY
Muscle tissue is the key to developing flexibility. In addition to the contractile proteins
within muscles that create movement, muscle contains collagenous tissues that provide
structure,  elasticity,  and  bulk  to  the  muscle. The  two  principle  collagenous  tissues  are
collagen, white fibers that provide structure and support, and elastin, yellow, elastic
and flexible fibers. The elastin fibers have the capacity to stretch and rapidly snap back
to their resting position when stretch is relieved. If the fibers are loaded at low levels of
stress, as during exercises that gently stress the joints to the limits of their ROM, then the
elastic fibers become longer‚Äîin other words, they stretch.

Of course, there is a limit to the amount of stretch a muscle will tolerate. If stretched
too much, the collagen does not return to its resting shape, and injury results. Collagen
increases in stiffness with stretching. When reaching the limits of its flexibility, the collagen
becomes more brittle and may rupture if over stretched. The stretch characteristics of
elastic tissue in muscle are important considerations in a stretching program. One must
stretch the muscles enough to lengthen the elastic tissue, but over‚Äìstretching a muscle
may cause a serious injury. The effective and safe stretching program is one that stresses
the  muscle  enough  to  slightly  elongate  and  stretch  the  elastic  fibers,  but  allows  them
return to their normal shape without injury.

International Sports Sciences Association132  |  unit 10

golgi tendon organs
(gTOs): Compressed sensory
nerve endings embedded in
the tendon and activated by
any increase of the tendon‚Äôs
tension, caused either by active
contraction or passive muscle
stretch.

pacinian corpuscles:
Structures that detect movement
and pressure changes in the
muscles and joints.

nervouS SYSTem
conTrol oF muScle lengTh
The  nervous  system  controls  muscle  length  through  feedback  from  nerve  structures
called proprioceptors; these include stretch receptors, golgi tendon organs (GTOs),
and  pacinian  corpuscles.  Proprioceptors  help  the  nervous  system  regulate  muscle
length and flexibility, and help to control the speed, strength, and coordination of muscle
contractions.  They  detect  even  small  changes  in  position  or  force  in  the  muscles  and
joints.  The  stretch  receptors,  which  lie  parallel  with  the  contractile  tissue  in  muscle,
detect changes in muscle length. The GTOs, located within tendons, detect increases in
muscle force. The pacinian corpuscles, which lie close to the GTOs, detect movement
and pressure changes in the muscles and joints.

Stretch receptors help to maintain muscle tone and protect the joints and muscles from
injury. Sudden changes in length cause a stronger stretch reflex. The stretch receptors
monitor muscle length and the rate of any change in length. A rapid change in muscle
length causes a stretch reflex. The stretch receptor then sends a signal to the spinal cord,
triggering  a  muscle  contraction  that  resists  the  change  in  muscle  length.  Ballistic  or

golgI Tendon organ

muSlce SPIndle

PacInIan corPuScle

bounce  stretching  can  be  dangerous  because  of  increased  risk  of  injury:  each  bounce
stretch  causes  a  reflex  contraction,  so  you  could  easily  stretch  the  muscle  while  it  is
contracting. It is easier and safer to stretch a muscle when it is not trying to contract. A
muscle overload technique used by power athletes called plyometrics takes advantage of
the stretch reflex to increase muscle strength and power.

The GTOs protect muscle from too much tension. They send signals to the spinal cord
about muscle tension and the rate of change of the tension. Excessive tension inhibits
muscle contraction and promotes relaxation and lengthening of the muscles and their
antagonists  (a  process  called  reciprocal  inhibition).  The  GTO  signals  that  promote
muscle  relaxation  are  stronger  than  signals  from  the  muscle  spindles  that  promote
muscle contraction.

Static  stretching (stretching and holding a position for 15 to 30 seconds) helps the
proprioceptors habituate or get used to a longer length. Combining stretching with muscle

static stretching: Stretching
by holding the muscle in a
stretched position without
movement.

Youth FitnessFlexibility  |  133

contractions‚Äîa  technique  called  proprioceptive  neuromuscular  facilitation  or
PNF‚Äîis  a  particularly  effective  technique  for  increasing  flexibility  because  it  inhibits
the  stretch  receptors  and  encourages  the  GTO  signals  for  greater  muscle  relaxation.
For  example,  vigorously  contracting  the  quadriceps  muscles  causes  relaxation  of  the
hamstrings,  making  it  easier  to  stretch  them.  Stretching  regularly  trains  all  of  the
proprioceptors to allow greater muscle lengthening.

proprioceptive
neuromuscular facilitation
(PNF): Training technique
that uses muscle and neural
reflexes to augment the training
stimulus.

Modifying  nervous  control  through  movement  and  specific  exercises  is  the  optimal
way to improve functional ROM. You must work on this every day (or several times a
day) because the length and tension sensors adapt quickly. For example, the multifidus
muscles of the spine are critical for controlling spinal stability. Proprioceptors associated
with these muscles signal the nervous system to restrict spinal motion when you sit for
several hours studying or watching TV. You may experience back pain if you sit regularly
without allowing the muscles in the thorax (‚Äúthe core‚Äù) to exercise through their normal
range of motion.

TYPES OF STRETChINg TEChNIQuES
Stretching  techniques  vary  from  simply  stretching  the  muscles  during  the  course  of
normal activities to sophisticated techniques that stretch the muscles with muscle reflexes
to obtain more stretch during the exercise. Clearly, improper stretching techniques can
do more harm than good.

STaTIc STreTchIng
In static stretching, you stretch the muscle slowly and gradually and hold the stretch for 15
to 30 seconds, rest for 30 to 60 seconds, and repeat, trying to increase the ROM. Because
the  stretch  occurs  slowly,  much  less  reaction  occurs  from  the  stretch  receptors.  The
process involves stretching the muscles and joints to the point where one feels a pull but
not to the point of pain. One must never stretch so pain occurs because over‚Äìstretching
the muscle can lead to injury. Static stretching is the type most often recommended by
fitness experts because it is as effective and safer than other types of stretching exercises.

International Sports Sciences Association134  |  unit 10

ballistic stretching:
Stretching the muscles during
movement.

elastic component of
muscle: Muscle component
mainly composed of connective
tissue that acts much like
a rubber band to increase
elasticity in muscle.

passive stretching: Stretching
in which a partner assists in
moving joints through their
ranges of motion.

functional stretching:
Dynamic stretching through the
range of motion used in specific
sports or movements of daily
life.

ballISTIc STreTchIng
ballistic  stretching  involves  a  dynamic  muscle  action  where  the  muscles  stretch
suddenly in a bouncing action. For example, a ballistic stretch for the hamstrings might
involve  touching  your  toes  repeatedly  in  rapid  succession.  Ballistic  stretching  trains
the  elastic  component  of  muscle (mainly composed of connective tissue that acts
much like a rubber band to increase elasticity in muscle), so it may be an appropriate
training method for some well-trained athletes. For example, tennis players stretch their
hamstrings and quadriceps ballistically when they lunge for a ball and might benefit from
ballistic  stretching.  The  problem  with  this  stretching  technique  is  that  rapid  stretches
evoke a powerful stretch receptor response that can trigger an injury. Further, after doing
these exercises, the stretch receptors remain overactive. This may lead to injury during an
activity such as running or tennis.

PaSSIve STreTchIng
In passive stretching, a partner assists in moving joints through their ROM. One can
achieve a greater ROM passively than statically. However, because you are not controlling
the movement, there is a greater risk of injury. Passive stretching is a valuable technique
but should only be used by experienced people who thoroughly understand this method.
There must also be good communication between those performing and receiving passive
stretches.

FuncTIonal STreTchIng
Functional stretching involves dynamic stretching through the ROM used in specific
sports or movements of daily life. Do functional stretches slowly to lengthen the muscles
without  developing  high  tension  in  the  tissue,  and  do  them  in  many  directions  to
maximally remodel the tissues and train the neuromuscular system. Many people who
play tennis, golf, or volleyball but don‚Äôt stretch regularly have good flexibility because
they move through full ranges of motion during the activity. One can combine functional
stretching  and  functional  strength  exercises.  For  example,  lunge  curls  stretch  the  hip,
thigh, and calf muscles and stabilize the core muscles, while at the same time building
arm strength. Many activities build functional flexibility and strength at the same time,
including yoga, Pilates, Tai Chi, Olympic weight lifting, plyometrics, Swiss and bosu ball
training, medicine ball exercises, and functional training machines (Life Fitness, Cybex).

Youth FitnessFlexibility  |  135

ProPrIocePTIve neuromuScular
FacIlITaTIon (PnF)
PNF techniques improve strength and flexibility. These techniques use reflexes initiated
by  muscle  and  joint  receptors  to  produce  greater  training  effects.  The  most  popular
PNF stretching technique is the contract-relax stretching method‚Äîthe muscle actively
contracts before it is stretched. As discussed, contracting the muscle first causes it to relax
more so that it can be stretched more effectively. The technique is controversial. There is
no evidence to show that this procedure is any more effective than static stretching.

bENEFITS OF FLExIbILITY AND
STRETChINg ExERCISES
Flexibility  has  wide‚Äìranging  benefits  for  wellness  and  sports  performance.  Good
flexibility  may  help  prevent  injury,  enhance  joint  function,  and  make  movements  in
many sports skills easier and more efficient.

FlexIbIlITY and InjurY
Muscles  are  injured  when  they  are  loaded  more  than  they  can  tolerate.  Muscles  can
stretch, but only to a point. Stretching muscles too far can lead to injury. The flexible joint
can move further in the ROM before reaching critical stresses that cause injury.

Until recently, most experts recommended stretching before exercise. However, several
recent  studies  have  found  that  pre-exercise  stretching  decreases  muscle  strength  and
power and might increase the risk of injury. Exercising with sensitive stretch receptors
may  increase  the  chance  that  the  muscle  contracts  while  in  a  stretched  position,  a
scenario that may end in injury. Static stretching after the workout, when the muscles are
‚Äúwarmed-up‚Äù, increases joint ROM without increasing stretch receptor sensitivity.

Many  coaches  are  including  functional  stretching  before  workouts  or  competition  to
avoid disrupting the function of muscle and joint proprioceptors. We need more research
before we can recommend this technique.

International Sports Sciences Association136  |  unit 10

FlexIbIlITY and
joInT healTh
Researchers  believe  that  good  joint  health  requires  good
flexibility.  Joints  supported  by  inflexible  muscles  and  soft
tissues  are  subjected  to  abnormal  stresses  that  can  lead  to
joint  deterioration.  In  the  knee  joint,  for  example,  tight
quadriceps and hamstrings cause excessive pressure on the
kneecap  that  can  precipitate  pain  in  the  knee  joint.  Tight
shoulder muscles can compress sensitive soft tissues in the
shoulder that triggers pain and disability in the joint. Poor
joint  flexibility  can  also  produce  abnormalities  in  joint
lubrication that leads to further deterioration in the sensitive
cartilage cells that line the joint (i.e., chondrocytes).

joInT FlexIbIlITY
and SPInal alIgnmenT
Back  pain  affects  nearly  85  percent  of  the  population  at
some time in their lives. Back pain may be related to poor
spinal alignment, which puts pressure on the nerves leaving
the  spinal  column.  Poor  flexibility  in  the  muscles  around
the  hip  and  knees  and  poor  spinal  stability  can  increase
the  curve  of  the  lower  spine  and  cause  the  pelvis  to  tilt
forward excessively. Good fitness in these body areas, along
with good posture, helps to prevent abnormal pressures on
sensitive spinal nerves.

Flexibility and Body
Position in Sports
Good  flexibility  lets  you  move  and  thus  exert  more  force
through a greater ROM. Flexible people are able to assume
body  positions  that  allow  them  to  move  more  efficiently.
As examples, skiers with flexible hips and spines are better
able to get over the edge of their skis to make better turns.
Swimmers  with  more  flexible  shoulders  have  a  stronger
stroke because they can pull through the water in a direction
closer to the body‚Äôs mid-line. Hikers with flexible calves and
Achilles tendons are better able to climb steep hills without
pain because of their greater ROM in the lower leg.

FlexIbIlITY and STrengTh
Many  people  think  that  weight  lifting  creates  muscle-
boundness. A look at many weight lifters seems to reinforce
this impression‚Äîmany have large, bulky muscles, and their
skin  has  stretch  marks  from  trying  to  accommodate  the
tremendous bulk. However, it has always been apparent that

strong  people  also  can  be  flexible.  In  the  1950s,  Olympic
weight lifter and renowned body builder John Grimek was
famous for his displays of flexibility in addition to his great
feats  of  strength.  Many  top  strength  athletes  of  today  are
also flexible because they work at it in a systematic way and
on a regular basis.

Researchers have discovered a link between flexibility and
strength.  A  minimum  amount  of  flexibility  is  important
to  gain  strength  normally.  With  extremely  poor  flexibility,
strength is not gained as fast compared to individuals with
normal flexibility. Research has shown that lack of flexibility
in  antagonistic  muscle  groups  impedes  muscle  strength
development  and  hypertrophy  (e.g.,  increase  in  muscle
size). An antagonistic muscle opposes the action of another
muscle (i.e., hamstring muscles are the antagonistic muscle
group to the quadriceps).

Lack  of  hamstrings  flexibility  affects  quadriceps  strength
because of the principle of reciprocal inhibition. Reciprocal
inhibition  stimulates  muscle  spindles  of  the  antagonist
muscle  (e.g.,  the  hamstrings)  to  inhibit  excitation  of  the
agonist  muscle  (e.g.,  the  quadriceps)  through  a  neural
process that involves the spinal cord. The muscle spindles
are  receptors  within  the  muscles  sensitive  to  stretch.  In  a
squat, for example, if the muscle spindles in the hamstrings
are  stimulated  during  the  pushing  phase  of  the  lift,  this
inhibits  the  quadriceps.  This  action  prevents  exerting  full
force during the squat and constrains the  individual from
increasing strength in this lift.

Many  studies  have  examined  the  influence  of  reciprocal
inhibition  in  limb  movement.  In  the  knee  jerk  reflex,  for
example,  tapping  the  patellar  tendon  produces  a  stretch
reflex  by  the  muscle  spindles  in  the  quadriceps  muscles,
making them contract while inhibiting the hamstrings and
knee extension.

According to this principle, inflexible hamstring muscles may
make it more difficult to strengthen knee extensors. During
knee  extension,  premature  stimulation  of  the  hamstring
muscle  spindles  could  reflexly  inhibit  the  quadriceps.
Research shows that quadriceps strength tends to be less in
people  with  less  flexible  hamstrings. This  probably  occurs
because  the  muscle  spindles  in  the  hamstrings  ‚Äúturn  off ‚Äù
the  quadriceps  during  the  final  phase  of  most  lower  body
exercises. Chronic quadriceps inhibition keeps individuals
from becoming as strong as they would if they had normal
hamstring flexibility.

A  minimum  amount  of  flexibility  in  major  muscles  seems
necessary  to  avoid  impairing  antagonistic  muscle  strength.
For  example,  when  hamstring  flexibility  is  less  than  the
flexibility threshold, a significant portion of its muscle spindle

Youth FitnessFlexibility  |  137

may  be  prematurely  stimulated  during  knee  extension.
This  may  produce  a  premature  stretch  reflex  response  in
hamstrings and reflex inhibition in knee extensors.

Do  these  findings  mean  that  young  athletes  should  spend
four to five hours a week stretching? The answer is simply
‚ÄúNo.‚Äù Poor flexibility will only impair strength if the athlete
is extremely tight. Research does not suggest that improved
flexibility will improve strength at a more rapid rate. It shows
that inflexibility slows progress in strength training programs.
In an extremely inflexible person, working on flexibility may
make it easier to gain strength. Presently, the examination of
strength and flexibility has been limited to the relationship
between  hamstring  flexibility  and  quadriceps  strength.
However, there is no reason to believe that muscle spindles
work differently in areas other than the legs.

Several studies suggest that stretching before exercise might
decrease performance in maximum power exercises such as
jumping and throwing. As discussed previously, stretching

stimulates  the  GTOs,  which  act  to  limit  muscle  tension.
When you stretch, try to exert maximum power or strength
to  avoid  GTO  stimulation  that  can  hamper  performance.
It  is  better  to  warm-up  by  doing  low  intensity  dynamic
exercises. The ritual of stretching before exercise may lead to
injury and decreased performance. Pre-exercise stretching
will remain controversial for many years to come.

# Principles Of
FLExIbILITY
Ideally,  do  stretching  exercises  after  the  normal  workout.
Stretching  before  exercise  should  consist  of  functional
movements  similar  to  those  used  in  the  sport.  Do  static
stretching after training or competition. The muscles will be
‚Äúwarmer‚Äù and less at risk of injury.

Table  10.1  includes  the  eight  principles  that  should  be
followed as part of the young athlete‚Äôs stretching program.

Table 10.1

Eight Principles of Flexibility

1.  Stretch statically. Stretch and hold the position for 15 to 30 seconds, rest 30 to 60 seconds, and
repeat the sequence. Do not ‚Äúbounce‚Äù while stretching because it can increase the risk of injury.

2.  Practice stretching exercises regularly and develop flexibility gradually over time. Improved flexibility,

like other components of fitness, takes many months to develop.

3.  Do static stretching exercises following the workout when the muscles are warm and the neural changes

induced by stretching cannot impair performance or increase injury risk.

4.  When performing these exercises, strive for a mild stretch rather than pain. Emphasize relaxation.

The stretching program should not cause significant post-exercise soreness. If it does, then the stretching
routine was too severe.

5.  Relax and breathe easily during stretching. Try to relax the muscles being stretched.

6.  Perform all flexibility exercises on both sides of the body.

7.  Avoid positions that increase the risk of low back injury. For example, when performing straight leg

toe-touching exercises, bend the knees slightly when returning to a standing position.

8.  Understand that substantial individual differences exist in joint flexibility.

Avoid competition during stretching workouts.

International Sports Sciences Association138  |  unit 10

bASIC STRETChINg ExERCISES
There literally are hundreds of possible stretching exercises.
Choose  stretching  exercises  that  engage  the  body‚Äôs  major
muscle  groups.  Try  to  stretch  a  little  every  day,  or  at  least
five days a week. Choose a regular stretching time. A good
choice is in the morning after rising or in the evening before
retiring. Do not try to become flexible in one day. Build up
stretching intensity gradually.

whole bodY STreTcheS

‚ÄúGood-morning‚Äù Stretch
This simple stretch is particularly good to do after you wake
up in the morning to help stretch the spine and shoulders.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart and reach
up over your head, with your arms extended fully. Stretch
by trying to extend your arms as much as possible: first one
arm, then the other, and then both arms. Hold each stretch
for at least 10 seconds.

A

b

C

Good Morning Stretch (A,B) with Toe Touches  (B,C)

‚ÄúGood-morning‚Äù
Stretch with Toe Touches
This  exercise  is  similar  to  the  ‚ÄúGood-morning‚Äù  Stretch
except toe touches are added to the stretching sequence.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart and reach
up over your head, with your arms extended fully. Stretch
by trying to extend your arms as much as possible: first one
arm,  then  the  other,  and  then  both  arms.  Then,  flex  your
knees slightly and bend over at the waist and reach toward
your  toes.  Reach  down  until  you  feel  the  stretch  in  your
hamstring  muscles.  Hold  the  stretch  for  15  to  30  seconds.
Then, repeat the total exercise.

lower bodY STreTcheS

Supine Alternate Hamstring Stretch
This  is  an  excellent  exercise  for  stretching  the  back,
hamstrings, hips, knees, ankles, and buttocks.

Directions: Lie on your back with both legs straight. Grasp
the  back  of  your  right  thigh  and  bring  your  knee  to  your
chest  (this  segment  is  sometimes  called  the  knee-to-chest
exercise).  Pull  on  your  thigh  until  you  feel  a  stretch  in
your low back. Hold the stretch for 15 to 30 seconds. Then,
extend your knee so you feel a stretch in the back of your
right  hamstring  muscles  (supine  hamstring  stretch).  Hold
the stretch, and then return to the starting position. Repeat
with the other leg. You also can do knee-to-chest and supine
hamstring stretches separately.

Supine Alternate Hamstring Stretch

Modified Hurdler Stretch

This exercise stretches the hamstring muscles and lower
back. Avoid doing hurdler stretches where you sit with
one leg stretched out to the side as you stretch the other
extended leg. Turning out the bent leg can put excessive
strain on the knee ligaments.

Directions: Sit with your right leg straight and your left
leg tucked close to your body. Reach toward your right foot
as far as possible. Repeat for the other leg.

Modified Hurdler Stretch

Youth FitnessFlexibility  |  139

Standing Calf Stretch
Flexible calf muscles help prevent knee, ankle, and foot pain.
This  exercise  helps  to  stretch  calf  muscles  (gastrocnemius
and soleus) and the Achilles tendon.

Directions: This is a two-part exercise. Stand with one foot
about one to two feet in front of the other, with both feet
pointing forward. (a) Keeping your back leg straight, lunge
forward by bending your front knee and pushing your rear
heel  backward.  Hold  the  stretch  for  15  to  30  seconds.  (b)
Then,  pull  your  back  foot  in  slightly,  and  bend  your  back
knee. Shift your weight to your back leg and hold the stretch.
Repeat this exercise on the other side.

Groin Stretch
The groin muscles attach to the inside portion of your pelvis
and are extremely important for lateral movements. These
muscles  are  often  injured  in  strength-speed  sports  that
require  sprinting  and  quick  directional  change  (examples
include  tennis,  basketball,  soccer,  racquetball,  volleyball,
and field hockey).

Directions: Stand in a wide straddle with the legs turned
out from the hip joints and hands on the thighs. Go to one
side by bending one knee and keeping the other leg straight.
Feel a stretch in the muscles on the inside of the thigh of the
extended leg. Repeat on the other side.

Standing Calf Stretch

Groin Stretch

Lunge Stretch
This exercise stretches hip, thigh, and calf muscles.

Directions: Step forward in a lunge with your right foot and
flex  your  knee.  Keep  your  knee  directly  above  your  ankle.
Stretch  your  left  leg  back  so  that  it  is  parallel  to  the  floor.
Press your hips forward and down until you feel a stretch in
your quadriceps muscles. Hold the stretch. Balance yourself
by placing your arms at your sides, on the top of your knee,
or on the ground. Repeat the exercise on your left side.

Lunge Stretch

TrunK and bacK STreTcheS
Trunk and back flexibility is essential to fluid movement and
a pain-free back. An excellent exercise for stretching the low
back  muscles  and  the  power  hip  flexors  (iliopsoas  muscle
group) is the knee to chest exercise, which has already been
described.

Standing Side Stretch

This exercise stretches many muscles
in the trunk, particularly the obliques.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder
width apart, knees slightly bent, and
pelvis  tucked  under.  Raise  one  arm
over  the  head  and  bend  sideways
from  the  waist.  Support  the  trunk
by  placing  the  hand  or  forearm  of
the  other  arm  on  the  thigh  or  hip
for support. Be sure to bend directly
sideways, and do not move the body
below the waist. Then, repeat on the
other side.

Standing Side Stretch

International Sports Sciences Association140  |  unit 10

Trunk Rotation Stretch
This is an excellent stretch for the trunk, outer thigh and hip,
and lower back.

Directions: Sit with the right leg straight, left leg bent and
crossed over the right knee, and left hand on the floor next
to the left hip. Turn the trunk as far as possible to the left by
pushing against the left leg with the right forearm or elbow.
Keep the left foot on the floor. Repeat on the other side.

Directions:  Lie  on  one  side  with  the  top  knee  bent,  the
lower leg straight, the lower arm extended out in front on
the floor, and the upper arm at the side. Push down with the
upper knee while twisting the trunk backward. Try to place
the shoulders and upper body flat on the floor, turning the
head as well. Return to the starting position and then repeat
on the other side.

Supine Trunk Twist

Shoulder and
uPPer TorSo exercISeS
The shoulder is the most mobile joint in the body, so there
are  many  different  shoulder  stretches.  This  chapter  only
presents two: one for the muscles of the shoulder and upper
back, and one for the shoulder and chest.

Across-the-Body Shoulder Stretch
This exercise stretches the shoulder and upper back. It can
be done with a partner to increase the stretch.

Directions: From a standing position, cross the left arm in
front of the body and grasp it with the right hand. Stretch
the arm, shoulders, and back by gently pulling the arm as
close  to  the  body  as  possible.  Repeat  the  stretch  with  the
right arm.

Trunk Rotation Stretch

Pelvic Tilt
This is an excellent stretch for the hip and back muscles. It
helps to prevent excessive curvature in the low back.

Directions:  Lie  on  the  back  with  knees  bent  and  arms
extended to the side. Tilt the pelvis under, and try to flatten
the  lower  back  against  the  floor.  Tighten  the  buttock  and
abdominal muscles while holding this position for 5 to 10
seconds. Do not hold the breath. This exercise can also be
performed while standing or leaning against a wall.

Pelvic Tilt

Supine Trunk Twist
An excellent exercise for developing spine and hip flexibility.
It also is helpful for people with existing back pain.

Chest Stretch
This exercise stretches chest and shoulder muscles.

Directions: Stand to the side of a wall or post, approximately
two to three feet away. Place one arm on the wall at shoulder
level and walk forward until the stretch is felt in the chest
and  shoulder  muscles.  Hold  the  stretch,  and  then  repeat
using the other side of the body.

STreTchIng exercISeS
As  discussed  previously,  there  are  countless  stretching
exercises.  Individuals  with  tennis  elbow  or  shin  splint
injuries  may  require  special  exercises  to  help  rehabilitate
those parts of the body. Consult a physical therapist, athletic
trainer,  or  exercise  physiologist  for  stretching  exercises  to
meet special needs.

Chest Stretch

International Sports Sciences Association142  |  unit 10

SuMMARY

Children initially have excellent flexibility, but they lose it quickly if they are physi-
cally inactive or play sports that do not work the muscles through full ranges of
motion. The goal should be to attain normal flexibility in the major joints. Bal-
anced flexibility (not too much or too little) provides joint stability and facilitates
smooth, economical movement patterns. The effective and safe stretching pro-
gram is one that stresses the muscle enough to slightly elongate and stretch the
elastic fibers without causing injury. The nervous system controls muscle length
through feedback from nerve structures called proprioceptors. These include
stretch receptors, Golgi tendon organs, and pacinian corpuscles. Five types of
stretching include static, ballistic, passive, functional, and PNF. Flexibility has wide‚Äì
ranging benefits for wellness and sports performance.

Major stretching principles include the following:

1.  Most children should stretch statically, holding the stretch for 15 to 30

seconds, resting for 30 to 60 seconds, and then repeating the stretch.

2.  Stretching should be done following exercise rather than before. Athletes
may practice functional ROM exercises before practice or competition,
but they should avoid static stretching until after exercise.

3.

For preventing back pain, children should stretch the muscles around
the knees and hips and develop endurance in the muscles that support
the spine.

Summary

uNIT 11

# Muscle Strength

144  |  unit 11

Key Terms

isometric

free weight

weight machine

calisthenics

plyometrics

valsalva maneuver

collars

weight lifting belts

Children make substantial gains in strength, power, and muscle mass from lifting weights
and performing resistance exercises using body weight. More importantly, kids can do
these exercises safely and without damaging fragile bone growth centers. Weight training
is particularly good for girls because it helps to build bone mass and to strengthen bones,
muscles, and tendons. It also helps prevent future fractures during adulthood when bone
mass typically declines with age.

Not all gyms welcome children. Locate a health and fitness club that promotes weight
training  instruction,  especially  for  kids.  Keep  it  fun  and  build  strength  gradually  and
consistently.  Muscle  strength  is  an  important  fitness  component  for  everyone.  People
interested in fitness for health need to build adequate strength levels to maintain healthy
joints, strong bones, and metabolically active muscle tissue. Those interested in building
attractive,  healthy-looking  bodies  develop  strength  so  they  can  have  lean,  shapely
muscles. Young athletes need strength to make their movements forceful and powerful.

Increasing strength is quite simple‚Äîmake the muscles work intensely against increased
resistance. Resistance includes body weight, barbells and dumbbells, kettlebells, stones,
medicine  balls,  sledgehammers,  cables,  elastic  bands,  backpacks,  farmer‚Äôs  bars,  and
weight machines. Children can build strength in home gyms, health clubs, school weight
training classes, or with personal trainers who bring their equipment to the client‚Äôs home.
A personal trainer and coach can control how quickly athletes gain strength.

DEvELOPINg bASIC MOvEMENT
SKILLS FOR STRENgTh TRAININg
Building strength and power is important for young athletes as well as for youngsters
who are not athletes. Children should develop good whole body movement skills from
the  beginning.  A  foundational  principle  is  that  children  should  train  movements,  not
muscles.  Powerful  movements  usually  begin  in  the  lower  body  using  hip  and  thigh
muscles. Children should maintain a stiff, neutral spine during most movements so that
the muscles in the torso (core muscles) act as a spring for transferring force from the
lower body to the upper body. They should learn to hinge at the hips rather than in the
spine when performing squats, lifting from the floor, and performing rotational exercises
(e.g., hitting a golf ball or baseball, throwing a discus or javelin, or serving a volleyball).

Youth FitnessMuscle Strength  |  145

Figure 1.1 Seated Hip HInge with Proper Form.

ProPer hIP hInge movemenTS
Many experienced high school and college athletes actually fail to use the hip hinge when
doing squats and platform lifts. They often bend their spines, overuse their quads, and
fail to activate the gluteal and hamstring muscles. Children should develop basic lower
body movement skills before being allowed to perform squats, step-ups, cleans, snatches,
or deadlifts. These skills will help kids develop lower body power and a stable spine that
will promote musculoskeletal health for a lifetime.

aSSeSSIng and develoPIng movemenT
SKIllS For loaded lower bodY exercISeS
Most children do squats improperly, which increases the risk of knee and back pain. Poor
lower body movement skills impair the ability to learn other lifts, such as deadlifts, cleans,
and snatches. Each functional test is progressively more difficult, so the child should not
progress to the next test until they have scored at least a 3 on the current test. After giving
the young athletes instruction, rate their ability to perform the following movements that
begin on the following page (you can give partial credit with scores of 4 or 2).

International Sports Sciences Association146  |  unit 11

Chair Squats
Instructions: Sit up straight in a chair with the back resting
against  the  backrest  and  the  arms  at  the  sides.  The  feet
should be placed more than shoulder width apart. Begin the
motion of rising out of the chair by flexing at the hips and
not at the back. Then, squat up using a hip hinge movement
(no  spine  movement).  Stand  without  rocking  forward,
bending  the  back,  or  using  external  support  and  keep  the

head  in  a  neutral  position.  Return  to  the  sitting  position
(i.e., sit) while maintaining a flat back (neutral spine) and
keeping  the  weight  placed  over  the  center  of  the  feet. The
thighs should abduct (spread) when sitting back in the chair.
Use the hip extensor muscles as much as possible to stand
and the hip flexors to sit. Do five repetitions of this exercise.

Score:___________

(Note: Person should sit in chair with back on backrest, and arms should be at the side.)

Scoring Key

5 points

Child performed chair squat properly with good back and thigh position and weight centered over the middle of the feet.
There was good use of hip extensors on the way up and hip flexors on the way down. The thighs abducted when sitting, and
the head remained in a neutral position. Child maintained good form during all repetitions.

3 points Weight was forward on the toes while standing, and there was some rounding of the back. There was excessive use of knee

extensors and minimal use of hip extensors and flexors when standing and sitting. Form deteriorated with repetitions.

1 point

Child could only stand by rocking forward, and the back was rounded badly. There was little or no abduction of thighs. Child
used mainly knee extensors to stand and had poor muscular endurance.

0 points

Child could not stand from chair without assistance.

One-leg Step-ups
Stand  facing  a  bench  with  the  right  foot  placed  on  the
middle of the bench, the right knee bent at 90¬∞, and the arms
at the sides. Step up on the bench until the leg is straight,
maximizing  the  use  of  the  hip  extensors.  Return  to  the

starting position. Keep the hips stable, the back straight, the
chest up, the shoulders back, and the head neutral during
the entire movement. Do five repetitions. Repeat the same
number of repetitions with the left leg.

Unweighted Squats
Stand  with  feet  placed  slightly  more  than  shoulder  width
apart, toes pointed out slightly, hands on hips, head neutral,
and  back  straight.  Center  the  weight  over  the  arches  or
slightly  behind.  Squat  keeping  the  weight  centered  over
the arches and actively flexing the hips until the legs break
parallel.  During  the  exercise,  keep  the  back  straight,  the

shoulders back, and the chest out, and let the thighs part to
the side as if you were ‚Äúsquatting between your legs.‚Äù Push
back to the starting position, maximizing the use of the hip
extensors,  while  maintaining  a  straight  back  and  neutral
head position. Do five repetitions.

Single-leg Lunge Squat with Rear Foot Support
Stand  about  three  feet  in  front  of  a  bench  (back  to  the
bench).  Place  the  instep  of  the  left  foot  on  the  bench  and
put most of the weight on the right leg (left leg bent), with
hands  at  the  sides.  Squat  on  the  right  leg  until  the  thigh

is parallel with the floor. Keep the back straight, the chest
up, the shoulders back, and the head neutral. Return to the
starting position and repeat for a total of three repetitions.
Perform this functional test with both legs.

uSIng KeTTlebellS To Teach Young aThleTeS The hIP hInge

A  kettlebell  is  a  large  iron  ball  connected  to  a  handle.
Two-arm  and  one-arm  kettlebell  swings  are  excellent
exercises  for  learning  critical  lower  body  movement  skills
that involve the hip hinge and the stiff, elastic
core.  Children  as  young  as  age  five
light-
weight kettlebell swings.
It  is  vital  that  kids  use
technique
to
proper
prevent  injuries  and
develop  movement
to  enable
skills
them
train
to
harder as they age.

can  begin  doing

There  is  nothing  new
about  kettlebells.  19th
century
strongmen
such  as  Arthur  Saxon,
Eugene Sandow, and Ivan
Poddubny
them
to  build  lean,  powerful,
physiques
lightning-fast
that  allowed  them  to  perform  incredible  feats  of  strength
and  athleticism.  Russian  athletes  and  soldiers  have  used
kettlebells in their training program for more than 300 years.

used

On the surface, kettlebells do not look much different than
standard  dumbbells.  They  have  a  handle  connected  to  a
weight  just  like  dumbbells,  and  they  come  in  a  variety  of
sizes.  But  that‚Äôs  where  the  similarity  ends.  The  kettlebell
weight  is  located  at  the  end  of  the  handle  instead  of  on
either  side  of  it.  Also,  the  design  of  the  kettlebell  allows
performance  of  high-speed  ballistic  exercises  with  a
pendulum‚Äìlike  action.  Many  kettlebell  exercises  such  as
swings,  snatches,  and  cleans,  require  high-speed  eccentric
muscle contractions that produce surprisingly high muscle
forces. High-speed kettlebell training develops whole-body
fitness rapidly, with little or no muscle soreness.

Kettlebell training is not the end-all substitute for traditional
resistive  exercise.  However,  almost  any  exercise  that  can
be  done  with  free  weights  and  machines  can  be  done
with  kettlebells.  They  are  terrific  training  tools  that  build
strength,  power,  muscle  endurance,  and  aerobic  capacity,
and they promote fat loss. They also offer a training stimulus
(i.e., high speed ballistic and eccentric training) not offered
by other modes of exercise.

Most modern health clubs contain lots of expensive exercise

equipment  designed  to  isolate  specific  muscle  groups  in
the arms, shoulders, thighs, and abdomen. While isolation
training is important in bodybuilding, it does not develop
the  functional  fitness  that  is  important  for  young  athletes
and  that  they  can  use  in  sports  or  activities  of  daily  life.
Most kettlebell exercises work the body in a dynamic way
that links and coordinates large muscle contractions while
promoting smooth, powerful movements.

Choose the Appropriate Weight
Young children (5 to 10 years old) should begin with a light,
5-pound  kettlebell  and  build  up  gradually.  Older  children
can  use  heavier  weights‚Äîfrom  about  12  to  as  much  as
53  pounds,  depending  on
gender  and  strength
levels.
Emphasize
technique  over
weight.  Kettlebells  are  a  fun
and  enjoyable  way  for  kids  to
build  strength,  power,  muscle
endurance, spinal stabilization,
and  aerobic  capacity  (if  they
do enough reps).

Basic Kettlebell Exercises
There  are  countless  varieties  of  kettlebell  exercises,  with
the one- and two-arm swing and the one-arm snatch being
central  to  learning  the  hip  hinge  and  the  elastic  spine
concept critical for young athletes.

The Kettlebell Swing
(Two- and One-Arm Swings)
The  kettlebell  swing  is  the  principle  exercise  in  kettlebell
training routines. The exercise appears simple, but requires
coordinated,  linked  contractions  of  the  thigh,  buttocks,
core, and upper body muscles to do it properly. Stand a foot
or  so  behind  the  kettlebell,  sit  back,  and  grasp  the  handle
with both hands. With the weight on your heels, swing the
kettlebell backwards so that it pendulates (rocks back and
forth)  fairly  close  to  your  groin.  Drive  the  hips  forward
and contract the quads, glutes, pelvic floor, and abdominal
muscles, which causes a rapid acceleration of the kettlebell
upwards  to  shoulder  level.  Exhale  sharply  (but  not  fully)
at  the  top  of  the  swing  to  accentuate  the  bracing  motions
of the major muscles in the body. Keeping the spine in an
upright neutral position (i.e., maintaining the natural spinal

Youth Fitnesscurves),  let  the  kettlebell  accelerate  downward  as  you  flex
your  hips,  bend  at  the  knees,  and  maintain  straight  arms.
Perform 10 to 20 repetitions of this exercise. As your skill
increases, do the swings one arm at a time.

Muscle Strength  |  151

uninterrupted  motion  to  a  straight-arm  lockout,  with  the
biceps muscle close to the head. During the upward swing,
used  the  bracing  movement  created  by  simultaneously
contracting  the  quads,  glutes,  pelvic  floor,  and  abdominal
muscles to propel the kettlebell upward. Stop momentarily
with  the  arms  and  legs  straight  and  the  feet  and  body
stationery.  Lower  the  kettlebell  between  the  legs  with  one
loose, uninterrupted motion without touching the chest or
the shoulder and repeat the motion again. Do this exercise
with the right arm and then the left arm. More experienced
young  athletes  can  do  high  repetition  routines  that  build
high levels of fitness quickly.

One-Arm Kettlebell Snatch

TranSITIonIng InTo
whole bodY lIFTS
As  discussed,  young  athletes  should  develop  proper  body
mechanics  using  kettlebells,  chair-sits,  step-ups,  and
unloaded double and single leg squats. Gradually, they can
add  resistance  by  holding  dumbbells  or  kettlebells  during
these  movements.  They  should  not  begin  loaded  back  or
front  squats  or  pulling  exercises  from  the  floor  until  they
develop  good  technique  in  basic  movement  skills,  build
strong  thigh  and  buttock  muscles,  and  have  the  strength
and flexibility to maintain a neutral spine when performing
unloaded movements.

Children  also  benefit  from  more  traditional  strength
training  methods.  Almost  any  resistive  exercise  works,  as
long as the young athlete uses proper technique.

Two-Arm Kettlebell Swing

One-Arm Kettlebell Snatch
As young athletes hone their two-arm and one-arm swing
skills,  they  can  graduate  to  the  one-armed  snatch.  The
snatch  involves  raising  the  kettlebell  overhead  in  one
continuous  motion.  Pick  up  the  kettlebell,  swing  it  back
between the legs, and snatch the kettlebell overhead in one

International Sports Sciences Association152  |  unit 11

isometric: Exerting force
statically or against immovable
objects that does not result in
muscle shortening.

free weight: Equipment
moved in the performance of an
exercise, which is simply raised
and lowered as a complete unit.
The weight is free to move in
any direction and in any manner
the lifter can manage; free
weights includes barbell and
dumbbells.

weight machine: Equipment
moved in the performance
of an exercise, which is not
simply raised and lowered
as a complete unit. Exercise
machines guide or restrict
the direction and extent of a
movement. They use cams,
lever arms, pulleys, or cables to
redirect or alter the resistance,
or to generate the resistance
through springs, hydraulic or
pneumatic pistons, magnets,
or elastic bands rather than
weights.

calisthenics: Exercises that use
body weight as the resistance.

plyometrics: Resistance
training technique that uses
muscle stretch followed
immediately by muscle
contraction; an example is box
jumping.

Some exercises, such as sit-ups, are not recommended because they put excessive stress on
the spinal disks. Children will probably not experience immediate spinal disk problems
from inappropriate sit-ups on a slant board or back hyperextensions, but these exercises
can cause gradual spinal deterioration that might require back surgery when the person
reaches 30 or 40 years of age. A guiding principle for weight training or sports training
is that ‚Äúgood technique does not hurt.‚Äù Fatigue is a natural part of sports training but
pain‚Äîparticularly if it persists after exercise‚Äîindicates a training error.

reSISTance exercISe meThodS
There are four ways to perform resistance exercise: isometric (force against immovable
objects),  free  weights  (dumbbells,  barbells,  kettlebells),  weight  machines,  and
calisthenics  (exercises  that  use  body  weight  as  the  resistance).  Children  can  do
isometrics and calisthenics almost anywhere. They are a good way to develop strength in
young clients without having to invest in equipment. However, they are less effective than
free weights and weight machines for gaining strength.

Isometrics
Isometrics became popular shortly after World War II. Since then, considerable research
has provided useful information on this form of strengthening exercise and training. One
of the important findings is that strength increases only in the joint positions worked
during  the  exercise.  There  are  several  effective  isometric  exercises  for  abdominal  and
neck muscles that are easily added to a program. Several examples of simple, effective
isometric exercises are described in this section.

Free Weights
Most serious strength trainers prefer free weights because they are inexpensive, readily
available, and are easily adaptable to almost any movement or muscle action. Whole body
dynamic exercises, such as the clean and snatch, are impossible using weight machines.
These advanced exercises, described in the plyometrics and jumping unit (Unit 12) of
the course, are popular with serious power athletes.

Free weights include barbells and dumbbells. Standard barbells come in a variety of sizes,
with the bar weighing 15 to 30 pounds. Weight plates for these barbells usually range in
weight from 1-1/4 pounds to 25 pounds. Dumbbells are adjustable or fixed weight. Fixed
dumbbells, usually found in gyms and health clubs, range in weight from 1 pound to 150
pounds or more.

Youth FitnessMuscle Strength  |  153

Most  serious  weight  trainers  use  Olympic  barbells.  These
are  typically  7  feet  long  and  weigh  20  kg  (44.1  pounds).
American  Olympic  bars  weigh  45  pounds.  The  bars  are
better  balanced  than  standard  barbells  and  are  designed
to  hold  more  weight.  Typical  weight-plate  configurations
range  from  2  to  25  kilograms.  However,  smaller  weights
also  are  available  for  weight  lifting  contests.  Athletes  who
do dynamic lifts‚Äîsuch as the clean, snatch, and jerk‚Äîuse
rubber covered bumper plates that do not damage the floor
when dropped. Olympic bars are available in weights as low
as 15 pounds, which is a desirable weight for preadolescent
boys and for girls and women.

devices

Weight Machines
Weight machines are the
most  popular  resistive
for
exercise
recreational
exercise
enthusiasts.  They  are
preferable  for  beginning
young  athletes  because
they  present  a
lower
risk  of  injury.  Machines
are  safer,  easier  to  set

up,  provide  better  support,
require
less  skill,  and  are
more  comfortable  than  free
also  have
weights.  They
‚Äúhigh-tech‚Äù appeal and do not
clutter  the  floor  with  weight
plates.  Weight  machines  are
expensive and usually only are
available  in  health  and  fitness

clubs.  Be  careful  about  weight  machines  designed  for  the
home. Inexpensive versions are typically poorly constructed
and  can  be  potentially  dangerous.  Also,  they  are  unlikely
to fit children, so let the child try the machine before you
purchase  one.  There  are  several  companies  that  market
weight machines just for kids.

Weight machines come in a variety of designs that can be
overwhelming to even an experienced weight trainer. Some
incorporate  variable  resistance  so  that  the  load  increases
progressively  throughout  the  exercise  range  of  motion.
Machines  provide  resistance  using  weight  stacks,  weight
plates, air, rubber bands, and hydraulic fluid. Some provide
resistance  during  the  active  phase  of  the  lift  (concentric),
while  others  also  provide  resistance  during  the  active  and
recovery (eccentric) phases of the exercise. Kids can increase
strength on almost any weight machine if they exercise on it
regularly and consistently.

Calisthenics
Calisthenics  are  resistive  exercises  using  body  weight  as
resistance. Children should learn to handle their own body
weight. Exercises such as pull-ups, step-ups, and push-ups
that  use  the  child‚Äôs  bodyweight  as  resistance  are  excellent
for beginners. They are also excellent for people who want
to develop muscle strength but are unwilling to join a fitness
club or devote too much time to the activity. Children who
are  serious  about  increasing  strength  should  join  a  gym
or  fitness  club.  These  facilities  typically  offer  equipment
beyond  the  scope  of  a  home  gym.  Gyms  also  provide  the
opportunity  for  young  athletes  to  mingle  with  advanced
older athletes. This is important because observing skilled
and more physically fit athletes can be a source of motivation.
Some gyms have exercise machines built especially for kids.

International Sports Sciences Association154  |  unit 11

chooSIng The exercISeS
Choose  exercises  that  develop  the  major  muscle  groups‚Äî
the shoulders, chest, upper back, arms, abdomen, low back,
thighs  and  gluteals,  and  calves.  This  section  of  the  course
suggests ten popular and well-known exercises.

Young  athletes  interested  in  high  power  sports  (e.g.,
football,  basketball,  most  track  and  field  events,  baseball
and softball, and soccer) should focus their program around
whole body exercises such as squats, lunges, step-ups, push-
presses,  cleans,  snatches,  kettlebell  swings,  bench  presses,
and deadlifts. Power athletes should build core strength and
avoid  doing  too  many  bodybuilding  exercises.  Likewise,
bodybuilders should concentrate on developing muscle size
and definition.

daYS Per weeK
Increase  strength  by  making  the  muscles  work  against
increased resistance. This stress is called overload. It is best
to overload specific muscle groups two to three days a week.
Most  people  choose  6  to  10  exercises  and  do  them  three
times a week. Bodybuilders train four days a week (called
a split routine), alternating between exercises for the upper
and  lower  body.  Split  routines  might  include  exercises  for
the  chest,  arms,  shoulders,  back,  and  abdominal  muscles
two  days  a  week  (for  example,  Monday  and  Thursday),
and  exercises  for  the  gluteal,  thigh,  and  calf  muscles  the
other two days (e.g., Tuesday and Friday). Split routines are
excessive for most young athletes.

Children  should  balance  hard  work  in  the  gym  with  rest.
Muscles increase strength and size following the workout.
Individuals cannot make significant gains without allowing
sufficient rest between workouts. Sometimes, rest is just as
important as hard work for improving fitness.

rePeTITIonS, SeTS, and reST
Weight  training  programs  are  subdivided  into  repetitions
and sets. Each completion of an exercise movement is called
a repetition. In theory, you could do almost any number of
repetitions (one to thousands). However, in practice, most
people  attempt  5  to  10  repetitions  of  each  exercise.  Each
group of repetitions is called a set. Weight trainers generally
perform three to five sets of each exercise. For example, if
you were doing 3 sets of 10 repetitions of the bench press
exercise, you would perform 10 repetitions of the exercise,
rest  for  1  to  2  minutes,  then  repeat  the  process  two  more
times for a total of three sets consisting of 30 repetitions.

The American College of Sports Medicine (www.acsm.org),
the premier professional organization for sports scientists,
recommends  that  beginners  practice  only  one  set  of  each
exercise.  They  cite  studies  that  show  that  doing  one  set
increases strength just as much as doing two or more sets.
If you practice only one set, make sure it is high intensity.
The advantage of one-set training is that kids can work out
quickly and have time for skill training. Practice more sets
if you want to make faster progress or reach higher levels of
strength.

The  amount  of  rest  between  sets  depends  on  the  athlete‚Äôs
fitness  goals.  When  trying  to  develop  combined  strength
and endurance through resistance exercise, rest for one to
two minutes between sets. If the goal is to develop maximum
strength, one may rest up to five minutes or more between
sets. In this case, the goal is to exert maximum force during
the  exercise.  This  will  be  impossible  without  adequate
recovery from the prior exercise.

order oF exercISeS
If  the  primary  goal  is  to  gain  strength,  do  large  muscle
exercises such as presses, pulls, and multi-joint lower body
exercises  before  attempting  exercises  for  smaller  muscle
groups,  such  as  wrist  curls  and  calf  raises.  Doing  small
muscle  exercises  fatigues  these  muscles,  causing  them  to
limit performance when working on larger muscle groups.

SaFeTY and
PrevenTIng InjurY
Even  experienced  professional  athletes  often  forget  the
primary purpose of exercise training‚Äîto stimulate the body
to improve its function. Children who exercise improperly
or  overtrain  will  not  improve.  Worse,  they  may  become
injured or ill.

Youth FitnessMuscle Strength  |  155

Kids should perform all of the exercises properly. A child‚Äôs back is extremely vulnerable
to  the  unnecessary  stress  on  the  spinal  vertebrae  that  can  be  caused  by  poor  lifting
technique. Observe the following seven principles when lifting any weight:

1.  Lift the weight with the legs rather than the back, emphasizing hinging at the
hip and not at the spine. The legs and buttock muscles are the strongest in the
body. The muscles surrounding the spine are small and vulnerable.

2.  Keep the weight close to the body when lifting. This promotes lifting the

weight with the legs and hips rather than the back.

3.  Keep the back straight and the head level when picking up a weight from the

ground.

4.  Lift the weight smoothly from the ground.

5.  Do not twist the torso when lifting a weight. This puts abnormal pressure on

spinal muscles and disks.

6.  Children should not lift if they are tired and unable to use proper lifting tech-

nique. They should always lift within their capacity.

7.  When using weight machines, make sure they are adjusted properly to the

child‚Äôs body. Children should sit properly to support their spines.

Warm-up before performing resistance exercises. Warming up increases the temperature
of muscles, which makes them work more efficiently and prevents injury. It also helps
spread synovial fluid throughout the joint and protects vulnerable joint surfaces. Warming
up also helps reinforce motor patterns within the brain, which allows an individual to
perform the exercise more efficiently.

Children should complete a few ‚Äútotal body‚Äù warm-up exercises before beginning their
workouts,  such  as  easy  treadmill  running,  jumping  jacks,  or  stationary  cycling.  Also,
they should do some easy repetitions of each exercise before upping the resistance. For
example, an athlete doing squats with 150 to 300 pounds should do 6 to 10 repetitions of
the exercise with a lighter weight (i.e., 135 pounds or unloaded squats) before attempting
heavy weights.

Breathing
Proper  breathing  is  important  during  weight  training  exercise.  Breath  holding  and
straining (called a valsalva maneuver) increases blood pressure and may cause kids to
faint. In general, exhale during the active phase of the lift and inhale when returning to
the exercise starting position. For example, in the bench press, inhale when lowering the
weight to the chest and exhale when pushing the weight.

valsalva maneuver: Expiratory
effort against a closed glottis,
which increases pressure within
the thoracic cavity and thereby
impedes venous return of blood
to the heart.

International Sports Sciences Association156  |  unit 11

Decorum in the Weight Room
While being sociable is an enjoyable aspect of lifting weights, do not let it interfere with
progress  or  safety.  Children‚Äîeven  high  school  students‚Äîlove  to  play  around  in  the
weight room. This can be dangerous. The U.S. Consumer Product Safety Commission
(CPSC;  www.cpsc.gov/cpscpub/pubs/5028.html)  estimates  that  each  year  about
8,700 children under 5 years of age are injured with exercise equipment. There are an
additional 16,500 injuries per year to children ages 5 to 14. Teach kids to be aware of their
surroundings and follow these rules:

1.  Do not walk close to moving weight stacks or people doing lifts.

2.  Be particularly cautious around people doing dynamic lifts such as cleans,

snatches, or kettlebell swings.

3.  Do not disturb people doing heavy lifts. This may distract them and cause

them to either miss the lift or get injured.

collars: Devices to hold weight
plates on barbells or dumbbells.

Spotting
Use an experienced spotter whenever you are doing a lift that could result in injury from
a falling weight or a missed lift. Spotters can also assist when doing the lift, thus allowing
for an increase in the overall exercise intensity. A spotter can help a person complete a lift
that they ordinarily might miss. Instruct young athletes in proper spotting techniques.

Collars
Collars are devices that hold the weights on the bar. Athletes should always use collars
when they lift. Without collars, weights can easily slip off the bar and cause injury. Light-
weight collar clips are effective for keeping the weights secured to the bar.

Clothing
Wear workout clothes that will not get caught in the machines or interfere with exercise
performance. When doing leg exercises such as squats, cleans, or snatches, kids should
wear shorts that will not tear during the motion. Shoes should be closed toe and provide
good support when lifting dynamically. Sexually well-developed girls and young women
should wear sports bras that provide good breast support.

Youth FitnessMuscle Strength  |  157

Weight Lifting Supplies
Weight  lifting  belts  help  support  the  spine  and  help  an  individual  to  maintain
good posture during lifts. Young athletes should not wear belts because it prevents the
development of trunk muscles during weight lifting. However, belts will help the athlete
lift more weight. Serious weight trainers often wrap their knees and wrists for additional
support.  Weight  training  can  create  hand  calluses,  so  some  people  use  weight  lifting
gloves to prevent them. Weight lifting supplies such as belts, wraps, and shoes can be
purchased at sporting goods stores or through a variety of fitness magazines.

bASIC WEIghT TRAININg ExERCISES
There are literally hundreds of weight training exercises. The most popular exercises for
the  major  muscle  groups  are  listed  in  Table  11.1. This  unit  of  the  course  will  describe
some  of  the  most  popular  exercises  that  can  serve  as  the  basis  for  beginning  strength
training programs. They are appropriate for children interested in general fitness. Athletes
interested in developing power should do more whole body, functional training exercises.

cheST and Shoulder exercISeS
The major muscles include the pectoralis major (chest), deltoid (shoulders), and trapezius
(upper shoulder, upper back, and neck). Presses such as the bench press and the incline
press  are  best  for  developing  these  muscles.  Exercises  that  isolate  specific  muscles  are
also helpful. For example, fly exercises, which bring the arms across the chest, isolate the
pectoralis major muscles (large chest muscles, one on each side).

weight lifting belts: Belts
that stabilize the spine and
abdominal region during
weight lifting. Children should
avoid using them because they
hinder the development of core
stabilizing muscles.

Bench Press
This  is  a  popular  exercise  that  works  the  muscles  of  the
chest,  shoulders,  and  arms  (pectoralis  major,  deltoid,  and
triceps  muscles).  It  can  be  done  with  barbells,  dumbbells,
kettlebells, or weight machines. If you are using a barbell, it
is best to employ a spotter and a bench with a built-in rack.

Upright Row
This  exercise  develops  the  deltoids,  biceps,  and  trapezius
muscles. It is particularly useful for athletes who need strong
neck muscles such as football players, wrestlers, and martial
artists. This exercise can be done with barbells or dumbbells
in either an upright or bent over position.

Directions:  Lie  on  a  bench  face-up,  with  feet  flat  on  the
floor.  Start  with  arms  extended  and  the  weight  over  the
chest,  and  grasp  the  bar  at  approximately  shoulder  width.
Lower the weight to the chest under control, and then push
the weight to the starting position.

approximately four inches apart and

the collar bone. Return the bar to the

This  exercise  develops  the  deltoids,  triceps,  and  trapezius
muscles. The standing or military press has been a popular
exercise  for  more  than  150  years.  However,  many  people
bend  their  low  back  excessively  when  doing  this  exercise,
which  increases  the  risk  of  injury  to  spinal  muscles  and
vertebral  disks.  Variations  of  the  seated  press  include  the
standing  barbell  or  dumbbell  press  (military  press),  the
push  press,  and  the  seated  dumbbell  press.  There  are  also
many  weight  machines  for  performing  seated,    standing
overhead, and behind the neck presses.

Directions: Sit on a bench,
preferably  one  with  back
support. Begin by grasping
the bar at shoulder width,
with  the  bar  resting  on
the chest. Push the weight
straight overhead and fully

arm exercISeS
Arm  exercises  are  popular  with  weight  trainers.  The  two
major arm muscles include the biceps brachius, which flexes
or bends the elbow, and the triceps brachius, which extends
or straightens the elbow.

Biceps Curls
Kids can do biceps curls with barbells, dumbbells, or biceps
curl  machines.  Curl  bars,  which  position  the  forearms  at
a  45¬∞  angle,  are  popular  because  they  place  less  stress  on
the  forearms  during  the  exercise.  One  can  also  isolate  the
upper arm during the exercise by using special curl benches
(i.e.,  preacher  stand).  Many  other  exercises  also  develop
the  biceps;  these  include  lat  pulls,  pull-ups,  upright  rows,
cleans, and snatches.

Directions:  Grasp a  barbell at  shoulder width with palms
facing  outward.  Stand  with  the  elbows  extended  and  the
arms  against  the  body.  Keep  the  elbows  close  to  the  body
and  flex  the  elbows,  bringing  the  bar  to  the  chest.  Return
to  the  starting  position.  Use  the  biceps  muscles  to  lift  the
weight, and do not ‚Äúcheat‚Äù by swaying the body.

holding  a  dumbbell  in  each  hand.  With  palms  down  and
arms straight, raise the right arm to the rear as far as possible.
Lower  the  weight  to  the  starting  position  and  repeat  the
exercise using the left arm.

uPPer bacK exercISeS
The major muscles of the upper back include the trapezius
(‚Äútraps‚Äù),  the  latissimus  dorsi  (‚Äúlats‚Äù),  and  the  rhombiods.
The upright rowing exercise, already described, is excellent
for developing the traps. Pulling exercises, such as the clean
and  snatch,  kettlebell  snatches,  and  barbell  or  dumbbell
presses, are also excellent for developing the major muscles
of the upper back.

Lat Pulls
This  exercise  requires  a  lat  machine.  There  are  two  basic
types of lat machines: a free standing machine that uses the
person‚Äôs  body  weight  for  stabilization  during  the  exercise;
the other has a seat that restrains the body with a seat belt
or thigh support. If using heavy weights on a free-standing
machine, the lifter will always need a spotter. Variations of
this  exercise  include  pull-ups  and  pull-overs  (machine  or
free weights).

Directions: From a seated or kneeling position (depending
on  the  machine),  grasp  the  bar  overhead  with  the  hands
placed beyond shoulder width apart. Pull the bar down until
it reaches the chest, and then return to the starting position.

Biceps Curls

Triceps Pushdowns
This  exercise  uses  the  lat  machine  to  build  the  triceps
muscles (muscles on the back of each arm). Other exercises
that  isolate  the  triceps  using  barbells,  dumbbells,  and
exercise  machines  include  French  curls,  supine  bench
triceps extensions, and parallel bar dips. Pressing exercises
such as the bench press, the incline press, the military press,
and the jerk also develop the triceps muscles.

Directions: Stand facing the lat machine. Grasp the bar with
the hands approximately four to six inches apart and with
the palms facing the body. Fully extend the arms with the
elbows held closely at the side. From this starting position,
with  the  elbows  locked  to  the  side,  allow  the  hands  to  be

ThIgh and
gluTeal exercISeS
Leg and thigh muscles are much more important for sports
performance than arm, chest, and shoulder muscles. Yet visit
almost any gym and you will observe many people focusing
on bench presses and curls and very few people emphasizing
squats and power cleans. To improve performance in sports
where one runs, jumps, or transfers body weight from the
back  to  front  foot  (e.g.,  tennis,  golf,  baseball,  softball,  or
boxing),  perform  exercises  that  develop  the  large  muscles
of  the  legs  and  hips.  Exercises  for  these  muscles  include
squats, front squats, cleans, snatches, lunges, step-ups, knee
extensions, leg curls, jerks, push-presses, and calf raises.

The lower body major muscle groups include the quadriceps
(quads:  muscles  on  the  front  of  the  thighs),  the  gluteus
maximus  (the  large  buttocks  muscle),  the  hamstrings
(the back of the thighs), and the calf muscles (the back of
lower  legs).  The  quadriceps  and  hamstrings  move  the  hip
and  knee  joints.  The  quads  extend  or  straighten  the  knee
and flex or bend the hip. The hamstrings flex the knee and
extend the hip. Squats and step-ups use the knees and hips
to work the hamstrings and quadriceps during the pushing
or concentric phase of the lift.

Squats
This  exercise  requires  a  barbell  and  a  squat  rack.  These
racks might not fit some young athletes. Many people like
a squat rack with a safety platform to the side where they
can rest the weight if they fail to complete the lift. Use one
to  two  spotters  if  you  are  attempting  a  squat  with  heavier
weights.  During  this  lift,  it  is  crucial  to  use  collars  and  to
make sure to adjust the squat rack properly for the person‚Äôs

abdomInal exercISeS
Abdominal muscles are important for health, performance,
and  appearance.  These  muscles  help  stabilize  the  spine,
which  prevents  back  pain.  For  rotational  movements,
emphasize rotating from the hips while maintaining a stiff
and  neutral  spine.  Rotational  movements  are  critical  in
sports that require weight transfer such as tennis, baseball,
bowling, throwing (baseball, discus, javelin, and hammer),
karate, and boxing. Rotating with the spine without using
the  hips  produces  weak,  less  powerful  motions  and  puts
the spine at risk. Strong abdominal muscles are important
to  stabilize  the  trunk  in  almost  all  movements  that  use
large muscles (i.e., running and jumping). Muscles include
the  rectus  abdominis,  internal  and  external  obliques,  and
transversus abdominis. Curl-ups are the principle exercise
for  these  muscles.  However,  any  exercise  that  stabilizes
the  core  muscles‚Äîsuch  as  planks,  kettlebell  swings,  and
cleans‚Äîalso build these muscles.

Curl-ups
Curl-ups  are  a  variation  of  the  sit-up  abdominal  exercise.
Full  sit-ups  are  not  necessary  to  develop  fully  trained
abdominals. The abdominal muscles receive a tremendous
workout  by  moving  through  a  restricted  range  of  motion.
Curl-ups are just as effective as sit-ups, and they place less
stress on the back.

Directions: Lie on the back on the floor with one leg straight
and  the  other  foot  flat  on  the  floor.  With  the  arms  folded

Directions:  Using  a  calf  machine,  stand  with  the  toes  on
the  platform,  with  the  heels  extending  from  the  edge  of
the  board  and  weight  pads  resting  comfortably  on  each
shoulder. Rise up on the toes and then return to the starting
position. As children gain strength, they should allow their
heels  to  go  below  the  level  of  the  platform. This  increases
strength and calf-Achilles tendon flexibility.

hamstrings, and calf muscles.

Directions:  Place  the  bar  so  that  it  rests  on  the  fleshy

are  approximately  parallel  with  the  floor.  Drive  upward

Hamstring Curls
This exercise requires a curl machine. As discussed, squats

exercISeS For The calveS
The  gastrocnemius  and  soleus  are  the  two  major  calf
muscles. These muscles plantar flex the ankle, which means
they work when lifting the heels and going up on the toes.
These muscles are important for jumping and running, and
are developed by putting weights on the shoulders, using a
barbell or calf machine, and raising the heels off the ground.
Whole body exercises that can also bulid the calves include
the snatch, squat, and clean.

TraInIng ProgramS
There are countless variations of weight training programs.
You  will  need  to  individualize  each  program  to  meet  the
goals  of  the  client.  Start  with  a  general  warm-up  such  as
easy-pace  exercise  on  a  treadmill  or  stationary  bicycle  for
one to two minutes. Do large muscle bench press and squat
exercises before smaller muscle exercises, such as biceps and
hamstring curls. Do all the sets for an exercise before moving
to the next sequence of exercises. Refer to Table 11.1 on the
following page for recommended sets and repetitions.

Power athletes can develop significant strength and muscle
fitness by performing three to four whole body functional
training exercises in a workout. The take home message is
to do the program regularly and progressively.

SuMMARY

Children can make substantial gains in strength, power, and muscle mass safely by lifting weights and performing
resistance exercises using their own body weight. Keep it fun and build strength gradually and consistently. Children
should develop good whole body movement skills as soon as they begin their training and exercise programs. Chil-
dren should train movements, not muscles. They should maintain a stiff spine during most movements so that the
major core muscles act as a spring for force transfer from the lower body to the upper body. All children should
learn to hinge at the hips rather than in the spine when doing squats, lifting from the floor, and performing
rotational exercises.

Poor lower body movement skills impair the ability to learn deadlifts, cleans, and snatches. Test the child‚Äôs capacity to
perform basic lower body movements before including weight-bearing squats or step-ups in the weight training pro-
gram. Kettlebells are excellent for introducing the concept of the hip hinge and spinal stiffness. Children also benefit
from more traditional strength training methods. A guiding principle for weight training or sports training in general
is that ‚Äúgood technique does not hurt.‚Äù Fatigue is a natural part of sports training, but pain‚Äî particularly if it persists
after exercise‚Äîindicates a training error. Young athletes interested in high power sports should center their programs
around whole body exercises that includes squats, lunges, step-ups, push-presses, cleans, snatches, kettlebell swings,
bench press, and deadlifts.
