Summary

uNIT 12

# Jumping And Plyometrics

164  |  unit 12

Key Terms

plyometrics

stretch-shortening cycle

plyometrics: Resistance
training technique that uses
muscle stretch followed
immediately by muscle
contraction; an example is box
jumping.

stretch-shortening cycle:
An active stretch (eccentric
contraction) of a muscle
followed by an immediate
shortening (concentric
contraction) of that same
muscle.

JuMPINg AND PLYOMETRICS
Kids do jumping and plyometric exercises naturally. Plyometric exercises are excellent
for building basic muscle strength, power, and speed. They help build bone mass in girls,
which protects them against fractures as they age. The exercises are fun and challenging
for children and should be included into every physical education program.

Jumping  exercises  and  plyometrics  enhance  performance  in  strength-speed  sports
because they increase leg power and train the nervous system to activate large muscle
groups quickly during movement. The exercises in this section of the course enhance the
capacity for single ‚Äúexplosive‚Äù movements such as jumping, throwing a discus, baseball
or softball, and hitting a golf or tennis ball.

Plyometric exercises involve rapid stretching then shortening of a muscle group during
highly  dynamic  movements  (i.e.,  stretch-shortening  cycle).  Stretching  causes  a
stretch  reflex  and  elastic  recoil  in  muscles‚Äîwhich  combined  with  a  vigorous  muscle
contraction‚Äîcreates  great  force  that  overloads  the  muscles,  increases  strength  and
power, enhances the strength of connective tissues that promote muscle elasticity, and
improves tendon strength.

Plyometrics range in difficulty from calf jumps off the ground to multiple one-leg jumps
to and from boxes. Calf jumps are simple plyometric exercises involving jumping in place
repeatedly using mainly calf muscles. After landing on the ground after the first jump, the
calf muscles stretch as they help control the landing. The recoil from the stretch adds to
the force of the muscle contraction for the next jump.

The basic principle for all jumping and plyometric exercises involves absorbing the shock
with the arms or legs and then immediately contracting the muscles. For example, when
doing  a  series  of  squat  jumps,  jump  again  as  quickly  as  possible  after  landing.  More
muscle overload occurs when jumps are made in rapid succession. These exercises train
the nervous system to react quickly.

In untrained people, the nervous system reacts slowly when ‚Äúturning on‚Äù the muscles
during repeated muscle contractions, such as those that occur during calf jumping. This
protective reflex is designed to protect the legs from injury. With conditioning, a trained
nervous system reacts more quickly and activates leg muscles rapidly. Stronger muscles
and joints no longer need the reflex protection.

Jumping and plyometric exercises can cause great stress to the muscles, bones, and joints.
All exercises in this section are considered moderate to high impact.

Do not do these exercises more than two to three days per week. If you feel pain in your
muscles and joints for hours or days after a workout, modify your program or stop doing
the exercises that give you trouble.

Youth FitnessJumping and Plyometrics  |  165

This  section  begins  with  simple,  relatively  low  impact
exercises. More difficult exercises are presented later in the
section. Do not attempt advanced exercises until you are in
excellent condition and can perform the exercises without
pain. Start by doing one to two sets of about three to four
exercises.  As  you  become  better  conditioned,  build  up  to
three sets of 6 to 10 exercises. Do these exercises with perfect
form and intensity. It is better to do only one set correctly
than many sets incorrectly and at a reduced speed.

STaTIonarY PlYomeTrIcS
Start  with  these  simple  exercises  before  progressing  to
movements  that  place  more  stress  on  your  muscles  and
joints. With these exercises, you jump to and from the same
place on the ground. More advanced exercises will progress
to repeated distance jumps and then to box jumps.

Calf Jumps
This basic exercise helps to develop jumping power in the
calf  muscles.  This  is  an  excellent  beginning  exercise  for
children of all skill levels.

Directions:  Stand  with  feet  shoulder  width  apart  with
hands on hips. Bend at the knees slightly. Using mainly calf
muscles, jump rapidly in place for 10 repetitions.

Variations:  Advanced  variations  include  calf  jump  spins
and  one-leg  calf  jumps.  With  calf  jump  spins,  attempt  to

spin as you jump, eventually going 360¬∞ between jumps. Do
one-leg calf jumps the same way as two-leg jumps, except
lift one leg off the ground when doing the exercise.

Rope Skipping
Rope  skipping  is  essentially  the  same  as  calf  jumping,
except that it is more vigorous. This is an excellent exercise
for  conditioning  the  non-oxidative  energy  system  and
developing jumping power, particularly in the calf muscles.
Do this exercise using either ‚Äúboxer‚Äù or playground style. In
boxer style, you use a short rope and jump by yourself. In
playground style, two people swing the rope while a third
person jumps. The boxer style is the most practical of the
two types.

A  recent  television  program  describing  the  ‚Äúnew  physical
education‚Äù showed children skipping rope with an imaginary
rope. The idea was that rope skipping damaged self-esteem
because  of  its  difficulty.  While  the  personal  trainer  might
introduce the activity with an imaginary rope, encourage the
child to learn rope-skipping skills. Self-esteem is developed
best through accomplishment. If everyone is a winner, then
there is no winner and no competition.

Good jump ropes can be purchased at almost any sporting
goods store. The best ones are made of leather with wooden
handles and ball-bearing swivels. With these, the rope turns
easily  in  the  hand  without  tangling.  Buy  one  that  fits  the
athlete‚Äîit  should  not  be  so  short  that  the  athlete  cannot

Calf Jumps

Rope Skipping

International Sports Sciences Association166  |  unit 12

turn the rope without hunching over during the exercise or
so long that it is difficult to turn.

Directions:  Hold  one  handle  in  each  hand  with  the  rope
behind you. Swing the rope over your head and jump over
it  when  it  reaches  your  feet.  Continue  swinging  the  rope
and jumping over it. Speed up the tempo as skill improves.
Begin with 5 to 10, 15-second segments and progress to 5 to
20, 1 to 3 minute segments.

As  skill  improves,  teach  the  athlete  some  of  the  many
rope-skipping  variations.  These
include  crossing  the
hands  in  front  while  jumping  and  swinging  the  rope  for
two  revolutions  between  jumps.  Vary  foot  movements  so
they  resemble  running  or  dancing.  Using  a  heavy  rope  or
wearing a weighted vest increases the conditioning effect of
rope skipping.

Squat Jumps
These  are  similar  to  calf  jumps,  except  the  jumper  bends
the knees and squats between jumps. This basic exercise is
excellent for improving jumping power and is an important
part of any plyometric program.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart with knees
bent slightly. Jump up and drive the arms upward. As you
land, retract the arms and squat down and then jump again
as quickly and explosively as possible. Do 5 to 10 repetitions
per set.

Squat Jumps

Tuck Squat Jumps
These  are  similar  to  squat  jumps,  except  more  vigorous.
Jump higher off the ground to perform the knee tuck and
still achieve a balanced landing.

Directions:  Stand  with
feet shoulder width apart
with knees bent slightly.

arms  upward.  Tuck  the
knees underneath when

squat jumps.

Mule Kick Squat Jumps

360¬∞ Squat Jumps
This  exercise  is  another  variation  of  squat  jumps  similar
to  the  360¬∞  calf  jumps  described  earlier.  It  requires  more
fitness  than  most  squat  jumps.  Start  with  45  to  90¬∞  turns
and progress to 360¬∞ turns.

Youth FitnessDirections:  Stand  with  feet  shoulder  width  apart  with
knees  bent  slightly.  Jump  up  and  drive  the  arms  upward,
spinning in the air as much as possible. On landing, retract
the  arms  and  prepare  to  jump  again.  Begin  by  rotating  in
only  one  direction.  For  the  progression,  rotate  to  the  left
on one repetition and to the right on the next. Do 5 to 10
repetitions, taking as little time as possible between jumps.
Advanced  variations  of  this  exercise  include  tuck  and
mule-kick 360s.

Jumping and Plyometrics  |  167

One-Leg Squat Jumps
Do not attempt this exercise until the person has conditioned
their legs with two-leg squat jumps. Progress slowly; if any
ankle, knee, or hip pain occurs following this exercise, cut
down on the volume or eliminate it from the program.

Directions:  Stand  on  one  leg  with  the  knee  bent  slightly.
Jump up and drive the arms upward. On landing, retract the
arms and immediately jump again. Do 5 to 10 repetitions,
taking as little time as possible between jumps.

Variations  (all  advanced):  1-leg  tuck  squat  jumps,  1-leg
mule kick squat jumps, 1-leg 360¬∞ squat jumps.

360¬∞ Squat Jumps

Pike Jumps
This  is  another  advanced  exercise  that  requires  superior
jumping and power output capacity.

Directions:  Stand  with  feet  shoulder  width  apart  with
knees bent slightly. Jump up and drive the arms upward. At
the  height  of  the  jump,  extend  the  legs  outward  in  a  split
position. On landing, retract the legs and arms and prepare
to jump again. Do 3 to 10 repetitions, taking as little time as
possible between jumps.

One-Leg Squat Jumps

Ice Skaters
This exercise is excellent for developing the thigh muscles
for  lateral  movements  and  for  stabilizing  spinal  muscles
for  dynamic  movements.  Make  sure  shoes  provide  good
traction and choose an area that provides good footing.

PIke Jumps

Ice Skaters

International Sports Sciences Association168  |  unit 12

Directions:  Stand  with  weight  on  the  inside  part  of  the
feet. Using a speed skating motion, drive off the left leg and
swing both arms to the right, then immediately drive with
the right leg to the left. Move as quickly as possible when
going from one leg to the other.

Variations: Sand Ice Skaters. Do the ice skater exercise in the
sand. This is a good way to begin doing this exercise because
the knee, hip, and ankle joints receive less stress. This method
can  also  strengthen  stabilizer  muscles.  Another  variation
uses angled boxes. Wedge shaped boxes allow better footing
during  the  push-off  phase  and  are  popular  with  skaters
and  skiers.  A  slideboard  also  can  be  used.  Slideboards  are
available  commercially  or  can  be  manufactured  cheaply
using a piece of Formica and 2 x 4 lumber.

Lunge Jumps
The  lunge  or  split  jump  builds  thigh,  gluteal,  and  back
muscles. It is an excellent exercise to develop striding power
for sprinting and lower body flexibility.

Directions:  From  a  standing  position,  jump  up  and  then
land in a split position with the right leg bent and left leg
extended behind. After landing, immediately jump up and
again  land  in  a  split  position  with  the  legs  reversed.  One
repetition  occurs  when  each  leg  has  been  in  the  forward
position. During this exercise, try to keep the body straight
and jump up as high as possible.

Lunge Jumps

horIzonTal
jumPS and hoPS
These  more  advanced  exercises  involve  jumping  and
hopping  horizontally,  which  are  excellent  for  developing
basic leg power.

Standing Long Jumps
In  addition  to  increasing  basic  leg  power,  this  is  a  good
exercise for gauging progress. Measure the athlete‚Äôs standing
long jump every two weeks. Progress is rapid and rewarding
as the athlete improves.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart and toes
just  behind  the  starting  (scratch)  line.  Bend  the  knees,
bringing  the  hands  below  waist  level,  then  jump  as  far
forward  as  you  can  by  extending  ankles,  knees,  hips,  and
arms fully.

Standing Long Jumps

Multiple Standing Long Jump
This  is  similar  to  the  last  exercise,  except  that  the  athlete
takes three jumps in succession.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart and toes
just  behind  the  starting  (scratch)  line.  Bend  the  knees,
bringing the hands below waist level, and then jump as far
forward  as  you  can  by  extending  the  ankles,  knees,  hips,
and  arms  fully.  On  landing,  try  to  jump  again  as  soon  as
possible. Repeat for three total jumps.

Standing Triple Jump
The triple jump is contested in track and field competitions.
It used to be called the ‚Äúhop, step, and jump,‚Äù which describes
its basic movements.

Directions: Stand with feet shoulder width apart and toes
just  behind  the  starting  (scratch)  line.  Bend  the  knees,

Youth FitnessStanding Triple Jump

Jumping and Plyometrics  |  169

bringing the hands below waist level, and then jump as far
forward  as  you  can  by  extending  the  ankles,  knees,  hips,
and arms fully. Land on the same leg as the starting leg of
takeoff, then step vigorously with the other leg. On landing,
immediately jump with that leg and complete the exercise.
A sequence might be to hop with the right leg, extend and
land on the left leg (step), then complete the jump with the
left leg.

Cone Hops
This exercise is similar to the multiple standing long jumps,
except try to jump for height over the cones and for distance.

Directions:  Space  three  to  six  large,  two-foot  cones  (or
similar  objects)  approximately  three  feet  apart.  Stand  in
front of the first cone with feet shoulder width apart. Jump
over the cones as quickly as possible.

Skiers
Skiers is a good exercise for alpine and cross-country skiers,
skaters, and for people who must change direction rapidly
when running.

Directions:  Stand  with  feet  together.  Jump  with  both  feet
together  forward  and  to  the  left  side;  land;  then  jump
forward and to the right. Jump as quickly as possible for 5
to 10 repetitions. One repetition includes a jump to the left
and right sides.

Skiers

Four Squares
This exercise helps build leg power for lateral movements.

Directions: Stand with feet together on the inside of four
markers  (small  cones,  shirts,  shoes)  placed  approximately
five  feet  apart.  Keeping  the  feet  together,  jump  in  various
patterns  in  the  direction  of  the  markers.  For  example,  if
the  markers  are  numbered  1,  2,  3,  4,  a  jumping  sequence
might  be:  1,  center,  2,  center,  3,  center,  4,  center.  Another
combination  might  be:  center,  1,  2,  4,  3,  center.  Many
combinations are possible.

Cone Hops

Hurdle Hops
This is an advanced form of cone hopping using hurdles. Do
not  attempt  this  exercise  unless  you  are  well‚Äìconditioned
and have good jumping ability and technique. If the hurdles
have adjustable stabilization weights, make sure they are set
so that the hurdle falls down easily if hit. Construct mini-
hurdles from PVC pipe, as these are safer for the beginner.

Directions: Place three to five hurdles approximately three
feet  apart.  Start  with  the  hurdle  at  its  lowest  height.  Keep
feet shoulder width apart, and hop over the hurdles quickly
with both legs.

Hurdle Hops

bounce PuSh-uPS
Perform  bounce  push-ups  against  a  wall,  steeplechase
hurdle,  or  on  the  floor. These  are  excellent  for  developing
upper body pushing power.

Wall Bounce Push-ups
These  are  the  simplest,  least  stressful  push-ups.  Start  with
these  until  the  muscles  and  joints  become  accustomed  to
the stress of upper body plyometrics.

Directions:  Lean  against  a  wall  or  steeplechase  hurdle
at  a  45  to  60-degree  angle.  Push  up  forcefully,  then  allow
yourself to go back against with the arms absorbing the fall.

Floor Bounce Push-ups

off in a modified push-up position and graduate to the full
push-up position when you gain the necessary strength and
power to complete the exercise.

box jumPIng
Box jumping involves jumping to and from boxes, benches,
or steps. Landing imparts much more stress to the muscles
and joints, so these exercises should only be attempted after
doing  exercises  where  you  jump  to  and  from  the  ground.
Box height varies from approximately six inches to five feet.
Start off with smaller boxes and progress slowly to higher
ones.  As  with  other  plyometric  exercises,  the  object  is  to
jump as soon as possible after landing.

Step Downs
This is the simplest, least stressful box jump. Begin with a
low box approximately one to two feet high. This exercise
progresses  from  simply  stepping  down  from  the  box  and
absorbing the shock with the legs to jumping down, landing,
then vigorously jumping into the air. Finally, jump between
a series of boxes.

Directions: Phase 1: Step down. Stand on a box or bench
with feet shoulder width apart, knees bent, and spine erect.
Step off the box and land with knees bent.

Phase 2: Step down, jump up: Stand on a box or bench with
feet at shoulder width, knees bent, and spine erect. Step off
the bench and land with bent knees, then immediately jump
up using both legs and arms.

Wall Bounce Push-ups

Floor Bounce Push-ups
These are much more stressful and difficult than wall push-
ups. Do not attempt these until you can do at least 10 to 15
push-ups. Perform these from a regular or modified push-
up  position.  In  the  modified  push-up  position,  rest  your
weight on the knees instead of toes.

Directions: From a standard or modified push-up position,
push  up  forcefully,  extending  the  elbows  fully  until  your
hands leave the ground. Bounce back to your hands, then
repeat the exercise.

Variation: ‚ÄúClap‚Äù bounce push-ups (Marine push-ups). Do
this exercise as described above, except clap your hands after
your hands leave the ground. In this sequence, you push up
and leave the ground, clap your hands, bounce back to the
push-up position, and repeat.

Variation: ‚ÄúRocky Balboa‚Äù one-arm push-ups. This difficult
one arm exercise was popularized in the movie Rocky. Do
it the same way as bounce push-ups described above. Start

Ski Box Jumps
This exercise, similar to ‚Äúskiers‚Äù described earlier and occurs
when you jump to and from a box as you jump side-to-side.

Directions:  Stand  sideways  to  a  box  or  bench.  With  feet
together, jump up vigorously onto the box, then immediately
jump to the ground on the other side, then jump back to the
box, then jump back to the starting position, and so on.

Step Downs

Phase 3: Repeat the step down, jump up: Place three to six
boxes or benches approximately three feet apart. Jump from
the first box to the ground, then up to the next box, then
to  the  ground,  and  so  forth.  Jump  as  quickly  as  possible
between boxes.

Standing Long Jumps from a Box
This is similar to the standing long jumps described earlier,
except that it stresses your legs much more when you land.
This exercise is sometimes called ‚Äúdepth jumping.‚Äù

Directions:  Stand  on  a  box  or  bench  with  feet  placed
shoulder  width  apart.  Jump  as  far  forward  as  possible,
extending  fully  at  the  ankles,  knees,  hips,  and  arms.  Land
with  bent  knees.  Increase  box  height  as  fitness  improves.
A variation of this exercise is to land on the ground, then
standing long jump again immediately.

Ski Box Jumps

Single Leg Jump-ups
This  exercise  isolates  the  powerful  thigh  muscles  and
develops jumping power for activities that require a single
leg take-off (i.e., a lay-up in basketball or jumping for a ball
when running).

Directions: Stand on one leg with knee bent slightly. Drive
hard with the leg and extend fully at the ankle, knee, and hip
and jump into the air, land, and then repeat immediately for
a set number of repetitions. Repeat with the other leg.

Standing Long Jumps from a Box

medIcIne ball exercISeS
A medicine ball resembles a basketball in size but is heavier
and softer and is usually made of leather. Newer medicine
balls  are  sometimes  made  of  rubber,  and  some  even  have
handles.  Medicine  balls  weigh  between  2  to  20  pounds.
Because  of  their  weight,  medicine  balls  are  excellent  for
plyometrics.  When  you  catch  medicine  balls,  the  muscles
stretch  and  contract  eccentrically  as  you  slow  down  and
control the ball. You can perform medicine ball exercises by
yourself or with a partner.

Play Catch with Yourself
Do  this  exercise  with  the  ball  starting  from  your  chest,
behind the neck, or at the waist. These are excellent whole
body exercises because you must engage the legs, arms, and
trunk to perform them properly.

Directions:  Chest-high  catch:  Stand,  feet  shoulder  width
apart,  holding  the  ball  with  both  hands  at  chest  level.
Vigorously press the ball overhead with both hands until it
flies  into  the  air  straight  above  you.  Use  your  legs  to  help
push the ball overhead. Catch the ball with both hands, then
immediately throw the ball into the air again. Repeat.

Directions:  Behind-the-neck  catch:  Stand  with
feet
shoulder  width  apart  and  hold  the  ball  with  both  hands
behind your head. Vigorously press the ball overhead with
both hands until it flies into the air straight above you. Use
your  legs  to  help  push  the  ball  overhead.  Catch  the  ball
behind your head with both hands, then immediately throw
the ball into the air again. Repeat. Do not do this exercise
if you have existing shoulder problems. Instead, start with
a two to five-pound medicine ball before
progressing to a heavier one.

Directions:  Waist-high  catch:  Stand
with  feet  shoulder  width  apart  and  place
your  hands  under  the  ball  at  waist  level.
Vigorously  pull  the  ball  overhead  with
both hands until it flies into the air straight
above you. Use your legs to help push the
ball  overhead.  Catch  the  ball  with  both
hands,  then  immediately  throw  the  ball
into the air again. Repeat. As you become
accustomed to this exercise, jump into the
air  as  you  throw  the  ball,  land,  catch  the
ball, and repeat.

Play Catch with Yourself

Medicine Ball or Shot Put Throws
You  also  can  develop  power  by  throwing  a  medicine  ball
or  shot  put  in  various  ways.  Exercises  include  overhead,
underhand, and side-rotation throws.

Directions: Overhead throw: The exercise motion is similar
to  the  underhand  catch  described  previously,  except  that
you throw a medicine ball or shotput over your head behind
you. Throw  the  object  as  far  overhead  as  possible.  Extend
fully with the ankles, knees, hips, and arms, and jump from
the ground while releasing the ball.

Directions: Front-waist throw: This is also an underhand
throw initiated at the waist, except that you throw the ball or
shot put it in front of you. You can also throw the ball from
your waist in front of you or rotating to the side.

Directions: Waist throw to the side: Hold the ball or shot
in both hands. Rotate to the right then to the left and throw
the object as far as possible. Transfer your weight from the
rear to the front foot during the throw. Repeat the exercise
on the other side of the body.

medIcIne ball exercISeS
wITh a ParTner
These exercises, excellent for developing power in the upper
and lower body, have many variations limited only by your
imagination.  Try  changing  up  your  routines  to  keep  it
interesting and keep kids motivated.

Chest Passes
This  exercise  is  excellent  for  developing  the  upper  body
pushing  muscles,  including  the  muscles  of  the  trunk  and
lower body.

Directions: Stand with one foot in front of the other, with
knees bent slightly, approximately 6 to 10 feet from a partner.
Hold  the  ball  in  both  hands  at  chest  level  and  throw  it  to
your  partner  using  a  motion  similar  to  a  basketball  chest
pass.  Your  partner  should  catch  the  ball  and  immediately
throw  the  ball  back  to  you.  The  catching  motion  should
blend  continuously  with  the  throwing  motion  in  a  semi-
circular pattern. You also can perform this exercise from a
kneeling position to isolate upper body muscles.

Youth FitnessJumping and Plyometrics  |  173

Medicine Ball Sit-ups
Two athletes can do this together, or one person can do it
alone with a spotter. Do not attempt this exercise until you
have  conditioned  your  abdominal  and  back  muscles  with
standard sit-ups, crunches, and back extensions.

Directions: Two-person sit-ups: Sit facing each other with
knees bent, feet flat on the floor, and one person holding the
medicine ball. Both people should go backward with hands
overhead until their backs touch the ground, then both sit
up  at  the  same  time.  As  the  person  with  the  ball  reaches
the top of the sit-up, toss the ball to the other person who
then catches it. Repeat the exercise by tossing the ball to the
other person at the top of each sit-up. The sequence for each
person is as follows: Sit-up with the ball, throw the ball to
partner, sit-up without the ball, and catch the ball from the
partner, then repeat.

Directions: One person with spotter: This is similar to the
two-person sit-ups, except a spotter or coach stands three
to  five  feet  from  the  person  doing  the  sit-ups.  At  the  top
of  the  sit-up,  the  person  tosses  the  ball  to  the  spotter  and
then completes a sit-up without the medicine ball. He or she
returns to the top of the sit-up and catches the ball thrown
by  the  spotter. The  sequence  is  as  follows:  Sit-up  with  the
ball, throw the ball to spotter, sit-up without the ball, catch
the ball from spotter, then repeat.

Chest Passes

Overhead Passes
This  excellent  exercise  develops  power  in  the  triceps  and
shoulder muscles. Do not perform this exercise if you have
any type of shoulder injury.

Directions: Kneel facing your partner, approximately 6 to
10 feet away. Hold the ball in both hands behind your head.
Throw  the  ball  forward  over  your  head  so  your  partner
catches it with arms extended overhead. Your partner then
retracts the ball overhead and throws it back to you.

Overhead Passes

Medicine Ball Sit-ups

International Sports Sciences Association174  |  unit 12

SuMMARY

Jumping exercises and plyometrics enhance performance in strength-speed
sports because they increase leg power and train the nervous system to activate
large muscle groups quickly during movement. Plyometric exercises involve
rapid stretching then shortening of a muscle group during highly dynamic
movements (i.e., stretch-shortening cycles). Stretching causes a stretch reflex
and elastic recoil in the muscles, which combined with a vigorous muscle
contraction, creates great force that overloads the muscles, increases strength
and power, enhances the strength of connective tissues that promote muscle
elasticity, and improves tendon strength. The basic principle for all jumping and
plyometric exercises involves absorbing the shock with the arms or legs and
then immediately contracting the muscles. Jumping and plyometric exercises
can cause great stress to the muscles, bones, and joints. Start with simple exer-
cises before progressing to movements that place more stress on the muscles
and joints. More advanced exercises progress to repeated distance jumps and
then to box jumps using two legs and then one leg.

uNIT 13

# Power And Speed

176  |  unit 13

Key Terms

power

skill

agility

olympic weight lifting

power: Work per unit of time.
From a practical standpoint,
defined as ability to exert force
rapidly.

Children develop less power in sports and have a lower capacity for power development
than  adults  because  they  have  less  muscle  mass  and  are  neurologically  immature.
Nevertheless, research studies have shown that children as young as six can build strength
and power through training, which will help them perform better in sports. Also, they
can learn techniques to help them throughout their athletic careers.

In sports, power is the ability to produce force rapidly. It is the most important fitness
component in high intensity sports such as tennis, football, soccer, basketball, most track
and field events, field hockey, ice hockey, downhill skiing, golf, lacrosse, volleyball, and
baseball. People move quickly and forcefully in these sports. How hard you strike a ball,
how fast you move on the court or field, or how rapidly and precisely you apply force to
the edge of the ski dictates the quality of performance.

Power  is  also  important  for  distance  running,  cycling,  swimming,  and  cross-country
skiing.  Endurance  refers  to  the  capacity  to  sustain  a  given  exercise  intensity.  Except
perhaps for marathon dancing (an event popular during the 1930s where you danced ‚Äútill
you dropped‚Äù), there are no purely endurance events. Endurance sports are contested
over  finite  distances. The  winner  simply  completes  the  distance  in  the  fastest  time.  In
other words, the winner represents the most powerful person over the contested distance.
While endurance is obviously important in these events, athletes require power‚Äîeven in
endurance sports.

This section describes exercises to develop speed and power for gross movements such
as sprinting and changing directions on the court or field. These exercises compliment
plyometric exercises that also help to develop speed and power in large lower and upper
body muscle groups.

# Elements Of Power
Power in sports depends on five main factors:

1.  genetics

2.  metabolic capacity

3.  muscle size

4.

skill

5.  nervous system capacity

With the exception of genetics, the athlete or client‚Äôs training program must address each
factor to maximize power for sport.

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  177

geneTIcS
Great  athletes  such  as  Alex  Rodriguez  (baseball),  Michael
Jordan and LeBron James (basketball), Tiger Woods (golf),
Usain Bolt (sprinting), and Mohammed Ali (boxing), were
born with the capacity to produce great power when they
moved. They are the ‚Äúgeniuses‚Äù of the sports world. Just as
giants in other disciplines, such as Einstein, Beethoven, and
Newton,  were  born  with  great  intellect,  superstar  athletes
are  born  with  a  gift  for  generating  power  on  the  playing
field.  They  had  to  train  hard  to  become  stars,  but  they
started from a much higher level than the rest of us. Average
people cannot expect to equal the physical prowess of these
superstars, no matter how hard they train. However, almost
everyone can improve performance in strength-speed sports
by systematically developing the components of power.

meTabolIc caPacITY
We  have  already  discussed  the  metabolic  basis  of  exercise
in  Unit  2  of  this  course.  To  summarize,  each  sport  relies
primarily on one of the three energy systems‚Äîimmediate,
non-oxidative,  or  oxidative.  While  these  energy  systems
are  important  to  sustain  life,  specific  sports  tend  to  rely
on one system more than the others. For example, weight
lifters  rely  primarily  on  the  immediate  system;  400-meter
sprinters  rely  primarily  on  the  non-oxidative  system;  and
marathon  runners  rely  primarily  on  the  oxidative  system.
To  maximize  power,  one  must  develop  the  energy  system
that sustains movement in a particular sport. This is one of
the  major  tenants  of  the  specificity  of  metabolic  capacity
related to specific sports.

muScle SIze
For  more  than  150  years,  athletes  have  practiced  strength
training  to  make  them  more  powerful.  Today‚Äôs  athletes
are much bigger and stronger than those of 40 to 50 years
ago, largely because of weight training. In the 1950s, it was
common  to  see  200-pound  linemen  in  football.  Compare
that  with  average  weights  of  over  320  pounds  on  today‚Äôs
teams. Many of today‚Äôs baseball players have forearms like
Popeye,  while  pre-1960  players  usually  had  very  ordinary
looking muscular builds.

In women‚Äôs popular sports such as swimming, track and field,
softball, field hockey, softball, volleyball, alpine skiing, and
basketball, extensive weight training has produced athletes
with  well-developed  muscles.  Performance  differences
between men and women are due largely to differences in
power output capacity. Weight training has helped women
to narrow the gender gap in many sports. Children cannot
develop  power  as  well  as  adults  can,  but  they  can  make
significant progress. They can develop proper sports skills,
which are vital for powerful performance.

A  muscle‚Äôs  size  largely  determines  how  much  force  it  can
develop.  Larger  muscles  have  more  tissue  to  contract,  so
they exert more force. In sports, the rate at which muscles
exert force remains more important than the absolute force
in determining sports performance. While weight training
is  important  for  developing  power  for  sports,  it  will  not
be  effective  unless  one  learns  to  use  the  newly  acquired
strength  effectively  in  the  sport.  Effective  strength  (i.e.,
usable strength in the sport) is determined by how well the
nervous  system  controls  the  muscles  and  by  the  skill  and
efficiency of movement.

International Sports Sciences Association178  |  unit 13

skill: Ability to perform a
discrete motor task.

SKIll
Skilled performers make it look easy. They exhibit an economy of effort with little wasted
motion. They concentrate all their forces into their movements and perform seemingly
flawless actions. The power produced by an NBA superstar dunking the ball, an Olympic
discus thrower throwing over 70 meters, or a world-class ballet dancer soaring through
the air seems beyond human capacity. These feats are possible because these athletes can
precisely channel their incredible power into skilled movements.

Skill is the capacity to perform a specific movement. Skilled movements are orchestrated
by the nervous system so that body positions and muscle contractions occur in precise
sequences  and  speeds.  Practice  and  training  reinforce  correct  movements  to  produce
better, more skillful performances. At the same time, the object is to eliminate inefficient
or incorrect movements.

Skill is a prerequisite to successful performance in sports regardless of physical condition.
An individual cannot become effective in a sport without a certain amount of skill. A
highly skilled Olympic gymnast, for example, would do poorly on the golf course unless
they developed a mechanically sound and efficient golf swing. Gymnasts would need to
learn how to coordinate their upper and lower body movements during the golf swing
in  the  same  way  they  did  when  mastering  movements  on  the  balance  beam  or  rings.
Athletes in any skilled sport must direct most of their energies toward developing skill.
A basic premise of this course is that practicing the skill must never take a back seat to
general physical conditioning.

Many athletes mistakenly emphasize weight training at the expense of skill development.
Strength gained in the weight room transfers slowly to the playing field. In fact, one can
increase strength by 20 to 30 percent in the weight room without improvement in power
in a sports movement. Only if one carefully practices the desired skill will movements
become more powerful. Powerful, skilled movements depend on purposeful skill practice
and conditioning.

Skill can overcome physical and conditioning deficiencies. Proper body positions, use of
leverage, and timing can produce great force. Athletes are less likely to become injured
when  they  move  skillfully.  Skilled  movements  depend  on  good  movement  postures,
which place less stress on the bones, muscles, and joints.

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  179

Technology and Skill
We live in a marvelous age for honing sports skills. Even the
average person can use videotape and computers to improve
technique in any motion, even without a coach.

The procedure is simple. All one needs is a home computer
with  video  capture  and  a  video  camera.  Video  transfer
capability  is  now  standard  on  home  computers  (e.g.,
iMovie).  Download  a  computer  video  clip  of  a  sports
movement from the Internet. These are widely available for
any sport in both Macintosh and Windows formats. Take a
video of the client or athlete performing a skilled movement
(examples include hitting a tennis forehand, hitting a driver
in golf, sprinting, or crushing a softball). Transfer the video
into the computer. Compare your technique with that of the
expert.  A  knowledgeable  coach  can  detect  and  emphasize
important points.

nervouS SYSTem caPacITY
The  nervous  system‚Äôs  ability  to  recruit  or  activate  motor
units  (muscle  fibers  plus  a  motor  nerve)  is  critical  for
generating  power.  Scientists  have  discovered  that  motor
units are trained only if they are recruited during practice.
The large, powerful motor units are only recruited during

maximum,  powerful  movements.  To  train  those  motor
units, one must perform maximum, explosive movements
during the exercise sessions.

High  resistance  weight  training  helps  to  recruit  these
difficult-to-train  motor  units.  Doing  high  speed  training
exercises  and  plyometrics  overloads  the  motor  units  that
make  individuals  run  fast,  jump  high,  and  throw  far.  Not
only are these ‚Äúperformance‚Äù motor units turned on, they
are  also  activated  longer  to  be  able  to  sustain  powerful
movements in sports.

As  with  strength  training,  plyometric  and  speed  exercises
are only effective for improving sports performance if they
are accompanied by a mimicking of the skill during practice.
Research studies show that simultaneously practicing speed
and  plyometric  exercises  and  the  skill  produces  rapid
improvement  in  sports  performance.  Strength  training
takes  much  longer  to  transfer  to  skilled  movement.  In
contrast,  weight  training  and  speed-plyometric  training
both  eventually  produce  more  powerful  movements  if
integrated into a long-term, systematic program.

This  section  of  the  course  describes  basic  speed  and
power  exercises.  Combined  with  weight  training,  general
conditioning, and skill development, these exercises almost
assure improvement in sports performance.

International Sports Sciences Association180  |  unit 13

SPRINTINg
In  sprinting,  peak  power  occurs  during  the  first  20  to  30
meters.  One  can  only  sustain  peak  power  for  about  3
seconds before it drops off. In a sprint 100 or 200-meter run,
the winner is the person with the highest peak power output
and the least slow-down at the end of the race (power and
speed-endurance).

In  most  power  sports,  critical  movements  last  only  a  few
seconds but require great power. For example, in football,
plays  seldom  last  more  than  three  seconds.  In  tennis,  you
move from a ‚Äúready‚Äù position, set up for the shot, and then
execute  the  stroke.  Again,  these  critical  movements  take
only  a  few  seconds.  In  skiing,  power  applications  occur
when setting the edge. The remainder of the turn involves
unweighting  and  setting  up  for  the  next  turn.  Highly
skilled baseball and softball players exert great force when
hitting  the  ball,  base  running,  or  making  a  play  in  the
field. Movements in these sports are maximum and highly
explosive for a few seconds. The rest of the time, the players
are largely inactive.

When developing power for sports, one needs to enhance
the capacity to accelerate from a stationary position to full
speed in a short time while changing directions rapidly. The
athlete can build these capacities with a proper training and
conditioning program.

Technique  is  critical  for  powerful  movement.  When
sprinting,  either  laterally  or  in  a  straight  line,  powerful

movements  depend  on  getting  your  power  leg  under  you
(i.e., cut or claw foot) and on full extension of your legs and
hips. The large muscles in the front and back of the thighs
and hips are the strongest in the body. The athlete must use
these muscles to their full capacity for sprinting, moving to
hit a tennis ball, or jumping for a basketball.

If  you  train  your  athletes  to  move  efficiently  and  extend
fully during powerful movements, they will run faster and
jump  higher.  Be  systematic‚Äîanalyze  their  motions  and
change them systematically using videotape and computer
analysis. These insights are no longer limited to the research
lab. Computerized performance systems can turn you into a
space-age sports scientist.

Sprint Starts, Running
Sprint  starts  are  excellent  for  developing  power  and
acceleration capacity in the legs. The photo sequence shows
proper sprint starting technique. The athlete in this sequence
is a world-class sprinter. Notice the incredible extension he
gets as he bursts from the blocks. Training to develop this
kind of ‚Äúexplosive‚Äù strength may carry over to other sports
that require power and speed.

Use starting blocks when working on sprint starts, although
they  are  not  absolutely  necessary.  Football  players  should
do sprint starts from the football stance. Tennis, soccer, or

Sprint Starts, Running

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  181

baseball players should do starts from the ready position in
their sport. The ready position is the waiting position before
initiating a powerful first movement.

Directions:  ‚ÄúOn  your  marks  position‚Äù:  Your  feet  are
staggered 10 to 14 inches apart, with your front foot placed
approximately 20 inches from the starting line. Try to relax
in this position.

‚ÄúSet  position‚Äù:  Raise  your  back  and  hips.  Your  front  leg
bends  about  90¬∞  and  your  rear  leg  120¬∞.  Right-handed
people generally start with their left foot forward. Your back
is flat, and your hips are slightly higher than your shoulders.
You contact the ground with your fingertips so you can raise
your  shoulders  as  high  as  possible.  Do  not  put  too  much
weight on the fingertips.

‚ÄúGo‚Äù: Raise your shoulder so you can direct force with your
front driving leg through the length of your body. Drive the
front leg fully so the body forms a straight line from your hip
to your heal. Push your rear foot hard against the block or
ground as you powerfully drive your knee forward. As you
drive forward, the arm on the side of the front foot drives
straight  forward,  while  the  rear  foot  arm  drives  straight
backward. Both arms are bent approximately 90 to 110¬∞. Try
to extend fully with your hips and knees and use your arms
dynamically  during  the  driving  phases  of  the  movement.
After the start, run as fast as possible for three to five strides.

Start  off  with  three  sprints  starts  and  progress  to  10  to  20
starts  as  fitness  increases.  After  two  weeks,  have  someone
time your starts to gauge your progress. Also, videotape your
starts to insure you are extending fully at the hips and knees.

Sprint Starts, Swimming
Swimmers can develop acceleration capacity by working on
starts.  Starts  are  beneficial  for  those  who  want  to  develop
basic  leg  power  but  also  want  to  increase  variety  in  their
training program.

Modern  starting  technique  uses  an  arc  dive  to  enter  the
water.  The  swimmer  extends  the  body  up  and  out.  At  the
top of the arc, swimmers bend at the waist so they enter the
water at relatively steep trajectories. This technique allows
the swimmer to travel further over the water and enter the
pool more cleanly than using a flat dive technique. Serious
swimmers use the arc dive technique. However, it does not
matter which technique is used when swimming for general
fitness conditioning.

Directions:  Start  from  the  side  of  the  pool  or  from  a
swimming  starting  block.  Grasp  the  front  of  the  block  or
side  of  the  pool  with  head  down  and  knees  bent  slightly.
Pull against the block or poolside as you lift your head and

Sprint Starts, Swimming

flex  your  knees.  Drive  your  body  and  arms  upward  and
outward, fully extending the hips, knees, ankles. If doing an
arc dive, pike at the waist as you reach the peak of the dive.
In the flat dive, continue to extend fully with the hips, knees,
ankles, and shoulders. Enter the water as cleanly as possible
by  attempting  to  make  the  body  enter  the  same  ‚Äúhole‚Äù  in
the water.

As with sprinting, do no more than three to five strokes after
hitting the water. Start with three to five starts and progress
to 15 to 20 as fitness improves.

Short Sprints (running)
Shorts  sprints,  ranging  from  20  to  50  meters  help  to
develop  lower  body  power  and  acceleration  for  improved
performance in most strength-speed sports. As with sprint
starts, emphasize full extension of the knee and hip during
the  driving  phase  of  the  sprint  movement.  Powerful  arm
movement  is  critical  to  fast  sprinting.  Drive  the  arms
forcefully  in  the  direction  of  movement.  Do  not  flail  the
arms to the side.

International Sports Sciences Association182  |  unit 13

Short Sprints

Directions:  Start  with  longer
distances of 50 to 100 meters at
less  than  top  speed.  As  fitness
improves, increase speed until
you  can  sprint  at  100  percent
effort. Discontinue the exercise
if  you  feel  any  pain  or  muscle
cramping. Begin with three to
five  repetitions  with  30  to  60
seconds  rest  between  sprints;
gradually  increase  to  10  to  20
repetitions.

Proper  technique  helps  the
person  run  faster  and  with
more power. Try to extend the
hips  fully  during  the  push-off
phase  of  the  sprint.  Vigorous
arm action also helps one move
faster and more powerfully.

Lateral  sprinting:  Lateral  sprint  movements  are  excellent
for preparation for field sports such as football, soccer, and
field  hockey.  They  also  improve  power  in  sports  such  as
tennis, racquetball, basketball, and volleyball.

Using cones, shoes, or shirts, set up a zigzag course where
you run straight for five yards, run left at a 45¬∞ angle for five
yards, then run right at a 45¬∞ angle. Another variation is to
place two cone or shoe markers 10 yards apart. Sprint from
the first marker to the second, touch it, and then sprint back
to the first marker.

Backward sprinting: This is an excellent exercise for those
who  must  occasionally  sprint  backward  in  their  sport,
athletes such as defensive backs in football, basketball, and
soccer. Backward sprinting develops hamstring and gluteal
power.  Run  distances  of  10  to  50  yards  and  progress  in  a
manner similar to that of the short sprint exercises.

Downhill Sprinting
(2 to 3 percent grade)
Sprinting  downhill,  using  a  mild  downgrade,  allows  you
to  increase  your  running  speed  and  thus  overload  your
fast-twitch  motor  units.  This  technique  can  improve  your
sprint speed rapidly. The Dallas Cowboy football team has
a downhill track made of Tartan (expensive material used
in  all-weather  tracks)  for  developing  sprint  speed  in  their
athletes.

Directions:  Find  a  small  hill  that  declines  about  2  to  3
degrees. A football field with a steep crown (higher in the
middle than on the sides) is a good choice. Sprint 3 to 10

repetitions  at  distances  ranging  from  20  to  40  yards.  Do
not sprint down a steep hill (> 5¬∞) because you risk injury
and much of your energy will be devoted to keeping your
balance rather than running fast.

Speed Parachute
Speed  parachutes  also  overload  fast-twitch  motor  units
during  high-speed  sprinting.  Russian  sprint  coaches
developed  this  device.  You  can  purchase  power-training
parachutes at track and field supply companies and at some
sporting goods stores.

Directions:  Attach  one  to  three  parachutes  to  the  waist
belt. Start with one and add chutes as condition improves.
As  with  the  short  sprints,  run  20  to  50  yards  for  3  to  15
repetitions.  Do  not  try  parachute  exercises  until  you  have
first run short sprints for two to three weeks.

A useful technique is to run 10 to 20 yards with the chutes,
then release the chutes. This causes you to suddenly surge
forward, which overloads the larger fast-twitch motor units.

Harness Sprinting
A variation to the parachute, this involves wearing a harness
around  the  waist  and  pulling  a  weight  sled,  truck  tire,  or
another person who provides resistance. Vary the speed and
resistance‚Äîthe  more  resistance,  the  slower  the  speed  and
vice versa.

Harness Sprinting

Low Hurdles
Low, 32-inch hurdles are useful for developing sprint speed
and jumping power. Most high schools and colleges leave
their hurdles on or near the track, at least during the winter
and  spring  track  season.  If  hurdles  are  unavailable,  use
large cones or picnic benches. One can even use imaginary
hurdles. If using hurdle substitutes, make sure they topple
over when hit.

Directions:  Set  up  three  to  four  hurdles,  preferably  on
grass, 9.3 yards (8.5 meters) apart. The starting point is 15

Youth Fitnessyards  (13.72  meters)  from  the  first  hurdle.  Make  sure  the
hurdle supports face you; you want the hurdle to fall over if
you hit it. In hurdling, you want to stride rather than jump
over the hurdle. The take-off distance should be far enough
away  from  the  hurdle  so  the  leading  leg  sweeps  forward
and  upward  in  a  straight  line.  Jumping  too  close  to  the
hurdle causes you to jump over the hurdle. You will strike
the hurdle if the distance is too long. The stride across the
hurdle  must  follow  the  normal  sprinting  stride  smoothly.
This takes confidence, which only comes from practice.

The  hurdling  movement  starts  with  a  quick  forward  and
upward thrust of the leading leg toward the hurdle. As you
clear the hurdle, actively press your leg toward the ground.
The trailing leg extends fully as you stride toward the hurdle.
Once  you  clear  the  hurdle,  bend  your  knee  and  stretch  it
away  from  your  body.  Extend  your  opposite  arm  forward
for balance as you go over the hurdle. Try to take only three
strides between hurdles.

Start  off  with  only  one  hurdle.  When  you  hurdle  it  with
confidence, add more hurdles. When you can run the entire
three  to  four  hurdle  course,  move  to  three  to  four  sets  of
hurdles,  gradually  increasing  your  speed.  As  you  become
more proficient, run 6 to 10 sets of hurdles. Time your runs
to gauge progress.

Stadium Stairs
Running stairs is a ‚Äútried-and-true‚Äù technique for developing
leg power. The local football stadium is a great place to do
this  exercise  because  it  overloads  your  body  during  the
sprinting motion.

Directions:  Find  unobstructed  stairs  to  support  your
weight. Beware of old wooden football stadiums seats that
could  collapse  when  you  run  on  them.  The  number  of
repetitions will vary with the size of the stadium. Running
up the stadium stairs at a major university‚Äôs football stadium
will be much more difficult than at the local high school. As
with other kinds of sprint exercises, start conservatively and
build up the intensity and duration as fitness improves.

Variations  of  stadium  stair  running  include  running  two
steps  at  a  time,  hopping  up  the  stairs  on  one  or  two  legs,
and hopping up the stairs using a side-to-side motion. The
latter is a good exercise for alpine skiers. Advanced athletes
can increase the resistance by doing stadium stair exercises
while wearing a weighted vest. These are available at sporting
goods stores and through track and field supply catalogs.

Be  careful  when  running  or  hopping  up  stadium  stairs,
particularly  when  going  down  the  stairs.  You  can  easily
lose your balance and get seriously injured. Have the client

Power and Speed  |  183

stop if they lose their balance or equilibrium. In addition,
this  exercise  may  not  be  a  good  idea  for  individuals  with
kneecap  pain.  Stair  climbing,  particularly  going  down
stairs, produces considerable pressure on kneecaps. When
experiencing  kneecap  pain  the  next  day  or  several  hours
after  running  stadium  stairs,  the  athlete  should  cut  down
on  training  volume  or  eliminate  the  exercises  from  the
prescribed program.

Stair  climbing  machines,  while  excellent  for  developing
cardiovascular  endurance,  are  less  effective  than  running
stadium  stairs  for  developing  lower  body  power.  Doing
stadium  stairs  forces  the  legs  to  extend  vigorously  during
the push-off phase of the running stride and absorb shock
when landing. This develops dynamic strength in the legs,
which is not possible using stair climbing machines, because
it requires minimal stride length and little or no impact.

Stadium Stairs

High-knees, Fast Arms
This  exercise  is  excellent  for  developing  sprint  power.
It  helps  to  increase  stride  frequency,  one  of  two  factors
determining  sprint  speed  (the  other  being  stride  length).
Minimize impact by doing this exercise on a grass field or
wooden gym floor. This is a maximum intensity exercise. It
is better to do one set of 10 seconds at maximum effort than
10  sets  of  20  seconds  at  50  percent  effort.  This  exercise  is
effective only when performed at maximum intensity.

International Sports Sciences Associationpower  in  sports  provided  skill  practice  also  occurs.  This
type of training is extremely high intensity and has a higher
risk of injury. Also, it is easy to overtrain. Overtraining is an
imbalance between training and recovery. Athletes become
overtrained  when  they  do  not  get  enough  rest  between
workouts or the workouts are too hard.

Start gradually and progress slowly. Choose one or two of
these  exercises  and  add  more  as  the  athlete  gets  in  better
shape. Gauge the appropriateness of workouts by how clients
feel the next day. If they are extremely sore for one or two
days after a power workout, then they have done too much.
In other words, teach children to listen to their bodies.

Peak Power Training on the
Stationary Bicycle (Cycle Ergometer)
The stationary bicycle is excellent for developing leg power.
By training at peak power output, one develops ‚Äúexplosive‚Äù
leg strength that will transfer to other sports. Also, it builds
the energy systems important in power sports.

Power  on  a  stationary  bicycle  combines  pedal  revolutions
(rpm) and resistance (i.e., frictional resistance measured in
kiloponds  or  kps).  Achieving  peak  power  output  requires
an optimum combination of resistance (kps) and leg speed
(rpms).  Too  high  a  resistance  decreases  power  output
because the rpms will be low. Likewise, if the resistance is
too low, power will also be low because of limited capacity
to increase pedaling speed.

Determining  Optimal  Frictional  Resistance
for
Stationary  Bicycling  Power  Training  Find  a  bicycle
ergometer  that  allows  you  to  adjust  the  frictional
resistance (examples include Monark and Tunturi cycle
ergometers). Set the frictional resistance at 1 kp. Warm-up
by  pedaling  at  an  easy  pace  for  approximately  2  minutes.

184  |  unit 13

Directions: Simulate a sprint motion in a nearly stationary
position,  and  pump  the  arms  and  lift  the  knees  as  fast  as
possible. Try to complete 20 strides in only 10 yards. Begin
with three repetitions (3 sets of 10 yards). Gradually progress
to 10 to 20 repetitions.

High Knees and Fast Arms

Bounding Strides
This exercise helps build stride length for sprinting. Rather
than take many strides in a short distance (as in the high-
knee, fast arms exercise), attempt to take as few strides as
possible over a longer distance.

Directions:  Do  this  exercise  on  a  grass  field  or  running
track over a distance of 50 to 100 yards. Stride as long as
possible, moving the arms vigorously in synchrony with the
legs. Strides should resemble bounding jumps. Begin with
two to three repetitions and progress to 10 repetitions.

POWER TRAININg
Power training requires the athlete to exercise at maximum
intensity.  This  is  the  only  way  to  overload  the  large,  fast
motor  units.  This  training  is  very  effective  and  improves

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  185

Then, ride as fast as possible for 30 seconds and count the
total  number  of  pedal  revolutions.  Every  time  the  right
leg  extends,  count  as  one  pedal  revolution.  Multiply  the
pedal revolutions by 1; the result is the power index. Rest
for 5 minutes, and then repeat the procedure using 2 kps.
Multiply the pedal revolutions by 2. Rest for five minutes,
and  repeat  using  3  kps.  Repeat  the  procedure  until  the

Select the kp setting that produces the highest power rating.

Use  this  technique  to  develop  power  for  time  intervals
ranging  from  10  seconds  to  10  minutes.  The  principle
remains the same. Count pedal revolutions for the desired

exercise time. The longer the time interval, the lower the kp
setting. For longer time intervals, the goal is to maintain a
given power output (Table 13.1).

Peak Power Weight Training
One can use the peak power training technique with weight
training,  as  well.  The  principle  remains  the  same  as  for
stationary  cycling.  Calculate  your  peak  power  output  for
five repetitions of an exercise. In general, use a weight that
is 50 to 60 percent of the maximum weight you can lift for
one repetition. Time how long it takes you to complete five
repetitions  with  good  form.  Perform  the  lift  as  rapidly  as
possible.  It  is  essential  to  employ  good  lifting  technique
when using this method.

Directions:  Calculate  the  peak  power  workout  weight  for
your lift. Table 13.2 displays training weight for the bench
press. In this example, the lifter bench presses 300 pounds
for one repetition. Begin with a weight approximately 40 to
45 percent of the best one-rep lift. It is a good idea to use
a  rubber  bounce  pad  on  the  bar  to  protect  against  injury.
Time how long it takes to bench press five reps, lifting the
weight as rapidly as possible with good form. Do not cheat
on the lift; go all the way down and all the way up. Repeat this
procedure for weights that represent 50, 60, and 70 percent
of the one-rep-maximum bench press. Your workout weight
will be the one that renders the highest pounds per second.

In this example, peak power output occurred at 50 percent
of  maximum.  The  workout  weight  should  be  150  to  170
pounds. Perform three to five sets of five repetitions at that
weight, pushing the weight as quickly as possible. Increase

Bench Throws
The bench throw is a highly dynamic bench press that involves pushing the weight rapidly,
letting  go  of  the  bar  momentarily,  and  catching  it  again.  While  the  lift  is  effective  for
building power, it is potentially dangerous. Children should not do this exercise without
an experienced spotter (single spotter standing in back of the lifter). A safer alternative is
to do bench throws on a Smith machine‚Äîa weight machine with a bar attached to a track
allowing for vertical movment and little or no forward/backward movement.

AgILITY TRAININg
Agility  is  the  capacity  to  rapidly  change  speed  or  direction  during  whole  body
movements. Children‚Äôs games such as hopscotch build agility. Other common types of
exercises for building agility include speed ladders, dot drills, and cone drills. Trainers
and coaches can improvise to create agility drills appropriate for specific sports.

Speed Ladder
The main objective of agility ladder programs is to promote a wide range of different foot
and movement patterns. Through practice, these movements will become second nature
and the body will be able to respond quickly to various sport specific movement patterns.
With the use of an agility ladder, we can improve our agility by practicing movement
patterns in training. The standard ladder is 10 yards long with 18 inch squares. But you
can construct your own ladder using sticks, lino strips, or tape.

When beginning an agility ladder program, start with two to four drills. And once you
master these, then introduce new drills.

Forward Running, High-Knee Drill: This drill is great for improving foot
speed and coordination for all field sport athletes. Run with high knees
forward  through  the  ladder,  touching  every  ladder  space.  Land  on  the
balls of the feet and drive forward with your arms.

Lateral  Running,  Side-to-Side  Drill:  The  lateral  movement  of  this  drill
is great for court-sports and improves knee and ankle stability, as well.
Keep a low center of gravity and step side-to-side through the ladder one
foot at a time. Touch in each rung of the ladder with both feet. Land on
the balls of the feet and repeat right to left and left to right.

Dot Drills
Dot  Drills  develop  dynamic  leg  strength  and  increase  knee  and  ankle
strength and stability. This is a great agility drill for anyone who plays field
or racket sports or those who need to make quick changes of direction
and landings, such as skiers and basketball players.

Use a Dot Drill Mat or place small ‚ÄúX‚Äù marks with tape in a on the ground
in a pattern of a five on a Dice.

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  187

Begin with a warm up and jump from dot to dot with both
feet  at  a  time.  Progress  to  one  foot  hopping  and  follow  a
specific jump pattern.

Cone Drills (weave in/out)
This  is  an  excellent  drill  for  developing  and  maintaining
lateral (sideways) agility.

1.  Place four cones in a straight line about 3 yards/

meters apart.

2.

In between each pair of cones, place another cone
about 3 yards/meters to the left.

3.  Sprint from one cone to the next, touching each

one with your hand.

4.  Try to take quick side steps, rather than turning to

face the marker and running forward.

FuNCTIONAL TRAININg
All the exercises that follow are functional training exercises.
Trainers can design clever training programs using common
everyday  items  such  as  heavy  stones,  heavy  bars  (farmer‚Äôs
bars),  sledge  hammers,  throwing  implements  (e.g.,  shot,
hammer,  weight,  javelin,  discus),  sleds,  backpacks,  pulley
machines,  and  parachute  cord.  Trainers  can  also  design
whole body exercises using barbells, dumbbells, resistance
bands, and kettlebells.

Stone Training
These  stones  ranging  in
weight from 2 pounds to
more than 200 pounds are
excellent training aids for
children  and  adults.  The
best thing about stones is
that they are free. You can
find  smooth,  well-worn

of  weights  that  are  made
from  small  stones  and
cement.

Use  stones  the  way  you
would  conventional  free
weights. The children can
hold stones close to their

chests and do front squats, lunges, or sprints; they can hold
them  above  their  heads  and  do  overhead  squats;  or  they
can  throw  them  overhead  or  underhand  with  two  hands.
Children  can  also  use  them  to  add  resistance  to  common
movements  such  as  hopping  or  jumping.  Make  sure  the
child uses good posture when doing these exercises to avoid
injury.

Carry Exercises
Carrying  heavy  objects  is  an  effective,  low-cost  way  of
building  whole  body  strength  and  power.  Carry  exercises
build  grip,  core,  leg,  and  upper  body  strength  that  can
transfer to almost any sport. These exercises are simple: hold
a  kettlebell,  dumbbell,  farmers  bar,  or  weighted  suitcase
in either one or both hands and walk with them for 20 to
100 yards. Add variety by simultaneously pulling a sled or
wearing a backpack.

Sledgehammer Training
Hitting a sledgehammer against a tire or log is a great way
to  build  whole  body  strength  and  power.  The  handle  of  a
sledgehammer  is  made  of  wood  or  fiberglass  and  ranges
in  length  from  10  to  36  inches.  Sledgehammers  weight

OLYMPIC WEIghT LIFTINg
Olympic  weight  lifting‚Äîmore  correctly  called  weight
lifting‚Äîis  a  competitive  sport  and  includes  the  clean  and

jerk  and  the  snatch.  These  are  extremely  dynamic  lifts  that  work  the  large  muscles
of  the  upper  and  lower  body.  While  the  power  gained  in  these  lifts  does  not  transfer
immediately to sports skills, they provide a strength and power base that will eventually
help you improve in many power sports.

Posture and technique are critical for preventing injury and making satisfactory progress.
A good coach will help students avoid common mistakes and help them progress rapidly
in these exciting and beneficial exercises.

The Clean and Jerk
This  exercise  is  excellent  for  developing  power  in  the  muscles  used  for  jumping  and
pulling objects from the floor. It is a core exercise for power athletes such as throwers,
football players, volleyball players, and basketball players.

Brief Description
Clean: Place the bar on the floor in front of your shins. Keep your feet approximately two
feet apart. Grasp the bar with palms facing you and hands at shoulder width, and squat,
keeping  your  arms  and  back  straight  and  your  head  up.  Pull  the  weight  up  past  your
knees to your chest while throwing your hips forward and shoulders back. After pulling
the weight as high as you can, squat down suddenly and catch the bar on your chest at a
level just above your collarbones. Stand up straight with the bar at chest level, with feet
shoulder width apart. The main power for this exercise should come from your hips and
legs. Think of the middle phase of the lift as a vertical jump ‚Äîthis makes you drive up the
weight with your legs, rather than your arms.

Jerk: Holding the bar high on your chest, bend your knees and then extend fully, driving
the  bar  upward.  Drop  underneath  the  bar,  while  driving  your  bent  right  leg  forward
and  your  straight  left  leg  backwards.  Holding  the  bar  overhead  with  arms  extended,
stand with feet shoulder width apart. Return the bar to the floor. See the Clean and Jerk
Evaluation Checklist on page 189.

Youth FitnessPower and Speed  |  189

 ‚ùë At this point, your toes should be fully extended
and your shoulders fully shrugged. Keep your
arms straight and the barbell close to your body.

 ‚ùë Rack and Recovery

 ‚ùë With your body fully extended (ankles, knees,

hips with spine straight and shoulder shrugged),
bend your arms (upright row posture) and begin
to drop under the rising barbell by rotating the
elbows out in front and dropping to a full squat.
Your feet may have to widen slightly at this point
so that you assume a secure, balanced position.

 ‚ùë As the hips go below the knees, the barbell should

come to rest on your deltoids and clavicles.

 ‚ùë As you ride the weight down to a front squat
position, you have reached the clean catch
position.

 ‚ùë Stand up, keeping the elbows up, the torso rigid,

and the chest high, and hold the barbell tightly
across the deltoids.

 ‚ùë From this position, bring the feet back to the

starting stance.

 ‚ùë Bend your knees, lowering your body about three
to five inches. Straighten your legs explosively,
driving the barbell up from the shoulders with
your arms, while pushing upward on your toes.

 ‚ùë As the barbell passes above your head, push

your toes forcefully off of the floor and drive one
leg forward about one foot and the other foot
backward in the opposite direction about two to
three feet (in a ‚Äúlunge‚Äù position).

 ‚ùë As your feet land, lockout the barbell overhead

with your arms. This is the split jerk catch position.

 ‚ùë Return to the standing erect position by pushing
back with the front foot and stepping up with
the rear foot, pushing up hard to keep the barbell
overhead.

 ‚ùë The finish position of the clean and jerk is when

the barbell is locked out overhead with the feet in
line, and the body held in an erect position.

 ‚ùë Lower the bar under control back to the floor.

Clean and Jerk Evaluation Check List

The Starting Position

 ‚ùë Load the barbell correctly with the appropriate
weight and with the safety collars in place to
secure the plates.

 ‚ùë Stand with feet spaced hip to shoulders width
apart, with toes directly under the bar slightly
pointed out, with no stagger, feet flat on floor,
knees straight, and the barbell two inches from
the shins.

 ‚ùë Reach down for the barbell using a hook grip.

Grip the barbell as tightly as possible when using
heavier weights. Once you have the proper grip,
bend the knees so the bar is close to the shins.

 ‚ùë Keep your back flat, head up, chest out, shoulder
blades pulled together, trapezius muscles relaxed,
and arms straight with forearms pronated. Keep
your hips higher than the knees but lower than
your shoulders, with the barbell now touching
the shins.

The First Pull

 ‚ùë Tighten the torso muscles, and pull the bar off of

the floor with a smooth, slow, easy pull.

 ‚ùë Keep the hips higher than the knees but lower

than the shoulders, maintaining a flat back with
the shoulders still over the barbell.

 ‚ùë Keep elbows locked with arms extended and

forearms pronated.

 ‚ùë Keep your head in a neutral position with your

eyes focusing straight ahead (do not look at the
ceiling) and maintain a flat back.

 ‚ùë Keep your feet flat on the floor, balancing at

mid-foot.

The Second Pull

 ‚ùë You have reached the major power position of
the lift‚Äîthe starting point for the second pull‚Äî
when you have raised the bar to the level of your
kneecaps or slightly higher. At this point, pull the
bar upward quickly, keeping your arms straight
and the barbell close to your body.

 ‚ùë When the barbell reaches mid-thigh level, explode
upward using as much force as possible. Extend
up on your toes, shrug your shoulders upward,
and pull the barbell above your belly button‚Äî
keeping the barbell as close to the body as
possible.

The Squat Snatch
The snatch is more complex and difficult than the clean and
jerk. The object of the lift is to pull the bar over your head
in one movement and catch it overhead from a squat with
your arms straight. Good coaching is essential to mastering
this lift.

Brief Description
Place the bar on the floor in front of your shins, with your
feet approximately shoulder width apart. Grasp the bar with
palms  facing  you,  hands  placed  as  wide  apart  as  possible,
and  squat,  keeping  your  arms  and  back  straight  and  your
head up. Pull the weight up past your knees to your chest
while  throwing  your  hips  upward  and  shrugging  your
shoulders. After pulling the weight as high as you can, go
into  a  squat  and  catch  the  bar  overhead  with  your  arms
straight. Stand up straight with the bar overhead. Return the
bar to the starting position. See the Squat Snatch Evaluation
Checklist on page 191.

Brief Description
Place the bar on the floor in front of your shins, with your
feet approximately shoulder width apart. Grasp the bar with
palms facing you, hands placed as wide apart as possible and
squat, keeping your arms and back straight and your head
up. Pull the weight up past your knees to your chest while
throwing  your  hips  upward  and  keeping  your  shoulders
shrugged. After pulling the weight as high as you can, drop
into  a  lunge  position  with  your  bent  left  leg  in  front  and
straightened right leg in the rear, and catch the bar overhead
with  your  arms  straight.  Stand  up  straight  with  the  bar
overhead.  Return  the  bar  to  the  starting  position.  See  the
Split Snatch Evaluation Checklist on page 192.

modIFIed olYmPIc lIFTS
This section describes the power clean, jerk, and the power
snatch‚Äîmodifications of the exercises done by competitive
weight lifters. The squat clean and squat snatch take years
to  perfect.  For  most  young  athletes,  the  goal  of  explosive
whole body training is to increase strength and power for
their  sport.  Performing  modifications  of  the  complete  lift
requires  less  precise  technique  and  builds  high  levels  of
strength more quickly.

Squat Snatch

The Split Snatch
The  split  and  squat  snatches  are  similar  except  that  you
catch the bar in a lunge position in the split snatch. As with
the squat snatch, the object of the lift is to pull the bar over
your head in one movement and catch it overhead from a
squat  with  your  arms  straight.  While  the  split  technique
is  rarely  used  in  weight  lifting  competitions,  it  is  a  good
training  exercise  for  athletes  who  require  vigorous  weight
transfers,  such  as  baseball  or  softball  players,  discus  or
javelin throwers, or shot putters.

Power Clean

Power Clean
This  excellent  exercise  develops  power  in  the  muscles
responsible for jumping and pulling objects from the floor.
It  develops  strength  and  power  from  the  basic  athletic
position. It represents a core exercise for throwers, football
players, volleyball, and basketball power athletes.

 ‚ùë Load the barbell correctly with the appropriate

weight and the safety collars in place to secure the

 ‚ùë Use a wide hand spacing, keeping the arms

Power and Speed  |  193

Directions: Place the bar on the floor in front of your shins.
Keep your feet approximately two feet apart. Grasp the bar
with palms facing you, hands at shoulder width, and squat,
keeping your arms and back straight and head up. Pull the
weight up past your knees to your chest while throwing your
hips forward and shoulders back. After pulling the weight as
high as you can, bend the knees suddenly and catch the bar
on your chest at a level just above your collarbones. Stand
up straight with the bar at chest level. Return the bar to the
starting position. The main power for this exercise should
come from your hips and legs. Think of the middle phase of
the lift as a vertical jump‚Äîthis makes you propel the weight
up with your legs rather than your arms.

Variations: Variations include the high pull and squat clean.
The high pull is identical to the power clean except you do
not turn the bar over at the top of the lift and catch it at your
chest. This permits handling more weight with less stress on
wrists and forearms.

The squat clean is a technically more difficult lift than the
power clean. At the top of the pulling phase of the clean, bend
your knees fully and catch the weight at your chest while in
a full squat position. When you master this movement, you
will be able to clean more weight than you can power clean
because you do not have to pull the weight as high.

Power Snatch
The  power  snatch  is  more  complex  and  difficult  than  the
power clean. The objective is to pull the bar over your head
in one movement and catch it overhead with arms straight.
Good coaching is essential to mastering this lift.

Directions: Place the bar on the floor in front of your shins.
Keep  your  feet  approximately  two  feet  apart.  Grasp  the
bar  with  palms  facing  you,  hands  placed  as  wide  apart  as
possible,  and  squat,  keeping  your  arms  and  back  straight
and  your  head  up.  Pull  the  weight  up  past  your  knees  to
your chest while throwing your hips forward and shoulders
back.  After  pulling  the  weight  as  high  as  you  can,  bend
the  knees  suddenly  and  catch  it  overhead  with  your  arms
straight. Stand up straight with the bar overhead. Return the
bar to the starting position.

Jerk

Directions:  Begin  the  exercise  with  the  bar  at  the  top  of
the  clean  position,  resting  on  your  chest  approximately  at
the level of your collarbones. Take the bar to this position
by doing a clean or taking the bar from a rack and placing
it  at  a  chest-high  level  (performed  this  way,  the  exercise
is  called  ‚Äújerks  off  the  rack‚Äù).  Bend  your  knees,  and  then
thrust the bar up vigorously using your legs and arms. Drop
underneath the bar with a split movement, one leg forward
with bent knee, and the other leg extended straight to the
back. Then, bring your feet together so you are in a standing
position with the bar overhead and in control.

Other Exercises to Develop
Speed and Power
Variations  of  plyometric  exercises  are  limited  only  by
imagination. Obviously you cannot include all the exercises
presented here in your exercise program. Choose the ones
whose  movements  most  closely  resemble  your  favorite
sports.  In  general,  choose  about  6  to  12  speed  and  power
exercises, and integrate them into a program that includes
cardiovascular,  strength,  and  flexibility  exercises.  Sample
exercise routines and exercise programming are discussed
in a later section of the course.

Jerk
To the novice, the jerk looks very much like a shoulder or
military  press  described  in  the  weight  training  section  of
the  course.  Actually,  the  jerk  is  mainly  a  leg  exercise.  You
hoist the bar overhead by driving with your legs and then
dropping underneath the bar.

Remember,  the  power  the  athlete  develops  from  these
exercises does not transfer automatically to increased power
in  the  sport.  One  has  to  practice  the  skill  and  gradually
integrate the increased power into purposeful movements.
If  athletes  work  consistently  on  sports  skills  and  perform
exercises to increase strength and power, they will eventually
become more powerful in their sport.

International Sports Sciences Association194  |  unit 13

SuMMARY

Young children can build strength and power through training to help them
perform better in sport. Power in sports depends on genetics, metabolic capacity,
muscle size, skill, and nervous system capacity. Effective strength (usable strength
in the sport) is determined by how well the nervous system controls the muscles
and by the skill and efficiency of movement. Athletes in any skilled sport must direct
most of their energies toward developing skill. Practicing the skill must never take a
back seat to general physical conditioning.

Weight training, speed, and plyometric training eventually produce more powerful
movements if integrated into a long-term, systematic program that includes skill
training and physical conditioning.

Exercises to develop power and speed include sprint starts, sprinting, harness sprint-
ing, hurdles, stair running, high knee-fast arms, speed ladders, speed lifting, peak
power training on a stationary bike, whole body functional training, and Olympic
weight lifting.
