uNIT 14

# Assessing Fitness In Children

196  |  unit 14

Personal  trainers  should  assess  their  young  clients‚Äô  fitness
for  comparison  to  established  norms  to  assess  readiness
for  competition,  identify  weaknesses,  and  assess  progress.
Literally hundreds of fitness tests exist to assess endurance,
strength, flexibility, power, speed, agility, and skill. Select the
most  appropriate  tests  that  provide  information  about  the
athlete‚Äôs current status and progress. This section discusses
basic  fitness  tests  for  children.  Advanced  athletes  may
require more sophisticated testing.

A major theme throughout this course is that motor skills
(and  tests)  are  highly  specific.  This  means  that  a  young
football, soccer, or field hockey player who performs well on
endurance, sprint, and strength tests will not necessarily be
the best athlete in the sport. Choose tests that have validity‚Äî

the tasks in the test should closely reflect the requirements
of  the  sport.  For  example,  a  15-minute  endurance  test  on
the  track  does  not  measure  the  physical  requirements  to
successfully  throw  a  discus,  shoot  free  throws,  spike  a
volleyball,  perform  a  handstand,  or  swing  a  golf  club.  Be
sport or skill specific as much as possible.

Consider  safety  and  injury  prevention  when  testing.
Maximum strength or speed tests may increase the risk of
injury.  Make  sure  children  are  warmed  up  and  physically
prepared to take the tests.

This unit describes simple tests for measuring fitness. You
don‚Äôt need expensive equipment to do these tests. Most of
them are valid and have been used by coaches for decades.

Youth FitnessAssessing Fitness in Children  |  197

ThE PRESIDENT‚ÄôS ChALLENgE:
PhYSICAL ACTIvITY AND FITNESS PROgRAM
The President‚Äôs Council on Physical Fitness and Sports has a
series of fitness tests that can serve as a great starting point
for  measuring  strength,  power,  endurance,  and  flexibility.
These  tests  are  valuable  for  assessing  fitness  in  children
because they include extensive norms for boys and girls ages
6  to  18  years. The  tests  involve  five  parts:  (1)  curl-ups  or
partial  curl-ups,  (2)  the  shuttle  run,  (3)  an  endurance
run/walk, (4) pull-ups, right-angle push-ups, or bent
arm hang, and (5) the v-sit reach or sit and reach. The
norms  are  included  within  this  chapter.  The  President‚Äôs
Counsel  offers  awards  for  achieving  minimal  fitness
standards. The Council describes the tests as follows:

to the chest. Keeping this arm position, the athlete raises the
trunk curling up to touch elbows to thighs and then lowers
the back to the floor so that the scapulas (shoulder blades)
touch  the  floor  for  one  curl-up.  To  start,  a  timer  calls  out
the signal ‚ÄúReady? Go!‚Äù and begins timing athlete for one
minute. The athlete stops on the word ‚Äústop.‚Äù

Curl-ups  Scoring:  ‚ÄúBouncing‚Äù  off  the  floor  is  not
permitted. Keep the arms and elbows held close to the chest.
The curl-up should be counted only if performed correctly.

(1) Curl-ups or Partial Curl-ups
choose one for this test area

Curl-ups  Objective:  To  perform  as  many  curl-ups  as
possible in 60-seconds.

Curl-ups Procedure: Have the athlete lie on a cushioned,
clean  surface  with  knees  flexed  and  feet  about  12  inches
from the buttocks. Partner holds feet. Arms are crossed with
hands placed on opposite shoulders and elbows held close

# Curl-Up Norms For Boys And Girls

Partial Curl-ups Objective: This test measures abdominal
strength/endurance by maximum number of curl-ups.

12  inches  from  the  buttocks.  The  feet  are  NOT  held  or
anchored. Arms are extended forward with fingers resting
on  the  legs  and  pointing  toward  the  knees.  The  athlete‚Äôs
partner  is  behind  the  head  with  hands  cupped  under  the
athlete‚Äôs  head.  The  athlete  being  tested  curls  up  slowly
sliding the fingers up the legs until the fingertips touch the

knees, then back down until the head touches the partner‚Äôs
hands.  The  curl-ups  are  done  to  a  metronome  (or  audio
tape,  clapping,  drums)  with  one  complete  curl-up  every
three seconds, and are continued until the athlete can do no
more in rhythm (has not done the last three in rhythm) or
has reached the target number for the Presidential Physical
Fitness Award (50th or 85th percentile for the fitness tests).

Partial  Curl-ups  Scoring:  Record  only  those  curl-ups
done with proper form and in rhythm.

Partial  Curl-ups  Rationale:  Partial  (abdominal)  curl-
ups, done slowly with knees bent and feet not held, replace
the timed curl-ups because they are a better indicator of the
strength and endurance of the abdominal muscles.

(2) Shuttle Run

(3) Mile Run/Walk Test

Objective: To measure heart/lung endurance by the fastest
time to cover a one-mile distance.

Procedure:  On  a  safe,  one-mile  distance,  athletes  begin
running  on  the  count  ‚ÄúReady?  Go!‚Äù  Walking  may  be
interspersed  with  running.  However,  the  athletes  should
be  encouraged  to  cover  the  distance  in  as  short  a  time  as
possible.

Scoring:  Before  administering  this  test,  athletes‚Äô  health
status  should  be  reviewed.  Also,  athletes  should  be  given
ample instruction on proper pacing and should be allowed
to  practice  running  this  distance  against  time.  Sufficient
time  should  be  used  for  warming  up  and  cooling  down
before  and  after  the  test.  Times  are  recorded  in  minutes
and seconds.

Objective: To perform the shuttle run as fast as possible.

Options:

Procedure: Mark two parallel lines 30 feet apart and place
two blocks of wood or similar object behind one of the lines.
Athletes start behind opposite line. On the signal ‚ÄúReady?
Go!‚Äù the athlete runs to the blocks, picks one up, runs back
to  the  starting  line,  places  the  block  behind  the  line,  runs
back  and  picks  up  the  second  block  and  runs  back  across
starting line.

Scoring:  Blocks  should  not  be  thrown  across  the  lines.
Scores are recorded to the nearest tenth of a second.

‚Ä¢  Option for 6 to 7 years old: 400-meter run

‚Ä¢  Option for 8 to 9 years old: 800-meter run

The same objective and testing procedures are used as with
the mile run.

Rationale:  Shorter  distance  runs  are  included  as  options
for younger children. Younger children can be prepared to
run the mile; however, it is sometimes easier to administer

Meter-Yard Conversions: If you are not using a track that

(4) Upper body strength: pull-ups,
push-ups, or bent arm hangs
choose one for this test area

Pull-ups  Objective:  To  measure  upper  body  strength/
endurance by the maximum number of pull-ups completed.

extended and feet free from floor, using either an overhand
grasp  (palms  facing  away  from  the  body)  or  underhand
grip  (palms  facing  toward  the  body).  Small  athletes  may
be lifted to starting position. Athlete raises  the body  until
the  chin  clears  the  bar  and  then  lowers  body  to  full-hang
starting position. Athlete performs as many correct pull-ups
as possible.

Pull-ups  Scoring:  Pull-ups  should  be  done  in  a  smooth,
fluid motion rather than a jerky motion. Kicking or bending
the  legs  is  not  permitted  and  the  body  must  not  swing
during the movement.

or
Push-ups  Objective:  To  measure  upper  body  strength/
endurance according to the maximum number of push-ups
completed.

Push-ups  Procedure:  The  athlete  lies  face  down  on  the
mat  in  push-up  position  with  hands  under  shoulders,
fingers straight, and legs straight, parallel, and slightly apart,
with  the  toes  supporting  the  feet.  The  athlete  straightens
the arms, keeping the back and knees straight, then lowers
the body until there is a 90-degree angle at the elbows, with
the upper arms parallel to the floor. A partner holds her/his
hand at the point of the 90-degree angle so that the athlete
tested  goes  down  only  until  her/his  shoulder  touches  the
partner‚Äôs  hand,  then  back  up.  The  push-ups  are  done  to
a  metronome  (or  audio  tape,  clapping,  drums)  with  one
complete  push-up every  three seconds, and are  continued
until the athlete can do no more in rhythm (has not done
the last three in rhythm).

Push-ups Scoring: Record only those push-ups done with
proper form and in rhythm.

angle  push-ups

Push-ups  Rationale:  Right
are
recommended  for  upper  body  strength  and  endurance
testing  because  the  athletes‚Äô  body  weight  has  less  effect
than  it  does  on  pull-ups.  Right  angle  push-ups  provide  a
better  indicator  of  the  range  of  strength/endurance  found
in children and youth, whereas many are unable to do any
pull-ups.  Pull-ups  remain  an  option  for  those  athletes  at
higher levels of strength/endurance.

or
Flexed-Arm hang

Athletes  who  cannot  do  one  pull-up  may  do  the  flexed-
arm hang to qualify for the National or Participant Physical
Fitness  Awards.  To  qualify  for  the  Presidential  Award,
athletes are required to do pull-ups or right angle push-ups.

Pull-ups  Procedure:  Athlete  hangs  from  a  horizontal
bar  at  a  height  the  athlete  can  hang  from  with  arms  fully

Flexed-Arm  hang  Objective:  To  maintain  the  flexed-
arm hang position as long as possible.

Flexed-Arm hang Procedure:

Use  either  an  overhand  grip  (palms  facing  away  from  the
body)  or  underhand  grip  (palms  facing  toward  body),
the  athlete  assumes  a  flexed-arm  hang  position  with  chin
clearing the bar. Athletes may be lifted to this position. The
athlete holds this position as long as possible.

Flexed-Arm hang Scoring:

Chest should be held close to bar with legs hanging straight.
Timing  is  stopped  when  the  athlete‚Äôs  chin  touches  or  falls
below the bar.

(5) Flexibility test:
V-Sit Reach or Sit and Reach

on floor with measuring line between legs and soles of feet
placed  immediately  behind  baseline,  heels  8  to  12  inches
apart. The athlete clasps his/her thumbs so that the hands
are  together,  palms  down  and  then  places  them  on  the
measuring line. With the legs held flat by a partner, athlete
slowly reaches forward as far as possible, keeping fingers on
baseline and feet flexed. After three practice tries, the athlete
holds the fourth reach for three seconds while that distance
is recorded.

v-Sit  Reach  Rules:  Legs  must  remain  straight  with  the
soles of the feet held perpendicular to the floor (feet flexed).
Athletes should be encouraged to reach slowly rather than
‚Äúbounce‚Äù  while  stretching.  Scores,  recorded  to  the  nearest
half inch, are read as plus scores for reaches beyond baseline
and minus scores for reaches behind baseline.

v-Sit Reach Objective: To measure flexibility of the lower
back and hamstrings by reaching forward in the V position.

or

v-Sit  Reach  Procedure:  A  straight  line  two  feet  long
is  marked  on  the  floor  as  the  baseline.  A  measuring  line
is  drawn  perpendicular  to  the  midpoint  of  the  baseline
extending  two  feet  on  each  side  and  marked  off  in  half-
inches.  The  point  where  the  baseline  and  measuring  line
intersect  is  the  ‚Äú0‚Äù  point.  Athlete  removes  shoes  and  sits

Sit and Reach Objective: Farthest distance reached.

Sit and Reach Procedure: A specially constructed box is
placed,  with  a  measuring  scale  marked  in  centimeters  (23
centimeters  at  the  level  of  the  feet).  Athletes  remove  their
shoes and sit on floor with knees extended fully, feet shoulder
width apart and soles of the feet held flat against the end of

the box. With hands on top of each other, palms down, and
legs held flat, the athletes reach along the measuring line as
far as possible. After three practice reaches, the fourth reach
is held while the distance is recorded.

Sit  and  Reach  Rules:  The  legs  must  remain  straight,
with  the  soles  of  feet  against  box  and  fingertips  of  both
hands reaching evenly along the measuring line. Scores are
recorded to the nearest centimeter.

MEASuRINg
ENDuRANCE FITNESS
For most people, it is unnecessary to administer complicated,
expensive laboratory exercise tests. You can assess endurance
fitness  using  simple  field  tests.  These  include  the  1-mile
walking test, the Cooper 12-minute run, the 6-mile bicycle
test, and the Cooper 12-minute swim test. Use these tests to
assess starting fitness and measure progress in the program.
Choose  a  test  that  reflects  the  nature  of  the  athlete‚Äôs  sport
or  exercise  program.  For  example,  swimmers  should  take
the  swimming  test  rather  than  the  running  or  walking
test. Runners should take the running test rather than the
swimming test.

endurance and maxImal
oxYgen conSumPTIon
.
VO2max  by
Maximal  oxygen  consumption  (called
scientists) is an important measure of endurance capacity.
.
VO2max  measures  the  ability  to  deliver  and  use  oxygen.  It
also  measures  the  capacity  to  exercise  at  high  intensities.
in  sophisticated  exercise
Scientists  measure
science labs using expensive treadmills, gas analyzers, and
computers.  The  exercise  intensity  increases  periodically
during the test¬†until the test subject fatigues and continues
no  further.  Each  minute,  the  test  administrator  measures
and records oxygen uptake, heart rate,¬†blood pressure, the
electrocardiogram,¬† and¬† breathing  rate¬† and  volume.  The
highest level of oxygen consumption during the test is called
maximal oxygen consumption.

.
VO2max

During  the  test,  the  electrical  activity  of  the  heart  is  also
measured  (with  the  electrocardiogram).  This  helps  the
physician assess heart function during exercise. Physicians
will  sometimes  perform  an  exercise  electrocardiogram  to
assess the health of the heart or to determine if a patient can
safely participate in an exercise program. Such assessments
are not common in children unless the physician suspects
underlying cardiovascular disease.

Assessing Fitness in Children  |  205

FIeld TeSTS For meaSurIng endurance
You  can  estimate  fitness  for  walking,  running,  swimming,
and biking with simple field tests. If your client is starting
an  exercise  program,  use  the  walking  test.  Experienced,
fit  athletes  should  take  one  of  the  other,  more  rigorous
field tests. Choose the test that most closely fits the client‚Äôs
favorite  type  of  exercise.  This  is  important;  performance
on the running field test will tell you little about fitness for
swimming.

Most children do not need medical clearance before taking
maximum fitness tests. A physician should be consulted if
children:

‚Ä¢  have heart trouble

‚Ä¢

suffer from chest pain

‚Ä¢  have dizzy spells

‚Ä¢  have high blood pressure

‚Ä¢  have bone or joint problems

1-Mile Rockport Walking Test
The  Rockport  walking  test  estimates  maximal  oxygen
consumption. Take this test on a 400-meter track (440-yards).
Walk 1-mile (4 laps) as quickly as possible without running.
This  test  does  not  require  special  equipment  and  can  be
performed with large groups; it is only marginally accurate
in  children  but  still  a  good  starting  place  for  assessing
endurance  fitness.  Five  variables  are  needed  to  estimate
.
VO2max:
Record the following and enter it into the equation below:

1.  Age in years

2.  Sex (females are coded as 0 and males as 1)

3.  Time to complete the one-mile walk (in minutes

including a decimal), and weight (lb)

4.  Heart rate (in beats per minute) measured for 10

seconds immediately after completing the walk

5.  Multiply the answer by 6 to obtain the one-

minute heart rate.

.

This is a good fitness test for a person beginning an exercise
program. The best place to take this test is on a 400-meter
track (440 yards). If you do not have access to one, use a car
odometer to mark off a course on smooth, flat ground.

Begin with warm-up exercises, such as simple stretches and
walking  in  place.  Walk  the  mile  as  fast  as  possible.  Use  a
stopwatch  to  time  the  walk  to  the  nearest  second.  This  is
a walking test, so the walker always must have one foot in
contact with the ground. If both feet leave the ground at any
time during the movement, the child is running and must
return to a walk.

exercise  program,  and  do  exercise  that  involves  running
(e.g., jogging, tennis, basketball, or volleyball).

Take this test on a 400-meter running track (use Table 14.5
to convert to and from meters.) You will need a stopwatch
or  clock  with  a  second  hand.  Warm  up  with  easy  jogging
and stretching exercises. The object of the test is to run as
far  as  possible  in  12  minutes.  Record  distance  traveled  in
meters  using  a  decimal  figure.  For  example,  if  the  person
ran  exactly  six  laps  on  a  440-yard  track,  he  or  she  would
have covered 2414 meters. Check the young athlete‚Äôs fitness
rating in Table 14.12.

Cooper 12-Minute Run
This is one of the most popular field tests for assessing fitness
and is used by millions of people of all ages. Youth should
take this test if they are healthy, already participating in an

Six-Mile Bicycle Test
The six-mile bicycle test is appropriate if the person has been
riding a bike regularly for at least three to four weeks. Take
this test on the same bicycle in the exercise program. Bicycle

type (e.g., 10-speed, mountain bike, 3-speed, cruiser) affects
test performance. However, regardless of the bicycle used,
the test is good for approximating cycling fitness and useful
for identifying the starting point of the program.

Use  a  flat,  six-mile  course  void  of  interference  from
automobiles or other bicycles. A bike track is preferred. A
road with a bike lane or a park that does not allow cars is
excellent  for  taking  the  test.  Before  the  test,  warm  up  by
riding at a relatively slow pace for 4 to 10 minutes. Ride the
six-mile course as fast as possible (and safely). Assess fitness
by comparison to the norms in Table 14.13.

The 12-minute Swim Test
Use this test if swimming is a planned part of the exercise
program.  You  will  need  a  swimming  pool  and  stopwatch.
Before  taking  the  test,  have  the  athlete  warm-up  by
swimming two laps. The object is to swim as far as possible
in 12 minutes at a steady pace. Check rating in Table 14.14.

Step Test
This  test  requires  little  equipment.  You  will  need  a  12-
inch  bench  step  (with  at  least  a  24‚Äù  x  16‚Äù  stepping  area),
a  clock  with  a  sweep  hand  (seconds)  or  a  stopwatch,  and
a metronome. Set the metronome to 96 beats per minute.
Stepping cadence (96 bpm) is 24 steps per minute.

The  rhythm  of  the  steps  will  be  a  4  count:  up,  up,  down,
down.  Step  up  with  the  right  foot,  then  left  ,  followed  by
right  foot  down  then  left  foot  down.  Demonstrate  the
pattern to your client.

Allow 15 seconds of practice with
the  metronome.  Begin  the  test;
time  client  for  three  minutes.
After  three  minutes,  terminate
the  test  and  have  the  client  sit
down.  Five  seconds  after  the
3-minute  termination  mark,  take
the client‚Äôs pulse for one complete
minute.  Record  this  one-minute
post  exercise  rate.  Compare  to

the  norms  on  Table  14.15.  Please  note:  these  rates  are  for
adults. You will want to establish your own norms based on
a sampling of youth athletes from both sexes.

MEASuRINg FOR POWER

Power Quadrathlon
The quadrathlon was originally developed by British track
and field coach Max Jones to measure power in throwers.
However,  the  test  is  great  for  any  power  athlete.  Use  it  to
assess  fitness  and  gauge  progress  in  the  training  program.
The quadrathlon includes standing long jump, three jumps,
30-meter sprint, and overhead shot throw. (Table 14.17)

Standing Long Jump
Place  both  feet  over  the  edge  of  the  long-jump  pit  or  use
a  gym  floor.  The  athlete  starts  in  a  static  position,  with
feet  together,  shoulder  width  apart.  He  or  she  crouches,
leans  forward,  swings  the  arms  backwards,  then  jumps
horizontally  forward  as  far  as  possible,  landing  with  both
feet  into  the  jumping  area.  Measure  from  the  edge  of  the
take-off point to the nearest point of contact.

Three Jumps (Bunny Hops)
Do  this  test  on  a  track,  football  field,  or  gym  floor.  Start
with both feet comfortably apart with the toes just behind
the  take-off  mark.  The  athlete  takes  three  continuous  two
footed jumps. Measure the distance covered. The start must
be from a static position and both feet must be parallel on
each jump phase. Athletes may wear spikes.

30-Meter Sprint
The athlete begins in a crouched position on the track. Give
the command ‚Äúon your marks, set, go.‚Äù On ‚Äúgo‚Äù, the athlete
sprints from a stationary set position as fast as possible to
the finish line. Stand at the finish line and time the run from
the  moment  the  runner  contacts  the  ground  on  the  first
stride  to  the  moment  when  the  runner‚Äôs  torso  crosses  the
line. Spikes are allowed.

# Power Quadrathlon Equations

Standing Long Jump  Points = -36.14048+(D*37.268536)+(D*D*-0.128057)

Three bunny hop Jumps  Points = -36.36996+(D*12.478922)+(D*D*-0.007423)

30-Meter Sprint  Points = 209.70039+(T*-36.94427)+(T*T*0.165766)

Overhead Shot Throw  Points = -22.32216+(D*5.8318756)+(D*D*-0.000334)

ADDITIONAL POINTS:

Overhead Shot Throw
Use  the  appropriate  weight  for  the  age  and  gender  of  the
athlete‚Äîgirls and women use an 8 lb shot, high school boys
use a 12 lb shot, and college men use a 16 lb shot. The athlete
stands on top of the shot toe-board, facing away from the
landing  area,  with  feet  a  comfortable  distance  apart.  The
shot  is  held  cupped  in  both  hands.  The  athlete  crouches,
lowering the shot between the legs, then drives upwards to
propel the shot back over the head. The athlete must land
feet-first and remain upright or the throw counts as a foul.

Measurement is taken from the inside of the toe-board to
the nearest point of contact.

Scoring:  Use  the  quadrathlon  scoring  table  (Table  14.17)
to  compare  athletes  or  gauge  progress  in  a  single  athlete.
This  test  is  a  good  measure  of  general  power  and  should
be  administered  monthly  during  the  off-season.  The
athlete should take the test during the season if there is any
question concerning training status. Calculate points using
Table 14.16.

Youth FitnessAssessing Fitness in Children  |  209

Vertical Jump Test

Vertical Jump Test
The vertical jump test is a favorite for measuring explosive
leg power. Ideally, use a vertical jump device, such as a Vertec
or Vertical Jump Mat. If none is available, tack a tape on the
wall‚Äîhigh and low enough to assess the athletes‚Äô standing
reach and jump reach. The athlete applies chalk to the end of
the fingertips and stands sideways to the wall. Keeping both
feet on the ground, he or she reaches as high as possible with
one hand and makes a mark on the wall. This is the baseline

measurement. With feet still in a static position, he or she
flexes the knees and then jumps as high as possible, making
a mark at the highest point in the jump. The athlete‚Äôs score is
the highest of three trials. (Table 14.18).

The results of this test relate to performance in many power
sports. Repeat this test often.

Chest Throws
Chest Throws (Medicind Ball Chest Pass) is a good measure
of  upper  body  explosiveness.  The  test  is  moderately
correlated to bench press strength. No norms are available,
so  measure  performance  over  time  to  assess  changes
in  upper  body  power.  This  test  requires  a  medicine  ball
weighing approximately 2 percent of body weight.

Directions:  For  standing  chest  throws,  stand  with  legs
spread  approximately  shoulder  width  apart.  Hold  the
medicine ball with both hands at chest level as if performing
a  basketball  chest  pass.  Throw  the  medicine  ball  as  far  as
possible  straight  forward  using  a  two-handed  chest  pass.
Measure the distance between a starting line and the landing
site (closest to the starting line).

Overhead Medicine
Ball throw
This test uses a movement similar to that used during weight
lifting exercise such as the snatch and clean. No norms are
available,  so  measure  performance  changes  over  time  to
assess  changes  in  upper  body  power.  This  test  requires  a
medicine ball weighing approximately 5 percent of the users
body weight.

Directions:  Stand  behind  a  line  with  the  back  facing  the
direction  of  the  throw.  Hold  the  ball  with  both  hands
below  the  waist  with  arms  straight,  and  knees  and  hips
flexed slightly. Pull the ball quickly overhead using power
generated  from  the  lower  body  and  do  not  cross  the  foul
line. Measure the distance between a starting line and the
landing site (closest to the starting line).

Table 14.19

MEASuRINg FOR SPEED

Sprint tests

10- and 40-yard (or meter) dashes
Short  sprint  tests  are  important  evaluation  tools  for  high-
power sports such as football, basketball, soccer, volleyball,
and track and field. These tests are so important that some
coaches  use  them  as  screening  tools  to  weed  out  athletes
who do not achieve minimums for basic power. Administer
these  tests  regularly  to  conditioned  athletes  to  avoid
injury. Norms are available for the 40-yard dash (hand and
electronic timing), but reliable norms are not available for
the  10-yard  dash  for  high  school,  junior  high  school,  or
elementary school athletes. If you use the 10-yard dash test,
track progress with repeated tests.

Directions:  Use  a  standing,  crouch,  or  block  start.  The
starting cadence is ‚Äúon your marks; set; go.‚Äù Athletes should
complete the distance as quickly as possible. Use either hand
or electronic timing.

20-Meter Start from Blocks
Sprinting from starting blocks is a great way to develop and
measure  explosive  power.  Sprinting  from  blocks  is  a  skill
that  requires  instruction  and  practice.  Reliable  norms  are
not available for the 20-meter start from blocks for young
athletes. If you want to use the 20-meter start from blocks
test, measure changes in speed with repeated tests.

Directions: The test involves a sequence of ‚Äúon your marks,
set, and go.‚Äù

Youth FitnessAssessing Fitness in Children  |  211

‚ÄúOn your marks position‚Äù: The feet are staggered 10 to 14
inches  apart,  with  the  front  foot  placed  approximately  20
inches from the starting line. Try to relax in this position.

‚ÄúSet position‚Äù: Raise the back and hips. The front leg bends
about  90¬∞  and  the  rear  leg  120¬∞.  Right-handed  people
generally start with their left foot forward. The back is flat,
and  the  hips  are  slightly  higher  than  the  shoulders.  The
athletes  contact  the  ground  with  their  fingertips,  which
raises the shoulders as high as possible. They should not put
too much weight on the fingertips.

‚ÄúGo‚Äù: Raise the shoulders so that they can direct force with
the front driving leg through the length of the body. Drive
the front leg fully so that the body forms a straight line from
the hip to the heels. Push the rear foot hard against the block
or ground as they drive their knee forward. As they drive
forward, the arm on the side of the front foot drives straight
forward, while the rear foot arm drives straight backward.
Both arms are bent approximately 90 to 110¬∞. Try to extend
fully with the hips and knees and use the arms dynamically
during the driving phases of the movement. After the start,
run as fast as possible toward the 20-meter line.

100-, 200-, and 400-Meter Dashes
These  are  reliable  tests  of  fitness  and  power  output  for
young  athletes.  Reliable  norms  are  not  available  for  these
tests  for  high  school,  junior  high  school,  or  elementary
school  athletes.  If  you  use  these  tests,  track  progress  with
repeated tests.

Directions:  Use  a  standing,  crouch,  or  block  start.  The
starting cadence is ‚Äúon your marks; set; go.‚Äù Run the distance
as quickly as possible.

MEASuRINg FOR
STRENgTh

Hand Grip Dynamometer
The handgrip dynamometer test is a measure of static grip
strength.  While  it  has  limited  applicability  to  athletics,  it
does provide a rough estimate of upper body strength. The
test is easy to administer, so norms are available for children
and adolescents. (See Tables 14.20 and 14.21)

Directions: Use a commercially available grip dynamometer
calibrated  in  kilograms  and  perform  the  test  using  the
dominant and non-dominant hand. Grasp the dynamometer
and hold it to the side with the arm extended fully and elbow
straight.  Athletes  should  grip  as  hard  as  you  can.  Perform
three  trials  and  use  the  middle  score.  The  grip  strength
norms show reflect data gathered on youth baseball players
and recreationally active younger children.

Bench Press
The bench press is a good measure of upper body strength.
The exercise is a favorite of high school and college athletes.
Do this test with free weights or a weight machine.

Directions: Warm-up by bench-pressing light weights. Lie
on the bench with your feet flat on the floor. Grasp the bar
slightly  more  than  shoulder  width  apart.  Have  the  spotter
help you move the bar from the rack to a point over your
chest.  Lower  the  bar  in  a  straight  line  slightly  below  your
nipples (end of the breast bone). Push the weight straight up
to the starting position. (See Table 14.22)

Youth FitnessPREDICTINg ONE-REPETITION MAxIMuM
(1-RM) FROM MuLTIPLE REPETITIONS
Many  personal  trainers  prefer  predicting  1-RM  from  multiple  repetitions  at  a  specific  weight. The  Brzycki  equation  has  been
validated  on  the  bench  press.  It  is  accurate  when  the  maximum  repetitions  are  10  or  fewer.  Some  high  school  programs  use
modifications of the NFL Combine bench press test that might involve performing 20 or more repetitions at a specific weight. The
NFL Combine uses 225 pounds, while high school programs typically use 135 pounds or less. High repetition strength tests are
better measures of muscle endurance than strength. The chart makes calculations unnecessary.

The Brzycki equation:  1-RM= Weight / ( 1.0278 - ( 0.0278 * Number of repetitions ) )  Weights are in kg. 1 kg=2.2 lb.

# Weight
(KG)

# Repetitions

SOURCE: CALCULATED FROM THE BRZYCKI EQUATION: BRZYCKI, M. (1993) STRENGTH TESTING-PREDICTING A ONE-REP MAx FROM REPS-TO-FATIGUE. JOPERD, 68 (1), 88-90.

214  |  unit 14

Squat
Do not do a maximum squat test until you have pre-screened
your  client  using  the  lower  body  assessment  skills  test  in
section 2.4. Before taking the maximum squat test, children
should demonstrate good form on the chair squat, step-up,
unloaded  squat,  and  one-leg  squat.  When  squatting,  they
should hinge at the hips and maintain a neutral spine during
the  exercise.  Norms  (percentile  ranks)  for  the  squat  test
were obtained from a sample of high school football players.

Directions: Warm-up for this test by performing squats at
submaximal  weights.  Use  experienced  spotters  during  the
test.  Position  one  spotter  behind  the  lifter  if  the  weight  is
light  and  use  three  spotters‚Äîone  behind  and  two  on  the
sides‚Äîif the weight is heavy or the lifter is doing a maximum
lift. The athlete may wear wraps and a belt. Place the bar on
the rack so that the athlete has to lift it a few inches to begin
the  lift.  The  athlete  should  stand  with  feet  approximately
shoulder width apart. They should squat until their hips are
lower then their knees (thighs below parallel).

Athletes should push out of the bottom position following
the same path that they used during the descent phase. They
should keep their torso and back erect and maintain their
hips under the bar throughout the pushing phase of the lift.
The spinal muscles should contract statically to maintain a
stable spine. After the lift, spotters should assist the athlete

in placing the bar on the rack. They should make sure that
the  lifter‚Äôs  hands  are  clear  of  the  supports  when  reracking
the weight. Spotters should be vigilant when assisting with
this exercise.

Squat

Youth FitnessAssessing Fitness in Children  |  215

Overhead Press
The  overhead  press  (military  press)  is  a  good  exercise  if
athletes  use  good  form  and  do  not  overload  the  spine  or
shoulders. Reliable norms are not available for high school
athletes. If you want to use the overhead press test, measure
changes in strength with repeated tests.

Directions:  Grasp  barbell  from  rack  or  clean  the  barbell
from  floor.  Use  a  pronated  (overhand)  grip,  with  hands
placed  slightly  wider  than  shoulder  width  apart.  Position
the bar just above the collarbones. Press bar upward until
the  arms  are  extended  overhead.  Lower  to  the  bar  to  the
chest and then to the floor.

Dead Lift
The deadlift is an excellent measure of whole body strength.
It is highly related to the ability to sprint, jump, and throw.
However,  it  can  be  a  dangerous  test  for  untrained  young
athletes  who  do  the  lift  incorrectly.  The  exercise  involves
lifting a ‚Äòdead‚Äô weight from the floor to waist high. Athletes
should grip the barbell with a deadlift grip (one palm in, one
palm out), then lift the weight until they can stand upright
with the shoulders back, and then return the weight to the
starting position. This lift requires only a barbell and loaders.
A  leverage  bar  is  helpful  for  loading  and  unloading  large
plates. Reliable norms are not available for young athletes.
If  you  want  to  use  the  deadlift  test,  measure  changes  in
strength with repeated tests.

Leg Press
The  leg  press  is  the  most  common  and  perhaps  the  safest
way to measure leg strength in young athletes. Some experts
disagree.  The  exercise  requires  athletes  to  exert  maximal
force with the trunk in flexion, which puts excessive stress
on  the  spine.  In  addition,  the  available  norms  are  not
applicable to all leg press machines. The test is valuable for
gauging progress, particularly if leg presses are part of the
athlete‚Äôs training routine.

Directions:  Sit  or  lie  on  the  seat  or  bench,  depending  on
the  type  of  machine  and  the  manufacturer‚Äôs  instructions.
The  head,  back,  hips,  and  buttocks  should  be  pressed
against the machine pads. Loosely grasp the handles at the
side  of  the  machine.  Begin  with  your  feet  flat  on  the  foot
platform  about  shoulder  width  apart.  Extend  the  legs  but
do  not  forcefully  lock  the  knees.  Slowly  lower  the  weight
by  bending  the  knees  and  flexing  the  hips  until  the  knees
are  bent  at  about  a  90-degree  angle  or  the  heels  start  to
lift  off  the  foot  platform.  Keep  the  lower  back  flat  against
the support pad. Then extend the knees and return to the
starting position.

International Sports Sciences Association216  |  unit 14

Power Clean
The power clean is a popular strength test for high-power
athletes,  such  as  American  football  players  and  throwers.
The lift requires considerable skill, and athletes should not
attempt it without proper instruction or experience. Norms
(percentile  ranks)  for  the  power  clean  test  were  obtained
from a sample of high school football players.

Directions:  Athletes  should  place  the  bar  on  the  floor  in
front  of  their  shins,  with  feet  spaced  approximately  two-
feet  apart. They  should  use  a  pronated  grip  and  grasp  the
bar  with  their  hands  spaced  shoulder  width  apart.  They
should  squat,  keeping  the  arms  and  back  straight  and  the
head neutral. From the starting position, they should pull
the weight up past the knees to the chest while the hips and
shoulders move upward. After pulling the weight as high as
they  can,  they  should  bend  the  knees  suddenly  and  catch
the  bar  on  the  upper  chest.  Then,  they  should  stand  up
straight with the bar at chest level. After completing the lift,
they should return the bar to the starting position. The main
power for this exercise should come from the hips and legs.

MAINTAININg A gOOD
MEASuREMENT
PROgRAM
Measurement  is  central  for  gauging  progress  in  young
athletes. All athletes should keep a daily training diary, where
they record their weight, resting heart rate, and workouts.
The  personal  trainer  should  also  maintain  a  log  of  test
performances. Tests will vary by sport and age. They might
include  anthropometric  measures  (weight,  height,  girths),
endurance  (running,  swimming,  or  cycling  field  tests),
anaerobic  tests  (200-400  meter  run),  body  composition
(skinfolds or girths), flexibility (range of motion of specific
joints),  power  (power  quadrathlon,  Olympic  lifts,  vertical
jump, standing long jump), muscular strength (1-RM, grip
strength), muscular endurance (push-ups, pull-ups, lifting
endurance), speed (sprints), and agility (shuttle run).

Measure anthropometric measures frequently‚Äîat least once
a week. Perform more dynamic and exhausting tests every
four  to  six  weeks.  Make  measurement  an  important  part
of your program. It gives athletes and parents an objective
measure of progress and helps you gauge the effectiveness
of the workouts.

Power Clean

Youth FitnessAssessing Fitness in Children  |  217

about five or six tests and administer them often. Remember
the principle of specificity of training. Performance on the
squat, vertical jump, 12-minute run, or bench press is not
the same as playing the sport. However, performance tests
provide information on fitness and the effectiveness of the
conditioning program.

Measurement Frequency
Measuring  fitness  in  athletes  is  as  much  art  as  science.
Excessive  measurement  can  lead  to  burnout  and  injury.
Infrequent  measurement  can  result  in  lack  of  progress
and ignorance about the effectiveness of the program. This
chapter  presented  many  different  types  of  tests.  Choose

International Sports Sciences Association218  |  unit 14

gENETIC TESTINg IN ChILDREN
Gene variants called polymorphisms can give children a genetic advantage in physical
traits such as body composition, temperature regulation, speed, power, endurance, and
cardiorespiratory capacity and regulation. Several companies offer genetic screening to
identify traits that might provide an advantage in specific sports.

Are these tests valuable? While they are interesting, there are no genetic tests that will
identify a future Olympic champion in any sport. Excellence requires genetic advantage,
but personal ambition and drive, practice, good coaching, economics, and opportunity
are even more important. Children naturally gravitate toward activities that they enjoy
and  in  which  they  excel.  The  best  approach  is  to  give  kids  a  chance  to  play  a  variety
of  sports  and  see  which  they  like  best.  Parents  and  trainers  can  push  kids  in  specific
directions, but kids will never achieve elite levels without intrinsic motivation.

SuMMARY

Personal trainers should assess their young clients‚Äô fitness for comparison to estab-
lished norms to assess readiness for competition, to identify weaknesses, and to
assess progress. Literally hundreds of fitness tests exist to assess endurance, strength,
flexibility, power, speed, agility, and skill. Motor skills (and tests) are highly specific.
Young football, soccer, and field hockey players who perform well on endurance,
sprint, and strength tests will not necessarily be the best athletes in the sport. Sur-
prisingly, few physical fitness test norms exist for young athletes. However, national
norms established as part of the President‚Äôs Challenge provided information about
the relative fitness of a child. Also, strength, power, speed, and endurance tests for
specific athletic populations can help identify athletic talent and changes in physical
condition as a result of training. Laboratory tests, such as maximal oxygen con-
sumption, BodPod, and isokinetic dynamometry can provide precise evaluations of
fitness. However, more simple tests‚Äîsuch as sprints, pull-ups, the vertical jump, the
1.5 mile run, the standing long jump, and medicine ball throws for distance‚Äîpro-
vide just as much information as more sophisticated tests.

uNIT 15

# Structuring The Program

220  |  unit 15

Planning an effective exercise training program is as much
art  as  science.  Consider  the  athlete‚Äôs  age,  goals,  skills,
strengths  and  weaknesses,  likes  and  dislikes,  and  motiva-
tion.  Equipment  and  facilities  are  important‚Äîclients  will
not be able to swim if they do not have access to a pool or do
Olympic weight lifts if they cannot use free weights. When
designing a program, consider what is possible and what ex-
ercises the athlete will actually do. A scientifically sophisti-
cated training program is worthless if the athlete never goes
to the gym or will not cooperate.

Young  people  can  make  gains  on  almost  any  training
program.  At  least  in  boys,  physical  capacity,  strength,  and
power improve during growth without training. The impor-
tant thing is to stick with it. Plan a realistic, fun program,
and do it consistently. There is no single ‚Äúcorrect‚Äù training
program. Young athletes can gain considerable strength and
power  with  minimal  equipment.  Children  can  use  body
weight  as  resistance  or  gain  strength  doing  exercises  with
rocks, ropes, and elastic bands. Encourage athletes to learn
all they can about training from magazines, coaches, clinics,
and the personal trainer (you). Plan a program, try it, evalu-
ate it and then modify it. Soon, you will have a program that
works for your client. (Table 15.1)

DEvELOPMENTALLY
APPROPRIATE TRAININg
PROgRAMS
Children  are  not  miniature  adults,  so  their  training  pro-
grams  should  be  appropriate  for  their  age  and  maturity
levels.  Ideally,  physical  development  should  begin  during
early  childhood.  Children  should  have  the  opportunity  to
move, run, jump, throw, lift, and pull.

Many  gyms  have  movement  exploration  programs,  such
as  Kindergym,  that  provide  semi-structured  movement
experiences.  Children  can  climb  ladders  and  ropes,  carry
objects,  walk  in  sand,  run  up  and  down  hills,  jump  into
foam  boxes,  hang  from  bars,  and  tumble  on  mats.  These
programs allow children to learn movement skills and a love
for physical activity.

Parents  should  promote  physical  activity  and  discourage
sedentary activities from a young age. Hours spent in front
of  the  television  or  Internet  are  directly  related  to  excess
weight. Bad habits developed in pre-school can easily con-
tinue throughout childhood and adolescence.

It is best to begin conservatively. When your athlete can do
a minimal program, add exercises or train more intensely.
Consistency is the key. Follow the principles of training dis-
cussed in prior chapters and your athletes will likely achieve
their goals with a minimal risk of injury.

Developing training discipline takes years. Sports excellence
is  based  on  skill  development.  Emphasize  fundamentals
over competition in younger children. Competitive success
depends on competence in the sport. The age of specializa-
tion depends on the sport. Early training is critical in sports

Wednesday: 2 miles interval training on track: stride or sprint the straight-a-ways (100 meters),
jog or walk the turns; plyometrics (3 to 6 exercises); stretching.

Thursday: Rest.

Friday: Weight training (3 sets of 10 repetitions): squats or leg press, leg curls, cleans, bench
press, lat pulls or pull-ups, arm curls, crunches or sit-ups; stretching.

Saturday: Jog 2 to 3 miles in park, stretching after jog (5 to 10 exercises).

as much to do with the culture of these sports as it does with
physical  readiness.  In  swimming,  for  example,  older  ath-
letes‚Äîsuch as Dara Torres (who won three Olympic med-
als at age 41)‚Äîhave shown that elite athletes do not have to
retire at age 20. Training intensity can increase as children
develop  competence  in  the  sport  and  intrinsic  motivation
that makes them want to improve and win.

with walking up stairs (instead of taking the elevator) and
structured exercise at school with physically active house-

son  should  supplement  these  exercises  with  resistive  and
flexibility  exercises.  Combine  the  activity  program  with  a
well-balance  diet  high  in  fruits,  vegetables,  low-fat  dairy
foods, whole grains, and lean meats.

DESIgNINg ThE
TRAININg PROgRAM
First,  determine  the  young  person‚Äôs  goals.  If  the  goal  is
simply  to  improve  health,  then  the  program  will  be  quite
simple. If he or she wants to become a better skier, tennis
player, golfer, body builder, or discus thrower, then the pro-
gram becomes more complicated. Balance skill and fitness
development to improve sports performance.

hIgher levelS oF FITneSS
Children interested in higher levels of general fitness should
do some type of moderate to high intensity aerobic exercise
three to five times per week, resistive exercise two to three
times  a  week,  and  flexibility  exercises  five  to  seven  times
per  week.  If  your  client  wants  to  become  faster  and  more
powerful, incorporate interval training and power and plyo-
metric exercises in the program. The child can do the exercise
program at home, in a health club, or at the local track, park,
or pool.

general FITneSS For healTh
Fitness  for  health  was  described  earlier  in  this  section  of
the course. To summarize, try to do a total of  60 minutes
a day of some kind of exercise. The exercise does not have

Choose  aerobic  exercises  clients  enjoy.  Choices  include
walking, running, swimming, aerobic, cross-country skiing,
cycling, and sports such as tennis or basketball. Do aerobic
exercise for at least 20 minutes. If the client cannot perform
20 minutes at first, have him build up to it gradually.

International Sports Sciences Association222  |  unit 15

Children can do resistance exercises on the same day as they
do aerobic exercises. However, serious weight trainers often
do resistance and aerobic exercises on separate days. For ex-
ample,  an  athlete  might  run  on  Monday,  Wednesday,  and
Friday; lift weights on Tuesdays and Thursdays; and rest on
Saturday and Sunday.

Do  flexibility  exercises  at  the  end  of  the  workout.
Muscles are warm and will be much less susceptible to in-
jury. Be consistent with the stretching program. People de-
velop considerable improvements in flexibility by stretching
for as little as 10 minutes a day.

FITneSS ProgramS and
Power SPorTS
No  single  fitness  training  program  is  appropriate  for  all
sports.  This  section  presents  two  examples  that  serve  as
templates. Examples in Tables 15.2 and 15.3 show programs
for a recreational athlete (a skier) and a competitive athlete
(a football player). In general, during the off-season, devel-
op skill as well as general strength, endurance, and flexibility
fitness.  As  the  season  nears,  place  increasing  emphasis  on
skill, power, and speed.

The central goal is to develop movement skills for the sport.
A tennis player who can‚Äôt serve or hit a good forehand or
backhand will not do very well in a match, no matter how

good  his  or  her  physical  fitness  components  are.  Athletes
achieve the best results by systematically integrating fitness
and skill.

Skill Development
Young athletes should work on specific skills all year long.
This is important for competitive and recreational athletes.
If the sport is seasonal, try to work on skills at least two days
per week during the off-season. Make sure to practice the
correct movements. Repeating mistakes reinforces mistakes
and  poor  movement  patterns.  Remember  this  important
principle of sports training: ‚ÄúPerfect practice makes perfect.‚Äù

Do not let athletes use bad weather as an excuse. When it‚Äôs
raining, the tennis player can hit balls against a wall in the
gym, the golfer can work on the swing in an indoor range,
and the discus thrower can work on turns in the garage. If
all else fails, they can watch instructional videos on skiing,
golf, shot putting, tennis, football, volleyball, and softball.

During the season, increase the amount of time devoted to
practicing  skills.  During  the  golf,  softball,  tennis,  or  track
seasons, devote at least four days a week to skills practice.
Never  substitute  fitness  for  skill  development.  The  golfer
with a poor swing will shoot poorly even if he or she can
run fast or has a great bench press.

When combining sports and fitness, develop strength and
endurance during the off-season. As the season nears, place

more  emphasis  on  power,  speed,  and  plyometric  training.
During the season, the goal is to maintain strength and en-
durance fitness and master sports skills at full speed.

bodY buIldIng
Many  young  people‚Äîparticularly  high  school  students‚Äî
exercise so they can build and maintain healthy, attractive
looking  bodies.  The  basic  principle  behind  a  successful
bodybuilding  program  is  simple:  build  muscle  tissue  and
minimize body fat. This principle is the same for boys and
girls, men and women.

Bodybuilders generally do a high number of sets and rep-
etitions (5 to 10 sets of 10 to 15 repetitions) in a wide vari-
ety of exercises (10 to 25 exercises). Many successful body
builders also employ low repetition, high  weight exercises
for large muscle exercises such as squats and bench presses.
Many athletes will benefit from doing a single set of 8 to 12
repetition in 5 to 10 exercises.

Endurance training is important for young people who are
trying  to  improve  their  body  composition.  In  addition  to
promoting  health,  it  affords  an  effective  means  to  control
body  fat.  They  should  do  endurance  exercises  in  the  gym
on a stair climbing machine, treadmill, or stationary bicycle.
Many people enjoy outdoor activities such as jogging in the
park or cycling on the roads. (Table 15.4)

endurance FITneSS
Endurance  sports  are  extremely  popular  throughout  the
world. Endurance races, such as the Bay-to-Breakers in San
Francisco, the New York marathon, the Iron Man triathlon,
and  the  Western  States  100,  attract  thousands  of  contest-
ants.  Lesser-known  races  in  running,  cycling,  swimming,
triathlon,  snowshoeing,  cross-country  skiing,  and  roller-
blading are held in almost every community of any size in
America.  Most  of  the  contestants  are  not  Olympic  hope-
fuls. Rather, they are ordinary people who love endurance
competitions and high levels of fitness. Children and young
adults  have  also  been  attracted  to  endurance  sports.  They
often get their start in local fun runs before competing seri-
ously in the sport.

Endurance  athletes  should  practice  over-distance  training
to develop endurance capacity, interval training to develop
speed and pace, and weight training to develop strength and
power. During the off-season (the time of year when there
are few competitions), emphasize over-distance training. As
the season nears, include more interval training workouts.
Lift  weights  year  round.  During  the  competitive  season,
strive  only  to  maintain  strength  by  lifting  weights  one  to
two times weekly.

SuMMARY

There is no one best training program for everyone. Children are not miniature adults, so their training programs
should be appropriate to their age and maturity levels. Consider the athlete‚Äôs age, goals, skills, strengths and
weaknesses, likes and dislikes, and motivation when designing a training program. Plan realistic, fun programs for
children that they will do it consistently. Begin conservatively and then gradually increase the intensity and com-
plexity of the program. No single fitness training program is appropriate for all sports. Athletes achieve the best
results by systematically integrating fitness and skill.

uNIT 16

# Helping The Child Enjoy
# Sports And Physical Activity

230  |  unit 16

Athletics  and  physical  activity  can  be  among  the  most
valuable and cherished experiences in life, whether the child
becomes  an  Olympic  gold-medallist,  plays  third  string  on
the  high  school  football  team,  or  enjoys  a  run  or  walk  in
the  park.  Parents  and  personal  trainers  can  help  to  make
sports and exercise a positive experience by providing help
and  understanding  to  the  young  person.  Adult  guidance
is  the  same  for  both  serious  and  casual  participation  in
sports:  helping  the  child  to  achieve  his  or  her  goals  in  an
emotionally  healthy  environment.  To  the  national-class
athlete,  this  may  mean  coping  with  the  stress  of  intense
training and competition. To the average child, it may mean
developing skills in an atmosphere of fun.

Personal  trainers  who  work  with  young  people  must  be
applied  sports  psychologists.  They  should  strive  to  make
sports and physical activity enjoyable experiences that build
fitness and character. They must help children to cope with
emotional problems associated with training and try to keep
sports in their proper perspective. Children‚Äôs self‚Äìesteem is
vitally  important,  but  personal  trainers  and  coaches  must
instill in children that esteem comes from accomplishment
and that not everyone wins. Selecting the right sport is an
important part of insuring enjoyment and success.

hOW TO SELECT ThE
RIghT SPORT
It  is  crucial  that  children  select  a  sport  they  enjoy.  If  the
athletic  experience  is  enjoyable,  tremendous  achievement
will  not  be  a  necessity.  Participation  itself  produces  both
physical and psychological benefits.

It  seems  reasonable  that  a  certain  measure  of  success  is
usually  required  for  full  enjoyment  in  sport.  Athletics
usually caters to the physically gifted, and social pressures
exerted  on  the  less‚Äìskilled  and  uncoordinated  child  can
prove  overwhelming  (and  devastating  to  the  child).  The
parents,  personal  trainer,  and  child  should  consider  the
available options and the physical and emotional realities.

Determine the sport in which the child has potential. The
gym  class  serves  as  a  wonderful  proving  ground.  Can  the
child run fast? Is he bigger or stronger than the other kids?
Is she an early or late maturer? Is she good at any one sport
in  particular?  If  the  child  shows  good  performance  in
skills like catching, throwing, and running, he may wish to
consider  softball,  baseball,  basketball,  soccer,  field  hockey
(girls),  or  football  (boys).  In  contrast,  if  the  child  does
not  seem  as  coordinated  as  the  other  kids,  then  perhaps
an  alternative  such  as  weight  lifting,  distance  running,  or
swimming might be more appropriate.

Sports can be placed into one of three general types: team
and complex movement-oriented sports (basketball, soccer,
tennis,  and  football);  fitness-oriented  sports  (distance
running, weight lifting, and body building); and technique-
oriented sports (discus throwing, gymnastics, diving, golf,
and  figure  skating).  Some  overlap  exists,  but  the  general
categories can help you and the child select the right sports.

Complex movement sports are generally the most popular
and  most  highly  regarded  in  schools,  and  thus  the  most
competitive. A less‚Äìskilled child may be better off choosing
a  sport  from  one  of  the  other  categories.  However,  this
depends on the child‚Äôs desires. If a boy has always wanted
to be a football player, or a girl a basketball or tennis player,
do not discourage them. Often, there are junior varsity or
intramural teams that allow for participation of younger or
less‚Äìskilled children.

Success  in  sports  is  often  a  matter  of  sustained  focus.
A  popular  book  called  Outliers:  The  Story  of  Success  by
Malcolm Gladwell (Little, Brown and Company) contends
that  successful  people  must  devote  at  least  10,000  hours
of  focused  practice  to  achieve  success  in  sports.  Contrary
to  popular  belief,  they  do  not  have  to  possess  unusual
talent; they just have to be talented enough. Success comes
when  athletes  have  the  drive  to  achieve,  are  placed  in  an
environment  that  fosters  success,  and  devote  the  time
required to develop high skill levels.

Fitness-oriented sports offer the less‚Äìskilled child a chance
for  some  degree  of  athletic  success.  High  levels  of  success
at the national and international levels have certain genetic
prerequisites, yet any child can become proficient in sports
by  diligent,  hard  work.  Most  young  athletes  do  not  know
the meaning of the term hard work, and thus barely scratch
the surface of their potential. A child cannot rely on high
school  track  practice  or  the  weight  training  gym  class  to
attain  great  improvements;  they  must  practice  during  the
summer,  during  vacations,  and  on  weekends.  The  key  to
success in the less‚Äìskilled youngster is to work even harder
than the other kids. This requires self-discipline on the part
of the child and continued support from parents, coaches,
and the personal trainer.

Technique  sports  offer  unique  opportunities  for  athletic
success.  High  achievement  on  the  competitive  level  in
complex  technique  sports‚Äîhammer  throwing,  equestrian
games, diving, gymnastics, and fly casting‚Äîrequires years
of  practice.  But  many  of  these  activities  can  provide  the
smaller  girl  or  boy  with  a  chance  for  athletic  success  that
might  not  be  possible  in  football,  basketball,  track,  or
baseball.  It  is  sometimes  better  to  be  a  big  fish  in  a  small
pond than to be overwhelmed by the currents in the ocean.
Willie Sutton, the famous holdup man, was once asked why

Youth Fitnesshelping the Child Enjoy Sports and Physical Activity  |  231

he  robbed  banks.  He  answered,  ‚ÄúBecause  that‚Äôs  where  the
money is.‚Äù Technique sports can be where the success is. I
believe  participation  is  the  real  essence  of  sport,  and  that
participation is extremely satisfying if the child is successful
and good at the sport.

The PerSonal TraIner
can helP chooSe The
beST SPorT
Professional performance analysis can help the child assess
potential for various sports and pinpoint deficiencies. This
course includes test batteries that can help to assess power,
speed,  strength,  endurance,  and  body  composition.  They
are useful but not foolproof. Science can help to determine
sports  tendencies,  but  cannot  predict  absolute  success
or  failure.  Sports  medicine  can  distinguish  a  child  with
potential  for  endurance  or  speed  sports,  but  does  little  to
predict ability in technique sports. Technique requires years
of focused practice.

Years  ago,  an  all-star  professional  basketball  player  was
tested  in  an  exercise  physiology  laboratory  as  part  of  a
publicity  campaign.  The  local  newspaper  was  planning  to
print a story that compared the pro-basketball player with
the  average  weekend  jock.  This  was  the  question:  How  is

this  all-star  professional  different  from  the  average  guy?
The scientists tested both men using common physiological
measurements:  work  capacity  and  oxygen  uptake  on  the
treadmill, body fat and muscle mass, muscle strength and
power, flexibility, and muscle fiber type. Each of these tests
measured capacity rather than skill.

The findings were startling‚Äîthe weekend jock performed at
almost the same level on most of the tests as the pro player,
even though the basketball star was one of the top athletes of
his kind in the world. The truth is that the basketball skills of
the professional athlete were highly developed for basketball,
but lab tests could only measure fitness. The point is that lab
tests can be important for determining athletic capacity, but
should not be the only consideration in selecting a sport or
predicting success in the sport.

how Young Should
a chIld begIn
comPeTITIon?
The age at which a child should begin to compete depends
on the nature of the sport. Some sports require specialization
at a young age. For example, girls as young as 11 or 12 are
world-class competitors in gymnastics (they must be 14 to
compete in the Olympics). These girls begin their training

International Sports Sciences Association232  |  unit 16

at four to six years of age because early participation may
be essential for high-level performance in this sport. Older
teenage girls tend to have more fat and wider hips, factors
that can be a disadvantage in gymnastics. Football and track
and field events (discus, shot put, and hammer and javelin
throwing) require physical maturity for success.

Consider the following when helping a child choose a sport:

1.

Is early participation in sports in the interest of the
child‚Äôs emotional and physical health?

2.  Will early participation provide the child with
an advantage in skill development over other
children?

3.  Will early athletic participation compromise the
child‚Äôs development in other areas (other sports,
social development, music and art appreciation,
intellectual pursuits)?

Competition can compromise emotional health if begun too
early.  Early  childhood  sports  programs  should  emphasize
skill  and  fundamentals.  Winning  comes  naturally  when
athletes  have  developed  a  solid  foundation.  Preparation
and planning are the keys to winning and success in sports.
Children‚Äôs sports often seem to be conducted for the benefit
and gratification of adults. Young children are usually not
emotionally prepared for the adult standards they might be
exposed to in highly structured competitive athletics.

Competitive  athletics  are  not  necessarily  bad  for  children
under  age  10.  There  are  outstanding  programs  in  most
sports  for  young  children  that  stress  skill  development,
maximum participation, and fun.

Parents and personal trainers should scrutinize the prospective
team or athletic club and assess the organizational structure
in view of the following five factors:

1.  What is the nature of practices? Are they conduct-

ed with intelligence, knowledge, and humanity?

2.  How is the competitive structure organized? Is too
much emphasis placed on winning? At lower
levels, do all the kids get to play, or do better-
skilled children dominate games? Is too much
emphasis placed on awards, all-star games, and
championship participation at an early age?

3.  Does the structure of the program promote

development and excellence? Participation and
skill development are critical during the early
years of participation. Later, the program should
promote excellence through progressive develop-
ment for children who want to excel. This might
include competitions at the regional, national, and
international levels.

4.  Does the program consider maturational differ-

ences among children? Studies of athlete burnout
show that kids born later in the year (i.e., less
mature children) are less likely to achieve success
and more likely to suffer burnout in the sport.

5.  Who are the coaches? Are they professionals well

trained in skills, teaching, and child development?
Or are they parents who seem to be most inter-
ested in getting their own kids into the game?

Where  skill  is  concerned,  it  is  doubtful  that  participation
in  competitive  athletics  before  the  age  of  eight  or  ten  will
give the child the jump on other kids. For boys especially,
the rapid development of physical capacity and skill occurs
during puberty and adolescence. Children between the ages
of  five  and  ten  should  only  belong  to  organized  teams  if
those teams provide an atmosphere for learning a variety of
movement fundamentals and developing a love of the sport.

Some  sports  are  clearly  better  than  others  for  young
children. For example, gymnastics and soccer provide many
opportunities to develop fitness and movement skills, while
tennis  and  baseball  require  skills  that  five-  to  eight-year-
olds  do  not  have  the  maturity  to  develop.  Of  course,  you
want  the  child  to  learn  complex  ball-handling  skills.  But
these  are  developed  best  by  practicing  specific  basic  skills
and  not  necessarily  by  mere  participation  in  competitive
athletics. To become skilled, a child must specialize at some
point, but the specialization for most sports need not take
place before puberty.

Youth Fitnesshelping the Child Enjoy Sports and Physical Activity  |  233

Puberty  is  the  ideal  time  for  specialization.  Children  who
began  sports  like  skiing,  tennis,  or  golf  at  age  twenty  or
twenty-five are at a decided disadvantage compared to those
who  began  at  age  six.  Usually  the  gap  cannot  be  closed,
regardless  of  how  much  the  older  person  practices.  There
simply is no way a man of 60 who takes up golf, no matter
how skilled he may have been during his younger years, can
ever ‚Äúcatch-up‚Äù with the skills and proficiency gained by a
youngster who develops golf skills at age eight or nine and
plays regularly for the next 50 years! The child has the clear
advantage, and therein is the proof. Children who develop
basic skills at an early age and continue in their sport can
enjoy a lifetime of pleasure from regular participation.

are aThleTIcS SaFe?
Organized  sports  for  children,  particularly  junior-level
football  and  baseball,  often  receive  bad  press.  Much  of
this  criticism  is  unjustified.  Though  many  injuries  occur
in  high  school  and  college  football,  some  sports  critics
attempt to extrapolate the dangers of college football to little
league football. The argument goes like this: if football is a
dangerous sport for high school and college players, then it
must be even worse for young children with growing bones.
The truth is that the injury rate in young football players is
much lower than among high school, college, or professional
players.  Young  children  are  smaller,  slower,  and  generally
less  aggressive  than  older  athletes.  The  power  to  generate
the tremendous forces that can cause serious injuries in the
older players does not exist in junior-level players.

ProvIdIng a good SPorTS
exPerIence
Children sometimes specialize in a sport as early as ages 10
to 14. In general, kids should try a variety of sports for as
long as possible. No one can force a child to like and enjoy
sports.  Once  a  child  has  shown  an  interest,  though,  there
are  many  things  adults  can  do  to  help  make  the  athletic
experience positive.

The emoTIonal elemenTS
Remember that the child is the one involved in the sports
program, not you or the parent. Parents should not try to
relive  their  dreams  through  their  sons  or  daughters.  The
child should be central in the decision making process about
the degree of involvement in the sport. Some mistakes are
inevitable, but so is some measure of success. The bottom
line is‚Äîlet the child do it!

Consider  the  child‚Äôs  motivation.  Not  everyone  wants  to
be  an  Olympic  champion.  Some  kids  just  want  to  be  part
of  a  group  or  to  be  with  their  friends.  For  these  children,
participation in athletics is valuable even though they may
achieve little success.

Do not overreact to the situation. If the child expresses an
interest in being a ski racer, do not run out the next day to
buy $1500 worth of equipment and clothing. Children often
are  not  sure  what  they  really  want.  Provide  opportunities
for participation; if the child is genuinely serious about the
sport,  then  spend  the  money  on  equipment.  Once  a  child
is seriously involved in sports, buy the best equipment the
family  can  afford.  Competitive  sports  are  difficult  enough
without  the  athlete‚Äôs  being  hampered  by  poor  equipment.
A good pair of ice hockey skates can cost several hundred
dollars. Buying skates on special at a large department store
will  save  some  money,  but  it  might  also  be  the  cause  of
injury and discomfort for the child.

Perhaps  the  most  important  responsibility  a  parent  or
personal trainer has toward an athletic child is to provide
emotional support. It is difficult for a kid to sit on the bench
or perform poorly. Let the child know that you understand
the  problem.  Provide  guidance‚Äîsometimes  a  child  may
want  to  quit  the  team  when  things  get  tough.  Children
should learn to stick it out and overcome adversity. A little
understanding  and  direction  from  parents  can  keep  the
athletic experience in its proper perspective for the child.

International Sports Sciences Association234  |  unit 16

gIrlS and aThleTIcS
Although women and girls have many more opportunities
in sports today than in the past, there still is a great deal of
discrimination  and  stigma  attached  to  their  participation.
Athletics are a critical part of the education of all children,
not  just  boys.  For  example,  parents  should  insist  that  a
daughter (or, for that matter, anyone‚Äôs daughter) receive an
equal opportunity for athletic participation.

Girls should have the same quality of coaching as boys, but
forcing boys and girls to play on the same team is probably
not a good idea. Teenage boys are usually physically superior
to teenage girls. If there is only one team and it is coed, most
girls will not get a chance to play. If a girl is good enough
to  play  on  a  boys‚Äô  team,  she  should  have  the  opportunity
to do so; otherwise, she should play for a girls‚Äô team. Girls‚Äô
teams should not be forced to play on second-rate fields or
treated  differently  in  any  other  way,  and  this  includes  the
opportunities they are afforded at fitness and health clubs. If
disparities exist, become a vocal advocate for kids‚Äô equality
in sports participation.

TeachIng KIdS To
wIn and loSe

Athletics can be the most positive and enjoyable experience
of  a  lifetime.  Young  athletes  develop  friendships  and
psychological  benefits  that  carry  over  into  adulthood.
Competition  is  an  unavoidable  part  of  sports,  and  an
unavoidable  part  of  life.  Children  must  learn  to  enjoy
winning and cope with losing. Many athletes sacrifice almost
everything to win, only to have their hopes shattered. Defeat
can discourage and break some athletes. You must teach the
child to shake it off and move on to something else, or try
again. Fortunately, in sports, there always is another day for
another competition.

There is a price to pay for both success and failure in athletics.
But with the proper preparation and mental attitude a child
can  cope  with  both.  The  parent  and  the  personal  trainer
can  help  to  keep  things  in  their  proper  perspective  by
encouraging a healthy attitude.

Youth Fitnesshelping the Child Enjoy Sports and Physical Activity  |  235

Keeping things in perspective with the encouragement of a
healthy attitude:

1.  Teach children that winning or losing has little to

do with their worth as people. If they lose, they
are not bad people, and if they win, they are not
better than anyone else.

2.  Success requires sacrifice and hard work. Top

performance in any sport requires long hours of
training. Everybody who makes it to the Olympics
or a professional sports team has worked extreme-
ly hard. The secret of success in athletics is to work
hard, train intelligently, get good coaching, and
make the most of natural ability and luck.

3.  Tell kids to always do their best. As Bear Bryant,

the great football coach said, ‚ÄúMy 90-percent
player playing 100 percent will beat your 100-per-
cent ball player playing 90 percent every time.‚Äù
Concentrate on what you are doing. Leave noth-
ing to chance; if there is a weakness, correct it.

4.  Be in the best possible condition. Out-of-shape

athletes are in trouble, no matter how skilled they
are. Getting in top shape takes work. The divi-
dends are a reduced risk of injury and an
increased ability to learn basic movement skills.

5.  Teach kids to learn from their mistakes. Analyze
their technique and teach them to become as
efficient and skillful as possible.

The rIghTS oF
Young aThleTeS
The  National  Association  of  Sports  and  Physical  Education
has outlined ten basic rights of children involved in athletics:

1.  Right of the opportunity for participation in sports

regardless of ability level

2.  Right to participate at a level commensurate with

each child‚Äôs development level

3.  Right to have qualified adult leadership

4.  Right to participate in safe and healthy environ-

ments

5.  Right of each child to share in the leadership and
decision-making of their sports participation

6.  Right to play as a child and not as an adult

7.  Right to proper preparation for participation in

the sport

8.  Right to an equal opportunity to strive for success

9.  Right to be treated with dignity by all involved

10.  Right to have fun through sport

International Sports Sciences Association236  |  unit 16

SuMMARY

Personal trainers are in a unique and important position to help children enjoy
and benefit from sports. They can help them to choose the right sport and
maximize their chances for success. They can also help them to handle winning
and losing and teach them how to learn from their mistakes.

Seven key points in this section include:

1.  Hard work and dedication are the great equalizers in athletics. The
child who consistently works hard will eventually realize success.

2.  Success and enjoyment in athletics require selecting the right sport.

Selection should be based on preference, talent, body size, family
finances, and availability and practicality of the sport.

3.  Early competition is not usually in a child‚Äôs best interest. Sports

participation before age twelve should stress the development of basic
movement skills and fundamentals, rather than competition.

4.  Parents and personal trainers should encourage and help children get

fit and maintain fitness all year long.

5.  Personal trainers and parents should help children to keep athletics
in proper perspective. Shortsighted decisions may be detrimental.
Encourage children to develop intellectually, socially, and athletically.

6.

Insights from sports medicine and science can help children to im-
prove. Modern developments in equipment and training methods
enhance performance and future enjoyment in sport and physical
activity experiences.

7.  Girls should have the same opportunities for athletic participation

as boys.
