Summary

uNIT 17

# Psychology Of The Champion

238  |  unit 17

Part  of  long  term  athletic  development  of  children  in-
volves  teaching  them  training  discipline,  competitiveness,
and sportsmanship. Discipline includes the ability to train
systematically, work on mistakes, and become a student of
the  sport.  Competitiveness  includes  building  high  levels
of skill, learning to overcome adversity, setting short-term
and  long-term  goals,  learning  to  win  and  lose  with  grace,
learning from defeats, and striving for success. Becoming a
student of the sport involves learning training methods and
technique and understanding the process.

ThE WINNINg EDgE
Competitive  sports  serve  as  one  of  the  ultimate  proving
grounds  of  human  beings.  Competition  brings  together
exquisitely  prepared  men  and  women  in  a  pressure-cook-
er  atmosphere.  All  of  them  are  trying  to  achieve  the  same
thing:  victory.  The  winners  are  those  best  prepared  both
physically  and  mentally.  The  line  between  success  and
failure  is  sometimes  no  more  than  a  hundredth  of  a  sec-
ond or a few millimeters. The great champion always seems
to give that little extra that achieves the margin of victory,
no  matter  how  small  it  may  be.  Sports  immortals  such  as
Michael  Jordan,  Michael  Phelps,  Bruce  Jenner,  Al  Oerter,
Tiger Woods, and Jesse Owens have that special ‚Äúsomething‚Äù
that sets them apart and makes them great. What are these
mysterious psychological ingredients that come together to
constitute a winner? Why is it that a person who may hold
the world record and completely dominate an event or sport
will sometimes fold in the Olympics or Super Bowl, while an-
other person‚Äîperhaps injured and less physically skilled‚Äî
will rise up in major competitions for a great victory?

In  a  major  competition  like  the  Olympics,  about  six  or  so
athletes at the Olympic starting line have good chances; but
out of those six, one knows he or she is going to win. And
there is a big difference between wanting to win and knowing

you will win. Merely making a high school, college, Olym-
pic,  or  professional  team  requires  tremendous  drive  and
single-minded  dedication  to  one‚Äôs  sport.  Champions  must
exhibit exceptional mettle in the company of so many tough-
minded, highly skilled competitors. The personal trainer can
help children  begin  to develop the  mental attitude it takes
to succeed in sport. Every child cannot make the Olympic
team, but every child can learn to think like a winner.

Successful American athletes are criticized in some circles
because of their lifestyles: total devotion to their sport often
signals an expense to their careers, their family and, in some
instances, their health. Yet this lifestyle is the inevitable con-
sequence of the requirements of world-class performance.
Olympic competition is so severe that to become compla-
cent will mean the loss of the chance to compete.

Much of the appeal of sports stems from the fact that ath-
letes are forced to face the Moment of Truth. Sports places
success and failure directly before the public eye. Few en-
deavors of life hold achievement‚Äîor lack of it‚Äîso blatantly
visible. Such an environment demands a realistic perspec-
tive and eventually will weed out aspiring athletes who may
be unable to face the truth about their limited abilities.

ThE PSYChOLOgY OF ThE
ChAMPION AThLETE
Top-caliber  sports  have  been  called  the  ‚ÄúPTA  Club‚Äù‚Äîfor
Pain,  Tears,  and  Agony.  Sports  psychologists  have  studied
successful  athletes  in  great  detail  and  have  found‚Äîwith
few  remarkable  exceptions‚Äîthat  champion  level  athletes
are  extremely  stabile  emotionally.  The  survival-of-the-
fittest atmosphere of elite sport, coupled with the physically
exhausting  training  and  the  requisite  psychological  per-
spective  of  sports  at  this  level,  demands  a  great  degree  of
mental and emotional toughness even to participate.

Dr. Bruce Ogilvie (1920-2003), the grandfather of North American sports psychology who taught at California State Uni-
versity at San Jose, described the mental and emotional character of the champion caliber athlete: ‚ÄúSuccessful athletes
are achievement-oriented people who derive personal satisfaction from striving. High achievement needs are based upon
personal attitudes about the probability of success or failure associated with each investment of self or ego. All things
considered, the outstanding athlete is at his very best when the odds are slightly against him. Ambitious people derive
slight joy, if any, when their ability remains uncontested. The great athletes I have interviewed do not dwell upon their
losses but concentrate on that part of their performance that limits their excellence.

Great athletes have a great need to be on top, and show as a group an exceedingly high need to be successful.‚Äù Dr.
Ogilvie continued, ‚ÄúThey have a greater need for freedom and self-direction, preferring to make their own judgments as
to what‚Äôs good for them. They have a greater need for the spotlight than the average person and enjoy receiving more
attention and acclaim. Champion athletes seem to have an easier time expressing aggressive tendencies and standing
up for what they believe‚Äîthey have a greater tendency to fight for their point of view. Their ability to engage in abstract
thinking placed them in the upper 15 percent of the population. Champion athletes have a greater awareness of reality;
they ‚Äútell it like it is.‚Äù

Youth FitnessPsychology of the Champion  |  239

PSYchologIcal dIFFerenceS among aThleTeS
Sports  scientists  have  noted  few  psychological  differences
between  male  and  female  athletes.  Women  in  sports  tend
to  be  less  dominant  than  men,  but  more  impulsive,  more
aware of danger, more apt to punish themselves when they
lose, and more concerned with other people. These differ-
ences may stem from pressures on women athletes, however
subtle,  to  conform  to  traditional  ideas  of  femininity.  The
typical male ‚Äúwinning-at-all-cost‚Äù philosophy is considered
unfeminine by many segments of our society. Women are
not  supposed  to  sweat  and  get  strong.  Although  women
have more opportunities than ever in sports, culturally de-
fined role models of masculinity and femininity are slow to
change. Any pressures that are rooted in these traditionalist
roles can exert many negative effects on female athletes by
increasing the odds against success in top-level competition.

Most  sports  psychology  studies  show  little  difference  in
character  profile  among  athletes  in  various  sports.  There
are  exceptions,  however.  Runners  and  race  walkers  tend
to be more introverted and more insecure than other ath-
letes. There are also differences in character between medal
winners  and  non-medal  winners.  Probably  the  greatest
quality unique to medalists is their almost complete absence
of physical fear. This trait has obvious benefits for the cham-
pion. Breaking the pain threshold is usually associated with
top  performance‚Äîthe  winner  has  the  ability  to  maximize
his or her ability by teetering on the brink of disaster.

The  character  traits  of  typical  female  athletes  set  them
apart from most average women: they need and seek high
goals  and  high  achievement.  They  have  strong  desires  to
accomplish something of significance. They thrive on being
in the spotlight, and enjoy being at the center of attention.
They  have  a  strongly  developed  sense  of  independence
and autonomy and an ability to express aggression without
feeling  guilt.  Such  women  tend  to  be  tough-minded,  self-
assured,  self-confident,  very  trusting,  and  self-assertive.
They measure slightly above average in emotional maturity
and  rarely  experience  feelings  of  depression.  In  general,
the  character  profiles  of  successful  female  athletes  are
remarkably similar to their male counterparts.

Olympic  medal  winners  are  different  in  other  ways:
medalists  tend  to  be  more  independent,  less  inclined  to
want  to  be  members  of  a  group,  more  self-reliant  and
self-disciplined,  and  more  ambitious.  Many  medalists  are
stimulus addicts‚Äîthey have a much greater need to bring
change into their lives with new and different experiences.
This  trait  is  particularly  important  for  younger,  less
experienced athletes because it enables them to achieve one
goal and immediately set a new, higher one. Making a team
(high school, college, pro, or Olympic) can be a great shock
in  itself.  It  can  be  difficult  to  quickly  shift  goals  to  win  a
medal or produce at championship play levels.

Other  athletes  showing  deviations  from  the  norm  are
those in high-risk sports. This includes Alpine skiers, who
plummet  down  icy  mountainsides  at  speeds  exceeding  75
miles per hour, and athletes in X-Games. These performers

International Sports Sciences Association240  |  unit 17

excel in tests of abstract reasoning. They can derive a form
of  psychic  ecstasy  by  tempting  fate  during  sports  and  are
very  aware  of  the  risks  they  take.  Often  these  athletes  are
loners, but they also are leaders and expect to be treated as
such. In general, they are very together people.

There is little evidence to suggest that participation in high-
level athletic competition can actually benefit mental health.
Most  athletes  are  emotionally  stable  because  they  entered
sports that way in the first place. Four-time Olympic discus
gold medal winner Al Oerter showed his tough, competent
mental approach to sport when he said, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt play men-
tal games. I prepare myself for competition as best I can. I
never make any excuses. If I have any weak points, I correct
them. Once I‚Äôm in a big competition, I just concentrate on
doing the best I can‚ÄîI don‚Äôt worry about anything else.‚Äù

Athletes  have  to  be  psychologically  sound  to  survive  the
pressures of high-level sports, but the stress of competition
can sometimes be overpowering. About 40 to 50 percent of
Olympic athletes feel excessive stress concerned with their
performance. The psychologist can sometimes help athletes
deal with stress and anxiety and enable them to reach full
potential. People in sports are individuals first and athletes
second. The quest for athletic excellence must never be at the
expense of emotional health. People should not let accom-
plishment in sport become a disguise for personal growth.

ThE ELEMENTS OF SuCCESS
One  of  the  best  descriptions  of  the  psychological  factors
necessary  for  Olympic  success  was  made  by  John  Powell,
four-time Olympian, two-time bronze medal winner in the
discus throw, and former world record holder. He threw the
discus more than 236 feet when he was 40 years old. Powell
is an inspiration to aspiring young athletes because he has
gone a long way on much less natural physical ability than
his competitors. He continues to help young people through
his throwing clinics held every year at Dennison University
in Ohio (www.johnpowellassociates.com).

Through hard work and self-confidence, Powell developed
almost  flawless  technique  and  a  reputation  for  coming  up
with  a  crucial  performance  when  it  was  needed.  He  said,
‚ÄúThe psychological tenacity necessary for success in sports
can be broken down into three simple factors: belief, vision,
and method. Belief is self-confidence knowing your worth
as an athlete and knowing you can do well. Vision is your
goal.  You  have  to  know  what  you  want  in  order  to  get  it.
Method  is  the  process  you  use  to  fulfill  your  vision‚Äîhow
you train and how you handle yourself in the pressure and
excitement of competition.‚Äù

belIeF
Belief  means  having  confidence  in  your  abilities.  Self-
confidence  is  perhaps  the  athlete‚Äôs  most  important  mental
attribute.  You  must  have  it  for  success.  Self-confidence
takes  a  long  time  to  develop;  it  is  something  you  must
learn.  Self-confidence  is  based  on  accomplishment,  and
succeeding  in  a  series  of  short-term  goals  develops  it.  As
your  trainees  experience  a  number  of  small  victories  and
accomplishments, they will begin to realize that everything
is possible. Then, the only thing that can stop from getting
what  they  want  is  themselves.  If  they  do  not  believe  they
can  achieve  something,  there  is  no  way  they  will  do  it.
Confidence or belief is the cornerstone of athletic success‚Äî
or success in almost any endeavor.

Belief is critical in sports where athletes are constantly up
against  unexpected  situations  and  each  person  is  out  for
himself. Athletes must have confidence. The competition is
out  to  win  any  way  they  can.  Athletes  must  feel  they  can
take  care  of  themselves‚Äîwhatever  happens‚Äîand  must
have  confidence  in  their  own  judgments.  They  must  have
the  courage  to  choose  a  strategy  and  stick  to  it.  Without
that  kind  of  self-confidence,  they  are  vulnerable  and  their
competitor will psyche them out.

The psychology of athletic competition is often overt. Many
athletic  champions  use  intricate  psychological  tactics  to
thwart  the  self-confidence  of  opponents.  The  psych-out

Youth FitnessPsychology of the Champion  |  241

can be complex or simple. It can be applied a minute before
competition  or  it  can  be  continued  carefully  over  several
days, months, or even years. It can be directed against a single
rival or against the entire field. The techniques vary, but the
object is always the same: convince the competition that you
are certain to win the competition and they are going to lose.
In a psych-out, you always make a show of confidence while
you work to undermine the confidence of your competition.
In one of the great team ‚Äúpsych-outs,‚Äù two Nordic ski teams
were going head to head in a final competition. One team
with  Olympic  medalists  was  a  heavy  favorite  against  an
excellent but less experienced neighboring team. Four days
before the competition, the coach of the underdogs planted
a tube in the snow with a mysterious looking black and silver
canister  attached  to  it  at  the  site  of  the  training  runs.  His
athletes took turns ‚Äúbreathing‚Äù from the canister just before
their training workouts, easily viewed by the competitors (the
coach  planned  it  that  way). This  practice  with  the  ‚Äúsecret‚Äù
device continued during the last two days of practice. A local
sports scribe, in interviewing the coach, asked him what it
was.  The  coach  replied,  ‚ÄúI  really  can‚Äôt  tell  you  the  details,
but it boosts athletic performance by 20 percent. Every time
the athletes breathe from the tube, it supercharges their red
blood cells, boosting their skiing power.‚Äù This made the local
papers and heightened interest in the competition. Everyone
was talking  about the ‚Äúsecret training weapon.‚Äù Years after
the  competition,  won  by  the  slimmest  of  margins  by  the
underdogs,  the  coach  at  his  retirement  party,  revealed  he
had concocted the story about the device giving his athletes
a competitive edge: it had been a total fabrication. The key
points  were  first,  the  underdog  athletes  believed  to  their
core that it gave them an edge against the world champions;
and  second,  the  former  champions  came  to  believe  they
were  at  a  distinct  performance  disadvantage  when  in  fact
they  were  not.  The  ploy  had  worked‚Äîa  classic  example
of  enhancing  self-confidence  to  achieve  excellence  (with
negative self-confidence and just a hint of doubt instilled in
the opponents, even in athletes who were used to competing
at the Olympic level).

Few  athletes  are  born  with  strong  self-confidence  and  an
ironclad  belief  system.  Rather,  belief  is  part  of  a  develop-
ing process that takes many years to mature. Athletes must
learn to overcome mental barriers. They must learn to be-
lieve their goals are possible. The personal trainer or coach
is absolutely essential for instilling a strong belief system in
the young athlete. The coach or personal trainer can be in-
strumental  in  the  athlete‚Äôs  development  of  self-confidence
and vision. You have to help the athlete develop short-term,
easily achieved goals. A strong belief system develops with a
series of small successes.

You must strive to make each athlete feel like you are there
for them and no one else. Tell them they are going to be suc-

cessful and make them believe you. Give them enough con-
fidence, and they will not worry about winning. They will
realize that if they train properly, they will achieve success.
This way, success becomes a natural extension of thorough
preparation and hard work.

Experience is the key to genuine self-confidence: experienced
athletes can put athletics in their proper perspective. They
know their capabilities better, and they are much better able
to deal with pain. They become students of their sport and
learn quite a bit about the theory behind training methods
and about the physiology and psychology of athletics. Newly
acquired  information  and  knowledge  combines  to  instill
confidence. Younger or less experienced athletes are usually
not very confident because they have not had enough time
to learn the sport.

Experienced  athletes,  almost  without  exception,  know
themselves  extremely  well.  Those  who  have  been  around
for  a  while  are  certainly  more  philosophical  about  their
sport; they have had more time to think and learn about it.
Almost  all  the  great  athletes  have  positive  attitudes.  Most
of them believe they are capable of anything in almost any
aspect of their life‚Äînot only in sports, but also in whatever
they  might  attempt.  They  are  extreme  optimists.  Great
athletes extend their belief and positive self-image into their
performance‚Äîand they win because of it.

International Sports Sciences Association242  |  unit 17

vISIon
Vision  is  knowing  what  you  want  to  achieve.  Many
potentially great athletes never make it because their vision
was not strong enough. Vision, like self-confidence, takes a
long time to develop; in fact, the two things go hand in hand.
John  Powell  related  a  recent  experience  of  a  young  long-
jumper  who  told  him  he  was  going  to  make  the  Olympic
team and break the world record. He was jumping only 23
feet at the time. Powell asked him, ‚ÄúWhy don‚Äôt you shoot for
23 feet-6 inches before dreaming about winning an Olympic
gold  medal?‚Äù  He  was  not  being  realistic‚Äîpie-in-the-sky
beliefs lead many athletes into early retirement. The young
man did not realize that vision is built upon a series of small
steps. As you learn your capabilities, you adjust your goals
accordingly. I think it is important to enjoy the process of
development. Reaching the Olympics is a hollow victory if
you did not enjoy the trip ‚Äúup.‚Äù

Vision involves not only identifying goals, but also accepting
what  is  necessary  to  achieve  them.  Champions  are  often
described as having a ‚Äúkiller instinct.‚Äù That instinct may be
the  special  something  that  provides  that  extra  margin  for
victory. Whether this inner drive is inborn or inbred is not
known; however, it is an important ingredient in vision.

In his autobiography First Four Minutes, first sub-4-minute
miler  Roger  Bannister  described  the  mental  attributes
of  John  Landy,  the  great  miler  from  Australia;  ‚ÄúLandy
demonstrated a character capable of the greatest kindness,
gentleness  and  thoughtfulness,  and  on  the  other  side‚Äîas
there always must be‚Äîa ruthless lack of feeling for others
and a ferocity and antagonism, although it is mostly vented
on himself, that makes it possible on occasions for John to
rise to sublime heights of physical endeavor.‚Äù

During  the  early  stages  of  an  athlete‚Äôs  career,  vision  can
come from a variety of places‚Äîparents, coaches, personal

Youth FitnessPsychology of the Champion  |  243

trainers, and other athletes. However, the time must come
when  an  athlete‚Äôs  goals  are  inner-motivated.  The  training
is  too  hard,  the  competition  too  stiff,  and  the  stress  too
great for anything but intrinsic incentive. A world-class or
professional athlete will seldom make it to the top because
of trying to win for parents, coaches, or even her country.
These things may be factors in motivation, but in the end
each athlete must want more than anything to succeed for
himself or herself.

meThod
Method is the process of achieving vision. Most people are
familiar  with  the  physical  methods  of  athletics:  obviously,
consistent dedication to hard work is necessary to achieve
elite-level  success.  Mental  agility  also  is  necessary  for
that  kind  of  achievement.  Efficient  training  programs,
competitive  tactics,  an  athlete‚Äôs  coachability,  and  the
ability  to  employ  psyching  techniques  are  all  part  of  the
psychological aspect of athletics.

John  Powell  discussed  his  method:  ‚ÄúSelf-confidence  and
vision mean nothing if you can‚Äôt deliver the goods. Athletics
are so competitive that to be successful, you have to use your
practice time effectively. You can‚Äôt leave anything to chance.
I analyze every aspect of my technique and physical capacity.
I try to maximize all the strengths I have. There are a lot of
athletes who are much stronger than I, and the only way I
can beat them is by having better technique and by being
as  fast  as  possible  in  the  circle.  Changing  your  technique,
even by nuances, takes years; I will work on something until
I‚Äôve mastered it. I feel that I have the best technique of the
world  class  throwers‚ÄîI  have  to  because  they‚Äôre  all  bigger
and stronger than I am.‚Äù

Powell went on to say, ‚ÄúA lot of athletes aren‚Äôt as successful
as they could be because they don‚Äôt think. They tend to work
on their strong points rather than develop their weaknesses.
I  see  some  discus  throwers  who  can  bench  press  almost
600 pounds concentrating on gaining upper body strength
when they should be working on their throwing. They have
plenty of strength‚Äîwhat they need to do is work on their
poor technique.‚Äù

The  successful  champion  must  be  able  to  adjust  to  many
conditions  and  employ  many  different  strategies  and
methods  of  achieving  the  same  end‚Äîvictory.  This  ability
was aptly described by distance running great Ron Clarke
who  said:  ‚ÄúThe  distance  running  champion  who  sets  a
world record has to have a tough psychological approach to
his running. The Olympic, European, and Commonwealth
meets are harder to win than other meets because there are
more  people  with  similar  ability  who  may  just  happen  to
click under the tactics you use. When you can concentrate

on  dropping off just three or  four people in  a race,  you‚Äôre
almost  certainly  going  to  beat  them.  But  when  there  are
maybe a dozen people all around the same standard, your
tactics to defeat three or four may not fit so many. This is
what often happens in the big ones nowadays.‚Äù

Method relates to belief and vision: the ability and daring
to try certain strategies and tactics depends on an athlete‚Äôs
self-confidence  and  goals.  In  First  Four  Minutes,  Roger
Bannister shows this relationship as he describes a runner‚Äôs
decision to break from the pack and sprint to victory: ‚ÄúThe
decision to break away results from a mixture of confidence
and  lack  of  it.  The  breaker  is  confident  to  the  extent  that
he suddenly decides the speed has become slower than he
can  himself  sustain  to  the  finish.  Hence  he  can  accelerate
suddenly  and  maintain  his  new  speed  to  the  tape.  But  he
also lacks confidence, feeling that unless he makes a move
now, everyone else will do so and he will be left standing.‚Äù

Bryan  Clay,  decathlon  gold  medalist  in  the  Beijing  Olym-
pics, had all of the psychological prerequisites for success.
Bryan is a master technician who approaches the decathlon
meticulously. He weight trains from 7 to 9 A.M., visits with
his wife and children from 9 to 10 A.M., and then trains for
his events from 11 A.M to 3 P.M. He spends hours watching
videos of great athletes in each of the ten events and com-
pares them with his own technique. He makes changes and
then watches more videos. His strongest events are the 100
meters, long jump, 110-meter hurdles, shot-put, and discus,
but his technique is excellent in all the events. His basic ap-
proach to sports training is a good blueprint for any athlete:

Stay  healthy.  Sports  requires  fitness  and  specific  skills.
Pushing  too  hard  before  the  body  is  ready  will  cause  in-
jury. Young athletes should use good body mechanics and
try to maintain good posture and a neutral spine when they

International Sports Sciences Association244  |  unit 17

lift,  run,  throw,  jump,  and  sprint.  Good  technique  doesn‚Äôt
hurt. Also, they should not train intensely before the body is
ready. The easiest way to get injured is to train too hard, too
fast. Most sports injuries are training errors.

keep a training diary, which includes a structured program
for the next six to eight weeks, details of the training pro-
gram,  and  specific  goals  and  objectives.  A  training  diary
might consist of little more than a spiral-bound notebook.

Get in shape gradually. Peak performance in sports takes
years. Children should give their bodies a chance to adapt
to  the  rigors  of  training  when  attempting  to  build  general
fitness.  This  takes  time;  children  do  not  increase  strength,
power, endurance, and speed overnight. The body adapts at
its own rate and changes occur naturally as individuals grow.

Plan  the  program  meticulously.  The  trainer  should  help
the child develop a maturationally appropriate, structured,
scientifically based training program that improves perfor-
mance  progressively.  The  same  is  true  for  children  trying
to  develop  well-rounded  fitness.  Structure  the  program  to
emphasize specific types of fitness on different days. In high
skill sports such as tennis or volleyball, schedule activities
so that the fitness program compliments skill development
rather than inerferes with it.

All  serious  athletes  keep  a  training  diary‚Äîa  detailed
account of their training program, diet, and performances.
Young  athletes  who  are  serious  about  their  sport  should

Try  to  make  greater  gains  with  less  work.  Great  athletes
have economical training programs. Children can develop
well-rounded fitness using one to two-hour workouts if the
program is structured intelligently.

Work out consistently. Ninety percent of success in life is
showing up. That certainly applies for improving fitness and
sports performance. Young athletes should try not to miss
workouts and should include planned rest days.

Develop the mental toughness of a champion.  Elite  ath-
letes are extremely disciplined. They train even when they
are sore and tired. Young athletes should develop the mental
toughness to exercise and train, even when they do not feel
like it. A workout that seems impossible today will be easy
after several months of training.

Belief, vision, and method remain inseparable. Each serves
as an essential ingredient in the formula for athletic success,
and  together  they  comprise  the  inner  drive  of  the  true
champion. Although these character traits are hereditary to

Youth FitnessPsychology of the Champion  |  245

a degree, they can be developed to their fullest only through
single-minded  devotion  to  sports  and  perseverance‚Äîa
lifetime  of  perseverance.  In  reality,  athletic  champions
are  no  different  from  men  and  women  who  make  great
fortunes  in  the  business  world:  athletes  and  professional
people  alike  must  have  tremendous  focus  and  tenacity  to
succeed.  But  while  business  offers  the  lure  of  money  and
power, in sports these are only secondary. The real reward
of athletic excellence is less tangible. Even participation in
a  field  of  extraordinarily  talented,  intensely  competitive
athletes is a goal in itself. But for one athlete to weather the
competition and rise to victory against all the opponents‚Äî

that  is  something  else  again.  Olympic  gold  medal  winner
Bruce  Jenner  described  it  best  when  he  said  that  winning
the decathlon brought him a special kind of satisfaction: ‚ÄúIt
was everything.‚Äù

SuMMARY

Competitiveness includes building high levels of skill, learning to overcome adversity, setting short-term and long
term goals, learning to win and lose with grace, learning from defeats, and striving for success. The personal trainer
can help children begin to develop the mental attitude it takes to succeed in sport. Every child cannot make the
Olympic team; but every child can learn to think like a winner.

Sports psychologists have studied successful athletes in great detail and have found‚Äîwith a few remarkable excep-
tions‚Äîthat champion-level athletes are extremely stabile emotionally. The psychological tenacity necessary for
success in sports can be broken down into three simple factors: belief, vision, and method. Belief is self-confidence,
knowing your worth as an athlete and knowing you can do well. Vision is your goal. You have to know what you
want in order to achieve it. Method is the process you use to fulfill your vision‚Äîhow you train and how you handle
yourself in the pressure and excitement of competition. The champion must be able to adjust to many conditions
and employ many different strategies and methods to achieve success. Athletic champions are no different from
men and women who make great fortunes in the business world: athletes and professional people alike must have
tremendous focus and tenacity to succeed.

Nutrition is even more important to children than it is to adults. Not only do they have to
eat nutritious meals that provide energy and keep them disease-free, but their meals must
also supply the building blocks for growth. The personal trainer must give information
that helps the child perform better on the playing field, while considering the nutritional
demands of growth.

Every day, we are flooded with myriad pieces of dietary information seeking to improve
our eating habits. Dietary information on food containers lists the nutritional contents
of the foods we eat. Diet books preach the latest weight loss methods. Sports nutrition
companies  try  to  sell  the  latest  formula  ‚Äúguaranteed‚Äù  to  improve  performance.  Such
information  is  confusing.  Personal  trainers  need  to  know  the  principles  of  basic
nutrition and weight control in order to help young clients in this critical area of health
and  performance.  Dietary  information  is  important  because  good  nutrition  lays  the
foundation for health, appearance, and performance.

Poor diet is linked to many degenerative diseases. Proper diet can help to prevent many
health problems such as coronary heart disease, some cancers, diabetes, and obesity.
Nutritional factors such as dietary fat, fiber, and vitamins greatly affect health, longevity,
and well being.

Most people desire a healthy, lean looking body. You probably will never achieve this goal
unless you pay attention to the foods you consume. If you eat a lot of high density, fatty
foods, it will be difficult to achieve the kind of body you want‚Äîno matter how much
you exercise. You must combine sensible eating and exercise habits to achieve the kind
of body you want.

Sports and exercise performance depends on good nutrition. The energy to run, jump,
throw, and even rest comes from the foods we consume. Active young athletes need a
good  diet  to  exercise  and  play  sports  effectively.  Knowing  what  to  eat  and  when  can
determine whether you are running on empty during the day or have plenty of energy
to work and play.

Many  young  athletes  search  for  a  magic  nutritional  formula  to  help  them  feel  and
perform better and control their weight. Consequently, they often take food supplements
and  drugs  in  the  hope  of  reaching  their  goals.  The  performance  nutrition  industry  is
extremely  competitive  and  markets  a  wide  variety  of  products  that  promise  to  build
muscle,  enhance  performance,  and  help  people  attain  the  perfect  body.  How  do  you
separate reality from hype?

coronary heart (artery)
disease: Atherosclerosis‚Äîa
condition in which the coronary
arteries become narrowed or
completely blocked. This is the
main cause of a heart attack.

diabetes (mellitus): Con-
dition marked by relative or
absolute lack of insulin leading
to uncontrolled carbohydrate
metabolism. In Type 1 diabetes,
the insulin deficiency is almost
total, while in Type 2 diabetes,
the defect is related more to
insulin resistance, obesity, and
lack of exercise.

Youth FitnessNutrition for health and Performance  |  251

This section of the course presents an overview of nutritional principles for health and
performance. Topics include the elements of the healthy, high performance diet, diet and
weight control, food supplements, and performance enhancing drugs.

ESSENTIAL NuTRIENTS
The six types of essential nutrients you need in your diet include fats, carbohydrates,
proteins, vitamins, minerals, and water. The body either does not produce these or
makes them in insufficient quantities. You must include each component in your diet to
stay alive and healthy.

The body derives energy from fats, carbohydrates, and proteins, while vitamins, minerals,
and water help to regulate energy processes in your body. Nutrients drive metabolism‚Äî
the  total  of  all  the  chemical  reactions  occurring  in  your  body.  Running,  jumping,
throwing, skiing, skipping, lifting, and walking all require energy. This energy is supplied
by food. Proteins and fats also help to create body structures such as muscle, bone, and
cell membranes.

energY
The ability to convert chemical energy from fuels to muscle energy (muscle contraction)
largely determines exercise performance. As you deplete your body‚Äôs energy stores, you
fatigue  rapidly,  and  performance  decreases.  Active  people  fight  fatigue  by  consuming
enough  high-quality  calories  in  their  diet  and  restoring  vital  energy  stores  in  muscles
and the liver. Children always seem to work at a high energy level, so they need to eat a
high performance diet.

Fats  provide  the  most  energy,  9  kcal  (calories,  usually  expressed  as  kilocalories)  per
gram, while carbohydrates and proteins each supply 4 kcal per gram. Alcohol, which is
not an essential nutrient, supplies 7 kcal per gram. During the past 20+ years, experts
have advised people to cut down on the fats they eat. High fat diets, in addition to being
high in calories, also increase the risk for a variety of diseases. New studies show that
people lose weight faster on low carbohydrate‚Äìhigh fat, protein diets. While that may
be  true,  such  diets  are  inappropriate  for  children  involved  in  sport  or  high  intensity
exercise programs.

Children‚Äôs energy requirements vary with age. Between ages 7 and 9 years, the average
child should eat about 1800 kcal per day. This jumps to 2100 kcal between ages 10 and
12, moves to 2500 kcal for boys and 2200 kcal for girls from ages 13 to 15 years, and rises
to 2200 kcal for girls and 3000 kcal for boys from ages 16 to 18. Of course, the caloric
requirement will be higher if the child plays high-energy sports.

On any given day, the average adult requires about 2200 kcal. Adults who are more active
or larger than normal need more calories‚Äî2800 kcal or more for women and 3400 kcal
for men. When you take in more calories than you use through metabolism or exercise,
excess calories are stored as fat.

Energy intake is important, even among people trying to lose weight. Starving yourself
and over-exercising can adversely affect health, decrease performance, and make you feel
miserable. When trying to lose weight, the best strategy is to decrease your caloric intake
slightly so weight is lost slowly. Weight loss principles are discussed later in this unit.

Most  people  take  in  more  than  enough  energy  to  supply  their  daily  physical  needs.
Over  eating  and  under  exercising  are  main  reasons  why  more  than  one-half  of  the
United States adult population is overweight. Under-consumption can be a problem in

up of complex combinations of

(kcal). 1 kcal = the amount of
energy required to increase the
temperature of 1 liter of water 1
degree C.

International Sports Sciences Association252  |  unit 18

extremely active people. Nutritional studies of athletes show that the majority of them
do not consume enough calories. Calories are the most important nutritional factor for
exercise  performance.  Inadequate  caloric  intake  can  lead  to  loss  of  body  weight  and
muscle mass, depletion of carbohydrate stores in the muscles and liver, and sometimes
low blood sugar during training. This can be a critical concern in children because they
will have insufficient energy to supply the body‚Äôs energy needs for sports or growth.

FaTS
Fats are the most energy rich food source. Fats‚Äîalso called lipids‚Äîare mainly found in
adipocytes (fat cells). The body also stores small amounts of fat in skeletal muscle cells.
Many people try to carry as little fat as possible. However, it is far from being a useless
tissue‚Äîit serves as the body‚Äôs main energy storage depot. Fat cells release chemicals, such

adipocytes: Specialized cells
that makes and store fat.

cell membrane: Structure
surrounding a cell and enclosing
the cytoplasm that forms a bar-
rier that allows some substances,
such as oxygen, fuels, and
wastes, through its walls. It is
made of fats, proteins and some
carbohydrates.

triglycerides: Storage fats in
adipose tissue, largely made of
glycerol and fatty acids.

saturated fat: Fatty acid with
all potential hydrogen binding
sites filled. These pose the high-
est risk for the development of
atherosclerosis.

monounsaturated fat: Fatty
acid chain with at least two
empty spaces where a hydrogen
atom could be added. Saturated
fatty acids present a greater
health risk for the development
of coronary artery disease.

polyunsaturated fat: Fat that
contains more than one carbon-
carbon double bond.

as leptin, that help regulate appetite and metabolic rate. Fat also protects and insulates
the  internal  organs,  comprises  part  of  vital  structures  such  as  cell  membranes,  and
serves  as  building  blocks  for  many  important  hormones.  Dietary  fats  help  to  absorb
some vitamins and serve as a vital energy source for most tissues. They help to regulate
several important body functions, such as blood pressure, and are critical for optimal
fetal development during pregnancy.

While there are several types of fats, the most important are triglycerides and cholesterol.
Triglycerides  are  composed  of  glycerol  and  three  fatty  acid  molecules.  Fatty  acids
are  classified  according  to  their  structure  as  saturated,  monounsaturated,  or
polyunsaturated. Fats are called ‚Äúsaturated‚Äù if they contain high amounts of saturated
fatty  acids.  Foods  high  in  saturated  fat  include  meats,  such  as  hamburger  and  steak;
lunchmeats;  cheese;  butter;  and  whole  milk.  Monounsaturated  and  polyunsaturated
fats come from plants such as olives, peanuts, corn, soybeans, and sunflowers. Palm and
coconut oil, although from plant sources, are highly saturated. Food manufacturers can
also  ‚Äúpartially  hydrogenate‚Äù  vegetable  fats,  which  produces  trans  fats.  They  do  this  to
improve  the  texture  of  food  and  to  increase  the  shelf  life  of  their  products.  Trans  fats
contain  no  essential  fatty  acids,  and  consuming  them  increases  the  risk  of  coronary
artery disease. Several countries and several US states and cities have banned the use of
trans fats in preparing restaurant foods.

Youth FitnessNutrition for health and Performance  |  253

Most people consume 30 to 40 percent of their calories as fat. The body needs only a
fraction of this‚Äîabout a tablespoon of vegetable oil a day. The high fat diet increases
serum  cholesterol,  a  leading  risk  factor  for  coronary  heart  disease  and  some  types  of
cancer.  Nutritionists  recommend  that  fat  intake  be  less  than  30  percent  of  calories
consumed; some recommend even lower levels of fat intake.

Children  should  restrict  the  amount  of  saturated  and  trans  fats  in  the  diet.  Better  fat
sources include  olive oil,  high in monounsaturated fatty acids,  and  most  kinds of fish
(e.g., salmon, trout, and mackerel), which are high in omega-3 fatty acids. Omega-3 fatty
acids are thought to reduce the risk of coronary heart disease.

Consumer  information on food  containers  makes  it  relatively  easy  to  track  fat  intake.
The most important thing to look for on the label is percent of calories from fat. Total fat
intake for children 4 to 18 years of age should be between 30 to 35 percent of calories,
emphasizing  polyunsaturated  and  monounsaturated  fatty  acids  in  foods  such  as  fish,
nuts, and vegetable oils. Moderation is the key. If one food or meal contains more fat
than is desirable, balance it with food or a meal containing less than 30 to 35 percent fat.

carbohYdraTeS
Carbohydrates are the high performance fuel. They are the most important energy source
during exercise. Also, the brain, nervous system, and blood cells depend on them as their
fuel source. Your capacity for exercise and mental processing diminish quickly when you
run out of this critical fuel.

Carbohydrates are classified as simple and complex. Simple carbohydrates contain only
one to two sugar units per molecule, while complex carbohydrates consist of long chains of
sugar molecules. Sweet-tasting foods such as table sugar and honey are examples of simple
carbohydrates, while high fiber foods‚Äîsuch as fruits, some vegetables, and grains‚Äîare
complex carbohydrates. A diet high in dietary fiber is essential to good gastrointestinal
health and the prevention of colon cancer. Nutritional experts recommend an emphasis
on complex carbohydrates in the diet.

The  body  controls  blood  sugar  levels  by  absorbing  carbohydrates  from  food  and  by
breaking down stored carbohydrates. The latter process is particularly important when
you  need  energy  quickly,  especially  during  exercise.  Important  hormones,  including
insulin, glucagon, and epinephrine (adrenaline), control blood sugar. These hormones
activate when the energy level of your body changes, such after you have eaten a meal
or during exercise. While these hormones are critical to blood sugar regulation, they are
ineffective unless they have fuels to work with. The body gets the carbohydrates it needs
through the diet.

Carbohydrates are stored in cells as glycogen, which is a mass of blood sugar (glucose)
units linked together. The liver and muscles store most of the body‚Äôs glycogen. During
exercise, the most important carbohydrate source for muscular work is muscle glycogen.
The  body  uses  approximately  six  times  more  muscle  glycogen  than  blood  sugar  to
run, ride a bicycle, or lift weights. However, blood  sugar  is  important because it fuels
the  nervous  system.  Motivation  suffers  without  a  well-functioning  brain  and  nervous
system‚Äîno matter how much glycogen is stored in the muscles. Children should not
neglect  blood  sugar  when  considering  which  fuels  the  body  will  use  during  exercise.
Muscle  glycogen  may  provide  most  of  the  fuel  for  muscle  movements,  but  the  blood
glucose supplies the brain and nervous system.

Blood  sugar  can  also  be  manufactured  in  the  liver  from  substances  such  as  amino
acids,  lactate  (lactic  acid  without  the  acid),  and  pyruvate.  This  process  is  called

glycogen: Compound consist-
ing of grouped glucose units
that is an important fuel source
for high intensity exercise. It is
found in high quantities in the
liver and muscles.

amino acids: Nitrogen-con-
taining compounds that serve as
building blocks for proteins.

lactate: Important as the
terminal product of anaerobic
glycolysis (breakdown of glu-
cose or blood sugar). It is a very
important fuel for the body, but
it can interfere with metabolism
and cause fatigue.

pyruvate: Important as a
product of anaerobic glycolysis
(breakdown of glucose or blood
sugar).

gluconeogenesis: Chemical
process in the liver that converts
chemicals, such as lactate, pyru-
vate, and alanine, into glucose.

gluconeogenesis.  Fuel  sources  flowing  into  the  liver  via  the  blood  stream  enter  the
liver  where  it  converts  them  into  blood  sugar.  Through  this  process  and  by  breaking
down  liver  glycogen,  your  body
can  maintain  reasonably  high
levels  of  blood  sugar,  even  during
prolonged fasting.

During  exercise,  blood
sugar
control  processes  allow  blood
sugar  to  increase.  If  you  begin
an  exercise  session  with
full
glycogen  stores  in  your  liver,  you
can  maintain  elevated  levels  of
blood sugar (compared to rest) for
several  hours  of  exercise  before  it
begins to decline.

Unfortunately,  the  liver‚Äôs  storage
capacity is limited. When you‚Äôre on
a prolonged hard training program and don‚Äôt eat enough calories or carbohydrates, liver
glycogen stores can run low. Studies of athletes involved in heavy training show that due
to poor diet, liver glycogen levels are often inadequate. High carbohydrate diets are best
for maintaining adequate glycogen stores in the liver. Eating a good breakfast is the best
way of replenishing liver stores after the long night without any fuel intake.

ProTeInS
Proteins are the body‚Äôs most important structural material, making up much of its muscle,
bone, enzymes, some hormones, and cell membranes. Proteins are made of substances
called amino acids. Of the twenty amino acids in foods, nine are considered essential
in the diet because the body can‚Äôt make them. Essential amino acids include histidine,
isoleucine, leucine, lysine, methionine, phenylalanine, theonine, tryptophan, and valine.

Protein supplements are very popular with active young athletes, particularly those who
lift weights. However, most people take in much more protein than they need. The daily
protein requirement is 0.8 grams of protein per kilogram body weight. People who are
extremely active may need slightly more protein. However, since most American athletes
consume more than 1.5 grams of protein per kilogram body weight, a protein deficit in
the  diet  is  rarely  a  problem.  Active  children  need  plenty  of  protein‚Äîabout  1.5  grams
per kilogram body weight‚Äîbecause they must supply the demands for growth and the
protein  requirements  for  exercise
and post-exercise tissue building.

Proteins  are  important  for  people
involved  in  endurance  exercise.
The
liver  can  convert  amino
acids  to  blood  sugar  through  a
process  called  gluconeogenesis.
This  term  literally  means  making
new  glucose  or  blood  sugar.
Gluconeogenesis  is  an  important
way  the  body  maintains  blood
sugar during the hours in between
meals.  Amino  acids,  the  building

Youth FitnessNutrition for health and Performance  |  255

blocks of proteins, break down slowly and act like ‚Äútime-released blood sugar tablets.‚Äù
The  proteins  in  your  meals  helps  maintain  blood  sugar  for  many  hours  and  prevent
hunger sensations.

Amino acid use during exercise, particularly of the branched chain amino acids leucine,
isoleucine, and valine, increases the longer and faster you exercise. Protein in your meals
can help restore depleted amino acids.

Eating a protein supplement before or immediately after weight training increases the
rate at which amino acids travel into the muscle and speeds the rate at which muscles
build new protein. Have the athlete get into the habit of eating a protein energy bar (made
by many companies) before or after weight training. This is a simple sports nutrition tool
that promotes protein synthesis.

vITamInS and mIneralS
Americans spend an incredible amount of money on vitamin and mineral pills. Yet, the
only common documented deficiency in the United States is iron deficiency. It appears
that  with  a  couple  of  possible
exceptions,  anything  more  than  a
balanced diet and, perhaps, a basic
vitamin pill is useless and a waste
of money.

Vitamins act as co-enzymes (they
work  with  enzymes
to  drive
body‚Äôs  metabolism)  and  aid  in
production  and  protection  of  red
cells.  While  vitamins  are  not  produced  in  the  body  and  must
consumed in the diet, they are are required in extremely small amounts.
the body‚Äôs vitamins, only vitamin C, thiamin, pyridoxine, and riboflavin
affected by exercise. Of these, only vitamin C supplementation has been
shown to improve performance, and that was in vitamin C deficient adolescents.

Vitamin C supplementation has been a fertile area of debate since Linus Pauling suggested
mega-doses  of  the  vitamin  as  a  cure  for  the  common  cold.  His  contention  has  been
extremely controversial, and, in general, medical studies have not supported his claims.
Recent studies have shown a link between vitamin C intake and immune function. This
could  be  good  news  for  active  people  because  preventing  the  down-time  from  illness
can be just as effective as discovering a new ergogenic aid to enhance performance. It is
certain that the debate regarding vitamin C and health will continued for many years.

may  be  recommended  in  case  it  really
does  boost  immune  function.  As  long
as excessive amounts of the vitamin are not consumed, the extra vitamin C
probably won‚Äôt hurt. However, there does not appear to be any justification for
the mega-doses of vitamins taken by many athletes and active people.

free radicals: HIghly reactive
molecules which target your tis-
sue‚Äôs protein bonds, the DNA in
your cells‚Äô nuclei and the impor-
tant polyunsaturated fatty acids
within your cells‚Äô membranes.
Once initiated, a chain reaction
begins that ultimately results in
the total destruction of that cell.

Vitamins C and E may also help protect the body from destructive chemicals called free
radicals. During normal metabolism, your body produces chemicals called free radicals
that cause aging, degenerative diseases, and a breakdown of the immune system. Free
radicals can be thought of as biological rust that attacks vital cell membranes and genes.
Substances  called  antioxidants  fight  free  radicals  and  help  prevent  aging  and  diseases
such as hardening of the arteries (atherosclerosis). Vitamins C and E act as antioxidants
that  help  eliminate  free  radicals  and  protect  your  body  from  their  destructive  effects.
Scientists  are  just  starting  to  learn  about  the  relationship  between  vitamins  and  free
radicals.

waTer
Water  makes  up  sixty  percent  of  the  body.  It  is  vitally  important  for  controlling  your
metabolism, digesting food, delivering substances to your cells, cellular communication,
and temperature regulation. Even a two percent drop in body water will affect exercise
performance‚Äîparticularly in children.

In addition to plain water, water is found in almost all foods, particularly fruits, vegetables,
and liquids. While your body can make water as part of metabolism, 80 to 90 percent of
daily  water  turnover  comes  from  your  diet.  You  lose  water  every  day  in  urine,  sweat,
feces, and evaporation from the lungs.

Water  and  salt  intake  affects  your  body  water.  Your  body  divides  its  water  between
cells, blood, and the area outside the cells. Dehydration upsets the balance and disrupts
communication between different parts of the body and results in poor coordination and
muscle stiffness. The body also loses salt from sweating. This can cause muscle cramps
because dehydration speeds muscle fatigue‚Äîthe leading cause of cramps.

The best fluid replacement beverage is cold and contains some energy and electrolytes.
Most  commercially  available  fluid  replacement  products  are  specially  formulated  for
athletes and active people. Athletes should attempt to replace fluid losses during exercise.
During  recovery,  weight  lost  through  dehydration  should  be  repaired  before  the  next
practice or competition.

ThE hEALThY, hIgh-PERFORMANCE DIET AND MYPLATE
‚Ä¢  Grains: 6 oz; at least 3 oz should come from
The  basic  principles  of  good  nutrition  include  eating  a
variety  of  foods,  eating  a  balanced  diet,  and  eating  in
moderation. It is difficult to improve upon sensible eating
habits for maximizing the effects of a fitness program and
maintaining a trim, attractive body.

‚Ä¢  Vegetables: 2 1/2 cups per day from a variety

whole grains

of sources

‚Ä¢

Fruits: 2 cups per day from a variety of sources

In  2010,  the  United  States  Department  of  Agriculture
and  the  U.S.  Department  of  Health  and  Human  Services
issued dietary guidelines for healthy Americans. The report
stated,  ‚ÄúOn  average,  Americans  of  all  ages  consume  too
few vegetables, fruits, high-fiber whole grains, low-fat milk
and  milk  products,  and  seafood  and  they  eat  too  much
added  sugars,  solid  fats,  refined  grains,  and  sodium.‚Äù  The
organization  made  several  important  recommendations
and goals that apply to children:

‚Ä¢  Reduce the incidence and prevalence of over-

weight and obesity of the US population by re-
ducing overall calorie intake and increasing physi-
cal activity. Children should reduce consumption
of high sugar soft drinks, grain deserts, solid fats,
and foods containing added sugar.

‚Ä¢

‚Ä¢

Shift food intake patterns to a more plant-based
diet that emphasizes vegetables, cooked dry
beans and peas, fruits, whole grains, nuts, and
seeds. In addition, increase the intake of seafood
and fat-free and low-fat milk and milk products
and consume only moderate amounts of lean
meats, poultry, and eggs.

Significantly reduce intake of foods containing
added sugars and solid fats because these dietary
components contribute excess calories and few, if
any, nutrients. In addition, reduce sodium intake
and lower intake of refined grains, especially
refined grains that are coupled with added sugar,
solid fat, and sodium.

‚Ä¢  Meet the 2008 Physical Activity Guidelines for

Americans. Children age 6 to 18 years old should
exercise 60 minutes or more every day‚Äîinclud-
ing mostly moderate or vigorous intensity aerobic
exercise. Children and adolescents should do
vigorous-intensity activity on at least three days
per week and do muscle-strengthening and bone-
strengthening activity on at least three days per
week.

In  2011  the  guidelines  included  ‚ÄúMyPlate‚Äù,  suggests  the
elements  of  a  well‚Äìbalanced  diet.  The  healthy  diet  plays
a  critical  role  in  any  fitness‚Äìnutrition  program.  The  diet
provides  all  of  the  known  nutrients,  reduces  the  risk  of
coronary  artery  disease  and  some  types  of  cancer,  and
provides  enough  energy  to  sustain  a  vigorous  training
program. The balanced diet consists of at least three meals a
day of foods chosen from five basic food groups (quantities
from 2000 kcal per day diet):

‚Ä¢  Meat and beans: 5 1/2 oz per day from low-fat or

lean meats and poultry

‚Ä¢  Milk, yogurt, and cheese: 3 cups from low fat or
non-fat sources (2 cups for kids ages 2 to 8)

MyPlate was designed to persuade people to eat fewer meat
and dairy products containing cholesterol and saturated fats
and to eat more cereals, grains, fruits, and vegetables. The well-
balanced diet suggested in the pyramid provides all known
nutritional  requirements  and  satisfies  the  Recommended
Daily Allowances (RDA) for important nutrients. Because
it is possible that all nutritional requirements have not been
identified, people should consume a variety of foods from
the basic food groups. Remember, all the food supplements
in the world are not going to make up for a poor diet. You
cannot get optimal nutrition from a pill!

Milk and milk products are important sources of calcium,
riboflavin,  high-quality  protein,  carbohydrate,  fat,  and
other assorted vitamins and minerals. Protein foods, such
as meats, chicken, and fish, in addition to providing protein,
also  supply  iron,  thiamin,  riboflavin,  niacin,  phosphorus,
and zinc. Avoid eating meats with a high fat content because
they are associated with increased risk of heart disease.

Whole  grain  foods  supply  energy,  thiamin,  iron,  niacin,
and cellulose (fiber). These foods are critical for satisfying
the  energy  requirements  of  a  vigorous  exercise  program.
Fruit and vegetables are important for supplying vitamins,
minerals, and fiber. It is particularly important to eat dark
green  and  deep  yellow  vegetables  because  of  their  high
nutrient content and their possible influence in reducing the
risk of certain types of cancer.

  make half your plate fruits and vegetables.

  make at least half your grains whole grains.

  Switch to fat-free or low-fat (1%) milk.

Foods to reduce

  compare sodium in foods like soup, bread, and frozen meals ‚Äï and choose the foods with lower numbers.

  drink water instead of sugary drinks.

DIET, ExERCISE, AND WEIghT CONTROL
The goal when advising young athletes about losing weight
is to encourage them to maintain muscle mass and lose fat.
This  is  not  as  easy  as  it  sounds.  Most  people  want  instant
results. Children may try to lose weight lose in any way they
can‚Äîparticularly  girls  and  young  women.  Unfortunately,
the  results  are  rarely  satisfactory  when  people  try  to  lose
weight rapidly.

Rapid weight loss causes muscle loss. When a person loses
more  than  three  pounds  a  week,  40  to  50  percent  of  the
that  will  be  from  lean  body  mass.  Your  muscles  are  being
used for food by your starving body. That is the last thing
children need when trying to develop a fit, healthy body.

PrIncIPleS oF loSIng
weIghT For acTIve PeoPle
Weight loss is a worldwide obsession. Many older children
exercise in the hope of keeping their waistline under control.
However, a program that depends solely on exercise or diet
is doomed to failure in the long run. Individuals must eat a
healthy diet and exercise regularly if they are to achieve a fit,
healthy looking body.

program  demands  caloric  restriction.  Exercise  alone  is
rarely  effective.  Fat  loss  occurs  when  expended  energy
exceeds food intake.

The goal of a weight control program should be to lose body
fat and maintain the loss. Quick-loss programs often result
in the loss of muscle tissue and do nothing to instill healthy
long-term dietary habits that will maintain the new weight.
Also, ‚Äúyo-yo‚Äù diets are unhealthy and contribute to coronary
heart  disease.  There  are  several  principles  for  losing  body
fat that will increase the chances of success for your weight
control program.

Avoid Fad Diets
Rapid weight loss from fad diets usually causes loss of muscle
mass and water. Therefore, fat loss rather than weight loss
should  be  the  goal.  Each  pound  of  fat  has  3500  kcal.  The
energy requirement of the average active person (depending
on size, sex, and activity level) is typically between 2000 and
5000 kcal per day. Fat loss occurs slowly, even on starvation
diets.  Radical  diets  cause  more  than  fat  loss‚Äîthey  also
trigger muscle and water loses too.

The  body‚Äôs  energy  balance  determines  whether  body  fat
increases,  decreases,  or  remains  the  same.  Kids  gain  fat
when they eat more energy through meals than they burn
through  exercise  and  metabolism.  While  exercise  is  an
important part of a weight control program, the successful

Lose Weight Slowly
Lose no more than 1 kg (2.2 lb) per week. More rapid weight
loss results in the loss of lean tissue. Creating a mild caloric
deficit  through  moderate  caloric  restriction  and  physical

Youth FitnessNutrition for health and Performance  |  261

activity  promotes  fat  loss  and  preserves  muscle  or  water.
The weight stays off better. Muscle loss slows metabolism,
which  reduces  the  need  for  food.  When  people  return  to
their normal diet after having lost muscle through a rapid-
weight-loss diet, they gain weight rapidly because they no
longer need as much food as before.

type exercise. Studies have also shown that fats break down
faster during the post-exercise period after a high intensity
exercise work out. High intensity exercise also builds muscle
mass more than slow endurance training. The bottom line is
that both prolonged endurance and high intensity exercise
are beneficial for weight control in overweight children.

Stress  a  balanced  diet  that  is  relatively  high  in  complex
carbohydrates  and  low  in  fat.  Create  a  caloric  deficit  by
combining  caloric  restriction  with  more  exercise.  Take  in
enough  protein  to  help  control  appetite  and  maintain  the
muscles‚Äô needs. For active children, this is approximately 1.5
grams of protein per kilogram of body weight a day, a ratio
which helps sustain training and growth.

Exercise  is  critically  important  for  a  successful  weight
loss  program.  For  the  average  person,  the  best  exercises
for  losing  weight  include  running,  walking,  and  cycling.
Exercise increases your metabolism both  during and after
exercise, which causes you to burn calories at a faster rate
all  day  long.  Also,  exercise  helps  maintain  muscle  mass,
which is critical for maintaining metabolic rate. If you can
maintain your muscle mass during weight loss, you will be
much more likely to maintain your new weight.

The  best  exercises  for  promoting  fat  loss  are  prolonged
endurance  exercise.  However,  doing  intense  exercise  is
also beneficial. High intensity exercise increases your post
exercise metabolism more than doing long, slow endurance

Monitor Body Composition
Make sure that most of the weight loss is from a reduction
in  body  fat  rather  than  a  reduction  in  lean  body  mass.
The  best  way  to  do  this  is  with  an  underwater  weighing
or  BodPod  test.  These  are  widely  available  in  university
exercise physiology laboratories, health clubs, and medical
facilities.  Some  children  have  trouble  with  these  tests,  but
most  don‚Äôt.  Every  active  person  should  know  his  or  her
body composition.

If you don‚Äôt have access to underwater weighing or BodPod,
other  methods  are  available.  Perhaps  the  best  is  skin  fold
calipers. Almost every health club or high school physical
education department has these. Another popular methods
include  bioelectrical  impedance  and  infrared  methods.
These  techniques,  while  technologically  advanced,  are  no
more accurate than skinfolds. Use these techniques as guides
and rough indicators. The trained eye is just as effective in
evaluating body composition as sophisticated instruments.

International Sports Sciences Association262  |  unit 18

Avoid Weight Loss Aids
The  grocery  and  drug  store  shelves  are  full  of  weight  loss
products. Research has shown these over-the-counter drugs
are  largely  ineffective.  FDA  approved  weight  loss  drugs,
available  by  medical  prescription,  are  only  marginally
effective.  And  almost  all  the  patients  who  use  these  gain
back the weight they lost when they stop taking the drugs.
Also, the drugs have side effects, such as gas and abdominal
cramping. The best advice is to stay away from drugs that
promise to help you lose weight. They probably don‚Äôt work.
If they do, they may damage your health.

Only  orlistat  is  currently  approved  for  weight  loss  in  the
United  States,  but  it  is  expensive  and  can  have  a  negative
effect. Ephedrine-caffeine supplements were widely popular
with  young  athletes  for  boosting  energy  and  promoting
weight  loss,  but  were  banned  by  the  FDA  because  they
caused severe side effects.

The  best  strategy  for  body  composition  management  is  to
encourage young athletes to lose weight slowly. Teach them

to  change  their  body  composition  gradually  so  that  they
gain muscle and lose fat. Rapid weight loss cause muscle and
body water loss, which will impair health and performance.

ThE POLY-DRug AND
FOOD SuPPLEMENT PhE-
# Nomenon In Sports
The  effects  of  the  large  variety  and  combination  of
supplements  and  drugs  used  by  active  people  makes  it
extremely difficult to determine the efficacy of these products
or  to  predict  the  side  effects.  There  are  numerous  reports
in the literature that describe catastrophic side effects from
unsafe drug use and nutritional supplementation. Trainers
should  not  recommend  these  products  to  young  athletes
because we do not know much about their long-term side
effects or effectiveness. Children will get the best results by
following a sensible diet and training program and avoiding
dubious and potentially dangerous drugs and supplements.

SuMMARY

Sound nutrition is vital for physically active children because nutritious meals provide energy, prevent disease,
and supply the building blocks for growth. Active young athletes need a good diet to exercise and play sports
effectively. Knowing what to eat and when can determine whether they are running on empty during the day or
have plenty of energy to work and play.

The six types of essential nutrients children need in their diet include fats, carbohydrates, proteins, vitamins, min-
erals, and water. Children‚Äôs energy requirements vary with age. Between ages 7 and 9 years, the average child
should eat about 1800 kcal per day. This jumps to 2100 kcal from ages 10 to 12, move to 2500 kcal for boys
and 2200 kcal for girls from ages 13 to 15 years, and rises to 2200 kcal for girls and 3000 kcal for boys from ages
16 to 18. Children should restrict the amount of saturated and trans fats in the diet. Total fat intake for children
4 to 18 years of age should between 30 to 35 percent of calories, and emphasize polyunsaturated and mono-
unsaturated fatty acids in foods such as fish, nuts, and vegetable oils. Emphasize complex carbohydrates in the
diet. Active children need plenty of protein‚Äîabout 1.5 grams per kilogram body weight‚Äîto supply their bodies‚Äô
demands for growth and the protein requirements for exercise and post-exercise tissue building. The US Depart-
ment of Agriculture, in a 2010 dietary guidelines report, stated: ‚ÄúOn average, Americans of all ages consume
too few vegetables, fruits, high-fiber whole grains, low-fat milk and milk products, and seafood and they eat too
much added sugars, solid fats, refined grains, and sodium.‚Äù The guidelines included a ‚ÄúMyPlate‚Äù that suggested
the elements of a well‚Äìbalanced diet.

Substances  taken  to  enhance  performance  are  called
ergogenic  aids.  Active  people  consume  a  wide  variety
of  drugs  and  nutritional  supplements  in  the  quest  for
improved  performance  and  the  ‚Äúperfect  body.‚Äù  They  take
these  substances  to:  (1)  enhance  muscle  hypertrophy,  (2)
speed  recovery  and  prevent  the  effects  of  over  training,
(3)  increase  training  intensity  and  aggressiveness,  and  (4)
control fat, body water, and appetite.

Sports, fitness, and bodybuilding have become very popular,
high profile activities‚Äîparticularly in young people. Several
bodybuilding  champions  have  become  international  film
stars, and many make enormous sums of money endorsing
athletic  food  supplements.  The  majority  of  bodybuilding
and  fitness  magazines  serve  as  promotional  vehicles  for
‚Äúnutritional‚Äù  products  of  questionable  value.  As  aware,
educated fitness consumers, you should be able to separate

Youth FitnessErgogenic Aids: Drugs and Supplements  |  265

fact  from  fiction  in  evaluating  ‚Äúperformance  enhancing‚Äù  drugs
and food supplements. This section of the chapter reviews some
of the more popular drugs and food supplements used by active
people to promote appearance and performance.

EThICS OF ERgOgENIC AIDS
uSE IN ChILDREN‚ÄôS SPORTS
Sports  supplements  and  drugs  are  minefields  for  the  personal
trainer‚Äîespecially  when  working  with  young  people.  Many  of
these substances are banned for use in amateur and professional
sports.  Research  tells  us  little  about  the  effectiveness  and  long-
term  health  effects  of  these  substances  in  children.  The  trainer
must be aware of the latest information about these substances‚Äî
from the lab and from the street.

Personal  trainers  must  also  be  aware  of  the  ethical  dilemmas
posed  by  the  use  of  ergogenic  aids.  Many  successful  young
athletes  use  supplements  and  drugs.  Some  teenagers  use  them
to  improve  their  appearance.  The  personal  trainer  must  never  give  the  appearance  of
promoting or tolerating drug use. In addition to violating basic ethics of working with
young people, you run the risk of professional sanctions and arrest. You can be honest
about the effects of drugs on performance and health, but do not advocate using them.
Even supplements can be a problem. While most are safe and some are effective, you have
no control over product quality or the amount of supplements your athletes use. Some of
these substances are toxic at high levels. Many young athletes follow the philosophy that
if a little is good, more is better.

Is it ethical for young athletes to use legal supplements? Of course it is. However, the
trainer has to be very careful about recommending them. Discuss the pros and cons of
the products with the parents, and let them decide. That way, you will not be responsible
if the child has a side effect. It becomes a parent-child issue.

COMMON DRugS AND SuPPLEMENTS
AvAILAbLE TO YOuNg AThLETES
Young  athletes  can  get  almost  any  drug‚Äîeven  if  they  live  in  rural  communities.  The
Drug Enforcement Administration (DEA) has said that anabolic steroid seizures are a
low priority. People caught with small quantities of steroids at the border will have their
drugs confiscated, but the person will not be arrested. Also, athletes have easy access to
these drugs over the Internet. Some pharmacies in Mexico will mail the steroids from the
United States so there is little risk of seizure. The personal trainer should assume that the
drugs are available to any young athlete who has the money to pay for them.

Look for obvious signs of drug use such as sudden changes in behavior, unusual acne
breakouts, significant changes in body composition (increased muscle mass or decreased
fat), nosebleeds, and unusual increases in physical performance. Of course, these signs
do not necessarily mean the child is taking illegal drugs, but they should increase your
level of suspicion. Tables 19.1 and 19.2 list drugs and supplements popular with adult
and young athletes.
