anabolic steroids: Synthetic
derivatives of testosterone with
tissue building properties and
relatively weak androgenic
properties, used mainly to
promote growth and to repair
body tissues.

satellite cells: Small cells in
the muscle bundle believed to
play a role in muscle repair and
regeneration by fusing with
adjacent myofibers.

agenTS TaKen To PromoTe
muScle hYPerTroPhY
This category is of greatest concern to active people and constitutes the most significant
area of abuse. Personal trainers should never recommend that young athletes take ana-
bolic steroids. In addition to possible serious side effects, the drugs are illegal. Recom-
mending them could put the personal trainer in serious jeopardy with the law.

Anabolic-Androgenic Steroids
People take anabolic steroids (AS) in the hope of gaining weight, muscle size, strength,
power, speed, endurance, and aggressiveness. These drugs are widely used by both seri-
ous athletes and by active people trying to improve their appearance and performance

In the United States in 1990, the Anabolic Steroid Control Act classified anabolic steroids
as a Schedule III substance. The law gave the DEA power to restrict the importation, ex-
portation, distribution, and dispensing of anabolic steroids. The legislation was amended
in 2004 to include a ban on the sale of pro-hormones, such as androstenedione. This has
led to a thriving ‚Äúblack market‚Äù for illegal anabolic steroids in the United States.

AS increase protein synthesis, which enhances lean body mass. AS bind with receptor
molecules in the cells, which stimulates the cells to make proteins. They also interfere
with the activity of hormones that break down tissue after exercise, which helps steroid
users recover more quickly. AS may also increase aggressiveness, which allows athletes to
train harder. Recent studies show that steroids work better at higher dosages (more than
400 mg a week of testosterone). Unfortunately, the incidence of side effects increases with
increases in dosage.

hOW STEROIDS WORK IN ThE bODY
Increase in the Size and Perhaps the
Number of Muscle Cells
Steroids  make  muscles  larger,  but  they  also  have  several
effects that radically affect muscles‚Äô strength, power, speed,
and  endurance.  Steroids  may  increase  the  number  of
muscle  fibers  or  hyperplasia.  Muscle  biopsies  of  trained
steroid-using weight lifters show they have larger and more
numerous muscle fibers than control subjects. Steroids may
turn  on  cell  structures  called  satellite  cells,  which  may
make  new  muscle  cells.  Most  top  muscle  physiologists  do
not believe that hyperplasia is important in making muscles
bigger.

Increased Androgen Receptor Activity
Steroids  work  by  binding  to  androgen  receptors  in  the
tissues. Scientists have said that taking high doses of steroids
is  ineffective  because  there  are  not  enough  androgen
receptors  to  accommodate  the  high  levels  of  testosterone.
The  activity  of  the  receptor  increases  in  the  face  of  high
blood  levels  of  testosterone.  Also,  intense  weight  training
increases receptor number and activity. Heavy training and
high doses of testosterone is the ticket to muscle growth.

An interesting finding was that receptor  density  is greater
in the upper body than in the lower body. This suggests that
steroids  may  be  more  effective  for  increasing  the  strength
and muscle mass of the pecs over the quads.

Increased Speed of Calcium Release
Power athletes‚Äîranging from baseball players to Olympic
lifters‚Äîhave  long  appreciated  the  effects  of  steroids  on
quickness and power. Calcium triggers muscle contraction.
Steroids increase the speed of calcium release and make the
muscle cells more sensitive to calcium. This means that the
muscles contract faster and more powerfully.

Anticatabolic Effects of Steroids
Muscle growth occurs when protein synthesis in the muscle
exceeds  breakdown.  If  you  can  slow  breakdown  after  a
workout,  the  athlete  makes  gains  by  having  only  a  small
amount of net protein synthesis. Animal studies show that

Youth FitnessErgogenic Aids: Drugs and Supplements  |  269

steroids  can  cross  bind  with  corticosteroid  binding  sites.
These catabolic hormones cause protein breakdown. High
doses  of  steroids  may  promote  cross  binding,  but  not  all
studies show that steroids have anticatabolic effects.

Steroids trigger growth hormone and muscle growth fac-
tors. They initiate growth hormone release from the brain
and IgF-1 (muscle growth factor) from the liver. They only
do this when the weekly dosage of anabolic steroids equals
400 mg per week. IGF-1 and GH are highly anabolic. Ath-
letes do not experience significant gains by taking only GH.
The  gains  are  more  impressive  when  the  drugs  are  com-
bined with anabolic steroids.

Psychological Effects of Steroids
You cannot be a great bodybuilder‚Äîor a great anything‚Äîif
you cannot control your mind. Recent studies suggest that
‚Äúroid rage‚Äù is a myth‚Äîin most people. It does affect eupho-
ria, energy, and sexual arousal, which could have powerful
effects on the desire to train hard. High blood testosterone
increases androgen receptors in the brain‚Äîjust as it does in
muscle.  Steroids  increase  important  brain  chemicals,  such
as serotonin, GABA (gamma amino butyric acid), and ace-
tylcholine,  which  could  help  psych  you  up  and  encourage
you to train harder.

Health Risks of Anabolic Steroids
Side  effects  of  anabolic  steroids  include  liver  toxicity
and  tumors,  decreased  high  density  lipoproteins  (good
cholesterol),  cardiac  arrhythmia,  reduced  sperm  count,
lowered  testosterone  production,  high  blood  pressure,
increased risk of AIDS (due to shared needles), depressed
intolerance,  psychological
immune

function,  glucose

disturbances,  masculinization  in  women  and  children,
premature  closure  of  the  bone  growth  centers,  and  an
increased  cancer  risk.  Steroids  can  also  make  adolescent
acne  much  worse  in  young  men  and  women.  Severe  side
effects of anabolic steroid use have been reported in athletes,
including  myocardial  infarction  (heart  attack),  ventricular
tachycardia (accelerated heart rate), liver cancer, and severe
psychiatric disturbances. Athletes may be particularly prone
to side effects because they often take high dosages of the
drugs for prolonged times.

The message of this new research is clear: athletes must take
high doses of steroids if they want significant effects. There
is no purely anabolic steroid‚Äîandrogen receptors exist in
most of the body‚Äôs tissues, including the heart and prostate
gland.  The  grave  risk  is  that  taking  extremely  high  doses
of steroids may be setting the stage for heart enlargement,
eventual  heart  failure,  and  prostate  enlargement  and  can-
cer.  In  addition,  high  doses  of  steroids  increase  IGF-1‚Äîa
potent growth stimulator of muscle and cancer cells. Steroid
users from the 1970s and 1980s have not been dying in large
numbers  from  cancer  and  heart  disease,  but  they  did  not
take  the  same  amount  of  steroids  as  modern  athletes.  Do
everything in your power to persuade young athletes not to
take steroids.

Growth Hormone
Growth hormone (GH) is popular with athletes who use it to increase muscle mass and
strength. Reports in the news media suggest that, like anabolic steroids, its use has fil-
tered down to non-athlete high school students. Recombinant human growth hormone
has made the hormone more widely available.

GH speeds the rate at which proteins enter muscle cells, which increases their growth
rate.  It  also  stimulates  another  growth  promoting  hormone  called  insulin-like  growth
factor. In addition, GH mobilizes fats from the fat cells, so the drug is used in hopes of
modifying body composition.

Human  growth  hormone  studies  have  shown  no  beneficial  effects  on  muscle  or  per-
formance. Animal studies found that growth hormone administration stimulates muscle
growth. Observations in athletes suggest that GH is highly anabolic in intensely training
elite bodybuilders. However, the side effects can be severe.

Prolonged growth hormone administration may result in low blood sugar, elevated in-
sulin levels, heart enlargement, and elevated blood fats. Long-term use could also lead

anticatabolic: Preventative of
breakdown processes.

growth hormone: Hormone
produced by the pituitary gland
that regulates protein, fat, and
carbohydrate metabolism by
stimulating the liver to produce
tissue growth factors (i.e.,
insulin-like growth factor-1)

growth factor produced by the

from the normal rhythm of
the heartbeat, including sinus
arrhythmia, premature beat,
heart block, atrial fibrillation,
atrial flutter, and paroxysmal
tachycardia.

produced by excess growth
hormone production;
characterized by enlarged facial

frontal bone of skull, widely

extremity bones.

or inflammation of the heart

Nerve damage in the

following minimal trauma.

be infective, autoimmune, or

technology.

triggered by growth hormone.

(DhEA): Hormone secreted
by the adrenal gland that can

to  acromegaly  (characterized  by  enlarged  bones  in  the  head,  face,  and  hands),  car-
diomyopathy, peripheral nerve damage, weakening of the bones (osteoporosis),
arthritis, and heart disease.

Some  athletes  also  take  drugs  that  increase  natural  growth  hormone  secretion.  These
drugs include propranolol, vasopressin, clonidine, and levodopa. There is no evidence
that these practices enhance muscle hypertrophy.

Insulin-like Growth Factor (IGF-1)
The use of recombinant  IgF-1, also called somotomedin C, has recently become a
popular drug among athletes. IGF-1 production is stimulated by growth hormone. It is
released mainly by the liver but also may be secreted by the testes, fat cells, bones, and
heart. IGF-1 is an extremely anabolic (muscle-building) hormone. The side effects are
thought to be similar to those of growth hormone. Long-term use may promote or ac-
cellerate cancer growth.

Dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA) and
Androstenedione
Dehydroepiandrosterone (DhEA) and androstenedione are relatively weak andro-
gen hormones produced mainly in the adrenal glands. They are broken down to testo-
sterone, which explains their popularity with athletes. They are widely available in health
food stores and supermarkets. Athletes take this drug to stimulate muscle hypertrophy
and aid in weight control. DHEA or androstenedione administration in middle age and
older adults has been shown to result in improved ‚Äúenergy levels,‚Äù increased muscle mass,
better mental acuity, and enhanced immune function. However, the subjects had blood
levels of the hormone at least 20 percent below the average level of a 20-year old. The
substances‚Äô value as ergogenic aids may lie in their ability to increase serum testoster-
one concentrations. The sale of androstenedione without a prescription was banned in
the United States in 2005.

High doses of DHEA (1600 mg/d) in young men have been shown to lead to a reduction
in  HDL  cholesterol  (good  cholesterol).  However,  in  elderly  subjects,  DHEA  has  been
shown to reduce LDL (bad cholesterol) but have no effect on HDL. Doses of 150 to 300
mg per day have a marked effect on testosterone concentration. This could lead to mas-
culinization in women and interfere with hormone controls in both sexes. Androsten-
edione administration could have similar side effects to those seen with DHEA. These
‚Äúpro-hormones‚Äù will likely have the same side effects in young people as testosterone‚Äî
stunted growth, skin problems, interference with normal sex hormones, and psychologi-
cal disorders. Girls should avoid them because they could interfere with the menstrual
cycle and potentially cause reproductive and bone problems.

Insulin
Some physically active people take insulin injections to promote muscle hypertrophy. In-
sulin, aside from its influence on glucose and fat metabolism, affects protein metabolism.
The effects include enhanced amino acid transport into cells, increased rate of incorpora-
tion of amino acids into protein, and suppression of protein breakdown. The effectiveness
of insulin supplementation or elevation in stimulating muscle hypertrophy is not known.
The  most  serious  side-effect  of  exogenous  insulin  administration  in  exercising  body-
builders is insulin shock, a condition where blood sugar reaches dangerously low levels.

Youth FitnessErgogenic Aids: Drugs and Supplements  |  271

Clenubuterol
Athletes take this drug (a √ü2 adrenergic agonist) to prevent
muscle atrophy, increase lean body mass, and decrease body
fat. Beneficial effects have been shown in animals, but few
studies have been done in humans. Side effects include in-
somnia,  abnormal  heart  rhythm,  anxiety,  depressed  appe-
tite, and nausea. More serious side effects include enlarged
heart, heart failure, and heart attack. This drug is very easy
to get. Its side effects can be serious in young people, and the
personal trainer must strongly discourage its use.

Other Substances
Other substances sometimes used as ergogenic aids include
human  chorionic  gonadotropin  (HCG),  periactin,  conju-
gated linoleic acid (CLA), vanadyl sulfate, dibencozide, and
organ extracts. These agents are much less popular and their
effectiveness is questionable.

agenTS TaKen To
SPeed recoverY
The  primary  purposes  of  taking  these  agents  is  to  replen-
ish  depleted  body  fuels  (with  creatine  phosphate:  creatine
supplementation;  with  muscle  and  liver  glycogen:  glucose
and lactate polymers) and serve as a source for post-exercise
protein synthesis (i.e., amino acids).

Creatine Supplementation
Creatine  (i.e.,  creatine  monohydrate)  is  among  the  most
popular and widely used supplements by athletes. Individu-
als use creatine supplements to build strength and muscle
mass, enhance recovery, and improve exercise capacity.

Creatine feeding increases the creatine phosphate content of
the  muscle  by  about  20  percent.  Creatine  phosphate  is  an
important  high-energy  chemical  to  maintain  ATP  levels.
Recall from our discussion of exercise physiology that ATP
supplies  the  energy  for  most  of  the  body‚Äôs  physiological
functions. The most effective dosage of creatine is 3 to 5 g per
day (1 teaspoon). Taking more than that causes no further
increase in total muscle creatine. Creatine supplementation
has been shown to improve performance in short-term, high
intensity, repetitive exercise, which might make it a valuable
supplement  for  physically  active  people.  It  may  improve
performance  by  augmenting  the  availability  of  creatine
phosphate and possibly regulating the rate of muscle sugar
breakdown.  It  may  also  increase  muscle-building  capacity
during resistive exercise by allowing more intense training.

Creatine  monohydrate  supplementation  has  been  associ-
ated  with  muscle  cramping  during  and  after  exercise.  The
long-term safety and effectiveness are currently unknown.
More  than  100  studies  on  this  supplement  have  failed
to  report  any  significant  side  effects.  However,  creatine
supplements should be used with caution in young people.
Personal  trainers  should  be  wary  of  recommending  them
to their clients because of possible liability issues. The long-
term  effects  and  safety  of  this  supplement  in  children  are
not known.

Amino Acid and Polypeptide
Supplements
Athletes  take  amino  acid  and  polypeptide  supplements  to
accelerate muscle development, decrease body fat, and stim-
ulate growth hormone release. There is little scientific proof
to support amino acid or polypeptide supplementation. The
protein  requirement  of  active  people  is  not  much  higher
than  for  sedentary  individuals,  so  the  rate  of  amino  acid
absorption from the gastrointestinal track is not important.
Also,  substituting  amino  acid  or  polypeptide  supplements
for protein-rich foods may cause deficiencies in important
nutrients such as iron and the B vitamins.

The  protein  requirement  for  active  athletes  is  not  much
greater  than  for  the  average  person‚Äî0.8  to  1.5  grams  of
protein  per  kilogram  of  body  weight  per  day.  However,
recent research shows that the timing of protein and amino
acid  feeding  is  very  important  for  stimulating  muscle
growth. Radioactive tracer studies, in which the metabolism
of  various  fuels  is  followed,  found  that  taking  a  protein
supplement  either  30  minutes  before  weight  training  or
immediately  afterwards  speeds  the  movement  of  amino
acids  into  muscle  and  stimulates  protein  synthesis  (i.e.,
makes new muscle tissue faster).

ectopic beats: Heart beat
originating somewhere other
than at the heart‚Äôs pacemaker or
SA node.

paroxysmal atrial
tachycardia: Bouts of rapid,
regular heartbeats originating in
the upper heart chamber.

Other Substances
Carbohydrate beverages consumed during and immediately following exercise enhance
recovery from intense training, speeding the replenishment of liver and muscle glycogen.
The use of other substances to speed recovery‚Äîsuch as vitamin C, N-acetyl-L-Cysteine
(NAC),  inosine,  and  beta-hydroxy  beta-methylbutyrate  (HMB)‚Äîis  not  currently  sup-
ported by positive research findings.

SubSTanceS TaKen To IncreaSe
aggreSSIveneSS and TraInIng InTenSITY
Serious exercisers often spend several hours a day training for their favorite activities.
Monotony and fatigue sometimes make significant improvement difficult. Many athletes
use stimulants to help them train harder and combat fatigue.

Amphetamines
Individuals take amphetamines to prevent fatigue and to increase confidence and train-
ing intensity. Examples of amphetamines include benzedrine, dexedrine, dexamyl and
methedrine. These drugs act by stimulating the nervous system. Effects include increased
blood pressure, heart rate, arousal, wakefulness, confidence, and the feeling of enhanced
capability to make decisions.

Studies show the drugs mask fatigue but may not increase endurance performance. Most
studies have shown increases in static strength but mixed results in muscle endurance.
These substances appear to aid power oriented movement skills in activities that employ
constant motor patterns‚Äîsuch as shot putting and hammer throwing‚Äîand theoretically
they could provide some benefit to bodybuilders and weightlifters by allowing them to
train harder.

Amphetamines can cause severe neural and psychological effects. These include aggres-
siveness,  paranoia,  hallucinations,  compulsive  behavior,  restlessness,  and  irritability.
They can cause arrhythmias (abnormal heart rhythms), hypertension, and angina (heart
related chest pain). They are highly addictive and are particularly dangerous for young
people. Children must avoid them.

Caffeine
Caffeine‚Äîfound in coffee, cola, tea, and chocolate‚Äîis
a favorite stimulant of active people. It stimulates the
central  nervous  system  to  release  adrenaline  (epine-
phrine). In athletics, caffeine acts as a stimulant and
fatty acid mobilizer. While there is some evidence
that caffeine may improve  endurance, the drug  does
not  appear  to  enhance  short-term  maximal  exercise
capacity.

The  diuretic  and  cardiac  stimulatory  properties  of
this  substance  can  combine  to  increase  the  risk  of
arrhythmias  such  as  ventricular  ectopic  beats  and
paroxysmal atrial tachycardia. Caffeine produces
insomnia and is addicting.

Youth FitnessErgogenic Aids: Drugs and Supplements  |  273

Other Agents
Other agents used by physically active individuals to enhance training intensity include
cocaine, ephedrine, and ginseng. Cocaine use is not thought to be widespread in athletes,
but some reportedly use it to increase training intensity. Ephedrine, a weak stimulant, is
widely used by athletes during workouts. Studies have shown that despite a slight stimu-
lating effect on blood pressure and on exercise and recovery heart rates, ephedrine had
no effect on physical work capacity. Ginseng is also popular with athletes, but little evi-
dence exists to support its use as an ergogenic aid.

SubSTanceS TaKen To aId
weIghT conTrol
Drugs used in weight control include those that suppress appetite, thermogenic drugs,
drugs  affecting  the  gastrointestinal  tract,  and  diuretics  to  control  weight  and  increase
muscle  definition.  Gymnasts,  dancers,  figure  skaters,  and  weight-class  athletes  use  a
variety of techniques to decrease body fat. They may do this immediately before a contest
or chronically because of the requirements of the sport.

Appetite  suppressing  drugs  work  by  acting  on  catecholamine  neurotransmitters
or  serotonin  neurotransmitters  or  by  blocking  opioid  receptors.  Thermogenic
drugs affect metabolic rate. Gastrointestinal drugs attempt to affect nutrient absorption.
Other agents with questionable effectiveness and desirability include human chorionic
gonadotropin, growth hormone, glucagon, progesterone, and biguanides.

Appetite  suppressants  include  amphetamine,  ephedrine,  sibutramine,  diethylpropion,
fenfluramine, and phenylpropanolamine. While these drugs depress appetite and usually
trigger  weight  loss,  some  produce  serious  side  effects.  Amphetamine,  for  example,  is
highly addictive and can lead to cardiac arrhythmias and impair temperature regulation.

Thermogenic drugs include thyroid hormone, ephedrine, and dinitrophenol. Thyroid
hormones were in common use in the 1970s. They reduced lean body mass and increased
the incidence of cardiac arrhythmias. Ephedrine and √ü-agonists have been suggested as
useful  agents,  but more research  must establish their effectiveness. Uncoupling  agents
such as dinitrophenol are toxic at effective dosages and are associated with neuropathy
and cataracts.

Drugs  affecting  the  gastrointestinal  tract  include  orlistat,  dietary  fiber,  and  sucrose
polyester. Orlistat‚Äîone of two weight loss prescription drugs approved in the United
States‚Äîpartially blocks fat absorption. While effective, it has side effects such as abdomi-
nal  cramping  and  oily  stools,  and  the  possibility  of  severe  liver  injury‚Äîclearly  unac-
ceptable to most people. Dietary fiber causes gastrointestinal distention and may restrict
energy intake. Limited clinical trials suggest that including fiber supplements may aid
weight loss. Sucrose polyester (Olestra) is a new diet ingredient of some promise. This
substance cannot be digested and can be substituted for fats in the diet. The few studies
on Olestra have shown conflicting results.

The combination of ephedrine and caffeine is an effective weight loss agent comparable
to either orlistat or sibutramine. However, the use of these supplements remains contro-
versial. The personal trainer should not recommend this supplement weight loss stack
to young people without parental permission and the input of a health care professional.
While the supplements may be effective, you should not recommend them to young cli-
ents at this time due to the extensive controversy surrounding their use.

thermogenic: Heat
production.

thermogenic drugs: Drugs
or substances that increase
metabolic heat production.

catecholamine
neurotransmitters: Molecules
such as adrenaline and
noradrenaline (epinephrine and
norepinephrine) that trigger
specific physiological effects.

serotonin
neurotransmitters: Chemicals
found in the brain and
circulation.

receptors that bind with opioids
in the body, such as endorphins,
and produce specific
physiological effects.

dinitrophenol: Drug
that causes uncoupling of
metabolism and loss of energy
as heat instead of storage as fat.

disturbances or pathological
changes in the peripheral
nervous system.

cataract: Discoloration of
the lens of one or both eyes
that impairs vision or causes
blindness.

Orlistat (xenical): FDA
approved drug that inhibits
absorption of dietary fats.

International Sports Sciences Association274  |  unit 19

diuretics: Drugs or substances
that promote urine excretion.

Diuretics
Before  a  contest,  bodybuilders  attempt  to  accentuate  their  muscle  definition  by  using
diuretics, potassium supplements, and low calorie diets. Some bodybuilders also take
potassium supplements to promote fluid retention in muscle cells, thus hoping to increase
muscle size. Athletes combine these practices with very low calorie diets and dehydration
in the quest for leanness.

Efficacy and Side Effects
There  is  no  evidence  that  these  unhealthy  practices  improve  performance  in
bodybuilding.  Serious  medical  and  health  complications  have  developed,  including
rhabdomyolysis (suggested by elevated serum creatine kinase and phosphorous), hypo-
tension, marked hemoconcentration, hyperkalemia (due to the use of potassium-sparing
diuretics and potassium supplements), cardiac arrhythmias, and heart failure.

SuMMARY

Active young people‚Äîeven pre-teens‚Äîconsume a wide variety of drugs and
nutritional supplements in the quest to improve performance and create the
‚Äúperfect body.‚Äù They take these substances to: (1) enhance muscle mass, (2) speed
recovery and prevent the effects of over training, (3) increase training intensity and
aggressiveness, and (4) control fat, body water, and appetite. The personal trainer
must remain vigilant about not recommending any supplement or drug to a young
athlete unless it is done with the advice and consent of the parent and a health
professional. While most supplements are safe in adults, scientists do not know
about long-term health risks in children. Personal trainers should be conservative
when dealing with young athletes, drugs, and supplements.

uNIT 20

# Preventing Injuries

278  |  unit 20

Key Terms

rehabilitation

traumatic

It  was  first  down  and  ten  yards  to  go  on  the  Oakdale  Vikings‚Äô  own  30-yard  line.  Jim
Haynes was the quarterback, and he was having a great year. Only two more games and
the  season  would  be  over‚ÄîJim  was  sure  to  get  many  scholarship  offers  from  the  big
schools. He took the snap, dropped back into a pocket of blockers, and looked for an
open receiver. But things started to go wrong‚ÄîJim‚Äôs pass protection deteriorated, and he
was forced into the open with several defensive players breathing down his neck. All of a
sudden, a blitzing linebacker came at him like the night train. Jim went down hard as the
linebacker hit him with a crunch on the side of his knee. The knee seemed to snap like a
rubber band. Jim‚Äôs season‚Äîand possibly his career‚Äîwere over.

Scenes like this are repeated time and again on playing fields across the country. There are
risks involved in athletics, but most of us feel the risks are worth it because of the many
benefits sports has to offer. Coaches, athletes, personal trainers, and parents should take
steps  to  minimize  athletic  injuries  and  to  make  sure  they  are  adequately  rehabilitated
when they do occur. Athletics can be one of the profound influences in a child‚Äôs life. But
if an athletic program presents undue risk of serious injury, it may produce an adult with
chronically sore joints and lack of function. A safe athletic program requires physicians,
coaches, athletes, trainers, and parents to work together to insure that sports participation
remains a positive experience, and not one that produces athletic casualties. This section
presents the principles of prevention and treatment of athletic injuries for children.

Youth FitnessPreventing Injuries  |  279

PREvENTINg INJuRIES
There  are  certain  things  a  parent  can  do  at  home  to  help
a  child become  physically fit. But beyond that,  preventing
injuries  in  children‚Äôs  sports  often  requires  pressuring  the
athletic  establishment  to  maintain  minimal  standards.
Athletic  teams‚Äîwhether  they  are  junior  club  soccer,
peewee  football,  or  high  school  softball‚Äîshould  provide
proper  coaching,  good  equipment,  and  adequate  medical
supervision for its players.

ProPer coachIng
Coaches  are  in  a  unique  position‚Äîthey  can  shape  the
physical  and,  to  an  extent,  the  emotional  condition  of  the
team. The coach sets the tone of the practices and the games.
He or she decides whether players will be vicious or obey
the rules, sloppy or play with skill and finesse. The personal
trainer  is  in  a  similar  position.  Your  professionalism  and
programming  skill  can  make  the  difference  between  a
successful experience and one that ends in injury.

Most coaches are knowledgeable and extremely dedicated.
They teach techniques that prevent injuries and encourage
physical  fitness.  Many  of  them  also  keep  up-to-date  with
current knowledge of training and exercise physiology.

methods.  Good  sports  fundamentals  make  movements
more  efficient  and  predictable‚Äîit‚Äôs  the  unexpected  that
often produces an injury.

Most  children  will  participate  in  ‚Äúsandlot‚Äù  sports  if  they
cannot  participate  in  organized  athletics.  Sandlot  games
are notorious sites for serious injuries. I remember several
instances from my own childhood‚Äîone boy was in a body
cast for six months after a playground tackle football game,
and  another  broke  his  neck  and  became  a  quadriplegic
from an impromptu game of rugby. You can minimize the
incidence of tragic injuries by teaching proper fundamentals
and practicing according to established rules.

The  coach‚Äôs  attitude  is  important  in  preventing  injuries.
Coaches work with impressionable boys and girls. Coaches
who  tell  their  young  players  to  ‚Äúkill‚Äù  the  opponent  or
use  unsportsmanlike  behavior  can  expect  athletes  to  use
techniques that end in injury.

A  coach  has  an  opportunity  to  take  a  stand  against  what
seems to be an insatiable appetite for violence in America.
People  love  to  watch  a  professional  wrestler  who  knocks
an  opponent  out  of  the  ring.  The  crowd  cheers  when  a
hockey player gets hit in the jaw with a stick. The coach can
encourage  young  athletes  to  embrace  the  finer  aspects  of
sports, not the cheap thrills of violence.

A good coach or personal trainer can minimize even minor
injuries  by  teaching  proper  sports  fundamentals.  The
incidence of sore shoulders, elbows, and wrists will be fewer
when you teach proper throwing techniques. Back injuries
can  be  greatly  reduced  when  you  teach  correct  lifting

Coaches  and  personal  trainers  who  get  hung  up  on  the
fantasy of professional sports and try to apply it to school
or  club-level  teams  are  behaving  unprofessionally.  For
example,  one  of  the  most  dangerous  sports  techniques
taught by some football coaches is spearing, a blocking and

International Sports Sciences Association280  |  unit 20

tackling  technique  that  requires  initial  contact  with  the
head. Because of hard plastic football helmets, the head can
be used as a weapon to punish opponents, but spearing has
resulted  in  an  increase  in  serious  neck  injuries  that  often
have  tragic  consequences.  Although  the  governing  bodies
of football at all levels of play prohibit this technique, many
coaches still teach spearing. Coaches, personal trainers, and
parents should support teaching basic sports fundamentals
that promote sports safety rather than dangerous techniques
designed to hurt an opponent.

The  positive  aspects  of  sports  should  always  outweigh  the
negative ones. A lifetime with a bad knee is a big price to
pay  for  poor  judgment  by  an  overambitious  coach.
Fortunately,  serious  injuries  rarely  occur  in  children‚Äôs
sports  because  the  vast  majority  of  coaches  are  extremely
competent  and  take  every  precaution  to  insure  the  safety
of  young  athletes,  and  they  understand  that  children  are
smaller,  weaker,  and  slower  than  adult  athletes.  Coaches
sometimes  adopt  training  methods  and  attitudes  that
conflict  with  current  medical  knowledge  and  acceptable
coaching practices, however.

Every  fall  there  are  several  deaths  in  football  caused  by
heat stroke, a condition in which the body loses the ability
to  regulate  temperature.  These  deaths  are  tragic  and
unnecessary. Athletes should be encouraged (or even forced)
to drink plenty of water when they engage in heavy exercise,
particularly in hot and humid weather. It‚Äôs surprising how
many coaches are ignorant about the importance of fluids
during  exercise.  Some  coaches  even  withhold  water  from
players in the hope of ‚Äúmaking them tough.‚Äù

Hundreds  of  studies  show  that  adequate  fluid  intake  is
essential for controlling body temperature during exercise.
The natural thirst of the athlete is not enough to accomplish
this,  since  dehydration  and  rising  body  temperature  will
impact  the  thirst  for  water.  It  is  therefore  important  for
athletes  to  drink  water  or  athletic  beverages  anytime  they
feel thirsty.

Obesity  has  contributed  to  many  of  the  heat  injuries  seen
in  recent  years.  In  college  and  high  school  football,  for
example,  many  athletes  weight  more  than  300  pounds.
Large, fast athletes can inflict considerable damage on the
football field. However, they should also strive for leanness.

Youth FitnessPreventing Injuries  |  281

The role oF ParenTS
and The PerSonal TraIner

Physical Conditioning
Physical conditioning is one of the most important factors
in the prevention of athletic injuries. Parents and personal
trainers  can  encourage  their  children  to  remain  fit  during
the entire year‚Äînot just when the sport is in season. Frantic
fitness programs begun two weeks before the season begins
make  the  athlete  a  candidate  for  injury.  At  the  very  least,
this  last-minute  fitness  developmental  strategy  results  in
poor performance. Many schools have rules against sports
practices during the off-season. Although these rules were
instituted  to  prevent  overemphasis  of  sports,  they  often
result in unfit athletes. It is up to parents‚Äîworking with the
personal  trainer‚Äîto  assume  part  of  the  responsibility  for
encouraging fitness in their children.

Consistency
Continue the physical conditioning program throughout the
year. Immediately after the season, emphasize well-rounded
general conditioning. Encourage the child to systematically
develop endurance, strength, power, flexibility, agility, and
speed.  Consistency  remains  the  key.  The  intensity  does
not have to be as severe as during the actual season. Young
athletes should begin early to plan their training program
and keep a written record of it. Help children set attainable,
short-term goals. For example, a young runner might move

up to running 6 to 10 miles a day within a year; a football
player  might  have  a  goal  of  lifting  a  particular  weight  or
performing  a  certain  number  of  chin-ups.  People  always
work  harder  when  they  have  realistic  goals  and  can  see
improvement.  Parents  and  trainers  can  help  children  set
goals and provide encouragement to attain them.

Good Posture
Help  children  develop  good  posture.  Poor  posture  is  a
cause of many injuries, particularly to the back. With good
posture,  the  child  should  be  able  to  remain  standing  or
seated  for  a  reasonably  long  period  of  time  with  minimal
effort.  Properly  aligned  bones  and  their  ligamentous
attachments  require  little  muscular  exertion  to  maintain
efficient body positions. However, when the child assumes
an  inefficient  body  posture,  the  muscles  must  assist  the
skeleton  to  maintain  unnatural  positions.  These  muscles
fatigue easily and stress spinal joints and ligaments critical
for  maintaining  painless  postures.  Although  some  bad
posture  characteristics  come  from  structural  and  genetic
abnormalities, most posture problems stem from bad habits
developed during childhood.

Adolescents, in particular, often assume postures that reflect
the  way  they  feel  about  themselves  psychologically.  The
athlete,  after  losing  a  game  or  making  a  mistake,  is  often
slumped  and  dejected.  His  head  is  bowed  and  he  walks
with  a  slow  shuffle.  This  is  the  classic  ‚Äúagony  of  defeat‚Äù
posture. Children with negative attitudes about themselves
may develop this type of drooped stance as a chronic habit.
Slouching postures produce fatigue and stress the supportive

International Sports Sciences Association282  |  unit 20

rehabilitation: Return of
function after an injury, often
with the assistance of physical
therapists or athletic trainers.

structures  of  the  back  and  neck. This  serves  as  another  example  of  the  importance  of
developing  positive  attitudes  in  children.  Good  self-image  supports  emotional  and
physical well‚Äìbeing.

Coaches, personal trainers, and parents should encourage prudent living habits. Growing
children need adequate rest and a well-balanced diet. Unfortunately, our fast-food society
makes empty-calorie snacks look desirable. Children should be educated about the need
for proper nutrition and the hazards of drugs, smoking, and alcohol. They should get this
message from an early age‚Äîat home and at school. This will help them to confront the
social pressures to assume an unhealthy lifestyle.

good equIPmenT
Modern  synthetics  have  revolutionized  athletic  equipment,  from  tennis  racquets  and
skis to running surfaces and playing fields. Unfortunately, children often receive inferior
equipment  presented  under  the  guise  of  ‚Äújunior  models.‚Äù  Inadequate  equipment  can
diminish performance or even cause injury. A loose-fitting football helmet may cause
a  head  or  neck  injury.  Ski  bindings  that  do  not  release  can  result  in  a  broken  leg.  A
tennis racquet too large for a child may cause elbow or shoulder pain. Children must
have good-quality equipment that fits properly.

Safety  equipment  should  be  mandatory.  Children  should  wear  helmets  and  pads  in
skateboarding  and  a  life  jacket  when  sailing,  waterskiing,  or  river  rafting.  Kids  often
tempt fate by performing foolhardy feats. Although this seems to be part of growing up,
children should be required to use safety equipment for their own good.

medIcal SuPervISIon
Proper medical supervision of the young athlete falls to the physician who assesses the
child‚Äôs suitability for sports and directly assumes responsibility for treatment of injuries,
the physical therapist or athletic trainer who supervises rehabilitation of injuries, and
the coach or personal trainer who directly supervises the health care of the child athlete
on the field and at the health and fitness club. Each has definite responsibilities. In cases
where  money  is  a  problem,  parents  will  have  to  assume  the  first-aid  responsibilities
that  should  ideally  be  the  doctor‚Äôs.  Child  athletes  should  be  regularly  examined  by  a
licensed physician.

Youth FitnessPreventing Injuries  |  283

Ideally,  the  physician  should  have  a  good  knowledge  of  growth  and  development  and
a  familiarity  with  sports  medicine.  An  orthopedic  specialist  should  evaluate  injuries
involving bones and joints‚Äîparticularly in bone growth centers. Team doctors are usually
best  for  treating  athletic  injuries  because  most  of  them  have  had  a  lot  of  experience.
They  often  have  the  best  judgment  about  when  the  athlete  should  play  or  rest.  Most
club sports‚Äîyouth soccer, softball, or football‚Äîdo not have team physicians. So parents,
sometimes with the advice of the personal trainer, should choose a family physician who
understands athletics and exercise.

Duties of the athlete‚Äôs sports physician
The athlete‚Äôs physician should:

‚Ä¢  Determine if the child has the physical maturity to safely participate in a sport.

‚Ä¢  Restrict a child from a sport if he or she has medical problems.

‚Ä¢  Recommend to children with medical problems alternative sports and physical

activities appropriate for their condition.

‚Ä¢  Obtain prompt treatment for conditions encountered during the health

examination.

A physician should be present during any athletic contest that involves potential risk of
serious injury. Sports that particularly need direct medical supervision include football,
wrestling,  basketball,  gymnastics,  soccer,  water  polo,  and  ice  hockey.  If  possible,  a
physician  should  be  on  call  for  football  practices  because  most  injuries  occur  during
training and practice sessions.

Ideally, one physician should be involved in an organization‚Äôs entire athletic program to
provide consistency of health care and better readiness for emergencies. The physician
should always have emergency equipment available.

A surprising number of athletic teams, particularly at the elementary school and junior
high levels, have no medical supervision at all. The community should strive to improve
the health care in sports at all levels. At the very least, qualified athletic trainers should
be available to help fill the void. If no trainer is available, then coaches must have good
knowledge of first aid and emergency procedures.

when To call The docTor
During the course of organized athletics and impromptu games, children get all kinds of
bumps and bruises. If they ran to the doctor every time this happened, this would quickly
drain the time and resources of both the physician and the family.

Most minor injuries to muscles and joints do not require medical examination. People
should ‚Äúwait and see‚Äù before consulting a physician for most muscle and joint injuries. It
is difficult to list hard and fast rules for separating serious from relatively benign athletic
injuries, but here are some general guidelines:

‚Ä¢  The proper way to treat tennis elbow, sore muscles, Achilles tendon pains, and
other overuse injuries is to gradually progress from initial rest to stretching and
then to systematic strengthening. The child should see a physician if the pain
from an overuse injury does not disappear after several weeks.

‚Ä¢

Injuries that require immediate medical attention are those to the head and
eye; ‚Äútraumatic‚Äù injuries, caused by sudden blows or an overwhelming force,
that will usually result in swelling‚Äîparticularly to the ankle and knee; inju-
ries involving damage to the ligaments; injuries involving broken bones; and
internally-related disorders such as chest pains, fainting, and intolerance to

traumatic: Relating to or
resulting from physical or
psychological trauma, wound,
or injury.

International Sports Sciences Association284  |  unit 20

heat. Unless the injury is obviously minor, get the child to a doctor as quickly
as possible. Be careful not to underestimate the seriousness of an injury.

‚Ä¢  The personal trainer should have an emergency medical plan worked out in

advance with the parents and the health and fitness club.

‚Ä¢  Teach children to ‚Äúlisten‚Äù to their bodies. If the pain from an injury is beyond
tolerance, seek medical attention quickly. However, if the pain is only a spasm
or cramp from overexertion, there is very little a doctor can do. Think of how
the injury happened: a back injury sustained when five defensive players
stomped on the child‚Äôs spine may call for an x-ray; a backache caused by
hitting too many balls at the driving range may not.

SuMMARY

Parents, coaches, and personal trainers should help the child keep athletics and ath-
letic injuries in perspective. Injured young athletes who play with injuries or fail to
fully rehabilitate them may be risking a lifetime of discomfort and lack of function.

Prevent injuries with proper coaching, good equipment, proper medical supervision,
and physical conditioning. The immediate care of muscle and joint injuries includes
rest, immobilization, elevation, ice, and compression. Rehabilitation of these injuries
should include restoration of range of motion, strength, and endurance.

Specific treatment of athletic injuries in children‚Äôs athletics is beyond the scope of this course. However, it is important
that personal trainers know the general principles involved in sports injuries and disabilities commonly experienced
by children. (See Table 21.1 on page 293.)

INJuRIES TO JOINTS AND MuSCLES
Joint and muscle injuries are common in children‚Äôs sports but are usually relatively be-
nign if cared for properly. In general, the immediate care of these injuries involves rest,
immobilization, ice, elevation, and compression.

Very often after a child sustains an injury  such as  a pulled  muscle, sprained  ankle, or
twisted knee, function appears to be almost normal. The tendency is often to ‚Äúrun it off.‚Äù
Because the muscle is warm and initial swelling is minimal, a child can easily underesti-
mate an injury. Continued playing with an injury is only going to make things worse and
delay recovery. It is important that the injured area be immobilized and weight bearing
minimized or eliminated. Although the athlete may want to maintain motion in the joint,
he or she should try to do so without putting too much weight on it.

ImmedIaTe TreaTmenT
One of the best strategies an athlete can employ to speed recovery from an injury is to
ice the area. Ice numbs the injured area and blocks much of the pain by slowing down
the activity of the nerves that transmit pain sensations. A good ice treatment relaxes the
injured area and reduces muscle spasms that may occur, and simultaneously increases
blood flow deep in tissues without causing swelling.

Cryokinetics, which entails moving an injured area after an initial cooling with ice, has
brought  important  breakthroughs  in  athletic  injury  treatment.  Until  recently,  the  im-
mediate treatment of an injury was limited to immobilization followed by heat treatment
and massage. Cryokinetics requires the athlete to take the weight off the injured area to a
certain extent, but also involves an attempt to regain and maintain the area‚Äôs full range of
motion. Treat a sprained ankle, for example, by applying an ice pack, wrapping the ankle
in an elastic bandage, and elevating the foot. The injured athlete would then try to move
his or her foot forward and backward, and downward and upward. Immobilization of the
ankle is followed by an attempt to regain the full range of foot motion.

cryokinetics: Rehabilitation
technique that uses exercise and
ice.

Youth FitnessCommon Injuries in Children‚Äôs Sports  |  287

deconditioning: Loss of fitness
through rest or injury.

ice massage: Massaging a
body part with a small block of
ice.

An injured child can quickly recover full range of motion in the injured area and normal
use of his body through continued ice therapy. In addition, continued ice treatment pre-
vents much of the deconditioning from an injury because the therapy reduces pain and
muscle spasms. Ice treatment, because it does not eliminate all pain sensations, keeps the
child from overdoing it and possibly making the injury worse.

The most common types of ice treatment are ice massage, ice bath, and ice pack. The
ice massage is effective for cooling a particular region. Fill a paper cup with water and
freeze it. When the ice is formed, peel away the upper half of the cup and hold the ice
by the lower half of the cup. Using a circular motion, massage the injured area for 7 to
10 minutes, or until numbness sets in. You can avoid numbing your fingers by placing a
tongue depressor in the cup of water before making the ice. This will create a large frozen
lollipop with a convenient handle. A word of caution: never use dry ice.

An  ice  pack  is  valuable  for  immediate  treatment  of  muscle  and  joint  injuries.  Take  a
wet towel and place a layer of crushed ice on it, then wrap or place the towel directly
on the injury. Another method of ice packing involves placing a plastic bag filled with
ice  directly  on  the  injury.  However,  this  kind  of  ice  pack  is  less  effective  because  the
ice  is  often  unevenly  distributed.  Commercially  available  ice  packs  can  be  carried  in
your athletic bag until needed. Breaking a container inside the pack activates a chemical
reaction that creates a cold ice pack which lasts for 20 to 30 minutes.

Immersion in ice water can be effective for injuries to the ankles, hands, feet, elbows, and
sometimes knees. The ice bath can also be used during the later stages of injury rehabili-
tation when it is no longer necessary to elevate the injured part. Add about two-dozen ice
cubes to a sink or basin filled with cold water. Immerse the injured area in the ice water
and leave it there. If you are only able to tolerate a few minutes, take the injured part out
of the water for about a minute, and then reimmerse it for seven to eight minutes. Keep-
ing an injured area in the ice water for more than ten minutes will do little good. In fact,
you risk developing frostbite by immersing an injury for too long.

International Sports Sciences Association288  |  unit 21

hydrocollator packs: Canvas
bags filled with silicon that are
heated in water and used for
heat therapy.

After the swelling of an injured area has subsided,
use  heat  to  increase  circulation  and  relax  the
muscles.  Because  effective  healing  cannot
progress  until  swelling  decreases,  do  not  use
heat sooner than 48 to 72 hours after a muscle or
joint injury. Premature heat therapy may actually
increase swelling and retard the healing process.
This  is  particularly  true  for  muscle  pulls  and
joint damage, which are usually accompanied by
damage to the blood vessels.

Heat  can  be  administered  in  several  different
ways. The hot bath or whirlpool is probably easi-
est.  Water  temperature  should  be  kept  between
90¬∞  and  105¬∞  Fahrenheit,  with  104¬∞  being  com-
fortable for most people. Never allow your child to use a hot whirlpool bath if he or she
has  lost  the  tactile  sense;  in  this  kind  of  injury,  the  body  may  be  more  susceptible  to
burns. Other kinds of heat treatment include hot water bottles, heating pads, hydrocol-
lator packs (silicone-filled canvas bags heated in water), and heat lamps.

rehabIlITaTIon
Rehabilitation of muscle and joint injuries is critical. After an injury, the body attempts
to protect itself by producing muscle spasms and adhesions in joints that restrict motion.
Psychologically, the natural tendency is to baby the injury to keep it from getting worse.
Many athletes fail to restore normal function after an injury and never seem to get any
better. The athlete must work hard to restore as much strength, flexibility, and endurance
as possible. Start gradually, and systematically increase the intensity of rehabilitation ex-
ercises until normal function is restored.

An injured child should try to rehabilitate the injury as much as possible before returning
to full sports participation. For a serious injury, the decision to return should be made by
a physician. Optimal healing often requires rest from sports; an injured part of the body
that‚Äôs not completely healed is extremely vulnerable to re-injury. Here are some general
principles you can follow to help the child decide when to return to sports:

‚Ä¢  The child should not return to sports until the injured area is completely

restored to its full range of motion both actively and passively. Simply recover-
ing the ability to move the knee, shoulder, or elbow in a non-active situation
is not enough rehabilitation. The body must be able to withstand the rigors of
sports, as well as before the injury.

‚Ä¢  All compensating motions such as limping and favoring an injured area should
be eliminated. After a lower body injury, for instance, rehabilitation remains
incomplete unless the athlete can run forward, backward, and laterally, pain
free. They must also recover the ability to start and stop quickly.

‚Ä¢  The child should regain full strength and power before returning to active
sports. Compare the injured side with the uninjured side to see if size and
strength are equal. If not, more rehabilitation is required before resuming play.

‚Ä¢  The injured athlete should be relatively free of pain. Some pain is a natural

part of sports; the old football maxim, ‚ÄúYou‚Äôve got to play with small hurts,‚Äù
is true. However, if the pain in an injured area is significant, the injury is
not rehabilitated.

Youth FitnessCommon Injuries in Children‚Äôs Sports  |  289

overuse injuries: Injuries
developed through excessive
exercise or overtraining.

OvERuSE INJuRIES
Many young athletes can become over motivated, and they look as if they are trying to
make the Olympic team in a month. A variety of overuse injuries can occur when the
athlete trains beyond the body‚Äôs tolerance. Examples include shin splints, sore Achilles
tendons, and tennis elbow. These injuries occur from overworking a relatively weak area
and from improper sports techniques.

Modern training methods have produced an increase in overuse injuries. It is common
for young swimmers to put in as much as 15,000 meters per day or for young distance
runners to cover 100 miles a week. These brutal training programs do produce results.
Competitive records by young athletes continue to improve. However, the young athlete
is often pushed too far, too fast. Often, there is inadequate preparation or buildup for
these difficult exercise programs. Overuse injuries can be tricky to deal with, particularly
because the ultimate responsibility for rehabilitation lies with the individual athlete, the
family,  their  coaches,  and  personal  trainer.  Sometimes  athletes  must  rest  to  heal  and
improve performance.

The overuse injury is related to the overtraining syndrome. This condition is particularly
prevalent in highly motivated athletes. The overtrained athlete feels sluggish, tired, sore,
and  may  lack  enthusiasm.  Often  he  or  she  performs  well  during  practice  but  blows  it
during  competition.  Many  coaches  would  call  this  the  ‚Äúchoke‚Äù  syndrome.  However,
sports  scientists  have  recently  shown  that  overtraining  is  at  least  partially  due  to  lack
of  glycogen,  an  important  muscle  fuel.  Overtraining  depletes  glycogen,  the  complex
carbohydrate so important for muscular exercise. When muscles run low on this fuel, they
ache and do not perform as well. Other chemicals and fuel such as creatine phosphate,
testosterone, and cortisol are also linked to overtraining. Overtraining increases the risk
of overuse injuries when an athlete is fatigued and run down. In such cases, injury is just
around the corner.

Rest is very important for the overtrained athlete. Rest is the only thing that can break
the vicious cycle‚Äîthe hard training athlete does poorly in competition, so he trains even
harder. The harder he exercises, the more he gets overtrained. Competitive performances
get worse. The best thing for the overtrained athlete is to take a few days off and to check
the carbohydrate content of the diet. Any athlete involved in vigorous training should
consume large amounts of carbohydrates to maximize muscle fuel stores.

oSgood-SchlaTTer‚ÄôS dISeaSe
Osgood-Schlatter‚Äôs  disease is an overuse injury, not an actual disease. Adolescents
often experience pain, swelling, and tenderness just below the kneecap where the patella
ligament connects to the bony growth on the center of the upper part of the tibia (the
large bone of the lower leg). This condition, commonly known as Osgood-Schlatter‚Äôs dis-
ease, is caused by repeated small tears at the point where the patellar ligament connects
to the tibia.

Osgood-Schlatter‚Äôs  usually  afflicts  girls  between  eight  and  thirteen  years  and  boys  be-
tween ten and fifteen years. Boys are about three times more susceptible than girls. The
condition causes pain during kneeling, direct contact with objects, running, and climb-
ing. Pain relief comes from rest and restriction from physical activities. It usually does
not  disappear  completely  until  after  the  child  enters  adulthood  (about  age  21  to  23).
Advise parents to consult a physician if you suspect your client has this condition. Mild
hamstring and quad stretching, and cutting back on the volume and intensity of work-
outs usually brings relief.

IMbALANCE INJuRIES
Imbalance  injuries can be caused by poor posture, training that overdevelops some
muscle groups at the expense of others, and anatomical weaknesses. Normal movements
usually  do  not  involve  undue  risk  of  injury.  But  sports,  by  their  very  nature,  require
stretching a little further or trying a little harder. Add fatigue, anxiety, anger, poor body
mechanics, and bad luck, and any weak links in the body‚Äôs structure can be overwhelmed.
Injuries may be inevitable.

Muscle imbalance often causes injuries. Some people develop the muscles on the front
of the thigh (quadriceps), for example, without also developing the muscles to the same

Youth FitnessCommon Injuries in Children‚Äôs Sports  |  291

extent on the back of the thigh (hamstrings). This produces an imbalance that may pre-
cipitate hamstring pulls.

Back pain is also caused by muscle imbalances and lack of muscle endurance in the spine,
abdomen, hips, and thighs. Current treatment for the prevention and rehabilitation of
many kinds of back pain involves spinal stabilization, with exercises that increase muscle
endurance in the abdominal muscles (crunches), spinal muscles (birddog exercise), and
lateral trunk muscles (side bridges to increase the endurance of the obliques and quad-
ratus lumborum muscle).

Our upright posture causes many aches and pains that four-legged animals do not have.
Many children have minor anatomical imbalances that throw their bodies out of balance
and increase the risk of injury. For instance, if one leg is longer than the other, problems
may develop in the back, knees, or hips. A podiatrist (foot doctor) may be able to de-
sign orthotics to restore anatomical balance. Orthotics are inserts that fit into shoes and
place the feet and thus the legs in better anatomical alignment. They have proven valu-
able for many imbalance injuries. Orthotics can be expensive (about $300 to $500), but
often they improve sore knees and hips within a few days.

gROWTh PLATE INJuRIES
As you may recall from earlier in this text (Unit One), the growth plate, or epiphyseal
plate,  is  the  portion  of  the  bone  where  growth  occurs.  When  growth  is  complete,  the
epiphyses  fuse,  and  the  growth  plates  disappear.  Bone  growth  at  the  epiphyseal  plate
depends upon blood supply; growth may be compromised if this area is injured and the
blood is supply interrupted.

Sports and exercise can affect bone growth in different ways. The mild stress of active play
and sports makes bones stronger and more resilient, because bones adapt to stresses and
strains. Bones of active athletes have a larger diameter and marrow cavity (the portion of
bones where red blood cells are produced). Severe stress can cause bone inflammation
and interrupt normal bone growth.

The most common growth plate injuries in children‚Äôs  sports include  separation of the
growth plate (a bone break or fracture that goes through the growth plate) and crush
injuries to the growth plate. These injuries can occur by sudden contact, as when a child
tries  to  break  a  fall  with  outstretched  arms,  or  from  violent  twisting  motions  such  as
those that occur during skiing. Growth plate injuries can also occur more subtly from re-
peated throwing or running movements. These are classified as overuse injuries and can
be difficult to diagnose. So-called sprains around joints in the fingers, ankles, elbows,
knees, or shoulders may really be growth plate in-
juries.  Proper  diagnosis  often  requires  the  advice
of  an  orthopedic  specialist.  Chronic  pain  may  be
a sign of growth plate injury and should be investi-
gated by a qualified medical professional.

Growth  plate  injuries  are  fairly  uncommon  and
account for only six percent of all injuries in chil-
dren‚Äôs athletics. Children have relatively weak mus-
cles  and  low  body  weights  so  they  do  not  often
develop  the  high  forces  that  cause  these  injuries.
Certain  children,  however,  are  at  increased  risk
for  injury.  Overweight  children  can  create  forces
strong enough to cause growth plate injury. Muscle
development  can  lag  in  rapidly  growing  children

obliques: Important muscles
on the sides of the abdomen
that help to rotate and bend the
truck and stabilize the spine.

quadratus lumborum:
Important spinal stabilizing
muscles.

podiatrist: Physician who
specializes in foot problems.

orthotics: Shoe inserts that
provide foot support and correct
anatomical defects.

epiphyseal: Relating to the
bone growth centers.

sprain: Wrenching or laceration
of the ligaments of a joint.

Figure 21.2
Growth Plate Injuries.
Left to right, top row: Type I,

International Sports Sciences Association292  |  unit 21

and leave bones, joints, and growth plates more vulnerable to injury. After an illness or
injury, muscles become deconditioned to such an extent that growth plates are more sus-
ceptible to harm. Children should rebuild fitness after a period of deconditioning before
resuming active training and competition.

Throwing  sports  that  involve  pitching  or  javelin  throwing  place  an  unusual  strain  on
bone growth centers. The resulting injuries, called little league shoulder, javelin elbow, or
little league elbow, can be caused by too much throwing and poor technique. The high
incidence  of  shoulder  and  arm  injuries  has  created  a  bad  reputation  for  Little  League
baseball among some pediatricians.

Little League baseball limits the number of innings pitched. Total pitches in a game are
well within the bounds of safety. Unfortunately, most children pitch at home in addition
to official practice. The extra strain of the outside practice probably causes most injuries.

The method of delivery is an important factor in throwing injuries. There are many more
injuries reported when pitchers used a sidearm delivery than a throw that comes right
over  the  top.  Limit  or  stop  pitching  if  the  child  has  arm  or  shoulder  pain.  Continued
pitching with an arm injury could lead to problems later in life and compromise further
athletic development.

Javelin  throwing  can  easily  result  in  growth  plate  injuries  in  children.  Because  of  the
weight of the implement, errors in throwing technique can severely affect bone growth
centers. Many experts believe that children should not throw the javelin until they ap-
proach  skeletal  maturity‚Äîusually  at  age  16  to  18.  The  natural  tendency  of  beginning
throwers is to use a sidearm motion that disposes them to injury. Proper javelin tech-
nique requires expert coaching, which is rarely available to young adolescents.

OThER CONDITIONS

mononucleoSIS
Symptoms of infectious  mononucleosis include flu-like symptoms of sore throat, fe-
ver,  enlarged  lymph  nodes  in  the  neck,  and  fatigue. The  Epstein-Barr  virus  carried  in
the throat and bloodstream causes ‚Äúmono.‚Äù Many people are immune to mononucleosis
because their bodies produce an antibody to the disease. Those who do not produce the
antibody remain susceptible to the illness.

The symptoms usually last 14 to 21 days. The disease may produce an enlarged spleen,
which may delay an athlete‚Äôs return to practice. The spleen is a reservoir and cleanser of
blood;  if  enlarged,  it  can  easily  rupture  during  contact  sports.  An  athlete  with  mono-
nucleosis and an enlarged spleen should be examined and cleared by a physician. Most
physicians recommend a delay of three to four weeks after the disappearance of symp-
toms before returning to contact sports. Any vigorous exercise with an enlarged spleen
places the athlete at increased risk. A ruptured spleen is a major medical emergency.

Preventing mononucleosis is difficult. Transmission occurs by kissing or drinking from
a  contaminated  cup  or  pop  bottle.  Avoid  drinking  from  communal  glasses  or  cups  at
practice and tell athletes to wash their hands regularly, especially before meals and after
going to the bathroom.

mononucleosis: Caused by
infection with the Epstein Barr
virus. Characterized by extreme
fatigue, enlarged spleen, and
decreased exercise capacity.

spleen: Organ located on the
left side of the abdomen near
the stomach that produces
lymphocytes, filters the blood,
stores blood cells and destroys
those that are aging.

Youth FitnessCommon Injuries in Children‚Äôs Sports  |  293

asthma: Lung disease
characterized by narrowing of
the lung passageways, making
breathing difficult.

aSThma
Asthma is a breathing disorder characterized by choking, shortness of breath, tightness in
the chest, increased mucus production, and fatigue. There are over 1.5 million asthmatic
children in the United States, most of whom shun sports and vigorous physical activity.
This  is  understandable  because  exercise  is  one  of  the  prime  causes  of  asthma  attacks.
Many parents, coaches, school administrators, and even physicians advise asthmatics to
avoid sports and normal physical education activities. Advances in medicine have ena-
bled asthmatics to excel in vigorous sports, even to the point of winning Olympic medals.

Two factors are important in preventing asthmatic attacks during and following exer-
cise‚Äîmedication and environment. Modern medications enable asthmatics to partici-
pate in even the most vigorous sports by preventing exercise-induced asthma or reducing
its effects once they have occurred. If you suspect your young client has asthma, advise
the parents to take the child to a physician. In most instances asthmatics lead relatively
normal lives.

Environmental factors can cause an asthmatic attack. Modify or curtail exercise when
the  child  is  tired  or  under  excessive  emotional  stress,  on  cold,  foggy,  or  humid  days,
and during days with high smog and pollution levels. Swimming in cold pools can also
trigger an asthma attack. Under adverse environmental conditions, keep exercise bouts
short‚Äîless than three minutes at a time. However, the exercise levels should be adjusted
to individual tolerance.

294  |  unit 21

SuMMARY

An athlete with a muscle or joint injury should not return to normal athletic play
until full strength, power, and range of motion have been restored. Movement
should have returned to normal without compensating limping movements,
and the child should be relatively pain free.

Overuse and overtraining injuries are often caused by doing too much, too
soon. Rest is important in overuse conditions and often produces better
performance than heavy training. Imbalance injuries can produce pains in the
knees, hips, and back. Such injuries are caused by poor posture, unbalanced
muscle groups, or anatomical abnormalities. Growth plate injuries are of
great concern in children‚Äôs sports. They can be caused by sudden traumatic
injuries from sports such as skiing or football, or by overuse in throwing sports
or running. Asthmatic children can participate in sports if they receive the
proper medication and are afforded individual consideration during adverse
environmental, physical, or emotional conditions.
