gLOSSARY

# Glossary

320  |  glossary

A

absolute  zero:  Lowest  possible  temperature  (0¬∞  Kelvin,  -273.15¬∞
Celsius, -459.67¬∞ Fahrenheit) where all molecular motion ceases.

acclimatization:  Physiological  adaptation  to  an  environmental
stressor.

acromegaly: Condition produced by excess growth hormone pro-
duction;    characterized  by  enlarged  facial  features,  enlarged  jaw,
enlarged frontal bone of skull, widely spaced teeth, and enlarged
extremity bones.

arteries: Blood vessels that supply oxygenated blood to the tissues.

arthritis: Inflammatory condition that affects joints. Can be infec-
tive, autoimmune, or traumatic in origin.

articular: Referring to a joint.

asthma: Lung disease characterized by narrowing of the lung pas-
sageways, making breathing difficult.

atria: Two chambers found at the top of the heart that pump blood
to the ventricles.

activity  pyramid:  Model  for  meeting  the  suggested  amount  of
physical activity.

B

adenosine triphosphate (ATP): Chemical found in all living cells
that provides an energy source for many metabolic processes.

adipocytes: Specialized cells that makes and store fat.

adolescence:  Period  of  physical  and  psychological  development
from the onset of puberty to maturity.

agility: Ability to change directions rapidly.

air displacement plethysmography: A method of measuring body
density and estimating body composition by air displacement. The
BodPod is an example of an instrument that uses this technique.

alarm  reaction:  First  stage  of  the  general  adaptation  syndrome
(GAS) involving the initial response to a stressor; responsible for
mobilizing an organism.

aldosterone: Steroid hormone produced by the adrenal cortex that
controls salt and water balance in the kidney.

alevoli:  Terminal  air  sacs  in  the  lungs  surrounded  by  capillaries
where gas exchange occurs.

amino acids: Nitrogen-containing compounds that serve as build-
ing blocks for proteins.

anabolic: Chemical process involving synthesis.

anabolic  steroids:  Synthetic  derivatives  of  testosterone  with  tis-
sue building properties and relatively weak androgenic properties,
used mainly to promote growth and to repair body tissues.

androstenedione:  Hormone  secreted  by  the  adrenal  gland  that
can  degrade  to  testosterone  and  estrogen;  used  as  an  ergogenic
substance.

angina: Heart pain associated with the heart muscle‚Äôs not receiving
sufficient oxygen.

anthropometric:  Measurement  of  the  size,  weight,  and  propor-
tions of the body.

anticatabolic: Preventative of breakdown processes.

arrhythmia: Any variation from the normal rhythm of the heart-
beat,  including  sinus  arrhythmia,  premature  beat,  heart  block,
atrial fibrillation, atrial flutter, and paroxysmal tachycardia.

arterialvenous  oxygen  difference:  Difference  in  oxygen  content
between the arteries and the veins.

balance: Ability to remain steady and under control from a mov-
ing or stationary base.

ballistic stretching: Stretching the muscles during movement.

bioelectrical  impedance:  Body  composition  measurement  tech-
nique  that  measures  how  fast  an  electric  current  passes  through
the body‚Äôs fat and non-fat tissues.

bioimpedence: A body composition measurement technique that
measures the resistance of the body in response to a small electric
charge. High levels of body fat increase electrical resistance.

blood pressure: Force that the circulating blood exerts on arterial
walls. This measurement is divided into systolic (pressure during
contraction of the heart) and diastolic (pressure during relaxation)
phases.

BMI-for-age: Used to measure obesity risk in children. A child is
considered overweight if the BMI-for-age is greater than the 85th
percentile.

Bodpod:  Body  composition  measurement  technique  that  meas-
ures  body  volume  by  air  displacement  with  conversion  to  per-
centage body fat from body density (body density from Bodpod
= mass/volume)

body composition: Physical make-up of the human body that in-
cludes two main components‚Äîfat weight and fat-free weight.

body mass index (BMI): An index of the proportion of weight to
height. BMI= weight (kg)/(height (m)2; BMI= [weight (lb)/height
(in)/height (in)] x 703

breathing  frequency:  Number  of  breaths  a  person  takes  per
minute.

C

calisthenics: Exercises that use body weight as the resistance.

calorie:  A  measure  of  energy;  usually  expressed  as  kilocalorie
(kcal). 1 kcal = the amount of energy required to increase the tem-
perature of 1 liter of water 1 degree C.

capillaries: Small vessels lying between arteries and veins that de-
liver oxygen and fuels to tissues and that remove carbon dioxide
and wastes.

Youth Fitnessglossary  |  321

carbohydrates:  Major  energy  providing  substrates  including
starches,  sugars,  cellulose  and  hemicellulose.  All  carbohydrates
contain  carbon,  hydrogen,  and  oxygen,  and  are  usually  divided
into  two  fractions‚Äîstructural  (fiber)  and  non-structural  (sugars
and starches).

cardiac hypertrophy: Enlargement of the heart muscle (myocar-
dium).

cardiac output: Amount the heart pumps each minute. It is deter-
mined by stroke volume and heart rate.

cardiomyopathy: Disease or inflammation of the heart muscle.

cardiorespiratory  system:  Relating  to  the  heart  and  lungs  and
their functions.

cardiovascular drift: Tendency for the heart rate to increase grad-
ually with time during exercise.

catabolic: Chemical process involving breakdown.

cataract: Discoloration of the lens of one or both eyes that impairs
vision or causes blindness.

D

DEA: Drug Enforcement Agency: United States government agen-
cy that oversees enforcement of drug laws.

deconditioning: Loss of fitness through rest or injury.

dehydration: Excessive loss of body water.

dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA): Hormone secreted by the ad-
renal gland that can degrade to testosterone and estrogen; used as
an ergogenic substance.

delayed onset muscle soreness (DOMS): The pain  and stiffness
felt in muscles several hours to days after exercise. Believed to be
the result of microscopic damage to muscle fibers involved in the
exercise.

diabetes  (mellitus):  Condition  marked  by  relative  or  absolute
lack of insulin leading to uncontrolled carbohydrate metabolism.
In Type 1 diabetes, the insulin deficiency is almost total, while in
Type  2  diabetes  the  defect  is  related  more  to  insulin  resistance,
obesity, and lack of exercise.

catecholamine neurotransmitters: Molecules such as adrenaline
and noradrenaline (epinephrine and norepinephrine) that trigger
specific physiological effects.

diaphysis: Shaft of a long bone.

diastolic: Relaxation phase of the cardiac cycle.

cell  membrane:  Structure  surrounding  a  cell  and  enclosing  the
cytoplasm that forms a barrier that allows some substances, such
as oxygen, fuels, and wastes, through its walls. It is made of fats,
proteins and some carbohydrates.

central blood volume: Blood returning to the heart.

childhood:  Period  between  the  age  of  two  and  the  onset  of  pu-
berty.

collagen:  Protein  substance  of  the  white  fibers  of  skin,  tendon,
bone, cartilage, and other connective tissues.

collars: Devices to hold weight plates on barbells or dumbbells.

conduction: Transfer of heat between objects.

convection: Gain or loss of heat to or from air or water.

coronary artery blood flow: Blood flow through the heart‚Äôs blood
vessels.

coronary  heart  (artery)  disease:  Atherosclerosis‚Äîa  condition
in  which  the  coronary  arteries  become  narrowed  or  completely
blocked. This is the main cause of a heart attack.

correlation: Measure of the degree of relationship or association
between two variables such as height versus weight or maximum
arm strength versus maximum leg strength.

creatine phosphate (CP): Important energy storage chemical the
body uses to regenerate ATP.

cross‚Äìtraining: Including more than one kind of exercise in the
training program.

diffusion:  Spontaneous  migration  of  substances  from  regions
where their concentration is high to regions where concentration
is low.

Dinitrophenol: Drug that causes uncoupling of metabolism and
loss of energy as heat instead of storage as fat.

diseases of civilization: Diseases linked to lifestyle.

diuretics: Drugs or substances that promote urine excretion.

E

ectopic beats: Heart beat originating somewhere other than at the
heart‚Äôs pacemaker or SA node.

elastic  component  of  muscle:  Muscle  component  mainly  com-
posed  of  connective  tissue  that  acts  much  like  a  rubber  band  to
increase elasticity in muscle.

elastin:  Randomly  coiled  and  cross-linked  substance  that  forms
elastic fibers in connective tissue.

electrolyte: Chemical that dissociates into ions (has a negative or
positive charge) in a solution and thus conduct electricity (nerve
or muscle impulses).

epinephrine:  Adrenal  hormone  that  increases  systemic  vascular
resistance and improves coronary and cerebral blood flow. It in-
creases myocardial electrical activity and strength of contraction,
stimulates  carbohydrate  metabolism,  and  increases  heart  muscle
oxygen requirements and automaticity. It plays an important part
in the body‚Äôs response to danger and arousal.

cryokinetics: Rehabilitation technique that uses exercise and ice.

epiphyseal: Relating to the bone growth centers.

International Sports Sciences Association322  |  glossary

epiphyseal growth plate: Part of a long bone where bone growth
occurs.

epiphysis: The end of a long bone that is originally separated from
the main bond by a layer of cartilage but later becomes united to
the main bone through ossification.

essential fat: Fat stored in the marrow of bones and in the heart,
lungs,  liver,  spleen,  kidneys,  intestines,  muscles,  and  fat-rich  tis-
sues throughout the central nervous system.

evaporation: Change from a liquid to a vapor (gas) form.

exercise: Planned, structured, repetitive movement designed spe-
cifically to improve or maintain physical fitness.

glycogen: Compound consisting of grouped glucose units that is
an important fuel source for high intensity exercise. It is found in
high quantities in the liver and muscles.

Golgi tendon organs (GTOs): Compressed sensory nerve endings
embedded in the tendon and activated by any increase of the ten-
don‚Äôs tension, caused either by active contraction or passive muscle
stretch.

growth hormone: Hormone produced by the pituitary gland that
regulates protein, fat, and carbohydrate metabolism by stimulating
the liver to produce tissue growth factors (i.e., insulin-like growth
factor-1)

growth spurt: Period of rapid increase in height or weight.

F

H

fast-twitch muscle fibers: Skeletal muscle fibers having high my-
ofibrillar ATPase activity, high glycolytic enzyme activities, and an
intermediate glycogen content that produces a fast twitch. Types
include type IIa and type IIb.

fatigue resistance: Ability to exercise at a specific exercise inten-
sity for extended durations.

fats: Storage lipids in animal tissues. The primary components are
triglyceride esters of long chain fatty acids.

fatty acid mobilizer: Substance that speeds fat breakdown.

flexibility: The ability to move a joint through its range of motion
(ROM).

free radicals: highly reactive molecules which target your tissue‚Äôs
protein  bonds,  the  DNA  in  your  cells‚Äô  nuclei  and  the  important
polyunsaturated  fatty  acids  within  your  cells‚Äô  membranes.  Once
initiated, a chain reaction begins that ultimately results in the total
destruction of that cell.

free weight: Equipment moved in the performance of an exercise,
which is simply raised and lowered as a complete unit. The weight
is free to move in any direction and in any manner the lifter can
manage; free weights includes barbell and dumbbells.

frequency: Number of training sessions in a given period.

frostbite: Cold injury accompanied by pallor, numbness, and loss
of cold sensation.

functional stretching: Dynamic  stretching  through  the  range of
motion used in specific sports or movements of daily life.

G

general  adaptation  syndrome  (GAS):  Theory  developed  by  Dr.
Hans Selye to describe the way the body reacts and adapts (or fails
to adapt) to physical and emotional stresses.

gluconeogenesis:  Chemical  process  in  the  liver  that  converts
chemicals, such as lactate, pyruvate, and alanine, into glucose.

glucose: Chemical found in many foods, especially fruits. Serves
as the chief source of energy for living organisms. The hormone
insulin controls its use.

habituation:  Disappearance  of  responsiveness  to  accustomed
stimulation. Getting used to the stressor with no apparent adapta-
tion.

heart rate monitor: Electronic device worn on the body to meas-
ure heart rate during exercise.

heat cramps: Muscle spasms and pain caused by intense exercise
in the heat; sometimes related to salt deficiency, hyperventilation,
or overindulgence in alcohol.

heat  exhaustion:  Heat  illness  related  to  dehydration.  Common
symptoms  include  fatigue,  lightheadedness,  nausea,  vomiting,
headache, rapid heartbeat and low blood pressure.

heat stroke: Severe and often fatal illness produced by  exposure
to  excessively  high  temperatures,  especially  when  accompanied
by intense exercise. Symptoms may include increased body tem-
perature, lack of sweating (50% of victims continue to sweat), hot
dry skin, and neurological symptoms, unconsciousness, paralysis,
headache, vertigo, confusion. In severe cases very high fever, vas-
cular collapse, and coma develop.

heat transfer: Movement of heat from one place to another.

hemoglobin: Protein found in blood cells that transports oxygen
and carbon dioxide.

hemoglobinuria: High levels of hemoglobin in the urine.

homeotherm: Organism that maintains a constant internal body
temperature.

hydrocollator packs: Canvas bags filled with silicon that are heat-
ed in water and used for heat therapy.

hydrogen  ion:  Hydrogen  atom  minus  its  electron  and  therefore
carrying  a  positive  charge.  The  pH‚Äîan  important  measure  of
acid-base balance‚Äîis the negative logarithm of the hydrogen ion
concentration. High or low levels of hydrogen ions interfere with
chemical reactions in the body.

hydrostatic  weighing:  An  accurate  method  for  estimating  body
composition. Determines body density.

hypertrophy: Development of large muscle fibers.

Youth Fitnessglossary  |  323

hypothalamus:  Portion  of  the  lower  brain,  which  secretes  sub-
stances that control metabolism through the pituitary gland. The
hypothalamus helps to regulate body temperature, appetite, water
balance, blood sugar, and fat metabolism. The hypothalamus also
regulates other glands such as the ovaries, testes, parathyroids, and
thyroid.

hypothermia: Low body temperature due to exposure to the cold.
Opposite of hyperthermia: Abnormally high body temperature.

I

ice massage: Massaging a body part with a small block of ice.

imbalance injuries: Injuries stemming from muscles on one side
of the body having more strength or flexibility than opposing mus-
cles.

immediate energy system: Chemical system composed mainly of
ATP and CP that provides rapid bursts of energy to cells.

individual  differences:  People  respond  differently  to  the  same
stressor, largely due to genetic factors.

insensible water loss: Water loss from the body that does not oc-
cur through the sweat glands.

insulin: Hormone produced in the pancreas that regulates carbo-
hydrate, fat, and protein metabolism.

insulin  resistance:  Condition  where  the  body  produces  enough
insulin, but the insulin receptors do not respond to its action. This
may happen because the person is inactive or overweight and has
an excessive number of fat cells that do not respond well to insulin.

insulin-like growth factor (IGF-1): Insulin-like chemicals made
mainly by the liver and released into the blood when stimulated by
growth hormone. They are involved in RNA and DNA synthesis
(important substances involved in creating new proteins), which
trigger cell division and growth of the body.

interval  training:  High  intensity  training  technique  that  varies
load, repetition, distance, and rest using a variety of exercises such
as swimming, running, and cycling.

isometric: Exerting force statically or against immovable objects
that does not result in muscle shortening.

K

kidneys: Pair of organs located in the right and left side of the ab-
domen that clear toxins from the blood, regulate acid concentra-
tion, and maintain the body‚Äôs water balance by excreting urine.

L

lactate:  Important  as  the  terminal  product  of  anaerobic  glycoly-
sis (breakdown of glucose or blood sugar). It is a very important
fuel for the body, but it can interfere with metabolism and cause
fatigue.

lactic  acid:  Syrupy,  water-soluble  liquid  (C3H6O3)  produced  in
muscles from anaerobic glucose metabolism.

leptin: Hormone produced in fat cells and circulates in blood that
reduces food uptake and increases energy expenditure.

linked motions: sequential activation of muscle groups and joint
movements when performing motor skills.

load: Resistance or weight used during the exercise.

long bone: One of the long bones of the extremities, consisting of
a tubular shaft (diaphysis) and two ends (epiphyses) usually wider
than the shaft.

M

maximal oxygen consumption: Maximum amount of oxygen de-
livered to the body‚Äôs trillions of cells within tissues. It is a function
of maximum cardiac output and maximum arterialvenous oxygen
difference (maximum blood flow times maximum oxygen extrac-
.
tion). It is symbolized as
VO2max.

maximum heart rate: Highest rate the heart can reach‚Äîusually
during exercise‚Äîin one minute.

mean: Measure of central tendency or average.

mean arterial pressure: One third of systolic pressure plus diasto-
lic pressure. Most people have a mean arterial blood pressure of
100 mm Hg.

menarche: Beginning of menstruation.

menstruation:  Hormonally  controlled  monthly  discharge  of
blood and mucosal tissues through the vagina from the uterus that
occurs in non-pregnant women between puberty through meno-
pause that marks the end of the menstrual cycle.

metabolic acids: Chemicals produced during metabolism that re-
lease a hydrogen ion.

metabolic enzymes: Chemicals that tremendously speeds the rate
of chemical reactions during metabolism.

metabolic rate: Amount of energy expended in a given period‚Äî
usually expressed in METs (metabolic equivalents). One MET is
equivalent to resting metabolism.

metabolism: Sum of all the physical and chemical processes oc-
curring in the body, which includes anabolic (tissue building) and
catabolic (breakdown) reactions.

minerals: Inorganic food elements essential for life.

mitochondria:  Small  organelle  found  inside  the  cell  responsible
for energy production and cellular respiration.

mononucleosis: Caused by infection with the Epstein Barr virus.
Characterized by extreme fatigue, enlarged spleen, and decreased
exercise capacity.

monounsaturated  fat:  Fatty  acid  chain  with  at  least  two  empty
spaces where a hydrogen atom could be added. Saturated fatty ac-
ids present a greater health risk for the development of coronary
artery disease.

International Sports Sciences Association324  |  glossary

motor development: How children develop and learn motor tasks

motor learning: Study of how people learn physical skills.

motor skills: Performance of complex movement tasks.

motor unit: The functional unit of muscular contraction consist-
ing of a motor nerve and the muscle fibers it innervates.

motor  unit  recruitment:  Activation  of  more  motor  units  with
greater force output.

muscle endurance: Ability to exert force for an extended period
of time.

Orlistat  (Xenical):  FDA  approved  drug  that  inhibits  absorption
of dietary fats.

orthotics: Shoe inserts that provide foot support and correct ana-
tomical defects.

Osgood-Schlatter‚Äôs disease: Inflammation of the region where the
patellar  tendon  inserts  into  the  tibia.  This  condition  is  relatively
common in adolescents.

osmoreceptors:  Receptors  in  the  hypothalamus  that  respond  to
changes in the osmotic pressure (due to solid material in the blood
fluid) of the blood.

muscle fiber: Cellular components of a skeletal muscle that con-
tain myofibrils.

ossification: Formation of bone involving the conversion of car-
tilage into bone.

muscle hypertrophy: Increase in muscle fiber size.

myelination: Insulating layer surrounding peripheral nerves that
dramatically increases the speed of conduction.

myofibrils: Slender threads in a muscle fiber that run parallel with
the long axis of the fiber and are composed of numerous myofila-
ments.

myoglobinuria: High levels of myoglobin in the urine.

N

neuropathy:  Functional  disturbances  or  pathological  changes  in
the peripheral nervous system.

non‚Äìessential fat: Storage fat found in adipose tissue.

non‚Äìoxidative  energy  system:  Chemical  system  that  mainly  in-
volves the anaerobic breakdown of glucose and glycogen into lactic
acid. It provides rapid bursts of energy to cells.

non‚Äìshivering  thermogenesis:  Body  heat  production  triggered
by uncoupling proteins in the cells or brown fat.

norepinephrine:  Catecholamine  neurohormone,  the  major  neu-
rotransmitter of the sympathetic nervous system. It is stored and
released from the adrenal medulla.

osteoporosis: Reduction in bone mass that leads to fractures fol-
lowing minimal trauma.

overload: Progressively increasing exercise stress during training.

overtraining: Imbalance between training and recovery.

overuse injuries: Injuries developed through excessive exercise or
overtraining.

oxidative energy system: Chemical system that depends on oxy-
gen and involves the metabolism of fats, carbohydrates, and pro-
teins and that produces large amounts of ATP in the mitochondria.

oxygen:  Gas  making  up  approximately  21percent  of  the  atmos-
phere and that is essential for cell respiration‚Äîthe energy produc-
ing reactions in the cells.

P

pacinian corpuscles: Structures that detect movement and pres-
sure changes in the muscles and joints.

paroxysmal atrial tachycardia: Bouts of rapid, regular heartbeats
originating in the upper heart chamber.

passive stretching: Stretching in which a partner assists in moving
joints through their ranges of motion.

nucleus:  Genetic  ‚Äúmaster  controller‚Äù  of  the  cell  that  directs  cell
growth and repair.

percentile rank: Percent of scores falling above and below a spe-
cific point.

O

obesity:  Excess  body  fat,  typically  defined  as  a  body  fat  content
that exceeds 20 percent in males and 30 percent in females.

obliques: Important muscles on the sides of the abdomen that help
to rotate and bend the truck and stabilize the spine.

Olympic weight lifting: The two weight lifting movements used
in Olympic competitions: the snatch, and the clean and jerk. The
military press was eliminated as a contested lift after the 1972 Ol-
ympics.

opioid receptors: Cell receptors that bind with opioids in the body,
such as endorphins, and produce specific physiological effects.

periodization:  Cycling  the  volume  and  intensity  of  training  to
maximize the rate of adaptation.

peripheral nerve damage: Nerve damage in the extremities.

peripheral resistance: Total resistance to flow of blood in the sys-
temic circulation. It is determined by dividing mean arterial pres-
sure by cardiac output.

physical  fitness:  Capacity  to  increase  metabolism  and  meet  the
demands of physical effort.

piloerection:  Contraction  of  small  muscles  (arrectores  pilorum
muscles) surrounding hair follicles that causes the hairs to stand
straight up. Goose bumps.

pilomotor  center:  Center  in  the  hypothalamus  that  causes  pilo-
erection (goose bumps).

Youth Fitnessglossary  |  325

plasma: The fluid (noncellular) portion of the circulating blood.

plasma volume: The volume of plasma in circulating blood.

plyometrics:  Resistance  training  technique  that  uses  muscle
stretch followed immediately by muscle contraction; an example
is box jumping.

podiatrist: Physician who specializes in foot problems.

poikilotherm:  Cold-blooded  animal  such  as  a  fish  or  reptile,
whose internal body temperature varies with the environment.

polyunsaturated  fat:  Fat  that  contains  more  than  one  carbon-
carbon double bond.

power:  Work  per  unit  of  time.  From  a  practical  standpoint,  de-
fined  as  ability  to  exert  force  rapidly.  Measured  by  the  formula:
work equals force times distance divided by time. A combination
of strength and speed.

power output: Intensity of exercise expressed in watts.

progression: People adapt best to training when there are increas-
ing levels of difficulty in a training program.

proprioceptive  neuromuscular  facilitation  (PNF):  Training
technique  that  uses  muscle  and  neural  reflexes  to  augment  the
training stimulus.

proteins:  Naturally-occurring  compounds  containing  nitrogen,
carbon, hydrogen and oxygen, and sometimes sulfur or phospho-
rus. Proteins are made up of complex combinations of amino acids
and are essential for animal growth, production, and reproduction.

proteinuria: High levels of protein in the urine.

pubertal growth spurt: Increase in growth rate that occurs at the
onset of sexual maturation.

puberty:  Stage  of  adolescence  in  which  an  individual  becomes
physiologically capable of sexual reproduction.

pulmonary perfusion: Movement of blood into the lungs through
the pulmonary circulation.

pyruvate: Important as a product of anaerobic glycolysis (break-
down of glucose or blood sugar).

Q

Q10 Effect: Rate of chemical reactions double with each 10¬∞ C in-
crease in temperature.

quadratus lumborum: Important spinal stabilizing muscles.

R

radiation: Heat loss or gain in the form of infrared rays.

range of motion (ROM): The distance and direction a joint can
move between the flexed position and the fully extended position.

recombinant IFG-1: IGF-1 produced using gene splicing technol-
ogy.

rectal temperature: Temperature of the rectum. Often used in ex-
ercise studies as a measure of core or central temperature.

red blood cell (rbc): Also called an erythrocyte that carries oxygen
and carbon dioxide and helps regulate acid-base balance.

regression: Process for determining the extent of relationship or
prediction between an established variable (for example maximum
strength)  and  one  or  more  independent  variables  (for  example,
height, weight, arm length, forearm and biceps girth).

rehabilitation: Return of function after an injury, often with the
assistance of physical therapists or athletic trainers.

repetition: Number of times the athlete repeats the exercise.

repetition  maximum  (RM):  Most  weight  lifted  while  perform-
ing a prescribed number of repetitions. For example, 1-RM is the
maximum weight lifted once.

resistance development stage: Second stage of GAS, during which
the body actively attempts to adjust to the effects of a stressor.

rest: Amount of time spent not exercising between sets or work-
outs. Rest is essential for adaptation and should be incorporated
into every training program.

reversibility: Loss of fitness gains with removal of the stress of ex-
ercise.

S

satellite cells: Small cells in the muscle bundle believed to play a
role in muscle repair and regeneration by fusing with adjacent my-
ofibers.

saturated fat: Fatty acid with all potential hydrogen binding sites
filled. These pose the highest risk for the development of athero-
sclerosis.

self-image: How children perceive themselves.

serotonin neurotransmitters: Chemicals found in the brain and
circulation.

shivering: Physiologic method of heat production in humans and
other mammals that involves involuntary trembling or quivering
from contraction or twitching of the muscles.

shortness  of  breath:  Distressed  breathing  pattern,  also  called
dyspnea.

skill: Ability to perform a discrete motor task.

skinfold technique: Estimates body fatness by measuring skinfold
thickness at key sites on the body.

skinfold thickness: Layer of adipose (fat) tissue just below the skin
surface usually measured with a caliper at a specific anatomic loca-
tion to describe fatness in that region.

slow-twitch muscle fibers: Skeletal muscle fibers having low my-
ofibrillar  ATPase  activity,  low  glycogen  content,  high  myoglobin
content,  high  mitochondrial  oxidative  enzyme  activities,  and  an
intermediate  mitochondrial  content  that  produces  a  slow  twitch
and is fatigue-resistant.motor unit: A single motor nerve and the
group of muscle fibers it supplies.

International Sports Sciences Association326  |  glossary

somotomedin  C:  Growth  factor  produced  by  the  liver  and  trig-
gered by growth hormone.

T

specificity: Body adapts to the nature of the stressor.

specificity of training: The body‚Äôs unique reaction to a stressor.

speed: Ability to move quickly.

spleen:  Organ  located  on  the  left  side  of  the  abdomen  near  the
stomach that produces lymphocytes, filters the blood, stores blood
cells, and destroys aging blood cells.

sprain: Wrenching or laceration of the ligaments of a joint.

target heart rate: Desirable exercise heart rate calculated by mul-
tiplying  the  maximum  heart  rate  by  a  number  between  0.6  and
0.9, which sets the exercise intensity between 60 and 90 percent of
maximum effort.

temperature  regulation:  Physiological  processes  that  maintain
body temperature at a relatively constant level.

testosterone: Hormone (called an androgen) secreted by the testis
of mammals that triggers the development of sperm, muscle mass,
and many secondary sexual characteristics.

stage of exhaustion: Third stage of GAS, during which the organ-
ism can no longer resist stress.

thermogenic: Heat production.

standard deviation: Measure of variability used to describe a set
of data.

static stretching: Stretching by holding the muscle in a stretched
position without movement.

stimulus  variability:  Changing  the  nature  of  the  exercise  stress.
This may include varying the intensity and volume of exercise or
performing different modes of exercise.

stretch-shortening  cycle:  An  active  stretch  (eccentric  contrac-
tion) of a muscle followed by an immediate shortening (concentric
contraction) of that same muscle.

stroke  volume:  Volume  of  blood  pumped  from  a  ventricle  each
beat. Over a few beats, stroke volume from the left and right ven-
tricles remains equal.

subcutaneous fat: Fat stores lying directly beneath the skin.

submaximal: Being less than the maximum of which an individual
is capable.

sunstroke:  Severe  and  often  fatal  illness  produced  by  exposure
to  excessively  high  temperatures,  especially  when  accompanied
by intense exercise. Symptoms may include increased body tem-
perature, lack of sweating (50% of victims continue to sweat), hot
dry skin, and neurological symptoms; unconsciousness, paralysis,
headache, vertigo, confusion. In severe cases very high fever, vas-
cular collapse, and coma develop.

sympathetic nervous system: Part of the autonomic nervous sys-
tem  (the  parasympathetic  nervous  system  is  the  other  part)  that
innervates heart and blood vessels, sweat glands, viscera, and the
adrenal medulla. Most sympathetic nerves release norepinephrine
as the neurotransmitter.

sympathetic tone: Signals sent from the sympathetic nervous sys-
tem to the blood vessels that keep them partially closed at rest or
during submaximum exercise.

systolic: Active or pumping phase of the cardiac cycle.

thermal  gradient:  Temperature  differences  in  two  parts  of  the
body or between the body and the environment.

thermogenic drugs: Drugs or substances that increase metabolic
heat production.

thyroid hormone: Thyroxine and triiodothyronine, the hormones
secreted by the thyroid gland, influence growth and metabolism.

tidal volume: Volume of air inhaled and exhaled with each breath.

transfer:  Ability  to  incorporate  strength  or  fitness  developed  in
training to improved performance in sports skills.

transference:  Physical  attraction  developed  between  a  coach  or
trainer and athlete from working closely with each other.

traumatic: Relating to or resulting from physical or psychological
trauma, wound, or injury.

triglycerides: Storage fats in adipose tissue, largely made of glyc-
erol and fatty acids.

type 2 diabetes: Related to insulin resistance and also referred to
as adult-onset diabetes. It is more common in middle-age, over-
weight people, and is usually treated by exercise, body weight re-
duction, and oral hypoglycemic agents.

U

unacclimatized: People who have not adapted to environmental
stressors.

underwater  weighing:  Body  composition  measurement  tech-
nique that assesses the density of the body by water displacement.

V

valsalva  maneuver:  Expiratory  effort  against  a  closed  glottis,
which  increases  pressure  within  the  thoracic  cavity  and  thereby
impedes venous return of blood to the heart.

vasoconstriction:  Decrease  in  the  internal  diameter  of  a  blood
vessel that decreases blood flow.

Youth Fitnessglossary  |  327

vasodilation: Increase in the internal diameter of a blood vessel
that increases blood flow.

veins: A blood vessel carrying blood toward the heart; all the veins
except the pulmonary carry dark or oxygenated blood

ventilation: Movement of air in and out of the lungs.

ventricles:  Two  chambers  found  at  the  bottom  of  the  heart  that
pump blood to the lungs and general circulation.

visceral: Refers to the internal organs.

vitamins:  Organic  compounds  that  function  as  parts  of  enzyme
systems essential for many metabolic functions.
.
VO2max:    Maximum  volume  of  oxygen  consumed  by  the  body
each minute during exercise, while breathing air at sea level

W

weight lifting belts: Belts that stabilize the spine and abdominal
region  during  weight  lifting.  Children  should  avoid  using  them
because they hinder the development of core stabilizing muscles.

weight machine: Equipment moved in the performance of an ex-
ercise, which is not simply raised and lowered as a complete unit.
Exercise machines guide or restrict the direction and extent of a
movement. They use cams, lever arms, pulleys, or cables to redi-
rect or alter the resistance, or to generate the resistance through
springs, hydraulic or pneumatic pistons, magnets, or elastic bands
rather than weights.

weight  training:  Resistance  training  using  weights  (barbells,
dumbbells, weight plates).

windchill: Effects of air movement on reducing the effective tem-
perature.
